<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Good day, PM editors.
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service budget for
afternoon papers of Tuesday, April 14, 1998. All stories have moved.
For questions about Post stories, call (202) 334-7666 between 10 a.m.
and 1 a.m. EST; for questions about Times and other Times Mirror
stories, call (213) 237-7832 between 11 a.m. and 2 a.m. EST.
<p>
    &UR; Financial:
<p>
   BANKS-ASSESS  &MD;  Is the neighborhood bank gone for good? The answer
is no, but there's also no question that midsized regional banks in
the United States are being gobbled up or are merging at a record
pace. 720 words, by Debora Vrana and Michael Hiltzik (Times).
<p>
   BANKS-SANFRANCISCO  &MD;  SAN FRANCISCO  &MD;  Despite assurances from Bank
of America that little will change, San Franciscans are worried about
the loss of the headquarters of one of the city's best-known
institutions. 1,255 words, by Mary Curtius (Times) .
<p>
   BANKS-CONSUMERS  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  NationsBank and BankAmerica Corp.
executives laud the added customer convenience that could come from
their merger deal, but several NationsBank customers voice worries
that their bank's latest colossal merger could result in a new round
of fees, more bureaucracy and a decline in service. 1,040 words, by
Beth Berselli and Stephanie Stoughton (Post).
<p>
   BANKS-DEAL  &MD;  NEW YORK  &MD;  With the current merger frenzy in the
financial services arena  &MD;  Citicorp and Travelers, BancOne Corp. and
First Chicago NBD Corp, NationsBank and BankAmerica  &MD;  there will be a
number of men who are used to being at the top who find themselves
either sharing power or stepping into the unfamiliar position of
second banana; management experts uniformly warn that the pooling of
executive power in these circumstances is never easy. 1,000 words, by
Sharon Walsh (Post).
<p>
    &UR; National:
<p>
   GINGRICH  &MD;  MANCHESTER, N.H.  &MD;  Having weathered a grueling ethics
investigation, a GOP plot to dump him as House speaker and a steady
series of self-inflicted political wounds over the last three years,
Newt Gingrich is barnstorming the country to promote his new book  &MD; 
and inviting speculation that he is going to run for president. 1,140
words, by Janet Hook (Times).
<p>
   CLINTON-TAXES  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  While no longer millionaires,
President Clinton and first lady Hillary Rodham Clinton paid $91,964
in federal taxes last year on adjusted gross income of $569,511, the
White House reports. 370 words, by Jonathan Peterson (Times).
<p>
   CLINTON  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  President Clinton's personal lawyer launches
a broadside at independent counsel Kenneth W. Starr, charging in a
letter released that Starr cannot ``credibly or appropriately''
investigate allegations that key Whitewater witness David Hale was
paid off by conservative activists. 855 words, by Susan Schmidt and
Robert Suro (Post).
<p>
   ASHCROFT  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  Sen. John D. Ashcroft, R-Mo., has taken a
commanding lead in the battle to win the support of Christian right
leaders, eclipsing two better-known rivals in what amounts to the
first Republican presidential primary for 2000. 1,550 words, by Thomas
B. Edsall (Post).
<p>
   JACKSON  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  Jesse L. Jackson, preacher, civil rights
activist, television host, politician and sometime free-lance
diplomat, has taken up a new cause: rebuilding Liberia. 660 words, by
Thomas W. Lippman (Post).
<p>
   WATERGATE  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  A federal judge throws out a civil lawsuit
brought against convicted Watergate burglar G. Gordon Liddy by a
former employee of the Democratic National Committee who claimed she
was defamed by Liddy on his radio show and on other occasions. 590
words, by Walter Pincus (Post).
<p>
   AIRSAFETY  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  Intensified efforts to solve a variety of
problems affecting commercial aviation safety will be announced
Tuesday by Vice President Al Gore and top Federal Aviation
Administration officials, aviation sources say. 540 words, by Eric
Malnic and Robert L. Jackson (Times).
<p>
   WATER  &MD;  A team of scientists using the European Space Agency's
Infrared Space Observatory reports it has detected a gas cloud 1,500
light years from Earth that produces vast quantities of water. 770
words, by Frank D. Roylance (Sun).
<p>
    &UR; International:
<p>
   CUBA-POLICY  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  The Clinton administration, treading
gently to overcome opposition from Senate Foreign Relations Committee
Chairman Jesse Helms, is working behind the scenes to marshal support
for bipartisan legislation to exempt food and medicine from the U.S.
trade embargo against Cuba. 760 words, by Jack Nelson (Times).
<p>
   ULSTER  &MD;  BELFAST, Northern Ireland  &MD;  The opening of Belfast's
often-contentious ``marching season'' comes off quietly, marked by the
same spirit of cooperation that helped spawn Friday's far-reaching
peace accord. 525 words, by T.R. Reid (Post).
<p>
   PERSECUTE  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  One of two Christians in the 32-member
Egyptian Cabinet says he will lobby the U.S. Congress against American
legislation to penalize countries that persecute religious minorities
or fail to stop atrocities by members of a dominant faith. 600 words,
by Norman Kempster (Times).
<p>
   NATIONS  &MD;  UNITED NATIONS  &MD;  U.N. weapons experts say their most
recent inspections of sites in Iraq, including formerly off-limits
presidential buildings, have revealed no signs that Iraq possesses
prohibited nuclear weapons or materiel. 735 words, by John M. Goshko
(Post).
<p>
    &UR; Health and Science:
<p>
   CIGARS  &MD;  A national anti-smoking organization demands that the
Federal Trade Commission impose health warning labels on U.S. cigar
packaging and advertising; the FTC chairman says his agency might
respond within two months with what would be a landmark ruling. 690
words, by Alec Klein (Sun).
<p>
    &UR; Commentaries and news features are listed on the
NEWSFEATURES-LAT-WP budget that moves daily at noon EDT.

<BYLINE> By Mike Mills </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; High-Speed Data Network Fails &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   AT&AMP;T Corp.'s nationwide high-speed data network crashed Monday
afternoon, sending banks and stores and other users scrambling to
switch on backup systems. Most managed the changeover with minor
disruption; others said the failure of the ``frame relay'' network,
which continued Monday night, was causing major hardships.
<p>
   The American Red Cross, which uses the network to track more than
half of the nation's blood supply, experienced significant slowdowns.
A backup data system was designed only for regional outages, according
to Greg Hamil, senior director of wide-area networking and computer
operations.
<p>
   ``We did not anticipate our entire frame network being down. We are
operating at a significantly degraded mode,'' Hamil said. ``It's
making me very nervous. We're going to have a serious talk with
AT&AMP;T.''
<p>
   The failure underlined a risk of the emerging information age: that
as society becomes ever more linked by low-cost communications
systems, it also grows more vulnerable when they fail.
<p>
   AT&AMP;T officials could not explain the shutdown, which occurred
around 3 p.m. and had not been fixed as of Monday evening. AT&AMP;T data
networks other than frame relay were not affected. ``We are working
aggressively to identify, isolate and fix the problem,'' said AT&AMP;T
spokeswoman Ruth Lynn Newell. ``And we are currently contacting all of
our frame-relay customers to advise them of the difficulty.''
<p>
   Kim Kellogg, a spokeswoman for Wells Fargo Bank in San Francisco,
said half of the company's 1,070 bank branches in California were left
without working cash machines, phones or computer terminals. However,
the bank continued to do business. ``We're servicing customers
inside,'' Kellogg said. `` ... It just slows things down.''
<p>
   AT&AMP;T's frame-relay service customers include Fortune 500 companies,
many government agencies and large institutions, and Internet service
providers. Frame relay is a fairly new technology that has quickly
grown into a $2 billion-a-year market, according to the Boston-based
Yankee Group consulting firm.
<p>
   Newell denied the problem was comparable to a nine-hour outage that
AT&AMP;T's long-distance network suffered in 1990, disrupting voice
service coast-to-coast for millions of callers.
<p>
   The company's current problem ``is not nearly on that scale,'' she
said. ``This is not the AT&AMP;T network. The AT&AMP;T network is operating
normally. It's a subset of our data networking capabilities.''
</TEXT>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; A Clinton Promise on Debt &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The following editorial appeared in Tuesday's Washington Post:
<p>
   President Clinton went to Africa expecting to talk about the
benefits of increased trade. He found leaders who wanted to talk about
the urgent need for debt relief. And no wonder: For many of the
poorest nations of Africa (and Latin America), nothing matters more.
Many of them are struggling under a burden of debt so crushing that
the aid and trade income they receive simply gets recycled back north
in the form of interest payments. Meanwhile, little is left to pay for
health, education and other programs that could begin to relieve
poverty.
<p>
   Two years ago the major creditor nations, including the United
States, acknowledged the foolishness of this debt-service carousel.
Together with the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund, they
shaped the first comprehensive program of debt relief for countries
that were pursuing reasonable policies likely to produce economic
growth. Their plan was hailed as a major breakthrough for developing
nations.
<p>
   In practice, results have been modest. Only six countries have been
approved for debt relief (four in Africa and two in South America),
and only three will actually receive any assistance by the end of this
year. Many more countries that badly need help won't be eligible,
under the plan's stringent criteria, for years to come.
<p>
   These criteria include a record of six unbroken years of good
economic performance. The impulse behind this condition is right;
there's no point extending aid to regimes that will squander it. But
inflexibly making every country wait so long risks missing an
opportunity to help countries that really want to help themselves;
many governments dedicated to poverty reduction and economic growth
simply won't survive six years without debt relief.
<p>
   Oxfam International, one of the advocacy groups that has led the
fight for debt relief, notes that the international community quickly
mobilized more than $100 billion in credit to ease Asia's financial
crisis, but in Africa  &MD;  where the per-country needs often run in the
hundreds of millions  &MD;  the political will is missing. Mr. Clinton said
he had promised South African President Nelson Mandela and other
African leaders to ``see what I could do to make sure that we give as
much aid as we possibly can'' and ``to stay on top of this'' once he
returned to Washington. This week, with the world's finance ministers
in town for the annual meetings of the World Bank and IMF, would be a
good time to begin redeeming that promise.
</TEXT>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Check local listings for time and channel &QL; 
 &UR; Huxley's Story of a `New World' &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   In a TV-movie version of Aldous Huxley's landmark 1932 novel,
``Brave New World'' (Sunday at 9 on NBC), children are conceived and
born in high-tech factories and raised under conditions deemed ideal
to ensure that everyone will be happy, healthy, socially responsible
and productive.
<p>
   As a result, the civilized world is free of crime, pain, disease
and fear. But for one couple  &MD;  Bernard and Lenina  &MD;  something seems to
be lacking. Then they meet a ``savage,'' a man of depth and feeling
who has much to teach them about the ``old'' ways.
<p>
   MY DATE WITH THE PRESIDENT'S DAUGHTER
<p>
   Sunday at 7 on ABC
<p>
   Will Friedle plays a shy high-school junior whose first-ever date
is with Hallie Richmond. Turns out that she's the daughter of George
Richmond, president of the United States, but at first, Will doesn't
realize that  &MD;  after all, he asked her for a date on a bet. So when he
arrives at 1600 Pennsylvania Ave., he thinks he's the victim of a
practical joke.
<p>
   Still, determined to have fun, he and Hallie set out to give the
Secret Service the slip. Elisabeth Harnois plays Hallie, Dabney
Coleman is the president, Mimi Kuzyk is the first lady, Jay Thomas is
Will's dad and Ronald Reagan Jr. is a Secret Service agent. Music is
by the group the Presidents of the United States of America, with
Chris Ballew.
<p>
   THE PEOPLE'S CENTURY
<p>
   Sunday-Tuesday at 9 on PBS
<p>
   John Forsythe and Alfre Woodard narrate this extensive and
ambitious series spotlighting the turbulent 20th century, a time of
rising hopes and aspirations, widening inequality and mass
manipulation, freedom and prosperity, and social and political
transformation.
<p>
   The series begins with ``Age of Hope,'' as the bright new century
opens. But the optimism doesn't last long. The second hour, ``Killing
Fields,'' recounts how soldiers faced death in a ghastly global war.
Other shows in this 13-part series will be telecast this summer and in
1999. The series is a co-production of WGBH in Boston and the BBC.
<p>
   THE ECHO OF THUNDER
<p>
   Sunday at 9 on CBS
<p>
   Judy Davis and Jamey Sheridan play Australian Outback farmers whose
lives  &MD;  and those of their three children  &MD;  change dramatically with
the arrival of his 15-year-old daughter.
<p>
   WB SITCOMS
<p>
   THE ONE (Sunday at 9:30)  &MD;  Cynthia Geary plays a Southern belle and
Elon Gold is a Jewish fellow from Long Island who meet, marry and cope
with their in-laws.
<p>
   KELLY (Monday at 9)  &MD;  Shelley Long is Kelly Novak, a professor of
English who marries a fire chief with four children. Robert Hays plays
Chief Doug Kelly.
 &UR; End adv wknd apr 18-19 &QL; 
</TEXT>

<BYLINE> By Martie Zad </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; A Marilyn Monroe Trilogy &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Celebrating the 50th anniversary of Marilyn Monroe's film debut,
Twentieth Century Fox Home Entertainment is releasing a commemorative
collection of three of her popular movies in a set called ``Marilyn
Monroe: 50 Years a Star!'' on Tuesday.
<p>
   The three films: ``Gentlemen Prefer Blondes'' (1953) with Jane
Russell and Charles Coburn, ``The Seven Year Itch'' (1955) with Tom
Ewell and ``Let's Make Love'' (1960), also released as ``The
Billionaire'' and ``The Millionaire.''
<p>
   Monroe made 30 films in 15 years from ``The Shocking Miss Pilgrim''
in 1947 to ``The Misfits'' in 1961. Each of the three videos includes
the film's original theatrical trailer, rare Movietone clips of
Monroe, and is accompanied by a mail-in offer for ``Marilyn Monroe:
The Mortal Goddess,'' a one-hour ``A&AMP;E Biography'' video that will be
sent to purchasers of all three films for only a shipping and handling
charge.
<p>
   The triple-pack is listed at $39.98. Movies can be purchased
separately for $14.98.
<p>
    &UR; -O- &QC; 
<p>
   Another collectable due out Tuesday is ``Tom Jones,'' a six-volume
set on the legendary exploits of the 18th-century playboy that aired
on A&AMP;E Network recently.
<p>
   Max Beesley stars in the title role of the five-hour production of
Henry Fielding's 1749 novel. Samantha Morton co-stars as Sophia
Western. The A&AMP;E Home Video set lists for $99.95.
<p>
   Six glamorous and extraordinary women from Hollywood's Golden Age
star in seven films also being released Tuesday in Republic Pictures's
``Legendary Ladies of the Silver Screen,'' series at $14.98 each.
<p>
   Ava Gardner stars in two of them, ``One Touch of Venus'' (50th
anniversary) with Robert Walker and ``Tam Lin,'' which was directed by
Roddy McDowall. The other five stars and films are Angela Lansbury
with George Sanders in ``The Private Affairs of Bel Ami,'' Jean Arthur
with Charles Coburn in ``The Devil and Miss Jones,'' Joan Fontaine
with Louis Jordan in ``Letter From an Unknown Woman,'' Claudette
Colbert with Don Ameche in ``Guest Wife'' and Paulette Goddard with
Reginald Owen in ``Diary of a Chambermaid.''
<p>
   Due out next week: ``Alfred Hitchcock: Master of Suspense,'' a
58-minute retrospective of the legendary director who burned horror
into the minds of millions of moviegoers, although he preferred to be
called the ``Master of Suspense and Anxiety'' rather than the ``Master
of Horror.''
<p>
   The video is filled with clips of Hitchcock's classics including
``Psycho,'' ``North By Northwest'' ``The Birds'' and ``Frenzy.''
($19.98, 1-800-414-1690).
 &UR; End adv wknd apr 18-19 &QL;

<SLUG> bc-tv-eco </SLUG>

<PREAMBLE>
bc-tv-eco adv1819
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: TV editors) &QL; 
 &UR; Adv wknd apr 18-19 &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Harriet Winslow </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Check local listings for time and channel &QL; 
 &UR; Discovery's Adventure Race &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   It's not just a game, it's an adventure  &MD;  one where only the
world's most elite endurance athletes dare to compete.
<p>
   An international race that pushes teams of three men and one woman
to the physical and mental breaking point, the Discovery Channel
Eco-Challenge, returning to television Sunday, tires you out just
watching.
<p>
   This year's Eco-Challenge, completed in October in North
Queensland, Australia, airs over three nights (Sunday and Monday at 9,
Tuesday at 10) on the cable channel, and is rife with blood, sweat and
tears. The race takes place in a different country annually, but this
one is filled with biting spiders, hungry crocodiles, stinging plants
and, for 10 days, moving, sleep-deprived humans.
<p>
   All can be obstacles to the finish line of the Eco-Challenge, which
is gaining in popularity, said race director Mark Burnett, a Londoner
who immigrated to the United States in 1982. ``It's one of the few
major sporting events where the winner isn't the focus. It's not all
about winning,'' he said. ``It's about the experience. And so few
sports do that.''
<p>
   Burnett started the Eco-Challenge in 1992. In that one, in
Madagascar, he finished in the fastest American team.
<p>
   ``I also have failed to finish,'' he said. ``I know a lot from
being the most successful  &MD;  and not successful.''
<p>
   Burnett chooses the country for each year's race and maps a course,
then times it. He also charts rescue routes from all points.
<p>
   ``There's nothing on the course that's not done by us,'' said
Burnett.
<p>
   This year's event is 335 miles. Forty-eight teams begin with a
59-mile bushwalk. Then there's a 26-mile river canoe, a 30-mile
canyon-rim trek (with rappel), a 112-mile mountain bike, a 12-mile
whitewater raft, a 26-mile rain-forest trek, a 21-mile horseback ride
and a 49-mile sea kayak.
<p>
   There are 31 mandatory check-in points where passports must be
stamped. All food (except one hot meal) must be carried, and mandatory
safety gear, such as helmets and first-aid kits, must accompany the
teams over the finish line, which will take the winners fewer than six
days to reach. Teams are disqualified if they don't finish within 10
days.
<p>
   Sleep is sacrificed  &MD;  ``You can't have a reserve of it,'' said
Burnett  &MD;  and some people handle fatigue better than others.
<p>
   Discovery's five-hour miniseries is edited from 500 hours of film,
he said. ``We chose many more teams to focus on than you see on
television. Then when we come back we look at what happens. Some have
more interest than others.''
<p>
   This year's notables include the Japanese, an unfortunate team from
Northern Ireland and U.S. Navy SEALs.
<p>
   Despite the fact that the majority of entrants are American, a
number that Burnett now limits, he believes their emphasis on winning
``is why we don't see American teams do that well.''
<p>
   The key to enduring this race, Burnett insisted, is that 85 percent
depends on the team dynamic. The rest is physical.
<p>
   ``Helping each other is the attitude that helps win,'' he said.
``I'm hoping that Eco-Challenge-type behavior will spill over into
realms outside of sports.''
<p>
   Win or lose, it's when things go wrong that the Eco-Challenge gets
interesting. For example, despite Burnett's scrutinized screening of
applicants, a yearlong newsletter filled with warnings, plus the
teams' own rigorous training, he finds that teams will still ``hear
what they want to.'' And that means that some show up unprepared.
<p>
   Take this year's contestants from Northern Ireland, who quickly
discover that their backpacks are too heavy, with a cheeseboard, a
Walkman and sleeping bags. (Most teams wouldn't dream of lugging
sleeping bags, let alone housewares.)
<p>
   ``The Northern Ireland team was a mountain rescue team. These are
great athletes and good back-country people,'' said Burnett. Still, he
added, ``No matter how much we tell people, they have to experience
the first year to get it.''
<p>
   Indeed, despite the bruising course, every competitor has applied
to return.
<p>
   ``This is such an attractive thing for people,'' he said. ``There's
a shift towards nature-based things: (the Eco-Challenge) is totally
out of your control. So people are looking for something totally
uncontrollable.''
<p>
   Burnett foresees a time when each country stages its own race, then
enters one team. That way the race would become a kind of olympics for
the millennium, he said.
<p>
   In the next event, in Morocco, there will be 30 countries
represented, including India, Malaysia and the United Arab Emirates.
<p>
   ``We're so happy about the way this thing has grown,'' he said.
<p>
   It's not just physical prowess that will get a team accepted. ``It
really is trying to find a diversely experienced team that's going to
get along,'' Burnett emphasized.
<p>
   For example, late into this year's race, the Navy SEAL team faces a
crisis. But Burnett praised the team leader for being so calm. ``He's
a professional. That's the kind of people we're looking for. ... But
still, we can't protect people from themselves.''
<p>
   And without rest, humans become unpredictable.
<p>
   Also unpredictable is Mother Nature.
<p>
   ``In British Columbia there was an enormous storm,'' he recalled.
``In Australia, an ocean storm. In Morocco, I guess we'll have a
sandstorm.''
<p>
   Web-watchers can follow the race in Morocco from Oct. 6 to 16 on
www.discovery.com.
 &UR; End adv wknd apr 18-19 &QL; 
</TEXT>

bc-tv-singer adv1819

</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Scott Moore </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Check local listings for time and channel &QL; 
 &UR; The Heartfelt Soul of `Ally McBeal' &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
    &UR; I've been down this road walkin' the line
<p>
    &UR; That's painted by pride
<p>
    &UR; And I have made mistakes in my life
<p>
    &UR; That I just can't hide.
<p>
    &UR; Oh I believe I am ready for what love has to bring
<p>
    &UR; Got myself together, now I'm ready to sing.
<p>
    &MD;  Vonda Shepard, ``Searchin' My Soul''
<p>
    &UR; -O- &QC; 
<p>
   She's the striking blond piano player in the trendy yuppie bar
frequented on-camera by ``Ally McBeal's'' off-the-wall cast. She's the
soulful vocalist whose words parallel the thoughts of the title
character  &MD;  who in turn represents the working woman of the '90s.
<p>
   She's also the non-television watcher whose video will be played
for about 10 million Fox viewers before ``Ally McBeal's'' closing
credits on May 4. The following day, the show soundtrack  &MD;  for which
Sony paid a reported $3 million rights fee  &MD;  hits stores.
<p>
   Not bad for a 34-year-old musician who a year ago lugged her
keyboard through a New York City rain to play before a crowd of 35
people.
<p>
   Bygones.
<p>
   Although it took 20 years for her to achieve this ``sudden''
acclaim, Vonda Shepard's soul contains no animosity or bitterness.
<p>
   Hey, she's been here before. Shepard traveled the world as a band
member for Rickie Lee Jones, Al Jarreau and Jackson Browne. She hit
No. 6 on the pop charts with ``Can't We Try, '' a duet with Dan Hill
in 1987. She borrowed nearly $100,000 from friends, family and former
boyfriends to produce her third album, ``It's Good, Eve,'' in 1996.
<p>
   ``It's been a long road,'' said Shepard, 34. ``I've only wanted to
reach the public. I've just been given this opportunity of exposure.''
<p>
   She has no complaints about the 12- to 14-hour days on the set of
the Monday night Fox series, nor of the all-night recording sessions
to finish the soundtrack. And certainly no qualms about a two-month
summer tour that includes a New York City show at the 1,000-seat
Irving Plaza. (``Hopefully people will show this time,'' she joked.)
<p>
   She also has a fourth solo album in the works for next year.
<p>
   ``Everything is better,'' she said. ``I'm working every single day
and not sitting at home with all this energy, saying, `What happened?'
My father told me when I was younger that a spiritual leader said work
is good for the soul, and I totally believe that.''
<p>
    &UR; I've been searching my soul tonight
<p>
    &UR; I know there's so much more to life
<p>
    &UR; Now I know I can shine a light
<p>
    &UR; To find my way back home
<p>
   The light at the end of the tunnel for Vonda Shepard came courtesy
of David E. Kelley, who attended her show about the time he was
creating ``Ally McBeal.''
<p>
   In Shepard  &MD;  a longtime friend of Kelley's wife Michelle Pfeiffer,
who had the same voice coach  &MD;  the television producer found an
on-camera lounge singer whose original and cover songs give voice to
the conscious and subconscious thoughts of insecure young lawyer Ally
McBeal.
<p>
   Kelley also found the show's theme song, ``Searchin' My Soul,'' on
the lead track of Shepard's 1992 album ``The Radical Light,'' which
sold fewer than 6,000 copies.
<p>
   ``It's just a timing issue,'' Shepard said. ``Right now, obviously,
my music fits perfectly with what's happening. It just shows that if
you stay true to what you do, it's going to come around eventually.''
<p>
   Though she has written a few songs since the show started taping,
only the instrumental interludes are written expressly for the show.
``I just write what I write for my own personal albums, and David
picks what he wants,'' she said.
<p>
   So far, Kelley has used about a dozen Shepard songs from the 64 she
has submitted.
<p>
   The irony of the success is not lost on her. She has escaped bar
gigs through this very public bar gig. She has gained attention for
her original songs by doing other composers' songs for the first time.
And she has done it all on television, which she doesn't watch.
<p>
   ``I don't watch it at all,'' she admitted. ``I'm probably missing
some good stuff. I've seen only four `Seinfeld' episodes, and I'm
going to watch all the re-runs for the next nine years. I'm just not a
sitcom person, and dramas are too heavy for me.''
<p>
   Instead, she spends her time writing in a journal for an hour a
day, and up to five hours composing.
<p>
   ``It really starts with a feeling inside; it's almost visceral,''
she said of her songwriting, which tends toward introspective folk
rock. ``I can tell when I'm inspired, and even before I touch the
piano I turn on the tape recorder because I know something good is on
the horizon. Usually, in those moments of great inspiration, it comes
out as a melody and then sometimes gibberish or actual words. ...
<p>
   ``I think of it as letting go of the controlling part of the brain
and letting the subconscious take over.''
 &UR; End adv wknd apr 18-19 &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-tv-singer-theme </SLUG>
<HEADER> adv1819   04-14 0274 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-tv-singer-theme adv1819
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: TV editors) &QL; 
 &UR; Adv wknd apr 18-19 &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Scott Moore </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Check local listings for time and channel &QL; 
 &UR; There's a Theme Here &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   David E. Kelley's initial choice for the ``Ally McBeal'' theme song
was ``Tell Him,'' a '60s pop tune by the Exciters. He settled on Vonda
Shepard's ``Searchin' My Soul.''
<p>
   Conversely, on a recent episode, neurotic lawyer Ally McBeal,
played by Calista Flockhart, first chose ``Searchin' My Soul'' as her
personal theme. She ended up with ``Tell Him'' after her therapist,
guest star Tracey Ullman, vetoed ``Searchin' '' with a succinct
review: ``Bad song. Stinks!''
<p>
   ``I just cracked up,'' recalled Shepard of the Monday lunch-time
screening with Kelley and the actors. ``David's not afraid to make fun
of everybody, including his cast.''
<p>
   Such internal plot twists are enjoyable for Shepard, who needn't
worry about what Kelley really thinks of her songs. He reportedly
blares her CDs while writing the offbeat scripts.
<p>
   And the personal theme for the woman who supplies the beat for Ally
McBeal?
<p>
   ``I'm always writing in my head,'' she said after a moment. ``My
theme song is whatever is inspiring me at the time with whatever I'm
writing. Isn't that narcissistic? Only my own songs!''
<p>
   After more thought, and no doubt energized by a morning cup of
coffee after an all-night recording session, she finally belted out a
refrain: ``Forget your troubles, c'mon get happy. ... ''
<p>
   ``That's a nice corny one,'' she said with a hearty laugh.
``They'll probably use that one on `Ally McBeal' soon.''
 &UR; End adv wknd apr 18-19 &QL;

<SLUG> bc-fearless-traveler </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0780 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-fearless-traveler
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Travel editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Carol Sottili </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Ask the Fearless Traveler &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Q: We are planning a trip to Senegal for our honeymoon. Can you
suggest places for us to stay and visit? We want to spend some time in
Cap Skirring and the Casamance area.
<p>
    &MD;  Fatimah Mateen, Alexandria, Va.
<p>
   A. Senegal is the travel destination du jour. President Clinton's
recent visit there has helped spotlight this West African country, one
of the region's most economically developed and politically tolerant
nations. Plus it's an easy seven-hour nonstop flight from New York, it
has beautiful beaches and its history as a key debarkation point in
the slave trade has made it a popular destination for
African-Americans researching their heritage.
<p>
   But Senegal is not without problems. An episodic, 15-year-old
rebellion in the Casamance region flares up occasionally, and since
August, several hundred have been reported killed and Amnesty
International has criticized the government, citing human rights
violations. Senegal remains generally safe for tourists, however,
except for certain areas within the Casamance region. The State
Department has issued no travel warnings for Senegal, but it does urge
U.S. citizens traveling to the Casamance region to contact the U.S.
Embassy in Dakar for the latest travel and security information. Cap
Skirring, a tourist village in the Casamance region, has remained
largely insulated from the internal strife.
<p>
   If you're interested in a resort vacation, Cap Skirring is home to
a Club Med and the beachfront Savanna Hotel. If you'd rather avoid
Casamance altogether, an alternative is Mbour, about a 90-minute drive
south of Dakar. Beachfront resorts there include the Novotel Saly and
the Hotel Royam. To book hotels in Senegal, go through a travel agent.
Agents that specialize in West Africa include Haggins International
Tours, 212-563-2570, and Boston-based Spector Travel at
1-800-879-2374.
<p>
   These agents can also book a heritage tour or an outside adventure
tour. A week-long group trip generally runs about $1,300 per person,
including air fare from New York, some meals, accommodations,
transportation and guide.
<p>
   Q. My husband and I are planning a trip to celebrate our fifth
anniversary. He likes cool climates and skiing; I prefer warm climates
and the beach. We would have about a week and could travel anywhere.
Any suggestions?
<p>
    &MD;  Marianne La Rocco, Harrisonburg, Va.
<p>
   A. When we lived in San Diego, we used to take delight in calling
relatives back east to tell them we had hit the beach in the morning
and the ski slopes that afternoon. Because California is such a
vertical state, it's possible to sit on the beach and throw snowballs
on the same day. But to enjoy optimal conditions, you'll have to
travel from one end of the state to the other to do both.
<p>
   Depending on how ambitious you are, you can either hit Lake Tahoe
for skiing and then go down to Palm Springs, or you can go to several
ski resorts in the Sierras, stop in Palm Springs and then head to the
San Diego beaches. With only a week, I'd keep it simple.
<p>
   Reno is the best airport to fly into. From there, it's an easy
drive to Lake Tahoe, where the ski season often goes until mid-April.
There are lots of hotels in South Lake Tahoe, most of which offer ski
packages. I liked the Best Western Station House Inn (1-800-822-5953),
a reasonably priced, centrally located hotel. While your husband skis
Heavenly, you can hit the casinos or take a lake cruise. Information:
1-800-288-2463 (1-800-AT-TAHOE), http://www.virtualtahoe.com.
<p>
   From there, head to Palm Springs. It's a 500-mile drive from Tahoe
to Palm Springs, so flying is a better option. Because flying directly
into Palm Springs is expensive (about $230 round trip), you can take
Southwest Airlines' shuttle from Reno to Ontario, about 60 miles west
of Palm Springs; round-trip fare is about $120. Palm Springs is a land
of championship golf courses, swimming pools and shopping.
Information: Palm Spring Desert Resorts Convention &AMP; Visitors Bureau,
1-800-417-3529 (1-800-41-RELAX), http://www.desert-resorts.com.
<p>
   If you just have to see the Pacific, you can take a side trip to
San Diego, about 150 miles west of Palm Springs. With ocean
temperatures not much above 60, swimming may not be comfortable, but
the air will be a delightful 70 degrees or so. Stay at the La Jolla
Beach and Tennis Club (619-454-7126) and eat at least one meal at its
oceanfront restaurant. Shop on La Jolla's Prospect Street, watch the
Padres play ball at Qualcomm Stadium, and take boardwalk hikes at
Mission Beach.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-worldwise </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0307 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-worldwise
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Travel, Feature editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Carol Sottili </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1997, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Quick Travel Takes &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   AmeriSpan, a Philadelphia-based operator of Spanish-immersion
programs around the world, is doing its best to deal with U.S.
travelers' fears about Guatemala, newly ripe since five U.S. college
students were raped at gunpoint in January. But since the ``good''
news is that police now escort tourists to popular destinations  &MD;  and
that among AmeriSpan's tips you'll find the alarming tidbit ``Make
sure you trust your tour guide or shuttle bus driver''  &MD;  it's clear
that Guatemala, while awakened to its security problems, remains a
destination for the braver travelers among us.
<p>
    &UR; -O- &QC; 
<p>
   MetroJet, US Airways' low-fare spinoff based at
Baltimore-Washington International airport, hasn't carried its first
passengers, and it's already announcing three new destinations: Miami,
Jacksonville, Fla., and Tampa/St. Petersburg, Fla., to begin July 6.
Flights to Cleveland, Providence, R.I., Fort Lauderdale, Fla., and
Manchester, N.H., begin June 1. (With the exception of Cleveland, all
were previously served by US Airways.) MetroJet will offer lower
fares, fewer advance purchase restrictions and no-fee ticket
exchanges. Info: 1-888-638-7653; http://www.usairways.com
<p>
    &UR; -O- &QC; 
<p>
   The British Tourist Authority's new 80-site map of Literary Britain
is a gem, not for the expected sites (Hardy's Dorset, Austen's Bath)
but for the obscurities (Dylan Thomas' Wales boathouse; Lyme Regis,
the south coast resort where the Austens summered). Free:
1-800-462-2748.
<p>
    &UR; -O- &QC; 
<p>
   Air France is offering $299 round-trip fares to introduce its new
nonstop service between Atlanta and Paris. The fares are good for
midweek travel June 19-29. Friday-through-Sunday travel is $50 more.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-travel-newyork </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 1588 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-travel-newyork
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Carolyn Spencer Brown </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; 10 N.Y. Hotels That Won't Bust a Budget &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The most interesting new trend in New York hotels is the
mainstreaming of low-budget establishments. No longer is the penurious
traveler relegated to roach hotels in dodgy neighborhoods; ``cheap''
lodgings  &MD;  costing no more than $125 per night, slightly more than
half of the city's $200 per-day average (before the 13.25 percent
occupancy tax and $2 occupancy fee are tacked on)  &MD;  can be clean,
comfortable, secure and, occasionally, stylish.
<p>
   All of our favorites offer rooms with private baths and (in almost
all cases) color televisions and telephones. They're located in great
neighborhoods.
<p>
   But be warned: Our picks offer terrific value in one of the
nation's most expensive cities, but they are still tourist-class
establishments. A few toss in an upmarket amenity  &MD;  a VCR perhaps, or
an in-room CD player. But for the most part, you should count on
bringing your own hair dryer, alarm clock, bathrobe and shampoo.
<p>
   Pack lightly, as you may have to schlep your bags up stairs. If you
drive, staying at these places means no on-site parking. For air
conditioning, expect window units. Your room will be small. The view
likely will be uninspiring. You can usually forget about attentive,
personal service. Harried desk clerks are juggling too many other
tasks to check you in or out smoothly.
<p>
   And perhaps most important: Plan ahead. These places book up fast.
<p>
   All that said, you get a bed for a night in New York for a fee that
won't tap out your credit card. And this is one case where you do get
more than you pay for.
<p>
   Amsterdam Court Hotel (Midtown/Theater District)
<p>
   This elegant 110-room hotel is slightly at odds with its setting
next to the Peepland/Bare Elegance Topless Bar &AMP; Lounge. Rooms are
spare, stark and minimalist, all beige and black with tubular
furniture (open shelves serve as a bureau). Bathrooms are sleek, white
and functional. Perks: in-room CD players and hair dryers. Continental
breakfast included, but it's much more fun to walk a block over to
51st Street to Ellen's Stardust Diner and tuck into a real repast.
<p>
   226 W. 50th St. between Broadway and Eighth Avenue, (212) 459-1000.
Rates normally are $135 to $145, but Quikbook (1-800-789-9887) often
has doubles for $125. Overnight parking: $12 to $17 in numerous lots
between Broadway and Eighth Avenue.
<p>
   Beacon Hotel (Upper West Side)
<p>
   With such gourmet markets as Zabar's and Fairway as neighbors, the
best thing about this 200-room, 35-floor hotel is that all rooms come
with fully equipped kitchenettes. Otherwise, it's your basic corporate
hotel  &MD;  pleasant, characterless  &MD;  only with tinier rooms. The onetime
apartment hotel (there are still some permanent dwellers) offers
amenities like voice mail, clock radios, coffeemakers and hair dryers.
And elevators. Great neighborhood.
<p>
   2130 Broadway (at 75th Street), (212) 787-1100,
http://www.slsbeaconhotel.com. Weekend rates are $125 (weekdays run
$30 to $40 more). Overnight parking: $20 at 76th Street between
Broadway and Amsterdam.
<p>
   Broadway Inn (Midtown/Theater District)
<p>
   The second-floor lobby is magnificent  &MD;  exposed brick walls,
fireplace (decorative for now), cozy couches, bistro tables. The rest
of the place, though pleasant, doesn't rise to these lofty heights.
The rooms are minuscule but tastefully decorated, and you get warm and
attentive service. The 41-room inn, in a three-story Victorian
brownstone (no elevators), is across from the trendy Paramount Hotel
and the Lunt-Fontanne Theatre, currently showing ``Titanic.''
Amenities include clock radios, hair dryers and a continental
breakfast, and JR's Irish Pub next door gives guests a 20 percent
discount.
<p>
   264 W. 46th St. between Broadway and Eighth Avenue, (212) 997-9200,
http://www.broadwayinn.com. Rates are $85 to $135. Overnight parking:
$30 at numerous 46th Street lots.
<p>
   The Carlton Arms (Gramercy Park)
<p>
   In this wacky 54-room hotel, every guest room is an original work
of art. The clientele is as young, hip and adventurous as the
interiors. Among the favorites is the submarine room, with its
``view'' of the Lost City of Atlantis, and the stuffed-animal room,
with plush mammals even clinging to the ceiling. No TVs; there's a
public telephone in the second-floor lobby. No breakfast is included
(though Cafe Loon Loon is next door). Stay more than one night and you
make your own bed.
<p>
   160 E. 25th St. between Third and Lexington avenues, (212)
679-0680, http://www.hotwired.com/gallery/96/04/carlton.html. Prices
range from $62 to $76; some shared baths. Overnight parking: $18 on
Third Street between 24th and 25th streets.
<p>
   The Cosmopolitan (TriBeCa)
<p>
   The entrance  &MD;  a narrow glass door next to a deli  &MD;  is so tacky
you'll mistake the Cosmopolitan for a welfare hotel. But once you're
inside, the sleek blond wood lobby is reassuring. Rooms are cozy, with
white Formica furnishings, armoire, ceiling fan and a desk with
modem-ready phone. The bathroom, with three-foot tub, is tiny.
Fabulous location  &MD;  six steps from IRT express and close to SoHo and
Chinatown  &MD;  as well as all the hot TriBeCa restaurants.
<p>
   95 W. Broadway at Chambers, (212) 566-1900,
http://www.cosmohotel.com. Rates are $89 for a single, $119 for a
double. Overnight parking: $18 weekends on Reade, off of Hudson.
<p>
   The Gershwin (Chelsea)
<p>
   The Gershwin, with its lobby art by such pop icons as Lichtenstein
and Warhol, is so arty, funky and hip that people stay here as much
for the atmosphere as the bargains. Rooms in the 106-room, 13-story
hotel are stylish enough  &MD;  walls painted in pastel green or yellow
with hand-painted murals adding color. The minimalist furnishings are
either Philippe Starck-influenced or the real thing. But the real
scene is in the lobby, which doubles as an art gallery, and leads to
such hotel hideaways as the Red Room, an intimate bar, and the Back
Room, a comedy and music venue. A cafe serves light fare.
<p>
   7 E. 27th St. between Madison and Fifth avenues, (212) 545-8000,
http://www.gershwinhotel.com. Room rates range from $100 to $140.
Overnight parking: 30th between Fifth and Madison.
<p>
   The Malibu (Upper West Side)
<p>
   The Malibu is in the scruffy part of the Upper West Side, but its
proximity to Columbia University is a plus, and midtown, via the IRT,
is only eight minutes away. Ignore first impressions of the cavelike
second-floor lobby because the rooms are actually quite cheery, if
sparsely decorated, with sleek black tubular furniture, high ceilings,
white walls and tropical-themed bedspreads. Perks include a CD player,
clock radio and iron in each room. No elevators.
<p>
   2688 Broadway at 103rd Street, (212) 663-0275 or 1-800-647-2227,
http://www.malibuhotelnyc.com. Rates range from $79 to $129. Overnight
parking: $19 on 102nd Street, between Broadway and Amsterdam.
<p>
   The Park Savoy (Midtown)
<p>
   The nine-story, 75-room Park Savoy is unabashedly budget-minded.
Its own staffers will tell you it's nothing special, except for the
lofty address  &MD;  a block from Central Park South, and an easy walk to
Carnegie Hall and Bergdorf Goodman. You enter through a narrow,
wood-paneled hallway, where a tiny reservations desk is tucked under a
stairwell. Furnishings are in varnished wood. Rooms are often in some
state of disrepair; the cable to the television is unhooked, the
handle to the blinds is missing, you find a hair on your sheets. Hey,
you're in Midtown dirt-cheap. Waddaya expect?
<p>
   158 W. 58th St. between Sixth and Seventh avenues, (212) 245-5755.
Rates are $82.12 to $126.27. Overnight parking: $30, next door.
<p>
   Best Western Seaport Inn (Wall Street)
<p>
   Our only chain pick, this characterless 72-room hotel is a great
value. Located in a renovated 19th-century commercial loft, rooms are
outfitted with mahogany reproductions, TV/VCRs and fridges. Bathrooms
are spacious, complete with fancy soaps and hair dryers. Check out the
lobby's bizarre video fireplace, flanked by built-in bookshelves with
the very latest from Reader's Digest. Attractions: South Street
Seaport, the Fulton Fish Market and Wall Street. Better yet, don your
sneakers and hike across the Brooklyn Bridge.
<p>
   33 Peck Slip (one block south of the Brooklyn Bridge), (212)
766-6600, http://www.bestwestern.com. Weekend rates are $114 to $149.
Overnight parking: $20 at any one of three nearby lots.
<p>
   Washington Square Hotel(Greenwich Village)
<p>
   The Washington Square gets the basics right with precious little
flair. The 170-room hotel is steps away from Washington Square Park,
one of the best in the city for people watching. Rooms are merely
functional, but the rate includes a continental breakfast and use of a
small health club. Its restaurant, C3, is adjacent. The plexiglass
surrounding the front desk is jarring, but the action in the
neighborhood compensates.
<p>
   103 Waverly Pl., between Fifth and Sixth avenues at Washington
Square, 1-800-222-0418, http://www.wshotel.com. Rates are $110 to
$160. Overnight parking: $20 on Waverly Place between MacDougal and
Fifth Avenue.
<p>
   If you like pricier  &MD;  but still reasonable  &MD;  digs, you can find
bargains by using discount reservations services. Quikbook
(1-800-789-9887, http://www.quikbook.com) and Hotel Reservations
Network (1-800-964-6835, http://www.180096hotel.com) both offer a
decent selection of New York hotels with rates under $150.
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-04-14-98 0937EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>
</DOC>
<DOC>
<DOCID> latwp980414.0012 </DOCID>
<STORYID cat=t pri=r sel=tm--a> X1818 </STORYID>
<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-travel-newyork-circle </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0244 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-travel-newyork-circle
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Going Around in Circles &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   You simply must go on the Circle Line, our friends told us. It's
the way to see the city. And now that we've made the three-hour
circuit on the waterways bounding Manhattan, we're wondering how we
missed it for so long.
<p>
   From its 42nd Street pier, the Circle Line heads south down the
Hudson past the Statue of Liberty and Battery Park, then north up the
East River under the Brooklyn Bridge (go ahead, look up!), past Yankee
Stadium, up the Harlem River and then back down the Hudson. Tour
guides  &MD;  ours was the poetic John Keatts  &MD;  spotlight landmarks and
detritus alike.
<p>
   Our favorite part? Had to be when the craft cruised near Lady
Liberty, wind whipping and the air nippy despite the sun. About 50
shivering passengers stood on the bow  &MD;  a huddled mass if there ever
was one.
<p>
   But now we want more. We want to hit the sunset cruise, the jazz
cruise, the brunch cruise. And the Beast, a new Circle Line offering
described over the PA by its overexcited captain, got our blood
moving. Zipping around New York Harbor at 45 mph, the Beast blows by
most of the touristy sites.
<p>
   Bring it on.
<p>
   The three-hour CIRCLE LINE cruises ($22) depart from Pier 83, 12th
Avenue and West 42nd Street. Shorter cruises are offered at both Pier
83 and its location at South Street Seaport. Info: (212) 563-3200,
http://www.circleline.com.
</TEXT>

 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL;

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; The Cloisters: Good &AMP; Medieval &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Of all the special retreats recommended by friends of ours in New
York City, we were most intrigued by the Cloisters. This medieval art
museum perched on a hillside in upper Manhattan seems as far as one
can get from the bustle of Broadway and Times Square.
<p>
   ``I don't recommend it to just anybody,'' one friend confided,
``because it takes some energy to get there.''
<p>
   The Cloisters is, indeed, far afield, in the middle of Fort Tryon
Park on the banks of the Hudson River. But we found the trek to be
worth it. The building, composed of stones and walls brought from
medieval structures in France and other parts of Europe, has an
unusual mix of calm and grandeur.
<p>
   As if the architecture  &MD;  adorned with angels and priests carved
from stone  &MD;  were not stunning enough, the walls are decorated with
wonderful medieval artifacts and tapestries.
<p>
   Among the most remarkable art works displayed there are the Nine
Heroes Tapestries. Painted about 1385, the oversized wall hangings
memorialize both pagan and Christian heroes. Another favorite of ours
was the Gothic chapel, decorated with spectacular stained glass and
several stone tombs dating from the Middle Ages.
<p>
   Take some time to walk through Fort Tryon Park. Piped-in medieval
music enhances the peaceful ambiance. Occasional lectures and concerts
are held there, particularly on spring and summer weekends.
<p>
   THE CLOISTERS (212-923-3700) is about $15 by cab from Midtown. By
car, take the Henry Hudson Parkway north to the Fort Tryon/Cloisters
exit, one mile north of the George Washington Bridge. By bus, take the
Madison Avenue M4 bus north to the last stop. By subway, take the A
train to 190th Street and walk 10 minutes north, or board the M4 bus
at 190th Street and ride one stop north. Suggested donation: $8.
</TEXT>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; How the Planet Plays: A World of Weekends &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The weekend may seem like the quintessential American invention
(being, as it is, the perfect union of leisure, commerce and the
unabashed consumption of fossil fuels), but it's celebrated, in some
form or another, all over the globe.
<p>
   How, exactly, it's celebrated in foreign climes is something we
were curious about  &MD;  six far-flung Washington Post foreign
correspondents fill us in.

<SLUG> bc-weekend-russia </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0713 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-weekend-russia
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By David Hoffman </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Russians, Returning to the Land (Moscow) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   MOSCOW  &MD;  One of the great Moscow vistas is the approach to the city
on broad Kutuzovsky Prospekt, an impossibly wide artery that comes
roaring into the city, flanked on either side by stately old apartment
buildings. There are few sights more breathtaking than the one past
Poklonnaya Gora, the ``Bowing Hills,'' a great strolling park, with
the looming tower of Moscow State University in the background.
<p>
   But on a Friday afternoon in summer, when the air pollution hangs
like a cloud over the avenue, when all of Moscow seems to be gasping
for breath, Kutuzovsky Prospekt becomes the scene of a mammoth exodus.
No matter how wide, it seems incapable of containing the thousands and
thousands of cars  &MD;  laden down with chairs, picnic baskets,
televisions, blankets, children and pets  &MD;  that rush to escape the
metropolis.
<p>
   It is a weekend ritual: heading for the dacha. The word dacha
describes a house in the country, and there is no more typical way for
a Muscovite to spend the weekend than to flee the sweaty apartment
blocks and the headaches of work for the pleasant breezes and
camaraderie of the dacha.
<p>
   You don't have to be rich. A dacha is Everyman's escape. It can be
a small, one-room, wooden shack, sandwiched with a dozen others into a
clearing in the woods. Or maybe several rooms nestled near your own
apple orchard. And if you do have a few million dollars to spare,
there are always the unsightly, brick ``cottegi,'' the elaborate
walled three- and four-story country houses that New Russians are
building with abandon.
<p>
   No matter whether shack or mansion, the dacha routine is the same.
Here's how some of my Russian friends describe it:
<p>
   First, on Friday, there is shopping. Many dachas have primitive gas
stoves, so you want to keep cooking to a minimum. The dacha enthusiast
 &MD;  ``dachnik''  &MD;  looks for picnic food: sausage, vegetables, potatoes.
A big favorite is shashlik  &MD;  grilled marinated meat cooked over a
charcoal fire  &MD;  and many people begin the marinade on Friday morning.
<p>
   Then you have to pack the car. You really can't leave anything
valuable in the dacha during the week, so you have to bring it all: a
television, food, books, perhaps folding chairs, the dog, the kids
(and don't forget the seed packets). The most common Russian car is
the boxy, balky Zhiguli, which was modeled on a Fiat of 30 years ago.
There's not much space.
<p>
   Then you hit the road. What a zoo! The avenues and highways out of
Moscow are bumper-to-bumper for hours on Friday evening.
<p>
   It will probably be dusk when you finally arrive at the dacha, and
everyone is starving. The charcoal fires are lit up quickly and the
smell of shashlik wafts through the birches. There is a tasty late
dinner of seared shashlik, and maybe champagne or vodka. The weekend
has begun.
<p>
   In the thick of summer, the dacha is like a huge gulp of oxygen.
With trees and lush grasses, the climate is crystalline pure, compared
with the poisonous gases of the city. In visiting friends' dachas, I
often feel like it is time for a nap, so intoxicating is the fresh
air.
<p>
   But first there is work. Everyone has a garden plot to care for,
and they do so with gusto. The growing season is short and needs
enormous attention  &MD;  the land has to be tilled by hand, sown and
weeded. Working the land is deep in the Russian soul, even among the
newly rich. One of my friends says, ``Even if you look over the fence
at the fancy new cottegi,' you see they have three stories, bay
windows, Victorian roofs, and  &MD;  just like us  &MD;  they are out tilling
the soil. Potatoes and cabbages.''
<p>
   The garden is a good Saturday morning's work, at least for the
adults, while children dip in the Moscow River or race through the
woods. Then, as the sun gets hot, it's time for that nap, a long one,
and more work in the garden in the late afternoon. And evenings on
Saturday are for strolling, as the entire community roams around,
visiting friends and sharing old times. The adults have been digging
in the soil until their backs ache. And soon it will be Sunday, time
to load up the car and head back into the city on Kutuzovsky Prospekt.
</TEXT>

<HEADER> 04-14 0678 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-weekend-canada
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Howard Schneider </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Canada on Ice (Toronto) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   TORONTO  &MD;  There are two ways to deal with winter in Canada:
<p>
   Stay indoors and complain, in which case you're in for a six-month
deluge of American television, carryout pizza and hockey broadcasts.
<p>
   Or insulate your body as much as possible and do your complaining
outside. Recognizing that frozen toes and fingers are as much a part
of Canada's weekends as cream sauce in Paris or the talk-show circuit
in Washington, my family and I were determined not to be confined by
winter, a season that can last into April.
<p>
   What we learned is what most Canadians understand from the first
time they put on double-bladed skates and stumble around a rink: Cold
is a state of mind, and ice is one of the world's more-versatile
surfaces.
<p>
   You don't even have to go outside to enjoy it. People like ice here
so much they keep lots of it inside.
<p>
   Like at the Royal Canadian Curling Club. Hockey may be Canada's
passion, but in some ways curling is the national sport. Families  &MD; 
whole neighborhoods, even  &MD;  can do it together, and you don't need to
be well-coordinated or fit. As one Canadian commentator said: The
great thing about curling as a sport is that you can smoke while
you're doing it.
<p>
   Not that anyone in my family smokes. But we did kick off a recent
weekend by renting a ``sheet'' at the Royal's downtown Toronto
clubhouse and tossing rocks with our neighbors and their kids.
<p>
   For the uninitiated, curling is like shuffleboard on ice, with
large, rounded granite stones replacing the little ceramic disks.
There are bull's-eyes at either end of a rectangular patch of ice; the
aim is to slide your stone into the target, while dislodging any of
your opponent's.
<p>
   It's not as easy as it sounds. Most of our shots either stopped
pathetically short or went speeding off the far edge, forcing our
neighbors to jump aside or have their toes squished. And that's, of
course, when we weren't picking ourselves up from the ice and
inspecting for broken wrists or worse.
<p>
   Hey. It's slippery out there. And they give you slippery shoes to
make it even worse.
<p>
   That raises another important Canadian weekend topic: footwear.
This is a footwear nation. To take full advantage of winter, it
wouldn't be unusual to own a half-dozen or more sets of specialized
shoes, boots or other foot gear.
<p>
   Slippery curling shoes are only the beginning. On Saturday my son
and daughter and I laced up our skates twice, once for tag and racing
at the rink of a nearby park, the second time to join the
neighborhood's early evening game of ``shinny'' hockey.
<p>
   Shinny on a Saturday night is Canada's version of pick-up baseball
in the vacant lot on a summer evening. The equipment is minimal  &MD;  most
people play without gloves or helmets  &MD;  the conduct polite, and the
teams picked informally by throwing all the hockey sticks into a pile
at center ice, then randomly sorting them into two groups.
<p>
   At least in our neighborhood, there seemed to be room for all ages
and all skill levels  &MD;  from 7 year olds like my son, who are just
learning to skate, to 38 year olds, like myself, who are also just
well, you get the picture.
<p>
   Sunday is ski day; and while Toronto isn't exactly Aspen, it is
surprising how many places there are to pursue the sport nearby. Some
are little more than glorified sledding hills. For real snow, and a
reasonable facsimile of a downhill experience, we travel two hours
north to Collingwood and a cluster of resorts built around the hills
of the Niagara escarpment, near Georgian Bay. Feet jammed into ski
boots (of course), we ski from late morning until late afternoon, my
wife and I tending toward the more-modest slopes, while the kids dart
through the woods and push on to more-challenging terrain.
<p>
   The trips to the lodge are frequent. This is Canada, after all, and
the wind off the bay can be bitter. But they never last long.
<p>
   Who'd want to stay shut inside in such beautiful weather?
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-weekend-hongkong </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0881 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-weekend-hongkong
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Keith B. Richburg </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; What Asian Monetary Crisis? (Hong Kong) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   HONG KONG  &MD;  During any given week, this former British possession
might best be described as high-rise hell, a concrete conundrum of
glass and steel and brick, with the territory's 7 million inhabitants
toiling away in tiny cubicles mostly figuring out new ways to make
money. And that, after all, is what drives workaholic Hong Kong  &MD; 
money, or the pursuit of it  &MD;  here in the region's premier financial
center.
<p>
   But come the weekend  &MD;  ah, the glorious weekend  &MD;  and for two days,
Hong Kongers descend from the office towers and hillside apartment
blocks to enjoy the sparse few square feet set aside for leisure and
to think of new ways to spend all that money. Hong Kong becomes a city
transformed, a city in retreat from itself.
<p>
   For much of Hong Kong's expatriate community, the weekend begins
promptly at the close of business Friday. The youngish crowd of
bankers and brokers and those ubiquitous ``financial analysts''
converge on a tiny L-shaped corner called Lan Kwai Fong. The street,
and a few parallel side roads and alleyways, is dotted with such bars
as 1997, Oscar's and Yelt's Inn. The specialty at the Bit Point is
thick German beer on tap; at Al's Diner, a replica of an old
'50s-style American eatery, the drink specialty is the ``jello shot''
 &MD;  vodka mixed in a small container of hardened gelatin, swallowed in
one gulp.
<p>
   On Fridays, Lan Kwai Fong street  &MD;  blocked off to traffic for the
weekly tradition  &MD;  becomes a large street party, a place to loosen the
Hermes necktie, rendezvous with colleagues or simply observe the local
trendy set, the young Hong Kongers dressed head-to-toe in black or
young women with platform-soled boots high enough to qualify as
stilts.
<p>
   For the truly dedicated night-crawlers, the party of Lan Kwai Fong
inevitably deteriorates to a stopover at the more-downscale bar
district of Wanchai, perhaps best known as the setting for the novel
``The World of Suzie Wong.'' Suzie is now gone  &MD;  she went off to marry
William Holden in the movie version  &MD;  but some of her contemporaries
are still around, beckoning bleary-eyed expats inside. Some of the
more-notorious Wanchai discos, like Pussycat, Neptune and Strawberry,
barely get going before midnight, and imported Filipino house bands
turn out the same cover tunes week after week.
<p>
   Hong Kong is home to about 140,000 Filipinos, almost all women,
almost all employed as domestic housekeepers, or ``amahs.'' For most,
Sunday is their only day off  &MD;  meaning Saturday is the night for
bar-crawling. On Sundays, they pack the local churches, then turn
several blocks of downtown into a public plaza to sell cooked Filipino
delicacies, trade the latest gossip and meet with friends.
<p>
   Wanchai bars don't seem to close until dawn, just when the
more-hearty set is awakening for a day packed with sports activities,
a rarity in Hong Kong during the working week. Public tennis courts
here  &MD;  what few there are  &MD;  are jammed on weekends, and reservations
at least 10 days in advance are a must. In fact, with all of Hong Kong
reveling in the precious two days of weekend, advance reservations are
necessary for almost anything, including movies (reserved by phone,
including your assigned seat number) or a table for brunch at that
perennial outdoor favorite, the Peak Cafe, with a view overlooking the
picturesque southern side of Hong Kong island.
<p>
   One of the most popular retreats for a Saturday or Sunday is lunch
on one of what are known as the ``outer islands.'' Lamma, the largest
and best-known of the outer islands, has a string of open-air seafood
restaurants along the waterfront street known as, of course, Main
Street. There are no cars on Lamma, and the island's resident
population is just a few thousand or so, giving it a laid-back,
tropical feel even when the island becomes overrun by weekend
visitors.
<p>
   Lamma is accessible by a ferry boat, which leaves every two hours
or so from a downtown pier. The other route is by private junk, the
traditional name for a Chinese fishing boat, but that now means
virtually every type of pleasure craft. Most major companies here have
their own junks, and there are junks for hire on a daily basis. One
need be in Hong Kong only a matter of hours before the invitations for
a weekend junk trip start flowing. And most junk trips invariably go
to Lamma for the seafood.
<p>
   Another favorite weekend escape  &MD;  and that's what weekends are
really about in Hong Kong, escape  &MD;  is to venture further away, to the
neighboring territory of Macao. Macao is still run by Portugal, but is
scheduled to return to mainland China next year. But unlike
super-modern Hong Kong, Macao has managed to retain a sleepy,
almost-Mediterranean ambience, with its pastel buildings, Portuguese
forts, old cathedrals and the cobblestone square in the center of
town.
<p>
   Macao is also the place for legalized casino gambling, where Hong
Kong weekenders will throw down several thousand dollars a pop  &MD;  and
try to avoid getting hassled by the notorious loan sharks who hover
outside.
<p>
   Then it's back to Hong Kong, back to another Monday morning, and
another mad dash to make money.
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-04-14-98 0939EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

bc-weekend-argentina
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL;

<BYLINE> By Anthony Faiola </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Late Nights at the Tip of the Continent (Buenos Aires) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   BUENOS AIRES, Argentina  &MD;  The phone rang at 2:30 a.m., an hour at
which mny people would expect news of dead relatives. But here, in
what may well be the world's foremost city of late night, it was
merely the sound of another weekend about to begin. ``I hope you're
not in bed!'' chirped Cristian Cravello, an extroverted Argentine pal
of mine. Like most people in this country at the bottom of the world,
he believes no weekend should begin before the rest of the hemisphere
is soundly asleep. ``I'm picking you up in 30 minutes. Be ready to
party!''
<p>
   ``You realize, of course, that we'll be beginning our evening at 3
a.m.,'' I said, deadpan, lying in bed, eyes closed. ``This is
insane.''
<p>
   ``Get over it,'' he said ``You're in Buenos Aires now.''
<p>
   Indeed, while it's true that many Argentines can be seen during an
average weekend afternoon enjoying their lush parks and strolling
along European-style boulevards reminiscent of Madrid and Milan,
sensuous Buenos Aires, the mysterious and melancholy city of tango,
is, at its essence, all about the night.
<p>
   It is an evolution, say Argentines, of the cultural roots of
late-night dining passed on by immigrants from Spain and Italy, who
make up 80 percent of the country's population. But here in Argentina,
the tradition has somehow run dreadfully amok. Argentines are now so
nocturnal they make New Yorkers seem like lightweights.
<p>
   On an average weekend, Argentine restaurants open at, say, 9 p.m.
for dinner. But except for stray foreigners prowling the hemisphere's
southernmost metropolis, they are mostly empty until 10 or 10:30 p.m.
Peak dinner time starts around 11, with locals gorging on bloody
steaks and French fries until at least 1 in the morning, when the
younger crowd retreats for a quick ``disco nap'' to prepare them for
the evening of debauchery that awaits.
<p>
   The only thing early about the Argentine weekend is that it
generally starts on Thursday night. And like many major U.S. cities,
Buenos Aires has hip spots for each particular night. On Thursday
night, you're considered lower than dirt if you're not behind the
velvet rope at fashionable Buenos Aires News, where South American
paparazzi swarm, every woman looks like she graduated cum laude from
Pouty Model 101, and every man looks like Marky Mark in ``Boogie
Nights.''
<p>
   Owned by French actor Christopher Lambert, this restaurant/mega
disco is sizzling hot and the prime example of the new voracious
Argentine lust for VIP status in the land that Juan and Evita Peron
once tried to turn into a blue-collar, semi-socialist state.
<p>
   ``After the dictatorships ended (in the 1980s), we sort of
uncorked, like Spain after Franco,'' said Carolina Bilbao, an
Argentine programming producer lounging in the VIP section at Buenos
Aires News. ``We went totally frivolous and began worshiping star
culture because we really had never been allowed to do so before. Now,
the only thing that's important in Buenos Aires is to have a good car
and be invited to the VIP section of the hottest club.''
<p>
   Buenos Aires News, like most of the hot spots in this city of 14
million, is an androgynous mix of rich businessmen, Argentine polo,
soccer and tennis players, flamboyant fashion designers, leaders of
the art world and, of course, models, models, models. In a place that
used to frown upon public displays of flesh during the dictatorships,
Buenos Aires News paraded both men and women down a catwalk on a
recent Thursday clad in tight undergarments that made Victoria's
Secret look like an Amish clothing catalog.
<p>
   On Fridays, no self-respecting person in ``La Movida,'' or the B.A.
night scene, would be caught dead anywhere but La Morocha, or ``The
Dark Woman,'' an old train-station-turned-disco that opens around 1:30
a.m. and doesn't really roar until 4 a.m., when the line outside the
door can be blocks long. ``Everybody wants to be the last to arrive,''
said Claudio Lanzetta, PR czar of the local night scene. ``It's the
idea of being `fashionably late' to the extreme. Every year, the
people are pushing the peak hour later and later.''
<p>
   And of course, you cannot stay in one place too long. Maximum time
is two hours, slightly more if you've struck up a promising
conversation. Otherwise, it's time to hail the taxi and aim for the
next club  &MD;  a cycle that generally ends at 7 in the morning.
<p>
   But by then, good old American adrenaline has kicked in.
<p>
   At 5 a.m. on a recent Saturday, Cravello, my Argentine buddy, was
caught yawning. ``Come on, wake up,'' I said. ``We've got hours yet to
go.''
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-04-14-98 0940EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-weekend-mexico </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0605 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-weekend-mexico
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Molly Moore </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; South of the Border by Boat (Xochimilco, Mexico) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   XOCHIMILCO, Mexico  &MD;  It's Sunday afternoon on the canals of
Xochimilco, just outside Mexico City, and the tequila is flowing, the
mariachi bands are blasting and the movable feasts are being devoured.
<p>
   Families arrive by the dozens, pile into brilliantly painted wooden
craft and drift through narrow canals amid a floating fiesta of
dueling boat bands, tables heaped with picnic fare and aquatic lanes
so jam-packed that any change in course becomes a game of bumper
boats. Quick! Grab that tequila bottle before it goes overboard.
<p>
   Mexico City is one of the world's largest megalopolises  &MD;  home to
22 million people  &MD;  and every Sunday is cause for celebration. On the
one day of the week that virtually everyone has off from work, it
seems as though the entire citizenry deserts its crowded barrios and
teeming suburbs in search of a patch of grass, a sliver of water or a
breath of clean air.
<p>
   No speck of green space goes unused. The parks are packed, the
median strips between boulevard lanes are transformed into picnic
grounds and the colonial squares are alive with clowns, musicians and
vendors of every type.
<p>
   But no escape is more popular than Xochimilco (pronounced
so-chee-MIL-ko), just 30 minutes south of town, where you can find
remnants of canals that 500 years ago served as the primary
thoroughfares for a Venetian-like Aztec city. Elsewhere, the canals
have long since been filled in and covered over by concrete, but in
Xochimilco they survive, meandering through suburban neighborhoods and
bleeding into marshes populated by elegant herons and chattering
blackbirds.
<p>
   For $10 an hour, anyone can rent a boat propelled by a pole (and a
hard-working gondolier-type driver) and join the party. The regulars
come laden with ice coolers and picnic baskets, for these are no
ordinary boats: Their central feature is a long plank table that can
accommodate a sit-down picnic of up to 18 people beneath wooden crowns
of vibrantly colored flowers and birds. But even those who don't bring
their own smorgasbord don't go hungry. The canals are a virtual market
of waterborne vendors. There are beer boats, candy-apple boats and
floating kitchens that will cook up a custom-ordered lunch of tortas,
tacos and tamales. Need a rose to woo your sweetie or a flower basket
to dress up your picnic table? A flower boat is never far away.
<p>
   In fact, Xochimilco was created by the Aztecs as a series of
floating gardens for supplying fruits and vegetables to the city. The
floating gardens continue to produce many of the flowers and plants
sold to Mexican families for their courtyards and gardens.
<p>
   Photo boats will record your outing, gaudy sombreros and serapes
supplied at no extra charge. But this is no foreign tourist trap. In
fact, few gringos tread these waters and those who do usually consider
it far too raucous for their delicate sensibilities. But they miss the
point, they lack the spirit.
<p>
   Where else can you watch dad dance on the bow of a boat balancing a
tequila bottle on his head? Or hire a floating mariachi band to
serenade your party with songs of battles won and loves lost? What
better place to dress up the kids in their Sunday best and celebrate a
First Communion? Or take a bottle of wine and invite your girlfriend
on an affordable cruise, just you and her on a boat built for 18? Or
gather three generations to celebrate Grandma's birthday? What better
vantage for just leaning back and watching the passing parade?
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-weekend-africa </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0555 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-weekend-africa
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By James Rupert </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; On the Beach in Africa &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   LAGOS, Nigeria  &MD;  Weekends? In Africa, there are those who care and
those who don't.
<p>
   That image of Disastrous Africa we've gotten to know so well  &MD;  a
continent where dirt-poor people swelter in city slums or flee
incomprehensible civil wars  &MD;  doesn't mention days off. And there are
places in Africa where the stereotype fits, life is a struggle to
survive and weekends don't seem much on the agenda.
<p>
   But the disaster archipelago  &MD;  whose major islands these days might
be counted as Sierra Leone, Liberia, Sudan, Somalia and the two Congos
 &MD;  is not all, or even most, of Africa. And in Ordinary Africa  &MD;  poor,
troubled, vibrant and not on our TV screens very much  &MD;  weekends
happen. They seem to use less gas and money, and more aunts and
uncles, than the American rite.
<p>
   In Abidjan, Ivory Coast, home of what appears to be West Africa's
biggest middle class, some weekend venues are much like what you'd
find in the United States: downtown jazz clubs on Saturday night,
churches on Sunday; and for the past year or so, West Africa's first
suburban shopping mall, complete with a fast-food joint, video games
and the kids with time, and maybe money, to spend.
<p>
   For even more people, though, weekends are simpler  &MD;  a bus ride
across town or out to a village to visit extensive, extended family.
There's a lot of soccer  &MD;  either dusty and sweaty on often grass-free
fields, or televised.
<p>
   Lagos, Nigeria, like most big cities, is part Disastrous Africa and
part Ordinary. It's a sprawling, brawling conurbation of  &MD;  well,
nobody really knows how many people it has, but 8 million is a common
guess.
<p>
   For those with a little money, and that's a lot of people, weekends
seem to happen on the beach. No African city appears to have managed
much in the way of municipal parks. And with a few exceptions, zoos,
museums and the like are woefully underfunded and attract thin crowds.
<p>
   But in the coastal cities, beaches are beautiful, free and jammed.
On Lagos' Victoria Island, Nigerians jam onto the beach in what looks
like a carnival.
<p>
   A couple of dollars will rent you a shady cabana of woven palm
leaves, open to the breeze front and back. Sweating vendors in tiny
stalls sell yams, grilled bananas, grilled ``bush meat'' and soft
drinks. Or ice water  &MD;  a few for a glassful, except there are no
glasses. You get it in a small, hand-knotted, plastic bag, bite a hole
in a corner and squeeze it down.
<p>
   But James Anya, a semi-employed driver who shuttled me around the
city in recent days, says he dare not even come to look at Lagos'
weekend on the beach. Like a lot of folks here, he is just trying to
survive Nigeria's collapsed, corrupt economy.
<p>
   Whether you care about weekends depends on whether you're feeding
your family, said Anya. If you have a good job, then ``on Sunday you
can stay home with your family,'' he said. But only if you're managing
to make enough money to feed them.
<p>
   Anya's salary is $24 a month. ``If you have no money and the
children ask you for something, you cannot feel comfortable at home.
So on Sunday, you will go to church and pray to God for a job on
Monday,'' Anya said. ``Or you will go out and walk and think how you
can get money.''
</TEXT>

AP-NY-04-14-98 0941EDT &QL;
