<SLUG> bc-ulster-comment </SLUG>
<HEADER> repeat   04-14 1496 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-ulster-comment repeat
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Editorial-Page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Fintan O'Toole </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Repeating to add writer ID &QL; 
 &UR; Peace in N. Ireland Long in the Making &QL; 
 &UR; O'Toole is a columnist for the Irish Times and &QL; 
 &UR; the New York Daily News &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The last week of the Northern Ireland peace talks was anything but
a stately journey toward historic destiny. There were accusations,
histrionics, last-minute hitches. At an alarmingly late stage, it
seemed that basic questions had not been answered, that some key
concepts had not been worked through. And when, after a sleepless
night, a complex deal was reached by exhausted negotiators, suspicions
lingered. Is this real change or just a mutual respite for worn-out
enemies? Was the desire for peace driven by something more than the
momentum generated by deadlines, expectations and the massed ranks of
television news crews?
<p>
   The questions are not abstract. The deal announced Friday will have
to withstand terrible pressures. Fringe terror groups on both sides
will test it with atrocities. Two of the most important parties in the
process  &MD;  Gerry Adams' Sinn Fein and David Trimble's Ulster unionists
 &MD;  will face considerable internal dissent. The political mechanics of
establishing both a workable administration in Northern Ireland and
cross-border bodies with the Republic of Ireland will be extremely
complex. If the deal does not have deep roots, it will be blown away.
<p>
   There are, though, good reasons to believe that the peace package
arises not from a passing moment but from some fundamental shifts. For
one of the more paradoxical effects of 30 years of conflict has been
to give an illusion of continuity to a period of profound change. The
epic language of immemorial and irreconcilable divisions that tends to
be used about Northern Ireland has hidden the fact that many of the
assumptions that held good in 1968 when the killing began simply no
longer apply.
<p>
   In 1922, when the last attempt at a comprehensive settlement
between Ireland and Britain was being debated in the British House of
Commons, Winston Churchill famously contrasted the vast changes in
Europe after World War I with the obstinate constancy of religious
divisions in Northern Ireland. Everything else had undergone ``violent
and tremendous changes in Dublin in the deluge of the world,'' he
said. ``But as the deluge subsides and the waters fall short, we see
the dreary steeples of Fermanagh and Tyrone emerging once again. The
integrity of their quarrel is one of the few institutions that has
been unaltered in the cataclysm which has swept the world.''
<p>
   Now, as the flood of violence that has washed over Northern Ireland
begins to recede, the dreary steeples of sectarian division between
British Protestants and Irish Catholics are still the most prominent
features on the landscape. But beneath them, at ground level, almost
everything has changed. Only because the quarrel was conducted with
such ferocity was it so hard to notice how obsolete it was becoming.
<p>
   When the Northern Ireland conflict began in 1968, it was possible
to see the war between Irish Catholic nationalism and Protestant
British unionism as a clash of civilizations. The categories were, of
course, crude. Ordinary lives and attitudes were much more complex
than political or religious labels would allow. There was,
nevertheless, a real sense in which Catholics and Protestants belonged
to sharply opposed cultures. It was not simply that they worshiped in
different churches, or marched behind different flags. They also, on
the whole, lived different lives.
<p>
   The Republic of Ireland, with which Catholics identified, was still
a predominantly rural and agricultural society beyond Dublin. Northern
Irish Protestants, on the other hand, belonged to a strongly urban and
industrial world. In everyday life, the terms ``Irish'' and
``British'' were shorthand for a great deal more than national
identity. You could use them to guess with reasonable accuracy what
kind of work someone did, whether they used contraceptives (and
therefore how many children they had), what sort of sports they
played. Yet, precisely in the years of the conflict, this gradually
became impossible.
<p>
   When the Troubles started, it made some sense for Northern
Protestants to see themselves as part of a modern, prosperous economy
and the Republic of Ireland, by contrast, as an underdeveloped
backwater. Even in the early 1970s, gross domestic product per head in
the largely Catholic Republic was just half what it was in the largely
Protestant United Kingdom. And implicit in the contrast was a belief
that this was not coincidental. As the Protestants saw it, the Irish
were poor because they were Catholic. They were, in the Protestant
imagination, priest-ridden peasants.
<p>
   Now, in the late 1990s, the Republic has a higher GDP per capita
than the United Kingdom. It is a predominantly urban society. The
political power of the Catholic church has been broken  &MD;  a process
completed in 1996 when the Irish voted to permit divorce. Irish
Catholics, on the whole, take neither their morality nor their
politics from their clergy. They use contraceptives and have the same
number of children that Protestants do. They are more likely to be
found making Pentium microchips than growing potatoes.
<p>
   While all of this was going on, the world that Irish Protestants
knew 30 years ago was disappearing. The once-mighty industries of
Northern Ireland were becoming obsolete. The shipyards and engineering
works that were still the pride of Protestant Belfast in 1968 have
long since dwindled.
<p>
   British identity has been no more stable than Irish identity. In
1968, it was still possible to imagine Britain as an imperial power.
The symbols that Northern Irish Protestants valued  &MD;  the monarchy, the
empire, the United Kingdom  &MD;  have lost their glamour. After the royal
scandals and Princess Diana's death, the monarchy seems more a source
of confusion than a guarantor of eternal stability. After the handoff
of Hong Kong last year, Britain's overseas empire now contains just
168,000 people. And after Tony Blair's concession of devolved
parliaments for Scotland and Wales, the United Kingdom is being
transformed into a decentralized federation of nations.
<p>
   Yet both sides in the conflict tended to collude in a pretense that
all of this change was not happening. Protestants and Catholics had a
mutual interest in the image of the Republic of Ireland as a poor
backwater. For Protestants, it served both as a warning of what would
happen if they moved away from the British fold and as an excuse for
prejudice and discrimination against Catholics. For Catholics, it
served as evidence of British perfidy and proof that Ireland could
never prosper so long as any part of it remained ``unfree.''
<p>
   Likewise, the image of Britain as a mighty imperial power suited
each of the warring tribes. For Protestants, it was a source of pride
and comfort. For Catholics, it suggested that they were part of the
great decolonizing movement of the post-war world. For those in both
communities who sympathized with the proponents of armed terror, it
lent an aura of epic grandeur to squalid and intimate cruelties.
<p>
   The Protestant policeman murdered in front of his children wasn't
just a target for sectarian hatred. He was the representative of a
mighty empire. The Catholic shopkeeper murdered behind his counter
wasn't just a victim of venomous bigotry. He was an enemy of Her
Britannic Majesty the Queen. Somehow, the illusion seemed to make the
killing easier.
<p>
   Violent division has the strange effect of making people
overestimate their enemies. Catholics and Protestants could see that
their own communities were in flux. Neither could see that what was
happening to it also was happening to the other side. One of the wry
ironies of Northern Ireland, indeed, is that social surveys
consistently show that both Protestants and Catholics believe a) that
their own side is divided and incoherent, and b) that the other side
is an impressively unified monolith. Both, of course, were right about
themselves and wrong about the others.
<p>
   In a sense, the real question about the peace deal is not why it
has come now, but why it did not come a long time ago. The elements of
the package agreed upon Friday have been available for at least a
decade.
<p>
   Understanding came slowly because violence numbs the mind and
because a cycle of atrocity and repression continually renewed the
violence. Only when the cycle was broken by the paramilitary
cease-fires of 1994 did it become possible to see how much has
changed. Only then did it become obvious that British Protestants and
Irish Catholics are no longer different kinds of people. The peace
deal, at least, gives political and institutional recognition to the
fact that the integrity of their quarrel had not, after all, survived
the cataclysms that have swept the world.
</TEXT>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Nursing Homes &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Almost half of all Americans turning 65 this year will be admitted
into a nursing home at least once during their lives, according to the
U.S. Health Care Financing Administration.
<p>
   To educate Americans about long-term care options, HFCA has
produced a booklet, ``Medicare and Medicaid: Guide to Choosing a
Nursing Home.'' The booklet provides information on community
resources, payment, special care needs and quality of care, as well as
a checklist on how to select a home.
<p>
   To order a free copy, call the Medicare Hot Line at 1-800-638-6833
(voice) or 1-800-820-1202 (TTY/TDD); or visit HCFA's web site,
www.hcfa.gov.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-health-vitamin </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0811 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-health-vitamin
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Health editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Sally Squires </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Need for Vitamin B12 After 50 Is Emphasized &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The National Academy of Sciences targeted seniors last week as one
of the groups of Americans that need to take some vitamin supplements.
<p>
   Heading the list of recommendations from the academy is a call for
those 50 and older to take supplements of vitamin B12, an essential
nutrient that is found in animal products, including fish, meat and
dairy foods. Vitamin B12, also known as cobalamin, helps maintain red
blood cells and protect the integrity of nerve cells, from the hands
and feet to the brain.
<p>
   Special recommendations for older adults are something new in the
well-known ``recommended dietary allowances,'' which have been renamed
the ``dietary reference intakes.'' The latest guidelines have been
expanded to include two new age categories: 51 to 70 years old and 70
years and older.
<p>
   Past recommendations had nutritional requirements for older
Americans that were lower than those for younger people. ``The
thinking was that because older people were taking in fewer calories,
they would need fewer nutrients,'' said Robert M. Russell, professor
of medicine at the U.S. Department of Agriculture's Human Nutrition
Research Center on Aging at Tufts University in Boston.
<p>
   New research in older people shows just the opposite. ``Old people
need just as much as the young people and in some cases may need
more,'' Russell said.
<p>
   For this reason, the latest recommendations advise those 51 and
older to increase their consumption of vitamin B12 and other B
vitamins, including folic acid, thiamine and niacin. Additional
recommendations published last fall by the academy raised recommended
intakes of calcium and vitamin D to help maintain bones.
<p>
   In the case of vitamin B12, up to a third of those aged 50 and
older lose the ability to produce enough stomach acid to extract the
vitamin from food. Without it, ``vitamin B12 remains attached to
protein in food'' and is never absorbed into the body, said Bonnie
Liebman, director of nutrition for the Center for Science in the
Public Interest, a Washington-based consumer group. ``That's why older
people need to get vitamin B12 in vitamin supplements or in fortified
food.''
<p>
   Why this change occurs is unclear, but failure to get enough
vitamin B12 can lead to anemia, a common blood disorder characterized
by feeling tired, and to nerve damage.
<p>
   ``We are worried more about the nerve damage than we are about the
anemia,'' said Russell, who served on the academy committee that
drafted the new guidelines. ``Because it's not just nerve damage in
the hands and feet, which is bad enough, but it is also dementia,
confusion and memory problems.''
<p>
   To help prevent such deficiencies from occurring, the academy
recommended last week that ``adults over age 50 meet most of their
recommended intake with synthetic B12,'' said Roy M. Pitkin, professor
emeritus of obstetrics and gynecology at the University of California
in Los Angeles and chairman of the academy's panel that drafted the
new guidelines. The latest guidelines recommend 2.4 micrograms per day
of vitamin B12 for those aged 51 and older. Most daily multiple
vitamins supply this amount.
<p>
   But a few recent studies have suggested that older people who have
less stomach acid may also have more digestive bacteria that use the
vitamin B12  &MD;  and thereby deprive the body of what it requires. For
this reason, some experts, including Russell, recommend as much as 25
micrograms per day of vitamin B12. Many of the vitamins designed for
older Americans contain this amount.
<p>
   The new recommendations on vitamin B12 are closely linked with
concern that women of childbearing age get enough folic acid, a B
vitamin that helps prevent serious birth defects, such as spina
bifida, and appears to help reduce the risk of heart disease.
<p>
   Since Jan. 1, the Food and Drug Administration has required food
manufacturers to fortify bread, cereal, pasta, rice and flour with
folic acid to make sure that Americans consume adequate amounts of
this important vitamin.
<p>
   One problem, however, is that too much folic acid can mask a
deficiency of vitamin B12 in the elderly and some studies suggest it
may even worsen nerve damage. For this reason, the academy recommended
not exceeding 1,000 micrograms per day of folic acid.
<p>
   ``The chance of a young person having a vitamin B12 deficiency in
our society is very, very small,'' Russell said. But for an older
person, the odds are much greater, he said.
<p>
   ``We are learning,'' he said, ``that elderly people have some
requirements that are different because of specific metabolic or
absorptive differences, but that it is just as important for an older
person to keep good nutrition as it is for a younger person.''
</TEXT>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Travel Editors: &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The following travel stories are also listed on the main feature
budget that moved Tuesday to the general advisory category. The
feature editor is Rob Wishart in Los Angeles at (213) 237-7832.
<p>
    &UR; Category codes of stories are in parentheses. &QL; 
<p>
   TRAVEL-MONGOLIA  &MD;  ULAN BATOR, Mongolia  &MD;  Naadam, the ancient
Mongolian festival codified into a national holiday, centers on the
traditional sports of wrestling, archery and horse riding; the
spectacle of children racing horses is a highlight during a recent
visit to this country. 1,530 words, by Scott Martelle (Times). With
TRAVEL-MONGOLIA-INFO. Four photos (TPN1-4) (t).
<p>
   TRAVEL-SAUSALITO  &MD;  SAUSALITO, Calif.  &MD;  Sausalito is fabled as a
cultural never-never land, both light years ahead and decades behind;
with the Hotel Sausalito serving as a weekend home base, the city
makes for a great getaway. 1,020 words, by Patt Morrison (Times). With
TRAVEL-SAUSALITO-INFO. One photo (TPN5) (t).
<p>
   TRAVEL-HORROR  &MD;  Travelers share their horror stories from the road,
with advice on how you might avoid their mishaps. 1,300 words, by
Robin Stansbury (Courant) (t). No photos.
<p>
   TRAVEL-TRANSATLANTIC  &MD;  There's still plenty of romanticism
surrounding trans-Atlantic cruises, but there's not much demand. 980
words, by Christopher Reynolds (Times) (t). No photos.
<p>
   TRAVEL-BERMUDA  &MD;  It's no accident that people on motor scooters are
heavily featured in advertisements for Bermuda; that's because on this
22-mile-long island in the Atlantic, motor scooters are a travel
necessity  &MD;  there are no cars for rent for environmental reasons. 600
words, by Robin Stansbury (Courant) (t). No photos.
<p>
   TRAVEL-NEWYORK  &MD;  The most interesting new trend in New York hotels
is the mainstreaming of low-budget establishments; 10 New York hotels
that won't bust a budget. 1,525 words, by Carolyn Spencer Brown (Post
special) (t). With TRAVEL-NEWYORK-CIRCLE and TRAVEL-NEWYORK-CLOISTERS.
All moved. No photos
<p>
   WEEKEND  &MD;  How, exactly, the weekend is celebrated in foreign climes
is something we were curious about; how the planet plays: a world of
weekends. 200 words (Post) (t). With WEEKEND-RUSSIA, WEEKEND-CANADA,
WEEKEND-HONGKONG, WEEKEND-ARGENTINA, WEEKEND-MEXICO and
WEEKEND-AFRICA. All moved. No photos.
<p>
   TOURS&AMP;CRUISES  &MD;  Live chamber music on a rafting journey. By Karin
Esterhamer (Times) (t).
<p>
   TRAVEL-TIPS  &MD;  Timely tips for today's traveler. By Jane Engle
(Times) (t).
<p>
   TRAVEL-ECONOMY  &MD;  Plan to protect small airlines finds a surprising
foe. By Barbara Shea (Newsday) (t).
<p>
   TRAVEL-NOTES  &MD;  Finding frequent-flier seats to Europe getting
tougher. By Barbara Shea (Newsday) (t).
<p>
   TRAVEL-PLANNER  &MD;  New Year's in Luxury, plus Parks, Planes, and
Czars. By Martin Hollander (Newsday) (t).
<p>
   FEARLESS-TRAVELER  &MD;  Travel Q&AMP;A. By James T. Yenckel (Post) (t).
<p>
   WORLDWISE  &MD;  Travel notes from all over (Post special) (t).

 &UR; Entertainment Editors: &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The following stories about entertainment and the arts are also
listed on the main feature budget that moved Tuesday to the general
advisory category. The feature editor is Rob Wishart in Los Angeles at
(213) 237-7832.
<p>
   Category codes of stories are in parentheses.
<p>
   TV-MURPHYBROWN  &MD;  HOLLYWOOD  &MD;  Candice Bergen, who had a good cry
when ``Murphy Brown'' wrapped shooting for the final episode of its
10th and last season, thinks her longtime character has made her a
better person. 1,290 words, by Judith Michaelson (Times) (e).
<p>
   TV-SPADE  &MD;  David Spade's first HBO special, ``The 'Round Midnight
Special: David Spade: Take the Hit,'' proves that while a little of
his act is OK, an hour is way too much. 810 words, by James Endrst
(Courant) (e).
<p>
   MUSIC-BACHARACH  &MD;  SANTA MONICA, Calif.  &MD;  Burt Bacharach seems a bit
uncomfortable looking back at his successful composing career; at 69,
TV and stage revues and an album with Elvis Costello have him looking
ahead. 1,410 words, by Robert Hilburn (Times) (e).
<p>
   MAGAZINES  &MD;  Current magazine articles and industry trends. By Peter
Carlson (Post) (e).
<p>
   TV-TIPS  &MD;  Best bets for Wednesday (Times) (e). Sunday advance,
moved.
<p>
   Plus these weekend advances:
<p>
   TV-SINGER  &MD;  Vonda Shepard is the striking blond piano player in the
trendy yuppie bar frequented on-camera by ``Ally McBeal's''
off-the-wall cast; she's the soulful vocalist whose words parallel the
thoughts of the title character  &MD;  who in turn represents the working
woman of the '90s; interview. 750 words, by Scott Moore (Post) (e).
With TV-SINGER-THEME. Both weekend advances, moved Tuesday.
<p>
   TV-ECO  &MD;  It's not just a game, it's an adventure  &MD;  one where only
the world's most elite endurance athletes dare to compete; an
international race that pushes teams of three men and one woman to the
physical and mental breaking point, the Discovery Channel
Eco-Challenge, returning to television Sunday, tires you out just
watching. 850 words, by Harriet Winslow (Post) (e). Weekend advance,
moved Tuesday.
<p>
   TV-VIDEO  &MD;  A Marilyn Monroe trilogy. By Martie Zad (Post) (e).
Weekend advance, moved Tuesday.
<p>
   TV-CABLE  &MD;  Rats wage war in New York City. By Martie Zad (Post)
(e). Weekend advance, moved Thursday.
<p>
   TV-PICKS (e). Weekend advance, moved Tuesday.

<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-newsfeatures-lat-wp </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0758 04-14 1296 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-newsfeatures-lat-wp - a0758
 &UR; (wap) (bal) Editors:
</PREAMBLE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The following News Features from the Los Angeles Times-Washington
Post News Service are recommended for daily or Sunday use. All items
have moved, except those marked ``upcoming,'' which will move shortly.
<p>
   For questions about Post stories, call (202) 334-7666; for
questions about  &QC; 
Times and other Times Mirror stories, call (213) 237-7832. &HT;    
<p>
    &UR; National:
<p>
   PACKWOOD  &MD;  PORTLAND, Ore.  &MD;  Former Sen. Bob Packwood, who has
largely stayed away from Oregon since resigning in disgrace in 1995,
has hinted he might run for office, but observers say his higher
public profile may be aimed only at rehabilitating his image. 840
words, by Terry McDermott (Times) (a). Upcoming.
<p>
   CLINTON-PANETTA  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  Through nearly three months of
controversy about the nature of President Clinton's involvement with
onetime intern Monica S. Lewinsky, his former White House chief of
staff has been traveling the public airwaves, delivering the same
off-message message: Come forward, Mr. President, Leon Panetta keeps
saying almost plaintively; talk to us; tell us what happened. 1,200
words, by Peter Baker (Post) (w).
<p>
   DRUNK  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  A proposal to create a national
drunken-driving standard was quietly strangled in a House committee
the night of March 31, and despite what opponents and advocates say,
the standard may have a tough time surviving a fight on Capitol Hill.
1,700 words, by Eric Pianin (Post) (w). With DRUNK-ALCOHOL.
<p>
   KILLERS  &MD;  Sociologists, psychiatrists, criminologists and others
have long struggled to understand what makes some people turn violent;
researchers have wondered whether some people are born with the
tendency, and a new study suggests some are. 1,150 words, by Rob Stein
(Post) (a).
<p>
   FEDERAL-DIARY  &MD;  Reports on the federal workforce. By Mike Causey
(Post) (w).
<p>
    &UR; International
<p>
   MEXICO-PROTEST  &MD;  CUERNAVACA, Mexico  &MD;  In a microcosm of the crisis
in the nation's criminal justice system, Morelos state officials are
under arrest, accused of helping drug and kidnapping gangs or covering
up police abuse; citizens have launched protests that threaten to
bring down the powerful governor. 1,300 words, by Mary Beth Sheridan
(Times) (i). Upcoming.
<p>
   RUSSIA-CARS  &MD;  For years, Russian drivers were so grateful to be
able to buy a car that they never dared hope it would actually run
well, but now car buyers are finding themselves in an unfamiliar
position: masters of the market instead of slaves to it. 1,130 words,
by Kathy Lally (Sun). With RUSSIA-CARS-MAGAZINE (both i). Upcoming.
<p>
   TIBET  &MD;  KATMANDU, Nepal  &MD;  Thousands of Tibetans fleeing repression
in their own land take one of the world's most harrowing paths to
freedom; they climb across the Himalayas. 1,475 words, by Dexter
Filkins (Times) (i). With TIBET-REFUGEES. Upcoming.
<p>
   CHINA-BUSINESS  &MD;  GUIYANG, China  &MD;  The once-suppressed private
sector is growing fast  &MD;  and shaking the foundations of China; from
big foreign firms to entrepreneurs to street vendors, China's private
businesses are outpacing China's lumbering state-owned enterprises
with harder work, newer technology and better management; overview.
1,250 words, by Steven Mufson (Post) (i).
<p>
   JAPAN-CONSUMERS  &MD;  CHIBA, Japan  &MD;  The proof is not in the pudding  &MD; 
it's in the soy sauce; the family patriarch of the famous Kikkoman
brand can tell by people's soy-sauce-buying habits that Japan's
economy is in deep trouble  &MD;  it's expected to shrink for the first
time in a quarter-century. 1,200 words, by Mary Jordan and Kevin
Sullivan (Post) (i).
<p>
   ICEMAN  &MD;  BOLZANO, Italy  &MD;  ``Otzi'' the Bronze Age iceman has cometh
to the modern age; after lying undisturbed for 5,300 years under an
Alpine glacier near the Italian border with Austria, Otzi's perfectly
mummified remains have been installed in Bolzano's archaeology museum
and put on public view for the first time since German hikers
discovered them seven years ago. 875 words, by Vera Haller (Post
special) (i).
<p>
    &UR; Financial:
<p>
   MANAGERS  &MD;  Half a dozen years after the popular press and academia
wrote off the Organization Man and his descendants as an endangered
species being replaced by a brave new world of technology and lean
organizations, managers are now hot properties in the help-wanted ads.
1,410 words, by Tim Smart (Post) (f). MANAGERS-REENGINEER.
<p>
   HELPWANTED  &MD;  Weight-y issue aired in public by ex-boss. By Carrie
Mason-Draffen (Newsday) (f). Upcoming.
<p>
   There is no MUTUALFUNDS (Morgan, Newsday) feature this week.
<p>
    &UR; Opinion:
<p>
   CLINTON-COMMENT  &MD;  The Clintons have used private investigators,
secretly, for purposes well beyond the previously acknowledged
suppression of ``bimbo eruptions.'' 750 words, by William Bradley
(Times special) (k). Upcoming.
<p>
   FUHRMAN-COMMENT  &MD;  While the Justice Department says Mark Fuhrman
cannot be prosecuted in the courts because of the statute of
limitations, he ought to be prosecuted in the court of public opinion;
his police records should be made public. 335 words, by O.J. Simpson
(Times special) (k). Upcoming.
<p>
   MEDICINE-COMMENT  &MD;  We would be much more fully served by a practice
of medicine that knew its own limits in triumphing over suffering  &MD; 
limits to the benefits of research, limits to the struggle against
death, limits to relieving our pain and misery. 875 words, by Daniel
Callahan (Times special) (k). Upcoming.
<p>
   MIDEAST-COMMENT  &MD;  Ever since the Oslo accords between Israel and
the Palestinians, pro-peace Americans and American Jews have deceived
themselves into thinking that they no longer had to push much for the
peace process. 800 words, by Michael Lerner (Times special) (k).
Upcoming.
<p>
   ALGERIA-COMMENT  &MD;  The world organizations entrusted with protecting
human rights and lives must intervene in Algeria; just as in Bosnia
the world cannot remain a spectator; the raping of Muslim women must
be entered as a crime tried in international tribunals. 850 words, by
Fadwa El Guindi (Newsday special) (k). Upcoming.
<p>
   TURKEY-COMMENT  &MD;  In all likelihood, the 1981 plot to kill the pope
was not hatched by a foreign government; rather, it appears to have
been the work of renegade Turkish extremists who operated under the
protective umbrella of Turkey's secret service but did not always take
their orders from Ankara. 1,300 words, by Martin A. Lee (Times
special) (k). Upcoming.
<p>
   FASHION-COMMENT  &MD;  The danger in investing styles of dress with so
much emotional power is that a more-important reality is obscured:
Clothes cannot define us or empower us. 600 words, by Robin Givhan
(Post) (k).
<p>
   ULSTER-COMMENT  &MD;  There are good reasons to believe that the
Northern Ireland peace package arises not from a passing moment but
from some fundamental shifts; one of the more-paradoxical effects of
30 years of conflict has been to give an illusion of continuity to a
period of profound change. 1,500 words, by Fintan O'Toole (Post
special) (k).
<p>
   STARR-SUBPOENA-COMMENT  &MD;  Independent counsel Kenneth W. Starr Starr
has several times subpoenaed information from news organizations and
publishers; in some cases, he has not followed longstanding Justice
Department guidelines; moreover, he has said he is not bound by the
guidelines, a stance that appears to put him squarely at odds with
what Congress intended in enacting the independent-counsel law. 1,760
words, by Florence George Graves (Post special) (k).
<p>
   GLASSMAN  &MD;  Bigger, better banks. By James K. Glassman (Post
special) (k).
<p>
   POST EDITORIAL, moved; TIMES EDITORIAL, upcoming (both k).
<p>
   There is no Barabara T. ROESSNER (Courant) column this week.
<p>
    &UR; Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QL;

<BYLINE> By Roy Rivenburg </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998 Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; OFF-KILTER: Notes From the World of Weird &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
    &UR; Cowboys and Aliens: &LR;  Sure, other journalists might risk their
lives reporting from war zones, police shootouts or deadly hurricanes,
but only Off-Kilter has the courage to enter a grocery store and
actually buy the supermarket tabloids that most humans are too
embarrassed to be seen with.
<p>
   That's why, when we spotted the ``new and improved'' National
Enquirer, we took a deep breath, said a silent prayer and then begged
our girlfriend to please go back into the store and purchase it while
we stayed by the car as a lookout.
<p>
   One super-expensive dinner later, she handed over the copy and we
read it. Unfortunately, we couldn't tell the difference between the
``new'' Enquirer and the old one. But we did find a story inside about
Willie Nelson telling Texas Monthly magazine that he believes his
brain is able to pick up radio waves from ``somewhere in the
universe,'' which is how he gets his songs. Needless to say, we're
terrified. For starters, what if the extraterrestrials find out Nelson
is plagiarizing their music and decide to destroy the entire planet in
retaliation? Even scarier is the idea that space aliens, the most
sophisticated creatures in the galaxy, actually enjoy country music.
<p>
    &UR; Bumper Sticker of the Day: &LR;  ``Dyslexics Have More Fnu.''
<p>
    &UR; Titaniholics Anonymous: &LR;  It's time to create an international
12-step program for Titanic addiction. The latest victim of the
scourge is Halifax, Nova Scotia, which is swarming with
Titanic-obsessed tourists.
<p>
   The central attraction is a Titanic cemetery. In 1912, Halifax sent
a ship loaded with embalming fluid and coffins to recover bodies from
the sunken luxury liner. A Halifax minister went along and conducted
burial-at-sea rites for 119 victims, but 209 bodies were returned to
Halifax, according to the Montreal Gazette.
<p>
   In what now seems like an unwitting stroke of marketing genius, the
grave marker for engine-room worker James Dawson was inscribed simply
``J. Dawson.'' Although there's no connection to the fictional Jack
Dawson character played by Leonardo DiCaprio in the movie, J. Dawson
is now king of the nether world. Teen-age girls are flocking to his
tomb and leaving flowers, movie ticket stubs and love letters,
according to the London Daily Mail.
<p>
   Meanwhile, Halifax officials have opened a Titanic Web site
(http://titanic.gov.ns.ca) and expanded the city's Titanic museum
exhibit. Also, tour companies are offering Titanic-themed trips and
meals aimed at U.S. visitors.
<p>
    &UR; Odd Inventions Inc.: &LR;  Smokers who want to quit have the nicotine
patch. Overeaters who want to diet now have the ``anti-pastry patch.''
It's an almond-and-vanilla-scented strip that supposedly reduces the
craving for sweets  &MD;  but not by transmitting cholesterol into your
bloodstream a la its nicotine cousin. Instead, the ``aromacology
patch,'' which was invented by a British chef and recently featured on
``Extra,'' fills the air with almond and vanilla scents. Personally,
we think dessert odors would make people more hungry, but you can give
the $1.30 pastry patch a try by calling (888) 780-6824.
<p>
    &UR; Best Supermarket Tabloid Story: &LR;  We know some of you cheat and
look at this part first, but today's tabloid fix is in the top item of
the column.
<p>
   Contributor: Wireless Flash &QL; 
<p>
   E-mail Rivenburg at roy.rivenburg(at)latimes.com.
</TEXT>

<BYLINE> By Barbara Shea </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; TRAVEL DEALS: Plan to Protect Small Airlines Finds a Surprising Foe &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   In the name of fair competition, Transportation Secretary Rodney E.
Slater has announced that his department wants to start aggressively
protecting those feisty little budget airlines that leisure travelers
love.
<p>
   Who could object to that  &MD;  other than the major carriers? You'd be
surprised. You also might want to tell Slater your thoughts on the
plan, because future fares likely will hinge on the outcome.
<p>
   Slater's statement last week said there have been instances in
which a new, small airline offered a low-price service from a major
carrier's hub only to find the major carrier cutting fares, increasing
the number of seats on that route and ``sacrificing short-term profits
with only one goal in mind: to drive the new entrant out of the market
and then raise its own fares to their original level or higher and cut
back its level of service.''
<p>
   So, after a 60-day period of public comment, the Department of
Transportation will begin fining future violators if necessary, though
it hopes the threat of action will be enough. The department says the
policy isn't an attempt to put any controls on the market after 20
years of deregulation in the airline industry.
<p>
   Many small airlines naturally like the new idea. ``We think it's
wonderful because it'll help consumers by helping to keep us around,''
said Rob Kulat, spokesman for Kiwi International Air Lines, which came
out of bankruptcy last July and serves four U.S. cities and San Juan,
Puerto Rico, from Newark, N.J. ``Airlines like us serve as a level
against big airlines to keep fares reasonable over the long term,''
Kulat said. ``Business fares were up 14 percent at the beginning of
this year, while leisure fares were down 14 percent. Leisure travelers
use small airlines. Without the Kiwis of the world, there wouldn't be
a 14 percent decrease in fares.''
<p>
   Big airlines, however, express concern about unfair restrictions on
their ability to compete, and say small carriers made larger gains
than the big ones last year. And skeptical observers ask how the
Transportation Department could ever prove a case of unfair
competition against a major airline when some of the newest low-cost
carriers are the majors' own offshoots.
<p>
   ``We're probably going to end up siding with the major carriers on
this one,'' said Paul Hudson, executive director of Ralph Nader's
Aviation Consumer Action Project. ``We're very much concerned and
against anticompetitive practices, but this proposal is not the
answer. In effect, it's the government saying they're going to
eliminate fare wars  &MD;  and that's not appropriate in dereglated price
competition.''
<p>
   Hudson said that rather than protecting consumers, the plan would
protect small airlines and could have the perverse effect of raising
fares, because startup airlines won't set low prices if they know the
majors' fare reductions will be restricted. His group's
recommendation: Require airlines to lock in any new low fare for a
year and provide an adequate number of seats at that rate.
<p>
   Public comment will be accepted until early June on the plan. You
can find details at http://www.dot.gov/briefing.htm. Address letters
to: Room PL-401, Docket OST-98-3713, U.S. Department of
Transportation, 400 Seventh St. S.W., Washington, D.C. 20590.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; TRAVEL PLANNER: New Year's in Luxury, plus Parks, Planes, and Czars &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   It's not too early to book a reservation for  &UR; this &LR;  New Year's Eve
party  &MD;  the first in 210 years at Versailles (yes, France). For $1,300
a person, some 650 merrymakers can welcome 1999 at Le Bal du Roy and
indulge in: a four-course 18th-century dinner prepared by three-star
chef Guy Savoy, served in Galerie des Batailles; displays of the
dances of the time by costumed performers; a mile-long stroll through
the royal quarters and  &MD;  fireworks. Dancing, to protect the Versailles
floors, will be at the adjacent Trianon Palace. The organizers say
luminaries such as Lee Iaccoca and Stephanie Powers have already
signed on. The fete runs from 8:30 p.m. to 2 a.m. Call (212) 355-0400.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   NATIONAL PARKS: More than 20 national park tours in the United
States and Canada  &MD;  primarily motor coach with some rail  &MD;  are being
offered by Tauck Tours. Among them is an eight-day, seven-night
venture beginning in Phoenix that takes in Bryce and Zion national
parks with an overnight at the Kachina Lodge at the Grand Canyon.
There are frequent departures through Oct. 27. The price is $1,490 per
person, twin ($1,833 single), which covers accommodation and most
meals. A nine-day, eight-nighter takes in Yellowstone, the Grand
Tetons, the Black Hills and Mount Rushmore. The cost is $1,590 (single
$2,054) and includes nights at the Jackson Lake Lodge and Yellowstone
Inn and nearly all meals. Departures are May 13 through Sept. 15 from
Rapid City, S.D., or Salt Lake City. A 10-day, nine-night in the
Canadian Rockies, with a one day-one night rail sector, is $2,475
($2,890 in June, July and August; singles $3,123-$3,790). Most meals
and overnights at the Banff Springs Hotel and Chateau Lake Louise are
covered. Departures begin May 3 and end Oct. 2 from either Calgary or
Vancouver. Air fare is not included. Call (800) 214-5158.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   HIGH AND WILD: Why sit on a tour bus when you  &MD;  if you're up to a
``Women Born to Be Wild'' venture  &MD;  can try your hand at being a
fighter pilot. Pricing for this Rainbow Adventures trip hasn't been
set, but the plan is to have about 15 women spend a weekend flying as
co-pilots in an SF-260 Marchetti jet trainer. The pilots will be
former F-16 fliers, and the flights will follow the Air Force's air
combat training program. Plans are for one session sometime this
winter at Kissimmee, Fla., and another in the summer of 1999 near
Chicago. Meanwhile, among its 1998 tours, Rainbow is operating a
Himalaya trek in October at about the 8,000-foot level. But next year
(April 3-19), the company is raising the ante  &MD;  to 14,000 feet at the
Annapurna base camp. The price will be about $4,500 per person, double
occupancy, which includes Los Angeles-Katmandu air fare,
accommodations in lodges and high-elevation tea houses, guides and
most meals. Call (800) 804-4646 or (406) 587-3883.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   LONDON KREMLIN: Summer visitors to London can view the first
full-scale Moscow Kremlin armory treasures exhibition ever permitted
abroad. The ``Arsenal of the Russian Tsars'' will offer ceremonial
arms and armor made during 1600-1800, including the 1621 Jericho Cap,
a helmet inlaid with rubies, emeralds and diamonds. Items from the
collections of both Peter the Great and Catherine the Great will also
be on display. The show, at the Tower of London Royal Armouries, will
run daily June 13 through Sept. 13. Tickets are 8.50 pounds (about
$13.75 U.S.) for adults, 5.60 pounds ($6.20) for children, 6.40 pounds
($10.35) for seniors and 25.40 pounds ($41) for families of up to two
adults and three children. Call (800) 806-7187.
<p>
   Most packages can be booked through travel agencies. Details should
be confirmed with the appropriate travel companies.

<BYLINE> By Barbara Shea </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>

<HEADLINE>
 &UR; TRAVEL NOTES: Finding Frequent-Flier Seats to Europe Getting Tougher &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Having trouble finding frequent-flier seats to Europe for the
summer? No wonder. Americans made nearly a million more trips abroad
last year than in 1996, and the European Travel Commission has no
reason to doubt that 1998 will be the sixth record year in a row. So
you should have tried booking sooner.
<p>
   ``Sure it's true there aren't free seats left, because who plans a
vacation to Europe now?'' said Randy Petersen, editor/publisher of
Inside Flyer magazine. ``The rule of thumb is to plan six to nine
months in advance. It's rather late now.''
<p>
   Still, he holds out some hope for stragglers. First, he suggests,
look for airlines that are starting new routes. For example,
Continental Airlines plans to start daily service to Ireland's Shannon
Airport from Newark on June 15. The allotment of frequent-flier seats
on those flights probably isn't filled yet, Petersen said, because the
route hadn't been announced six months ago when savvy travelers were
booking their overseas trips.
<p>
   Also, Petersen said, there are always plenty of freebie seats for
passengers willing to fork over more miles to get one. ``The airlines
are really saying there are no free seats available at 35,000 miles or
whatever,'' he said. ``But you can call up and ask what's their
standard `Anytime Award' requirement  &MD;  which usually is twice as many
miles as the limited awards.''
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   DISCOUNT DEALS: Students and other young travelers ages 18 to 35
can save 10 percent on Contiki Holidays' last-minute vacations via the
company's Web site at http://www.contiki.com.
<p>
    &MD; Swissair is offering a six-night air-land package to Zurich or
Geneva starting at $1,158 per person midweek through May. Rates
include lodging at three-star hotels and the taxes and service
charges, breakfast and either a one-week car rental with unlimited
mileage or, for an added charge, an eight-day Swiss rail pass (from
$125 extra plus tax). June 1 through September, the package rate
starts at $1,363. Call (800) 688-7947.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   MORE TITANICA: Two National Park Service memorials in Washington,
D.C., honor the memory of the Titanic. The Butt-Millet Memorial,
between the Ellipse and White House, honors two prominent
Washingtonians who died when the ship sank on April 14, 1912. The
Titanic Memorial, which stands on the southwest waterfront, was built
by the Women's Titanic Memorial Association to honor the men who
perished to save the women and children aboard.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   QUICK TAKES: London's Kensington Palace State Apartments are due to
reopen May 1 with a new presentation of the royal ceremonial dress
collection.
<p>
    &MD; Nearly a third of U.S. adults say they will spend a large chunk of
their tax refund on a vacation, according to a poll by the Travel
Industry Association.
<p>
    &MD; For details about the annual Miami-Bahamas Goombay Festival in
Coconut Grove June 6-7, call (305) 567-1399.

    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC;

A0435--- R T BC-Train 04-10 0816 BC-Train,0816 &QL; 
<p>
   (ADVANCE WEEKEND FEATURES)
<p>
   (TRAIN)
<p>
   By Todd Richissin
<p>
   (c) 1998, The Baltimore Sun
<p>
   WESTMINSTER, Md. - In Johanssons, one of the most flavorful, quaint
- and usually peaceful - restaurants in downtown Westminster, there is
a standard lunchtime conversation that goes something like this:
<p>
   First Person: `` ... ''
<p>
   Second Person: ``Huh?''
<p>
   Then there is a pause. And the conversation does not pick up again
until the Maryland Midland train finishes its jolting, jarring,
clanking, blaring, altogether-way-too-LOUD jaunt through town.
<p>
   Baltimore may have its honking highways and construction
screeching, but even Maryland's largest city has no noise like quiet
little Westminster's noise.
<p>
   Twice a day, sometimes three, a train of 50 cars brings downtown to
a halt. That is because the train, part of Maryland Midland Railway,
does not pass by downtown but through it.
<p>
   ``When that train comes by,'' says Jamie Wehler, who works
downtown, ``I swear to you, it physically hurts.''
<p>
   Blue and orange engines tug the cargo down a set of tracks parallel
to Railroad Avenue and into a lane of automobile traffic at
Westminster's main intersection. The train then moves onto the road
and into a turning lane, takes a brief curve on Main Street and then
harmlessly snakes away.
<p>
   Along its route through downtown, though, it brushes within a coal
spark of businesses, most of them brick, which amplifies the train's
whistle so it is only slightly less loud than a sonic boom. It does
not ``choo-choo'' so much as blast into submission anything that comes
near its path.
<p>
   ``There's no sense fighting it,'' says Suzanne Ricciardelli, a
manager of Johanssons Dining House on Main Street and Railroad Avenue,
whose back steps reach within three feet of the passing trains.
<p>
   ``The experienced people just give up talking till it passes,'' she
says. ``Some new people to the restaurant might try to talk over it,
but you can't.''
<p>
   In more excitable places, the train and its whistle might be cause
for some kind of protest. Not in Westminster. Residents here see it as
much a part of the character of Westminster and the horse-dotted hills
that surround downtown.
<p>
   ``A lot of the people who come in here want to sit by the window
where they can see the train,'' Ricciardelli says. ``They think it's
exciting.''
<p>
   The track that passes through Westminster was built in 1862, in
time to serve as a main artery to supply the Union Army at Gettysburg
and to haul away the battle's casualties.
<p>
   It was taken over by Maryland Midland in the 1970s and runs 50
miles, from Highfield in the west to Glyndon.
<p>
   A separate line of 17 miles passes through Union Bridge, running
from Taneytown to Walkersville.
<p>
   Railroad traffic through cities is nothing new and has become more
common with the deregulation of the railroad lines in 1980. There are
more than 540 freight railroads chugging along, carrying more than 1.8
billion tons of cargo a year over more than 146,000 miles of track,
according to the Association of American Railroads.
<p>
   But it is a rarity for trains to pass so close through a downtown
business district. When passing through Westminster, Maryland Midland
trains go no faster than 10 mph, according to the company's president,
Paul Denton.
<p>
   ``We've voluntarily put that speed limit on ourselves,'' he says.
``We're doing everything we can to have no accidents.''
<p>
   As far as anyone can remember, there have been no accidents in
Westminster involving Maryland Midland, and rail safety has improved
over the past several years.
<p>
   Typically, the Maryland Midland cars carry cement, stone, coal and
lumber. Maryland Midland's largest customer is Lehigh Cement, in Union
Bridge. The engineer pulls the trains into Lehigh, workers fill them
with cement or mortar, and then the cars roll away.
<p>
   If a load is destined for, say, Richmond, Va., Maryland Midland
will drop the cargo cars off in Glyndon, and another railroad will
haul them the rest of the way.
<p>
   ``It's a good operation that usually doesn't get too much
attention,'' says Denton.
<p>
   ``It may be an inconvenience to some people at times, but most
people tend to forget we're even around.''
<p>
   Take, for example, last year's Halloween parade.
<p>
   A good-sized crowd had lined Main Street to watch an assortment of
Bill Clintons and Batmans and Crayola crayons and other costumed
paraders march from one end of town to the other.
<p>
   ``Well, here comes the train,'' recalls Jean Geroft, 56, one of the
spectators. ``The train, it wasn't going to stop. So the police and
the firemen stopped the parade, told all the people to move and the
train just kept on going. It was more fun than an inconvenience.''
<p>
   And there is another upside to the trains, even when they $INS4;
Line TM RCV Overrun error occurred. Story '59940'. blast loud enough
that her apartment is drowned in sound.
<p>
   ``My boyfriend really loves it,'' she says. ``For a couple minutes
he doesn't have to listen to me talk.''
<p>

<HEADLINE>

By BEVERLY BEYETTE &QL;

<TEXT>
<p>
   initial,4 M EYNES, France &MD; Can five couples find happiness sharing a
villa for a week in a small village in Provence? Mais oui!
<p>
   When friends and acquaintances were told of our plan to do just
that, the universal reaction was, ``Are you crazy?'' They then
reminded us that you never really know people until you travel with
them &MD; and then it's too late.
<p>
   Undeterred, we plunged ahead. Did our grand adventure turn out to
be all we'd hoped? Absolutely. Were we all still speaking by week's
end? We were, in fact, a well functioning family. There were no cross
words, no quibbling (publicly, at least) about who had the better
accommodations, no hurt feelings when one or more couples opted out of
some planned excursion to spend time alone or go elsewhere.
<p>
   We had started our odyssey as six couples, but an accident in
Barcelona en route forced one couple to return home to get medical
attention. Old friends all &MD; save for two spouses who have been embraced
by the group &MD; we include journalists, a historian, an FBI agent and an
attorney. Recently, we had started a tradition, gathering each year in
Seattle for a long Fourth of July break. And some of us had traveled
abroad in pairs. But we were well aware that is quite different from
being under the same roof 24 hours a day.
<p>
   Finding the villa of our dreams wasn't easy. For weeks, we pored
over catalogs solicited from agencies from Massachusetts to Oregon,
answered ads in newspapers and magazines including the New Yorker and
San Francisco magazine, scoured the Internet and compared notes by
telephone. After all this searching,  &QL;

two of us, working with different agents, zeroed in on the same

possible. Our ideal villa would have six bedrooms and six baths. (OK,

   Having heard horror stories about villa rentals &MD; swimming pools

handles only French properties and, we were assured, scouts their

seemed to be on our wavelength. Where other agents had tried to reel
us in by assuring us that we had no chance of finding 6BR/6BA,
Kaufmann said it would be difficult but not impossible.
<p>
   Then one day she called with good news. Though she hadn't seen the
property, she had learned of a listing for Domaine Amen, an ancient
former convent in the village of Meynes. A colleague had told her it

A-1 &MD; and previous renters had made a glowing report. It sounded almost

scrutinized the photographs and the drawing of the floor plan and

<p>
   Booking through At Home in France, the rental was $4,200 a week,
plus a refundable $1,000 security deposit &MD; to be paid on our return &MD; and
utilities and telephone, which came to about $150. The couple who
couldn't come paid their share out of pocket as, unlike some of us,
they hadn't opted for trip cancellation insurance. Most agents
recommend it and will refer you to the Automobile Club, American
Express or a travel agent handling such insurance. Because rental
agencies have contracts with the property owners, monies are generally
nonrefundable in case of a last-minute problem &MD; unless the property can
be re-rented.
<p>
   Split six ways, Domaine Amen came out to about $700 a couple for
the week. Where in France can you rent a luxury hotel room for that?
And hotel rooms don't come with garden, cherry orchard, vast public
rooms and Charles-Louis La Salle, our genial host, descended, he told
us, from a Gen. La Salle who fought with Napoleon.
<p>
   (Currently, the guest house is not available; the villa, now
expanded to sleep 14, rents from $3,580 to $7,585 a week, depending
upon the season.)
<p>
   sg &QL; 
initial,2 &HT;    
<p>
   O ur adventure began on a Saturday in mid-May last year as we
rendezvoused in the village of Meynes, which we reached by a minor
road off Route D986, midway between Ni &UR; mes and Avignon and about 15
miles from each. We came from as far north as Seattle, as far south as
San Diego. Three couples flew into Milan, one into Cannes; another
drove down from Paris. With directions provided by Kaufmann, all found
their way easily to Domaine Amen, filled with guarded anticipation.
<p>
   Anticipation changed quickly to delight. La Salle, the villa owner,
who speaks some English, was there to welcome us. He and his
companion, Joel Schneider, occupy one wing of the villa, which has a
separate entrance. They were always available when needed (as they
were the day we very briefly had neither heat nor electricity nor
water), but they never intruded.
<p>
   By evening, there were five rental cars lined up in the villa's
gated courtyard. Each arriving couple was directed by the already
entrenched guests around the side COLUMN 2 ENDS HERE. FILM DEPTH AT

<p>
   When Charles-Louis and Joel came for drinks on our last evening, we
learned the history of the villa. The oldest part, the pigeon
tower &MD; now an enclosed stairwell leading from the dining room to an
upstairs bedroom &MD; dates from the 16th century. The property was
originally a farm, and the main part of the house is 17th century.
Much later, it was an orphanage run by Catholic nuns (thus, Domaine
Amen). When the state took over care of its orphans after World War
II, the orphanage closed. The property sat vacant for 15 years;
squatters moved in and stole the fixtures.
<p>
   kern,0
<p>
   Twelve years ago, living in Toulon and looking for a peaceful place
for a home-atelier, Charles-Louis found the Domaine. Together, he and
Joel restored it. They added the pool &MD; in an area where the nuns once
laundered their habits and winged headdresses. They created a grand
dining room where once there were four small sleeping rooms for the
sisters. kern,2
<p>
   On reflection, our only regret is that we didn't spend more time
just hanging out at the villa. But there was so much to fit in &MD; market
day in beautiful Ile sur Sorgue, lunch in St. Remy-de-Provence where,
by luck, the Provencal national choir also was lunching and treated us
to a spontaneous mini-concert.
<p>
   kern,0
<p>
   We walked the Van Gogh walk, visited the asylum where he was a
patient, toured excavated Roman ruins and saw the Palace of the Popes
at Avignon.
<p>
   We took beautiful drives through vineyards and valleys, to fabled
hilltop towns such as Les Baux and Fontvielle, to the picturesque
village of Seguret in the Vaucluse, a postcard of cobbled streets and
fountains.
<p>
   One night we were the only (very pampered) guests dining at Auberge
Escavarats, a charming restaurant in a centuries-old building in
Remoulins, the nearest town of any size to Meynes. Remoulins, it
should be noted, is also home to a Supermarche that, I confess, we
frequented for household basics. kern,2
<p>
   Our big splurge was a birthday celebration for one of our group.
Everyone got gussied up for once, and we made the 30-minute drive to
Villeneuve-les-Avignon, where we dined on squab salad, veal Roquefort
and creme bru &UR; lee, served by white-gloved waiters at the very
beautiful La Magnaneraie in a 15th century country house.
<p>
   kern,0

this year. For two weeks. sg &QL;

<SLUG> bc-travel-transatlantic </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0807 04-14 0969 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-travel-transatlantic - a0807
 &UR; (ATTN: Travel editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Christopher Reynolds </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; No photos. &QL; 
 &UR; Trans-Atlantic Cruise Market is Hardly Titanic &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   ``Titanic,'' the biggest-grossing movie in history, has countless
Americans day-dreaming about the romance of an old-fashioned cruise.
The cruise industry, riding a global boom, is building new ships as
fast as it can. And the Atlantic, the planet's second-largest ocean,
certainly has plenty of room. So the trans-Atlantic cruise business is
booming, right?
<p>
   Wrong. That great ocean remains, in a sense, the world's smallest
cruise market.
<p>
   It sustains only a single ship with regularly scheduled
trans-Atlantic crossings: Cunard's famed Queen Elizabeth 2, age 31,
capacity 1,715 passengers  &MD;  and the QE2 spends less than one-third of
its time on that itinerary.
<p>
   Every year, many ships carry passengers across the ocean as they
reposition themselves between winters in the Caribbean and summers in
the Mediterranean, but on the day  &MD;  whenever it comes  &MD;  when QE2 stops
regular trans-Atlantic service, experts say, a chapter in travel
history will close. With its broad decks and clubby interiors, the QE2
is one of the cruise industry's last links with the old-fashioned
luxury, the romanticism and the class system symbolized by the
Titanic. But icebergs are not a factor here. Technology, demographics
and corporate consolidations are.
<p>
   First, in the late 1950s, came the advent of daily passenger jet
service between the United States and Europe, which quickly stole away
most cruise ship customers. In the early 1970s came the rise of
Carnival Cruise Lines, which reinvented the industry by selling
cruises to the masses and, once you were outside of your cabin, making
virtually no class distinctions.
<p>
   Then on April 3 the old and new worlds of cruising met. The
relative upstart Carnival Corp. announced that it, with some Norwegian
minority partners, was buying venerated Cunard from its Norwegian
parent company for $500 million. Carnival officials said they would
merge Cunard with Seabourn Cruise Line (half owned by Carnival) to
form the world's largest luxury line.
<p>
   A Carnival spokesman said he expected no immediate changes in the
QE2's schedule, which calls for crossing the Atlantic 17 times this
year on six-day routes between New York and Southhampton, England, but
the owners aren't saying much more.
<p>
   Cunard officials aren't saying much, either, except to note that
thanks to the ship's thick hull, it has ``many, many good years of
sailing left.''
<p>
   But the Carnival purchase, along with the brisk sales for the QE2
around-the-world cruise that concludes next month, leads Cruise Week
newsletter editor Mike Driscoll to ponder ``a question mark in the
QE2's future. You have to wonder if they might be thinking of
(forsaking trans-Atlantic passages for) longer itineraries.'' And with
Carnival's wealth and willingness to build new ships, Driscoll points
out, there is now ``the possibility that there might be a QE3
somewhere down the line.''
<p>
   For now, however, the QE2 endures in a sort of twilight zone,
clinging to its traditional route for about 100 days a year while
newer ships jostle each other in the Caribbean, the Pacific coast of
Mexico, Alaska and the Mediterranean.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Aside from its point-to-point service, the QE2 is set apart by its
``cabin grades'' system, which directs passengers to specific dining
rooms based on what they've paid for their cabins. Only passengers in
the priciest cabins can dine in the Queens Grill and drink in the
Queens Grill Lounge. At the next level are those in the Princess and
Britannia grill restaurants, then those in the Caronia restaurant, and
finally those in the Mauretania restaurant, who, unlike their fellow
passengers, are assigned to either early or late seatings.
<p>
   Ties and jackets are expected on men in all dining rooms each
night, and on the three formal nights of a standard crossing, black
tie or dark business suits are requested. For those who rebel, the
ship does offer a casual cafe open to all.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   This year's first trans-Atlantic cruise leaves Southhampton this
week. The last arrives in New York Dec. 20. Twelve have themes  &MD; 
British theater; food and wine; and Atlantic cruise liner history, for
instance. Fares begin at about $400 per person per day, including
one-way coach air fare across the Atlantic.
<p>
   ``One of the things on everybody's list (of dream trips) is
crossing the Atlantic on the QE2. It just isn't the same crossing on
anything else. ... And I hope that that doesn't go away,'' says
Matthew Upchurch, a veteran of ``nine or 10'' QE2 crossings.
<p>
   Upchurch is managing principal of Fort Worth, Texas-based API
Travel Consultants, a consortium of about 200 luxury travel agencies
nationwide, and a major deliverer of customers to Cunard. Upchurch
speculates that the QE2 could easily sail for another two decades.
<p>
   ``She is the only North Atlantic express liner left  &MD;  what Cunard
started, Cunard will finish,'' wrote author John Maxtone-Graham of the
QE2 in a 1989 volume on Cunard's history.
<p>
   The QE2, christened by Queen Elizabeth II herself in 1967, carried
British troops to the Falklands War in 1982, and in 1987 switched from
a steam turbine engine to diesel-electric power. In 1994, a $45
million upgrade went awry when post-renovation passengers boarded to
find faulty plumbing and tradesmen still at work. But in the fall of
1996, after Cunard's parent company was bought out by the Norwegian
conglomerate Kvaerner ASA, the ship got a crucial vote of confidence:
an $18 million upgrade that brought it into compliance with recently
stiffened maritime Safety of Life at Sea codes.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-travel-bermuda </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0808 04-14 0573 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-travel-bermuda - a0808
 &UR; (ATTN: Travel editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Robin Stansbury </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Hartford Courant
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; No photos. &QL; 
 &UR; Eco-Friendly Scooters Let Tourists Get Around on Bermuda &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   It's no accident that people on motor scooters are heavily featured
in advertisements for Bermuda.
<p>
   That's because on this 22-mile-long island in the Atlantic, motor
scooters are a travel necessity  &MD;  there are no cars for rent.
<p>
   The ban on rental cars is part of the island's
environmental-preservation efforts, many developed before such
movements gained popularity in the United States. Other
environmentally friendly practices include limiting residents to one
car per household, as well as bans on outdoor advertising and neon
signs.
<p>
   So the scooters  &MD;  like motorcycles but smaller and easier to use  &MD; 
are the best way to see the pink-sand beaches, shopping districts and
major tourists attractions on the island.
<p>
   No driver's license is required to rent a scooter, which costs
about $30 to $45 a day depending on the size. But children under 16
cannot drive one of the motor-assisted cycles, and everyone is
required to wear safety helmets. Children may ride as passengers,
sitting behind the driver.
<p>
   Learning to ride a moped is not difficult. Brief instructions are
given by rental agents located throughout the island, and most hotels
and resorts can help you with reservations.
<p>
   A warning for American drivers: Bermuda is a British colony and
driving is done on the left side of the road. It takes some
concentration to stay in the proper lane, and right turns are
especially tricky.
<p>
   A key is used to start the motor. The motorbikes are propelled
forward with a hand throttle and stopped by using two hand brakes. The
scooters don't need to be locked like a bicycle; a kickstand sets the
moped in place, and a turn of the front wheel locks the scooter so it
can't be moved without the key.
<p>
   The seat on most bikes lifts for storage of valuables while driving
and is a handy place to store helmets when not in use.
<p>
   Still, if you don't like the thought of moped transportation, more
than 600 taxis, as well as buses, are available to cover the island's
approximately 150 miles of roads. So too are a few horse-drawn
carriages, as well as island ferries.
<p>
   Here are a few more tips when traveling to Bermuda:
<p>
    &MD; Passports are the preferred document for entry to the country.
Without a passport, proof of citizenship and identification are
required of all travelers, including children, and may be demonstrated
with a birth certificate  &MD;  with a raised seal or certified copy  &MD;  and
a driver's license.
<p>
    &MD; Legal tender is the Bermuda dollar, which is equal in value to the
U.S. dollar. This means that U.S. currency is accepted at shops,
restaurants and hotels at face value. Other foreign currency is not
accepted but may be exchanged at local banks. U.S. traveler's checks
are accepted almost everywhere, as are credit cards.
<p>
    &MD; The dress code on the island is conservative. Bathing suits,
cutoff tops and short shorts are not acceptable except at beaches and
pools. There are no nude or semi-nude beaches, and in public  &MD; 
including general-use areas of hotels  &MD;  beach wear must be covered.
Always wear shoes. The islanders consider it an offense to ride mopeds
or appear in public shirtless or without a shirt over one's bathing
suit top.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>

</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Jane Engle </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998 Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; TRAVEL TIPS: An April Shower of New Exhibits &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   April usually brings a shower of exhibit openings in the United
States and abroad, and this year is no exception. Some notable shows:
<p>
    &UR;  &MD; Memphis, Tenn.: &LR;  ``Ancestors of the Incas: The Lost Civilizations
of Peru,'' at the Memphis Pyramid, is billed as the largest exhibit of
Peruvian artifacts ever to travel to the United States. It includes
three mummies never seen outside Peru, and more than 300 artifacts
spanning 3,000 years. Thursday to Sept. 16. Information: (800)
263-6744.
<p>
    &UR;  &MD; Washington, D.C.: &LR;  ``Degas at the Races,'' at the National
Gallery of Art, is devoted to the French Impressionist's fascination
with horses. It includes more than 120 paintings, pastels, drawings
and sculpted figures. Through July 12. Information: (202) 737-4215.
<p>
    &UR;  &MD; London: &LR;  ``The Power of the Poster,'' at the Victoria and Albert
Museum, covers 120 years of posters by Toulouse-Lautrec, Aubrey
Beardsley, Saatchi &AMP; Saatchi and others drawn from the museum's
10,000-piece collection  &MD;  its largest such exhibit in six decades.
Through July 26. Information: 011-44-171-938-8349.
<p>
    &UR;  &MD; New York: &LR;  Three galleries devoted to Edward Hopper, Georgia
O'Keeffe and Alexander Caldwell are among eight newly opened in a
major expansion of the Whitney Museum of American Art. More than 100
works from the museum's permanent collection are displayed.
Information: (212) 570-3676.
<p>
   ``Paul Robeson: Bearer of a Culture,'' at the New-York Historical
Society, explores the life of the African American artist and activist
100 years after his birth. Through July 12. Information: (212)
873-3400. &QL; 
 &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   ROYAL APARTMENTS TO REOPEN IN LONDON: The Kensington Palace State
Apartments in London, which include paintings and furniture from the
royal collection, are scheduled to reopen May 1 after being closed
since September 1997 for renovation. Also starting May 1, the royal
ceremonial dress collection, which includes the 1893 wedding dress of
Queen Mary and several dresses of Queen Elizabeth II, will be
displayed in the palace. Palace admission is about $13 for adults;
lower rates for children and seniors. Information: (800) 806-7187. &QL; 
 &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   YOSEMITE LODGING TIGHT FOR SUMMER: Getting a place to stay
overnight at Yosemite National Park this summer is expected to be
especially tough. While heavier-than-usual precipitation continues to
feed the famous falls, offering hope for an exceptionally scenic
season, there are 44 percent fewer campsites and 15 percent fewer
hotel rooms than there were two years ago. The reason: The park still
hasn't rebuilt lodging damaged by January 1997 floods.
<p>
   Yosemite spokesman Kendell Thompson said last week that
construction can't begin until the park's long-term development plan,
which is still being revised, is approved. Best bets for now: Reserve
early, go off-season or consider lodging outside the park. &QL; 
 &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   ONE-STOP SHOPPING FOR MAYAN TOURS: An unusual alliance of eight
private travel agencies in five countries, aided by Continental
Airlines, is creating ``one-stop shopping'' for travelers interested
in exploring Mayan ruins throughout Central America.
<p>
   Working through Gurnee, Ill.-based MILA, a 17-year-old cultural
tour agency, tourists and travel agents can call a toll-free number,
(800) 367-7378,
<p>
   to design individual or group itineraries involving any or all of
five countries: Mexico, Guatemala, El Salvador, Honduras and Belize.
MILA President C. Luis Vasquez said daily land costs for the ``Maya
Path Adventures'' program are about $120 to $140 per person, double
occupancy. &QL; 
 &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   TRAVELER'S NOTE ...: Starting April 29, twice-weekly flights to
remote Midway Atoll, the former military site that opened to tourists
in 1996, begin leaving from Honolulu rather than Kauai, and round-trip
air fare drops to $750 from $1,049. Reservations: (888) 574-9000.  &QL; 
 &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   DEAL OF THE WEEK: The Breakers, a AAA Five-Diamond and Mobil
Five-Star resort in Palm Beach, Fla., is offering credit vouchers for
$200 worth of golf or other sports from May 26 through Sept. 30. The
``Oceanfront Getaway'' deal costs $163.90 per night Sunday through
Wednesday and $176 Thursday through Saturday, single or double
occupancy, including tax; there is a two-night minimum.
<p>
   The deal includes a room, normally priced at $176, plus the $200
credit  &MD;  more than enough for two rounds of golf ($85 per round, with
cart). The credit also can be applied to tennis, fitness center
admission and cabana or bicycle rentals. Up to two children 16 and
under can stay free in the room. The average summer high at the
oceanfront resort is 91 degrees. The resort includes 572 rooms, two
golf courses and 14 tennis courts. Reservations: (888) 273-2537. &QL; 
 &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   FREE FOR THE ASKING: Highlights of 360 miles of U.S. Highway 101 in
Oregon, from Astoria south to Brookings, are profiled in a new
brochure, ``Pacific Coast Scenic Byway.'' Sights include sand dunes,
redwoods, cranberry bogs, a shipwreck and the West's largest cheese
factory. To order, contact the nonprofit Oregon Coast Visitors
Association, P.O. Box 74, Newport, OR 97365; tel. (888) 628-2101.  &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-travel-mongolia </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0810 04-14 1459 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-travel-mongolia - a0810
 &UR; (ATTN: Travel editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Scott Martelle </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Four photos (TPN1-4) &QL; 
 &UR; Young Mongolian Riders Kick Up Dust of Ancient Times (Ulan Bator) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   ULAN BATOR, Mongolia  &MD;  You have to rise early to beat the horses.
<p>
   Very early.
<p>
   We're already up and out by 5:30 a.m., walking in the predawn haze
through a tent city at the base of a sweeping mountainside. The camp
is stirring, and soft voices and the gentle whinny of horses float
among the tents. In the distance, beyond the low smoke of cook-fires
and the tethered horses, we can see a small grandstand, and, facing
the grandstand from 50 feet away, a short wooden observation tower.
<p>
   This is where the day will really begin when, in about an hour,
about 400 horses and their young riders move from the camp to the
tower, then on to a nearby ridge. Then, when all the horses have
gathered, they will trot en masse 15 miles out from the city, turn
around and, at the sound of a gunshot, sprint back to this little
wooden tower.
<p>
   That cold recitation does little to brace us for how all this
actually transpires.
<p>
   This is Naadam, the ancient Mongolian festival codified into a
national holiday after Mongolia's successful fight in 1921, with the
help of the Russian Red Army, for independence from China. Held every
July 9-11, the festival centers on the traditional Mongolian sports of
wrestling, archery and horse riding.
<p>
   But it is the spectacle of children racing horses that has brought
us to this camp at the crack of dawn, for the first of three days of
such races.
<p>
   A Los Angeles Times photographer and I were in Mongolia to cover a
group of Los Angeles-based physicians who traveled here to help
Mongolian doctors improve medical care. The doctors' visit coincided
with Naadam, so the photographer, Gail Fisher, and I decided to take
in the festivities with them.
<p>
   We began with a few days in a tourist camp of ``gers''  &MD;  portable
felt-and-canvas huts known to most of us by the Russian name,
``yurts''  &MD;  before moving on to Ulan Bator and the Naadam festival,
with other side trips planned during the two-week visit. Most
travelers come to Mongolia with tour groups, and ours was arranged by
the Ulan Bator Foundation of the Venice district of Los Angeles, which
also put together the medical mission. But it's also possible to make
independent travel plans.
<p>
   Given the decades of Soviet domination of Mongolia, English is not
widely spoken, so hiring a driver and interpreter makes a huge
difference in both freedom and comprehension. We lucked out by getting
a fellow named Sodh, a hydrologist with passable English skills and a
pleasing sense of humor who seemed to know everyone in the country .
<p>
   As significant as Naadam is for Mongolians, its informality is
refreshing. Tickets ($25 a day for foreigners) are required for the
opening and closing ceremonies, and to watch the wrestling. Those
events take place in the National Stadium, which is surrounded for the
three days by vendors hawking everything from soft drinks to maps. No
tickets are required for the archery competition  &MD;  with both men's and
women's divisions  &MD;  which is held just outside the stadium.
<p>
   The horse races take place at the city's edge and involve both
girls and boys. You can buy grandstand tickets, but you really don't
need to because the best views are from ground level.
<p>
   We cross the dusty grasslands from the camp and step beneath the
wooden observation tower, which will later serve as the finish line.
<p>
   Then the swirl begins.
<p>
   For more than an hour the horses enter the grounds, singly and in
groups, circling the observation stand as the riders  &MD;  children
between 4 and 11 years old  &MD;  chant the traditional song ``Guiin Goo''
for luck and inspiration before riding off to a staging area atop a
nearby ridge. It is pure bedlam, a carnival of billowing dust and
snorting horses and the light, excited voices of youth.
<p>
   The children must be strong enough to race 15 miles, and light
enough to ride quickly. Most of the riders are in saddles  &MD;  a
Mongolian type that keeps the rider leaning forward  &MD;  but many ride
bareback. All are dressed in traditional robes of deep red or vibrant
yellow or mixtures somewhere in between. Few ride alone. Fathers and
brothers and uncles trot alongside, slowly swelling the group at the
staging area to more than 2,000 horses.
<p>
   From a distance, it looks like the return of Genghis Khan's
thundering hordes.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Tourism exists as a nascent capitalist dream here, with rudimentary
accommodations and the occasional upscale hotel in the capital, Ulan
Bator, and little available elsewhere. A former Soviet satellite
(Mongolia was the second nation, in 1921, to undergo a Communist
revolution, opening the door to Soviet domination just after
Mongolians won independence from China), Mongolia has only welcomed
tourists for about the last five years, and the nation's lack of
creature comforts only adds to the mystique.
<p>
   Our group's visit was limited to the regions just outside Ulan
Bator, though smaller Naadam festivals are held outside the capital in
the ``aymags,'' or regional centers. Ulan Bator is the easiest way
into the country, but easy is a relative term. International flights
connect from Beijing, Moscow or Osaka, Japan. We flew from Beijing .
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   For our first few nights of our visit we stayed at the Undur Dov
ger camp 40 miles from Ulan Bator. Out here, the city gives way
quickly to vast sweeps of emptiness, wide valleys defined by sloping
brown hills covered with a light green fuzz of grass and occasional
stands of larch and birch trees.
<p>
   Such views suck you into the middle of them. Or to the top. Gail
and I and four others from the doctors group climbed the deceivingly
low hill behind the camp and were rewarded with stunning views of
steppes and sloping hills stretching as far as the eye can see, dotted
with gers (real ones, not tourist huts) and crisscrossed with dirt
pathways traversed by horses and Russian four-wheel-drive vehicles,
the country's two main modes of transportation.
<p>
   After two days in the countryside, we shifted our base to Ulan
Bator's twin-towered Bayangol Hotel, easing life a bit. The hotel has
three restaurants, one on the main floor that was marked by slow
service and uninspired food. But the two small restaurants in the
towers were much better: quicker service, more reliable quality and an
intimate European atmosphere.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   There are other equally satisfying spots around town. The White
House Hotel has a restaurant on the first floor and an English
pub-style jazz club and bar upstairs. The Capital, owned by a woman
from Cameroon, serves a mix of African and Continental dishes.
<p>
   We spent one day sightseeing in the capital. The national Museum of
Fine Arts was limited but pleasant, with some nice examples of
Mongolian paintings and sculpture going back several centuries. The
Gandan Buddhist monastery and the Bogd Khan Palace, home of the last
khan, were subtly intriguing, enhanced, oddly enough, by their
down-at-the-heels condition.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   It was horses that formed the trip's most memorable moments. In
many cultures, traditions are sustained as relics, marketed examples
of the way things once were. There's a touch of that in the horse
races of Naadam too. But in a land where half the country still leads
semi-nomadic lives  &MD;  following herds of sheep, goats, cattle and yaks
 &MD;  a cross-country race represents skills still nurtured for use, not
for nostalgia.
<p>
   It is a harsh race. From the start there is jostling and flailing
of whips, stumbling and occasional falls. During the weekend, about 20
horses died from the heat  &MD;  over 100 degrees in midafternoon  &MD;  and
scores of young riders suffered injuries, though it was not clear that
any were serious.
<p>
   The view from the finish line is intense. In the distance a thin
cloud of dust appears on the horizon, then grows as the pack moves
closer, hooves pounding the turf with a distant rumble. The pace car  &MD; 
a Russian vehicle  &MD;  bounces over the ridge first, careening wildly,
quickly followed by the horses, spread out at intervals of several
yards.
<p>
   As the lead horses near the finish line, the growing thunder of
hooves merges with the cheers of the crowd, several thousand strong
now. The winning rider, who looks to be about 8 years old, flashes
across the line standing high in his stirrups, right hand triumphantly
pumping above his head before he is swallowed by the crowd arrayed in
a semicircle beyond the finish line.
<p>
   It is a magical, and timeless, moment.
</TEXT>
