<SLUG> bc-books-pageturner </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0822 04-14 0569 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-books-pageturner - a0822
 &UR; (ATTN: Book editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Hartford Courant
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; ``The Page Turner,'' by David Leavitt (Houghton Mifflin, $24, &QL; 
 &UR; 192 pp.) &QL; 
 &UR; Reviewed by Greg Morago &QC; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Literary scandal appears to have had a numbing effect on David
Leavitt.
<p>
   ``The Page Turner''  &MD;  his first novel since the shocking charge of
plagiarism with 1993's ``While England Sleeps'' and the brouhaha
surrounding the aborted excerpting of the too-hot-to-handle
``Arkansas'' in Esquire last year  &MD;  suffers from a bad case of
melancholy verging on boredom.
<p>
   Leavitt plays it safe with this slim story of Paul Porterfield, an
18-year-old pianist who meets and falls in love with his idol,
classical pianist Richard Kennington, an international superstar twice
Paul's age. The occasion of their meeting is a concert for which music
student Paul has been recruited to act as Richard's ``page turner''
for the sheet music. Richard makes a lame attempt at seducing the
preppy Paul, who appears game but not out. The two go their separate
ways.
<p>
   What follows are coincidences that are not only unbelievable,
they're almost irritating to read. First, Paul and his mother, Pamela,
go on vacation to Rome where Paul discovers, quite accidentally, that
Richard is in town for a concert. The two enjoy a torrid love affair
but the guilt-ridden Richard abruptly flees Rome, without so much as a
goodbye kiss to Paul, to return to the waiting bed of his lover/agent
Joseph Mansourian. In the meantime, Pamela, who has no idea her son is
gay, let alone sleeping with the maestro, falls in love with Richard.
<p>
   Flash-forwarding a couple of months, we find Paul living in New
York attending Juilliard and acting as the kept boy of a well-to-do
theater big shot named Alden Hayes. Alden lives in high-style comfort
on Central Park West in the same building where  &MD;  ta-da!  &MD;  Joseph
Mansourian lives. Paul, who hasn't seen Richard or talked to him since
their Roman holiday, accidentally meets the much older Joseph on the
elevator. Soon Paul and Joseph become lovers while Richard is on a
world tour.
<p>
   It gets worse. At home during Christmas vacation, Pamela discovers
her son is gay in the most cliche way: gay porno magazine under his
bed! She rifles through his luggage only to find a picture of (gasp!)
Richard.
<p>
   Leavitt wraps up this simplistic, clumsy story in the most awkward
manner. But before we get there we must endure a scene in which Pamela
attends a support group meeting for mothers of gay children. Here
Leavitt's bag of circumstances is truly fantastic: At the meeting
Pamela meets the very woman her husband left her for.
<p>
   They say it's a small world. But could it really be this alarmingly
tiny?
<p>
   Worse yet, Leavitt's characters are astonishingly self-absorbed and
humorless. It's hard to imagine a protagonist less likable than the
shallow Paul or a couple more irresponsible than Richard and Joseph.
Their dialogue  &MD;  rigid and distancing  &MD;  bears no semblance to the way
real people talk.
<p>
   Leavitt is a gifted writer whose talents are not on display here.
The author of ``Family Dancing,'' ``Arkansas'' and ``The Lost Language
of Cranes'' is capable of deep, telling truths and painful,
unforgettable images. Honesty and reason are hard to come by in the
wearying ``The Page Turner,'' a title that is sadly ironic.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-04-14-98 1125EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<STORYID cat=e pri=r sel=tm--a> X1867 </STORYID>
<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-books-mcclurg </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0822 04-14 0821 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-books-mcclurg - a0822
 &UR; (ATTN: Book editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Jocelyn McClurg </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Hartford Courant
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; McCLURG ON BOOKS: Why Johnny Reb Spends the Weekend at War &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Imagine a Civil War re-enactor so obsessed with authenticity that
he soaks his jacket buttons in urine to obtain just the right
19th-century patina. Or one who wears wool socks that smell as though
they haven't been washed since Bull Run. Or one who eagerly chows down
on hardtack and sowbelly and then compulsively diets so he'll have the
gaunt look of a starving Confederate soldier.
<p>
   If you can't imagine it, you've ``farbed out.''
<p>
   But if it sounds like your idea of a rollicking good time, you're
``super hardcore!''
<p>
   Tony Horwitz, in his amusing, enlightening and sometimes disturbing
journey through the neo-Confederate South, keeps stumbling upon
curious little subcultures of Civil War obsession, like the
``hardcores'' from the ``Southern Guard.''
<p>
   ``Hardcores,'' who look down on mere weekend re-enactors
(``farbs'') with their fake blood and reproduction muskets, seek
``absolute fidelity to the 1860s,'' which gives them a ``time-travel
high'' or ``period rush,'' Horwitz writes in ``Confederates in the
Attic: Dispatches From the Unfinished Civil War.''
<p>
   Horwitz, who has a Pulitzer Prize winning-reporter's knack for
turning up real-life characters who belong in novels, discovers a
goldmine in Robert Lee Hodge, the ultimate hardcore.
<p>
   Rob, a 28-year-old waiter, has elevated his Civil War hobby into a
way of life and a bizarre performance art. He's a genius at
``bloating''  &MD;  Horwitz calls him the ``Marlon Brando of battlefield
bloating''  &MD;  or swelling his belly like a casualty photographed at
Gettysburg. (``Hands are a problem,'' the eccentric Rob admits. ``It's
hard to make them look bloated unless you've really been dead for a
while.'')
<p>
   Later, Horwitz, done up like a Yankee, joins Confederate-clad Rob
in a weeklong ``Civil Wargasm''  &MD;  a madcap car-and-camping pilgrimage
to Virginia's Civil War sites  &MD;  and the highlight of the book.
<p>
   Horwitz, a senior writer for the Wall Street Journal, returned from
nine years abroad writing about Bosnian battlefields and the Middle
East to discover a United States enthralled with the Civil War, thanks
to Ken Burns.
<p>
   A Civil War buff himself as a child, Horwitz decided to ``spend a
year at war, searching out the places and people who kept memory of
the conflict alive in the present day.'' That meant a journey into
present-day Dixie, where the ``War Between the States'' still rages in
certain quarters.
<p>
   As he did in ``Baghdad Without a Map,'' his book about the Middle
East, Horwitz approaches the South almost as a stranger in a strange
land. He obviously has the gift of empathy, because people open up to
him and give him great quotes. And while he has a journalist's
necessary detachment, Horwitz's full-immersion technique and
first-person voice make ``Confederates in the Attic'' at once
humorous, involving and sympathetic, even when his subjects are
appalling. (``There was a feisty iconoclasm about Walt that I couldn't
help admiring, even if he was on the mailing list of every hate group
in America,'' Horwitz says of one South Carolina white supremacist he
meets.)
<p>
   If ``Confederates in the Attic'' has a failing, it's in its
(high-quality) magazine-style journalism. (In fact, part of the book
was excerpted in The New Yorker recently.) While Horwitz is a
compelling, witty writer and a diligent researcher, he sometimes can
only scratch the surface of complex, troubling issues  &MD;  racism, in
particular.
<p>
   It's tough to show up in a town for a week or so and get to the
heart of centuries-old hatreds. But Horwitz still manages to unearth
skeletons that are rattling around in too many Southern closets. He
wades right into the fray in Guthrie, Ky., and tackles the case of
Michael Westerman, shot and killed by a black youth for brandishing
the Rebel flag.
<p>
   But for most of ``Confederates in the Attic,'' Horwitz has a
lighter touch. It's like having your brightest, most observant friend
around, the one whose descriptive powers always crack you up.
<p>
   Horwitz's wide-ranging amble through the tacky New South brings him
across people you won't easily forget:
<p>
    &MD; A modern Southern belle who impersonates Scarlett O'Hara for
Japanese tourists.
<p>
    &MD; An ill-informed high school kid who cites ``The Autobiography of
Scottie Pippen'' as a Civil War text.
<p>
    &MD; The real ``only living Confederate widow.''
<p>
    &MD; Shelby Foote, reluctant Civil War celebrity.
<p>
    &MD; The woman who started the first chapter of the Cats of the
Confederacy.
<p>
   And then there's Horwitz himself, intrepid traveler, clever writer,
a guy so dedicated to getting the story he shows up in uniform at a
Gettysburg re-enactment with a live chicken slung over his shoulder.
<p>
   Super hardcore!
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-04-14-98 1126EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<DOCID> latwp980414.0059 </DOCID>
<STORYID cat=e pri=r sel=tm--a> X1868 </STORYID>
<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-books-scorpion </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0823 04-14 0511 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-books-scorpion - a0823
 &UR; (ATTN: Book editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Hartford Courant
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; ``Scorpion Tongues: Gossip, Celebrity and American Politics,'' &QL; 
 &UR; by Gail Collins (William Morrow, $25, 322 pp.) &QL; 
 &UR; Reviewed by Carole Goldberg &QC; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   When Gail Collins ran the Connecticut State News Bureau in the
1970s and wrote columns for many newspapers, she was admired for her
political savvy and sense of what makes a timely story.
<p>
   She's even sharper now.
<p>
   Now a member of The New York Times editorial board after stints as
a columnist for New York Newsday and The New York Daily News, Collins
has written the couldn't-be-timelier ``Scorpion Tongues: Gossip,
Celebrity and American Politics.''
<p>
   This well-researched book gives a valuable historical context to
the hoohah over President Clinton's personal peccadilloes.
<p>
   Delicious dish  &MD;  or, as Collins puts it, ``unverified information
about a person's private life that he or she might prefer to keep
hidden''  &MD;  has been savored ``since privacy was invented.''
<p>
   The American public has always passed these tales around. But the
process reaches dizzying speed today through such unbridled media as
talk radio and the Internet.
<p>
   While much of it is pure rumor, the dirt that won't wash away tells
us something about its target and illuminates our national anxieties,
Collins says. For example, gossip about politicians with black
mistresses abounded during the pre-Civil War era and rumors of
wife-beating presidents arose during the women's suffrage movement.
<p>
   She sees three great arcs of national gossip. The first began in
the 1820s and ran roughly  &MD;  and it was pretty rough, she makes clear  &MD; 
until the end of that century.
<p>
   The second, a quieter time, focused on entertainment and sports
celebs and gave rise to such sleazy publications as ``Confidential.''
Those old enough may remember such gems as ``The Dollies That John
Foster Dulles Couldn't Handle.''
<p>
   The third, which still goes on, began with Vietnam and Watergate.
As respect for politicians plummeted, the level of nasty public
commentary skyrocketed.
<p>
   Some things have changed. The sleaziest tales now wag the formerly
cautious media dog: Outrageous and unverified rumors are reported
simply because they exist, not because they've been proved accurate.
Worse, the Internet  &MD;  ``a vanity press for the demented,'' as her
friend Lars-Erik Nelson of The Daily News calls it  &MD;  transmits the
most rank slanders to the gullible.
<p>
   Ironically entertainers, who once were the targets of the juiciest
gossip, are now so carefully managed they have become less vulnerable.
Politicians, however, ``lack the critical armor of popularity that
would allow their handlers to bargain for the best possible
treatment.''
<p>
   Where do we go from here? Collins suggests we may be so nauseated
or numbed by ugly political gossip  &MD;  and polls seem to bear her out  &MD; 
that we may be ready for another quiescent period in which we will
choose more civilized political discourse.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-books-bitch </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0824 04-14 0576 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-books-bitch - a0824
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Book editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Editors: Language may be offensive to some readers. &QL; 
 &UR; ``Bitch,'' by Elizabeth Wurtzel (Doubleday, $23.95, 400 pp.) &QL; 
 &UR; Strauss is the author of six books, among them ``Virgins and Other &QL; 
 &UR; Species,'' and ``Under the Canopy.'' She was publisher of Harper's &QL; 
 &UR; Bazaar and the Partisan Review. &QL; 
 &UR; Reviewed by Dorothea Strauss &QC; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   I take ``Bitch'' by Elizabeth Wurtzel to be a rebuttal of ``The
Rules'': a shriek, protesting the hypocritical, no-saying, ladylike
directives for snaring a man found in that bestseller by two
triumphant housewives.
<p>
   Wurtzel's treatise presents an exhaustive and exhausting cast of
``bitches'' (in heat, of course), whose quests for power depend,
largely, on beauty and sexual dynamism. The list is headed by the
biblical seductress Delilah and proceeds as far as Princess Diana. It
includes such famous/infamous names as Amy Fisher, Margaux Hemingway,
Ann Sexton, Zelda Fitzgerald, Edie Sedgwick, Nicole Brown Simpson,
Sylvia Plath, Marilyn Monroe and many others. Though very different,
each, these victim-sisters-in-passion, branded ``bad'' by society,
share violent ends via suicide, murder and drug abuse.
<p>
   We are all familiar with the stories of Nicole Brown Simpson,
doll-beauty, besotted by O.J. Simpson, whose courting of him, even
after their divorce, assured her own death possibly at his hands, and
poet Sylvia Plath, her nature and art, dark and intense,
irreconcilable with her persona of pretty, flirtatious Smith College
belle, led to her suicide as young wife and mother some 30 years ago  &MD; 
her tragedy endures to this very moment.
<p>
   The pages of ``Bitch'' surpass the tabloids in scandalous gossip
and vulgarity of language. And Monica Lewinsky is missing only because
she arrived on the scene too late for inclusion.
<p>
   Like Virginia Woolf, who speaks for the impecunious unmarried woman
in ``A Room of One's Own,'' from the padded comfort of connubial
domesticity, Wurtzel identifies with ``bitches'' across the authorial
divide of publication. ``I root for the bitch,'' she writes, ``who
derails the applecart, who throws the system off guard, and makes a
mockery of some poor fellow's life. I cheer for her because she has
broken the chain, she has taken a taste of what he has completely
consumed.''
<p>
   It appears that, after all, careers for women have not produced a
peace treaty in the gender war. Is the outpouring in ``Bitch'' mere
sensationalism, or is it another bid for legitimate, equal
empowerment? I incline toward the latter, despite the sales figures of
``The Rules,'' and the young women I queried, who claim to have
benefited from its pseudo-Victorian guidelines. The human equation
does not alter, only mores do. Wurtzel implies that Nora, who leaves
home in Ibsen's 19th-century play ``The Doll's House,'' deserting her
husband and children to try her wings outside, is only the reverse
side of Hillary Clinton, who entered captivity through the White House
door, renouncing a successful law practice to be wife to a president.
<p>
   Despite an extended bibliography, the author of ``Bitch'' rarely
quotes anyone: neither sociologist, fellow feminist, rabbi, guru, nor
psychologist. Films seem to provide both springboard and confirmation
for her ideas. This particular trendiness should be congenial to a
youthful audience.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

</TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-books-beatingodds </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0824 04-14 0604 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-books-beatingodds - a0824
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Book editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; ``Beating the Odds: Raising Academically Successful African &QL; 
 &UR; American Males,'' by Freeman A. Hrabowski III, Kenneth I. Maton and &QL; 
 &UR; Geoffrey L. Greif. (Oxford University Press, 236 pp., $24.) &QL; 
 &UR; Kane is a Sun news columnist who writes frequently about race &QL; 
 &UR; relations. &QL; 
 &UR; Reviewed by Gregory Kane &QC; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   ``The significance of this book,'' the authors write in the first
chapter, ``is its assertion, to the surprise of many, that thousands
of young black males are succeeding.'' Indeed they are, in spite of
those dreadful statistics about young black men that so often inundate
us.
<p>
   One out of every three young black males is either in jail, on
parole or on probation. The homicide rate among young black males is
the highest of any racial or ethnic group in the country. More young
black men are in prison than in college.
<p>
   On the educational side, the outlook is just as bleak, as the
authors point out early in their book. Black boys in elementary and
high school are more likely to be suspended than whites. They lag in
reading and math skills and routinely are tracked into the lowest
ability group.
<p>
   Not so with the young men who are the subject of this book. The
authors interviewed the young men and their parents in the University
of Maryland Baltimore County's Meyerhoff Program. The Meyerhoff
Program gives scholarships in engineering to young black men who show
promise in math and science. These young men were able to beat those
odds.
<p>
   They did it, the authors show, mainly through the efforts of their
parents. Consider this a ``how-to'' book  &MD;  as the title indicates  &MD;  on
how black parents can help their sons achieve in school. The formula
is a tried and effective one: high expectations, devotion to religion,
a passion for the work ethic. Add to that the parents' devotion to
their sons' education at every grade level, even when it meant
confronting educators when the parents felt their children's education
was being shortchanged.
<p>
   Two parents insisted that their son be placed in a gifted and
talented class  &MD;  that area of the educational cosmos renowned for
being bereft of black boys.
<p>
   ``I was the one who would question why there weren't more black
students in the gifted and talented program,'' one father said. ``...
I got the usual: `We couldn't find enough who were qualified.' I knew
my son could do to the work. ... We always insisted on advanced
placement and told the teachers to get back to us if there was a
problem, and we'd make sure he gets the help he needs.''
<p>
   The authors  &MD;  educators all  &MD;  to their credit eschew didactic
jargon and fill their book with anecdotes. A book about motivated
parents  &MD;  and their sons  &MD;  is long overdue. Black America needs more
of them and less of the professional mis-educators  &MD;  such as those in
the Oakland, Calif., schools  &MD;  who continue to make excuses for why
black students don't achieve.
<p>
   The only weakness in ``Beating the Odds'' is the authors' refusal
to identify the interviewees. Names, ages, body language and
idiosyncrasies bring subjects to life for readers.
<p>
   Despite that flaw, ``Beating the Odds'' is a crucial book. For
black parents concerned about their sons' academic achievement it is
crucial reading, as it is for everyone concerned about the general
decline in American education. But the most vexing question is this:
How do we get ``Beating the Odds'' into the hands of those black
parents who care little about their sons' education?
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-books-toughjews </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0825 04-14 0552 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-books-toughjews - a0825
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Book editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; ``Tough Jews  &MD;  Fathers, Sons and Gangster Dreams in Jewish &QL; 
 &UR; America,'' by Rich Cohen (Simon &AMP; Schuster, $25, 256 pp.) &QL; 
 &UR; Barra writes a sports column for the Wall Street Journal and &QL; 
 &UR; reviews regularly for The New York Times and Los Angeles Times. &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
 &UR; Reviewed by Allen Barra &QC; 
 &UR; Special to The Baltimore Sun &QC; 
<p>
   Contrary to Hollywood and popular fiction, hoodlums with names that
ended in vowels didn't begin to dominate the newspapers until the late
1920s. Before then, from about World War I almost until the end of
Prohibition, many if not most of the most capable and creative
gangsters in America  &MD;  Monk Eastman, Arnod Rothstein, Moe Dalitz,
Waxey Gordon, King Solomon, Meyer Lansky, Doc Stacher, Lepke
Buchhalter, Jacob Shapiro, Bo Weinberg, Arthur Flegenheimer (a.k.a.
Dutch Schultz), Ben ``Bugsy'' Siegel, Mickey Cohen  &MD;  were Jewish.
<p>
   Rich Cohen's ``Tough Jews'' is about the suppressed if not entirely
forgotten lore of the Jewish gangster in America, ``Less'' writes
Cohen, ``a straight history than the story of a Brooklyn gang as seen
through the eyes of my father and his friends.''
<p>
   The book's subtitle is ``Fathers, Sons and Gangster Dreams in
Jewish America''; it might also have been called ``A Secret History of
the Jewish Gangster.'' Jews of Cohen's father's generation have a
favorite gangster the way Roman Catholics ``have a patron saint.''
Cohen is quite good at evoking the vanished world of all-Jewish
neighborhoods that spawned Meyer Lansky, Bugsy Siegel and Dutch
Schultz, the delis and barber shops where the facts metamorphosed into
legend.
<p>
   He's also good at pinning down the nature of the Jewish-Italian
alliance that grew into what would be known popularly as the
Syndicate. ``It was a marriage of convenience,'' one underworld
observer tells Cohen. ``It was a marriage of the three M's: moxie,
muscle, money.
<p>
   ``The Jews put up the moxie, the Italians supplied the muscle and
together they split the money.'' Cohen can't help but share his
father's sneaking admiration for these thugs: ``Here were men who had
no idea Jews were supposed to be weak, so they weren't.''
<p>
   The problem with ``Tough Jews'' is that Cohen is lost when he
wanders from the old neighborhoods. Despite Cohen's fascination with
the now-vanished Jewish underworld, he really hasn't done much
research on it.
<p>
   ``Tough Jews'' is an intriguing idea that starts out well and then
comes apart when the author stretches his material too thin. Short on
facts, Cohen revives a bad idea that should have died with the pulp
nonfiction of the 1940s and '50s, namely inventing dialogue for his
figures.
<p>
   What Cohen doesn't invent he often guesses at: ``As Lepke listened
to the killers, he probably asked questions. Maybe he asked them to
repeat things. ... Maybe he spoke softly. ... Probably he took his
time. ... Hearing him talk on such occasions was probably like hearing
someone read a key to a crossword puzzle out loud.'' Cohen  &UR; probably &LR; 
shouldn't have written a novel.
<p>
   ``Tough Jews'' isn't shoddy, but it often seems as though Cohen
hasn't thought through his fascinating subject and it's
disappointingly immature in its near-approval of its characters.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-04-14-98 1128EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-books-girlfriend </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0826 04-14 0608 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-books-girlfriend - a0826
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Book editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; ``Girlfriend in a Coma,'' by Douglas Coupland (Regan Books, &QL; 
 &UR; $24, 284 pp.) &QL; 
 &UR; Vowell is the author of ``Radio On: A Listener's Diary'' and a &QL; 
 &UR; contributing editor to ``This American Life'' on Public Radio &QL; 
 &UR; International. &QL; 
 &UR; Reviewed by Sarah Vowell &QC; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   It's worth remembering that the works of novelist Douglas Coupland,
like ``The X-Files,'' are produced in Canada. British Columbia, to be
exact. And even though the supernatural conspiracy theory television
show and Coupland's fictional expositions of the banalities of life in
the 1990s are tied in various ways to the promise and products of
Hollywood and Washington, there's still an evergreen scent floating
around both bodies of work. Because the thing Canada has that the
United States often lacks is the sense that nature  &MD;  not man  &MD;  is in
charge, this reminder that we live on a planet.
<p>
   Coupland became famous earlier in the decade for his debut
``Generation X,'' the paperback that launched a million dreadful,
Whither youth? articles about ``McJobs.'' Its inner Americanness
captured the United States' freaked-out, baby-boomer heart.
``Girlfriend in a Coma,'' Coupland's new novel, is perhaps his most
Canadian book yet. For one thing, it situates the center of the
apocalypse in British Columbia! (Everyone knows America's got a
copyright on the end of the world.) This story might be his most
universal, offering an exploration of life's deepest questions as seen
through the lives of five and a half Vancouver losers.
<p>
   Karen Ann McNeil, the girlfriend in the title, spends much of the
narrative half there. As a barely veiled version of the 1970s' most
famous vegetable  &MD;  Karen Ann Quinlan  &MD;  her living death acts as
Coupland's vortex. She is the book's still center, a blank screen onto
which both plot development and questions into the meaning of life can
be projected. She can be an indictment of the lunacy of contemporary
medicine: ``Comas are as modern as polyester, jet travel, and
microchips,'' says her friend Linus.
<p>
   But not only that. Karen's coma is the reason her boyfriend,
Richard, can put his entire life on hold. Waiting around for her to
wake up gives him an excuse not to grow up.
<p>
   This is at odds with the coma patient's proper role as a cautionary
tale. Like overdose victims and car crash fatalities, their presence
is meant to remind us all that life sure is short, that there are
places to go and things to do, etc. One minute Karen's a teen-ager
partying with her friends and the next thing they know, she won't wake
up. And since her friends aren't the most passionate people north of
the border, they don't really learn anything from her illness.
<p>
   At first. For years. Throughout their 20s and most of their 30s,
actually. It literally takes the end of the world to wake these
characters up out of their walking near-comas, though they do perk up
a little when they all get jobs working on the crew of a television
program suspiciously resembling ``The X-Files.''
<p>
   Richard, contemplating the group's lack of luster, remarks that
``losers are the dark side of society and they frighten people into
submission.'' But isn't the submission of which he speaks a kind of
inspiration? The inspiration to think and act, the inspiration to move
forward, to make a mark? That is ``Girlfriend in a Coma's'' function:
It's a self-help book masquerading as a novel. But in a good way. Just
don't read it in bed, because all you'll want to do is crawl right out
and do something.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-04-14-98 1128EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-books-roberts </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0826 04-14 1192 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-books-roberts - a0826
 &UR; (ATTN: Book, Feature editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Sandra Crockett </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; One photo (TPN6) &QL; 
 &UR; Queen of Romance Novels Says Writing Isn't Work (Keedysville, Md.) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   KEEDYSVILLE, Md.  &MD;  It's a pleasant enough home, a sort of
naturalist retreat tucked into a corner of rural western Maryland. A
place where the cows may very well outnumber the residents, and folks
like it that way.
<p>
   The bright, blue deck is the first thing that catches the eye. Then
the country silence is broken by four dogs whose feisty barks announce
the presence of unfamiliar arrivals.
<p>
   Inside, the home is filled with books, and the occasional pet
meanders in and out. There's an indoor swimming pool and a bright,
cozy second-floor office with a window that looks out at woods filled
with poplar, dogwood and oak.
<p>
   Even with the pool and the shiny BMW parked outside, it isn't a
particularly imposing house. Nothing remotely on the mansion level, no
grand foyer that would befit a queen.
<p>
   It is not what one might expect from one of the most prolific and
successful writers in America. Yet Nora Roberts, a reigning queen of
the romance novel and author of more than 100 books, is quite content
in this place she has called home for more than 20 years.
<p>
   ``As soon as I saw this area, I knew it was home,'' Roberts says,
taking a drag on a cigarette.
<p>
   Roberts will not be at her home in Keedysville for some weeks now.
She is on yet another book tour. This one is for her latest novel,
``Homeport,'' which is hardcover mainstream fiction. Her publishers
forked over $250,000 for promotion and did an initial printing of
250,000, according to Publisher's Weekly.
<p>
   Although she is probably best known for her romance novels  &MD;  the
genre she began with  &MD;  Roberts also writes mainstream novels, in
addition to a series of futuristic suspense books under the pseudonym
J.D. Robb.
<p>
   ``She creates characters that readers love,'' says Isabell Swift,
editorial vice president at Harlequin Enterprises and one of Roberts'
editors. She creates characters from witches of the past to detectives
of the future.
<p>
   A new Robb novel, ``Holiday in Death,'' is due out in June. In the
romance genre, there will be a book titled ``The MacGregor Groom'' in
November.
<p>
   ``I am very disciplined,'' says Roberts, who can whip out a book in
roughly two months.
<p>
   That may be an understatement.
<p>
   By the end of 1998, Roberts will have 126 published novels under
her belt.
<p>
   Note to all the aspiring novelists who have suffered the agony of
rejection slips: Roberts, who had six books on The New York Times
best-seller list in 1997 alone, couldn't get her first manuscript
published.
<p>
   Roberts was raised in Silver Spring, Md., the youngest of five
children and the only girl. Her father, Bernard Robertson, was a
television lighting technician who, upon retirement, opened a
theatrical lighting equipment company with his wife, Eleanor.
<p>
   After high school, Roberts married and moved to Keedysville with
her first husband and their two children. While her husband commuted
to his job in Washington, she did ``the earth mother thing.'' And she
was a huge fan of romance novels, reading them in her spare time.
<p>
   She first took pen to paper while keeping an eye on her two young
sons during one of western Maryland's legendary long, cold, snowy
winters.
<p>
   That first story, she says now, was quite forgettable. But the
first rejection only spurred her to keep at it.
<p>
   ``One of the things about writing is, if you want it, you will keep
at it. You can't buckle at that first slap back. I was writing for my
own pleasure,'' she says.
<p>
   Her first published novel, the romance ``Irish Thoroughbred,'' came
out in 1981, and by then, she already had started on the next one.
<p>
   ``I was awe-struck,'' she says at receiving that first acceptance
letter. ``It was the best feeling in the world. But even better was
seeing the first book in print. I was completely dazzled. It was
better than sex.''
<p>
   Now, Roberts writes on a computer in her home office and has her
own home page on the Internet (http://www.lightst.com/nora).
<p>
   She generally receives good reviews from Publishers Weekly and
Kirkus Reviews, which use words such as ``lively'' and ``sexy'' to
describe her books.
<p>
   There have been a few bumps along the way.
<p>
   Most notable is the well-publicized plagiarism suit that Roberts
filed against fellow romance author Janet Dailey. Last year, Dailey
admitted that at least three of her books contained work cribbed from
Roberts. And the lawsuit estimates there may be at least 13.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   In a previous statement released to the press, Dailey has said she
was under psychological stress due partly to the deaths from cancer of
her two brothers and her husband's lung-cancer surgery.
<p>
   ``We are still in settlement talks,'' Roberts says about the
lawsuit. Roberts is trying to read as many of Dailey's books as
possible but says the task is wearing her out.
<p>
   ``I just can't read any more,'' she says. ``Your eyes start to
bleed.''
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   Roberts is not requesting any monetary damages from the lawsuit but
is asking for a contribution to the Literacy Volunteers of America.
<p>
   Plagiarism aside, the other thorn in Roberts' side is the lack of
respect romance novels receive from some who criticize the genre as
fluff that gives women unrealistic expectations.
<p>
   ``I have to roll my eyes at that because it is an insult to
women,'' Roberts says. ``I don't think guys who read Ian Fleming want
to go out and be James Bond. Women can tell the difference between
what is real and what isn't.''
<p>
   The critics don't bother her enough to give up what she loves:
writing, writing and more writing.
<p>
   She credits her discipline to an early Catholic school experience.
<p>
   ``I was educated by nuns. I have to have structure. I write from 9
to 5, five days a week. On weekends, I might work a couple of hours.
If it's really going good, I write a lot on weekends,'' she says.
<p>
   Her system works because it doesn't feel like work.
<p>
   ``I also love this. It would be harder being disciplined if I
didn't love what I was doing,'' she says.
<p>
   Now, her sons are adults, ages 22 and 25. Roberts was divorced in
1983 and married her current husband, Bruce Wilder, in 1985.
<p>
   It was a courtship that could have come straight from a romance
novel.
<p>
   ``I hired him!'' she says. ``And then I kept him.''
<p>
   What she means is that Roberts decided to remodel her home, and
Wilder was a carpenter who was hired to build a bookcase. Wilder now
runs a bookstore in Boonsboro, a small town about 10 miles from their
home.
<p>
   The bookstore, called Turn the Page, was Roberts' first stop on her
book tour, which will take her to 20 cities in less than a month.
<p>
   Then she will be back home in Keedysville, in the cozy office, and
writing yet another manuscript. It is all very gratifying to her.
<p>
   ``It is just sort of a miracle to me,'' Roberts says.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; As Mergers Shake Industry, Smart Banks Will Find Advantage &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
    &UR; The following editorial appeared in Tuesday's Los Angeles Times:
<p>
   A dramatic wave of proposed financial company mergers is sweeping
the country  &MD;  last week's between Citicorp and Travelers Group Inc.
would be the largest ever and this week's between BankAmerica Corp.
and NationsBank Corp. the second-largest. The consolidations should
increase efficiencies in the U.S. financial services industry; a dozen
banks in all of Europe far more economically produce the kinds of
services that hundreds of major banks provide in America. From that
perspective, the mergers are a sensible attempt to boost the bottom
line by wringing out overlapping operations and gaining economies of
scale.
<p>
   However, consumers are right to question what the superbanks will
mean for mundane services like personal checking accounts and home
mortgage loans. And some employees of the merged entities are also
right to fear for their jobs, because ``efficiencies'' almost always
mean smaller work forces.
<p>
   Consolidation in the industry began more than a decade ago, and its
clip has picked up recently because banks are eager to broaden their
bases so they can better weather losses in one region or industry.
<p>
   Mergers are also seen as a way to meet the escalating costs of
technology. Banks have spent fortunes reprogramming their computers to
avoid the year-2000 conversion problem. They also need size and
diversity to create sophisticated computer networks that combine
lucrative data from other industries like insurance and brokerage
companies.
<p>
   By serving as one-stop shopping centers for checking, investment
and insurance services, the new financial companies will be able to
offer consumers services in a new way. But ordinary Americans could
also get shortchanged.
<p>
   It remains to be seen whether the new financial companies will pass
their cost savings to consumers or use their industry dominance to
hike fees. For instance, after their merger, NationsBank and
BankAmerica would control roughly 15,000 automated teller machines
nationwide; with a network that big, some financial analysts argue,
they could raise ATM fees with impunity.
<p>
   The remaining regional banks view the mergers with concern, and
many will rush toward mergers themselves. But they could also see
opportunity and reach out to the alienated customers the big banks
leave behind. There will no doubt be plenty of them. Credit unions
should be given bigger opportunities as well to fill in the gap.
<p>
   Finally, the financial titans should create a new variation of the
historical role that banks have played in giving something back to
communities through donations to civic causes. As banks become
super-regional and national, donations and volunteer hours should be
allocated to all areas where the institution does business, halting
the donation decline that has accompanied the merger wave. Community
investment is a low-priced way for these huge institutions to keep a
human face.
</TEXT>

bc-packwood - a0828
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Terry McDermott </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Packwood May Be Campaigning Only to Rehabilitate Image (Portland) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   PORTLAND, Ore.  &MD;  Whatever else he had hoped to accomplish with his
little toe-dipping tour around Oregon this spring, former Sen. Bob
Packwood returns to Washington with at least one modest achievement  &MD; 
the ability to appear in public in his home state without a lynch mob
in hot pursuit.
<p>
   That might seem like being damned with very faint praise, but there
was a time not long ago when Packwood could not have hoped for even
that much.
<p>
   He resigned from the Senate in disgrace in 1995 after the Ethics
Committee found he had made a series of unwanted sexual advances over
a period of decades. His resignation brought to an abrupt and
ignominious end a 32-year career in Oregon politics.
<p>
   At least that is what most people assumed. After Packwood left the
Senate, he opened a Washington lobbying office, made lots of money and
largely stayed away from Oregon. That changed this spring, when he
began accepting speaking engagements. At several of them, he joked
about plans for a new campaign and he told a reporter he would ``never
rule out'' running for office again.
<p>
   Few people even bothered to ask what office before speculation and
response followed one another in a familiar cycle of escalation.
Packwood abetted the speculation by saying he was ``dipping his toe''
in the political waters to gauge reaction to a possible future
campaign.
<p>
   ``That was an inadvertent expression,'' Packwood said in an
interview Monday.
<p>
   While he is adamant about preserving his options  &MD;  ``I would never,
never, never ever say I would not run again,'' is the way he put it
most recently  &MD;  he says he is a long way from making any plans for a
renewed political career.
<p>
   He intends to continue his lobbying business until he retires. At
least a couple years, he estimates.
<p>
   On this trip, Packwood, 65, has spoken to civic groups, party
activists, and Monday to a closed luncheon at one of Portland's most
exclusive private clubs.
<p>
   An accomplished storyteller, Packwood has been regaling the largely
friendly audiences with little backstage dramas from his long career  &MD; 
an anecdote about confronting Ronald Reagan here, one about kibitzing
with Cary Grant there. He has mixed such tale-telling with his own
diagnosis of policy issues.
<p>
   He appears to be operating completely on his own. He has no staff,
no circle of advisers. He arrived by himself for Monday's luncheon,
just another man in a gray suit walking down the sidewalk.
<p>
   He was in a bantering, coy mood, pretending not to understand what
all the fuss is about. He clearly enjoys being back in the public eye.
He has been received respectfully and at times with some genuine,
back-slapping enthusiasm. It is a sharp contrast to his last tour of
the state four years ago, when hecklers and pickets greeted him almost
everywhere he went.
<p>
   Much has changed since then, most particularly the unstinting
barrage of allegations about President Clinton's own sexual travails
and the apparent public indifference to them.
<p>
   Long regarded as one of the Senate's most adept strategists,
Packwood declined to speculate on changes  &MD;  if any  &MD;  in the political
climate or what effect such change would portend for his own tattered
reputation.
<p>
   ``I haven't even thought about it,'' he said.
<p>
   Others have.
<p>
   Deanna Smith, chairwoman of the Oregon Republican Party, said she
welcomed Packwood back to the fray.
<p>
   ``It's so ridiculous now that Clinton has been exonerated and
Packwood did nothing near what Clinton did,'' she said. ``I don't know
why Packwood can't run. He was one of the best senators we ever had.''
<p>
   Others foresee a bit steeper climb toward respectability with
voters. One local pollster called him delusional to even consider
running again.
<p>
   A number of political professionals speculated that Packwood was
fully aware of what he was doing and, while he might not have a future
office in mind, was definitely in the midst of a campaign. The goal of
the campaign is rehabilitation of his reputation, they say, and he
appears to have made a good beginning toward achieving it.
<p>
   The problem with playing a game like that, one Republican
politician said, is you can dupe yourself.
<p>
   ``Pretty soon you're thinking, `Hey, they really like me. Maybe I
should run.' ''
<p>
   If he does, he might run and suffer an embarrassing loss  &MD;  even for
a lesser office such as the state legislature.
<p>
   There is also the possibility of winning. Then he would have to go
to Salem, the state capital, where he started his political career.
<p>
   Nobody here thinks Packwood would regard a return to the decidedly
smaller stage of statehouse politics as a triumph. Cary Grant didn't
play state capitals.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-tibet </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0828 04-14 1410 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-tibet - a0828
 &UR; (ATTN: Foreign editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Dexter Filkins </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Tibetans Take the High Road in Fleeing Repression (Katmandu) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   KATMANDU, Nepal  &MD;  Thousands of Tibetans fleeing repression in their
own land take one of the world's most harrowing paths to freedom.
<p>
   They climb across the Himalayas.
<p>
   Each year, hundreds of Tibetans trying to escape the alien vise of
Chinese rule set out across the world's highest mountains  &MD;  and into
blizzards, crevasses and ice  &MD;  hoping to reach Nepal.
<p>
   The unlucky are captured or turned back by Chinese authorities. Or
they die in the snow.
<p>
   The rest wander into this city frostbitten and starved, often
missing fingers and toes.
<p>
   ``Everyone was fainting, falling. The snow was at my chest and I
could not see,'' recalled Taga, a 22-year-old man who left his Tibetan
village in December with a dream of studying English in India.
<p>
   Weeks after he left home, Taga staggered down from the peaks and
into a Nepalese settlement. His feet, blackened with frostbite, were
removed.
<p>
   ``It would have been better to die,'' Taga said, staring down at
the stumps of his legs.
<p>
   While exact numbers are impossible to verify, officials with
Tibet's India-based government-in-exile say the number of refugees
going over the Himalayas into Nepal doubled last year to 2,639  &MD;  about
seven people per day.
<p>
   The drama in the mountains is unfolding against a backdrop of
Chinese-Tibetan enmity. The Chinese army rolled into Tibet in 1950,
following the Communist Revolution, and crushed an uprising in 1959.
<p>
   In a recent statement announced over Chinese state-run radio,
President Jiang Zemin said Tibetans are now living a ``happy life''
thanks to Chinese economic policies. Chinese rule, Jiang said, is
``bright, progressive and democratic.''
<p>
   But many Tibetans resist, inspired by their spiritual leader, the
Dalai Lama, who heads the Tibetan government-in-exile in Dharmshala,
120 miles from the Chinese border. The Dalai Lama, regarded by many
Tibetans as the living Buddha, urges his people to resist Chinese rule
without violence, and even to return to Tibet to keep the culture
alive.
<p>
   In its 1997 report, Amnesty International accused the Chinese
government of ``continuing massive human rights violations'' in Tibet,
including torture, forced labor and religious persecution. The United
States recently asserted that the Beijing government was waging an
intensifying campaign of religious and political suppression that
threatens Tibet's unique culture.
<p>
   The human rights reports say little about the journeys undertaken
by the Tibetans to escape Chinese rule  &MD;  or what happens to them along
the way. The stories are recounted by the refugees themselves as they
wander into the Tibetan Reception Center in Katmandu.
<p>
   The tales begin in the villages of far western China, on a remote
plateau that fascinated Westerners long before Hollywood's current
taste for all things Tibetan. The stories recall the epic flights of
those who risked their lives to scale the Berlin Wall or float across
the Straits of Florida on an inner tube or homemade raft.
<p>
   Nyima Dickyi, 23, was a Buddhist nun living in the Tibetan village
of Nyiriku when her nunnery, Phenop Potay, was taken over by Chinese
government agents. Day after day, Dickyi recalled, she and the other
nuns sat listening to lectures on the rightness of Chinese rule and
the follies of Tibetan pride.
<p>
   Reject the Dalai Lama, the teachers said.
<p>
   ``It was a re-education program,'' Dickyi said.
<p>
   On a sunny afternoon in a Katmandu clinic where she was recovering
from her ordeal, Dickyi recalled the day when her tutors passed out
note pads and pens and asked the nuns to record everything they had
learned.
<p>
   Dickyi remembers exactly what she wrote.
<p>
   ``We want to save our country.''
<p>
   She was promptly expelled from the nunnery.
<p>
   Within days, Dickyi was on a bus to the Tibetan border town of
Lhatse, having paid 1,000 Chinese yuan  &MD;  about $120  &MD;  to a guide,
Kalsang Gurmey, who said he could spirit her safely into Nepal.
<p>
   Once in Lhatse, Dickyi met 20 other would-be refugees, and in early
December they began their journey on foot. Of the 21 in her group, six
were monks and seven were children. She was the only nun.
<p>
   Dickyi carried a blanket, about $200 in Chinese currency and a
small sack of ``sampa,'' a Tibetan staple made of toasted wheat. She
wore a traditional Tibetan dress, a nylon jacket and a pair of rubber
shoes.
<p>
   By day, Dickyi recalls, the group hid in rocks and caves. By night,
they traveled in silence.
<p>
   ``We did not want to alert the Chinese,'' Dickyi said.
<p>
   Eight days later, the group was in the Himalayas, at the Nagpa La
pass on the Nepalese border  &MD;  18,000 feet up and 30 miles from Mount
Everest.
<p>
   That's when everything went wrong.
<p>
   First, Dickyi said, the group ran out of food. Then a blizzard
swept in.
<p>
   David R. Shlim, an American doctor living in Nepal who has treated
refugees, recalled how the same storm had dumped rain on Katmandu for
three days.
<p>
   ``We knew that it was probably bad up in the mountains,'' Shlim
said. ``I remember thinking, `Anyone up there is going to be in
trouble.' ''
<p>
   One of the group's members was Taga, the young man whose feet later
had to be amputated. When the children began to collapse, Taga picked
one up, an 11-year-old girl named Shilok Dolma, and threw her on his
back. He carried Shilok for a day but finally set her down.
<p>
   ``She was dead on my back,'' Taga said.
<p>
   Then Taga, his feet going cold, collapsed himself. His sweetheart,
Chemi, picked him up and carried him. She went like that for two days.
Taga remembered that his dangling feet hurt so much that he bit into
his Chemi's back.
<p>
   ``I held Taga's feet close to my chest to keep them warm,'' Chemi
said.
<p>
   When the storm let up, five members of the group were dead  &MD;  four
of them children. Three had collapsed in the storm, and two had frozen
to death in their sleep.
<p>
   ``We put blankets over the dead,'' said Dickyi, the nun.
<p>
   Like many of the Tibetans interviewed for this article, Dickyi
harbored an almost ethereal desire to see the Dalai Lama. She said a
vision of the Dalai Lama helped keep her alive.
<p>
   ``I was senseless,'' Dickyi said. ``I kept thinking, `I am going to
die, and I will not live to see His Holiness.'''
<p>
   Eight days after the blizzard had begun, and more than two weeks
after the group had begun its hike, the 16 survivors limped into
Namche Bazar, a Nepalese town at 11,000 feet. Dickyi, who had by this
time wandered off alone, was discovered by a pair of Scottish hikers.
<p>
   In addition to the five dead, six of the survivors suffered such
severe frostbite that doctors had to amputate their toes or feet.
Dickyi lost seven toes.
<p>
   One of the surviving children was Dadon, 10, who had been sent by
her parents to study in India. She lost all her toes.
<p>
   ``I have seen my friends die in the snow,'' said Dadon, who now
walks on her heels. ``Compared to that, my toes are nothing.''
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   The toll this winter: 15 Tibetans requiring amputations and six
dead. (In December, a 3-year-old died with a group of Tibetans just
after they had crossed into Nepal.)
<p>
   Workers at the Tibetan Reception Center worry there may have been
disasters they've heard nothing about.
<p>
   ``So many die in the snow. We just don't know,'' said Tsering
Lhamo, a nurse.
<p>
   Recently, there have been other complications. Since 1989, Tibetans
entering Nepal have been allowed to stay only as long as it takes to
recover and move on to India. Many go to Dharmshala, the home of the
Dalai Lama and the seat of the Tibetan exile government.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   Last fall, refugees coming from Nepal say, it began to become
increasingly difficult to gain entrance into India. Late last year,
Tibetan officials say, a bus carrying Tibetan refugees from Nepal was
turned back by Indian border guards. By December, so few Tibetans were
getting into India that the population at the Katmandu reception
center, designed to hold 100, had swollen to 600.
<p>
   ``It was difficult to get anyone through,'' said Samdup Lhatse,
secretary of the Dalai Lama's office in Katmandu.
<p>
   A few weeks ago, the Tibetans started moving again. Tibetan
officials say India began easing up on its border restrictions once
national elections ended.
</TEXT>

<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Tibetans Flee Chinese Cultural Crackdown (Katmandu) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   KATMANDU, Nepal  &MD;  Tibetan refugees say they are fleeing Chinese
repression that has worsened since 1996, when Beijing stepped up its
efforts to re-educate Buddhist religious leaders and launched a
``Strike Hard'' campaign against dissent.
<p>
   Many of the latest refugees are Buddhist monks and nuns expelled
from their monasteries and nunneries by agents of the Chinese
government. Others are children, sent by their parents to schools in
India where they can learn their language and history, which is often
suppressed in Chinese-run schools.
<p>
   The surge threatens to upset the delicate and largely unspoken
arrangement among China, India and Nepal to tolerate a trickle of
refugees out of Tibet since the Chinese invaded in 1950. And it adds a
new poignancy to the Tibetans' struggle to secure self-rule and
preserve their culture in the face of a sustained Chinese onslaught.
<p>
   ``What the Tibetans endure is almost unbelievable,'' said David R.
Shlim, an American doctor living in Nepal who has treated dozens of
refugees. ``They cross the highest mountains in the world, they have
no tents, no sleeping bags, and they are often wearing only
Windbreakers and cheap Chinese sneakers.''
</TEXT>

<HEADER> - a0830 04-14 0512 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-helpwanted - a0830
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: Financial editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Carrie Mason-Draffen </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; HELP WANTED: Weight-y Issue Aired in Public by Ex-Boss &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Dear Help Wanted: Can a supervisor legally humiliate you in front
of your co-workers about your weight? My supervisor told me in front
of everybody that I had gained so much weight that she would have to
buy me another chair. She said on another occasion in front of
everyone that my clothes were so wrinkled I looked like I slept in my
car. I became so stressed that I quit. Is there any action I can take
against the company or the supervisor?  &MD; On-the-Job-Torment
<p>
   Dear On-the-Job: You probably have no legal recourse here. But boy,
does your ex-boss need to be forced to sit with Miss Manners.
<p>
   An employer has the right to regulate what workers wear and how
they act, especially in an office setting, said New York labor lawyer
Jonathan Sack.
<p>
   Employers have a reasonable expectation that their employees will
look a certain way and act a certain way, he said.
<p>
   So, your former boss's approach, though barbarous, is legal. And
she proves once more that not everyone who is a manager knows how to
treat people.
<p>
   The outcome might have been different if she simply had conveyed
her concerns in private. She could still have gotten her point across,
but without humiliating you.
<p>
   Now, if she had chastised you publicly for things unrelated to the
office, that could have constituted defamation, Sack said. But that
violation is tough to prove, he added.
<p>
   If you should confront a similarly cretinous boss  &MD;  one should have
that misfortune only once in a lifetime  &MD;  tell him or her that you
prefer to discuss problems in a private meeting.
<p>
   If that doesn't work, make a note of the insults and write a letter
to management asking them to put a stop to the behavior.
<p>
   That kind of conduct should not be tolerated in the workplace, Sack
said.
 &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Dear Readers: My sources at the U.S. Labor Department's Long
Island, N.Y., office, which enforces the federal Fair Labor Standards
Act in the area, called to point out an error in last week's answer
about comp time vs. overtime. In that column I quoted someone who said
that when hourly employees are paid every two weeks, and they work 50
hours in one week, their employer can legally give them comp time the
next week to avoid paying overtime.
<p>
   The comp time is illegal in that instance, said Irv Miljoner, who
heads the Long Island office, because whether hourly employees get
overtime depends on how many hours they work in a single week. If the
hourly employees work more than 40 hours in a single week, they must
be paid overtime for every hour over 40.
<p>
   Each workweek stands alone to determie compliance, he said. One
cannot be credited against the other.
<p>
   Send questions to Help Wanted, Business Desk, Newsday, 235 Pinelawn
Rd., Melville, N.Y. 11747-4250. Please give your name, address and
phone number; they won't be published.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-mexico-protest </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0830 04-14 1309 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-mexico-protest - a0830
 &UR; (ATTN: Foreign editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Mary Beth Sheridan </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Scandal Fuels Protest Movement in Mexican State (Cuernavaca) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   CUERNAVACA, Mexico  &MD;  To U.S. tourists and well-heeled Mexicans,
this resort city is a haven of sunny weather, elegant restaurants and
riotous gardens of purple and magenta bougainvillea.
<p>
   But in recent years, Cuernavaca has become a haven of a more
sinister sort. Kidnappers have sown terror in the city and region,
abducting hundreds of people. Drug lords have moved into the walled
mansions of the ``city of eternal spring.'' Authorities seemed
helpless in the face of chaos.
<p>
   Now Mexicans are learning why. In a microcosm of the crisis in the
nation's criminal justice system, top state officials are under
arrest, accused of helping drug and kidnapping gangs or covering up
police abuse.
<p>
   Rarely in Mexico have the authorities been tied so openly to
criminals. Even more startling has been the response: In a glimpse of
the new Mexico, citizens in Cuernavaca and the surrounding state,
Morelos, have launched protests that now threaten to bring down the
powerful governor.
<p>
   ``People have had it,'' said Patricia Edelen, 46, a Cuernavaca
interior decorator who joined the demonstrations after several friends
were abducted. ``People have been kidnapped and robbed, and they're
not going to be quiet about it.''
<p>
   Just 90 minutes from Mexico City, Cuernavaca has long been a
weekend refuge for residents fleeing the giant capital's smog. It also
has been a magnet for foreigners, ranging from Spanish-language
students to U.S. retirees to socialites such as the late Barbara
Hutton. But a four-year wave of crime in the state  &MD;  including an
estimated 350 kidnappings  &MD;  has led to an exodus.
<p>
   As in other parts of Mexico, where officials have lost control over
police as the authoritarian political system crumbles, residents here
had suspected that the cops were involved in crime. What set Morelos
apart was the startling event that confirmed those suspicions.
<p>
   On Jan. 28, a highway patrol cruiser spotted a black Ram pickup
that had pulled off a deserted freeway in Guerrero, the state next to
Morelos. Stopping to investigate, the officers found a body stuffed
behind the driver's seat  &MD;  and three Morelos state police officers at
the wheel, including the commander of the anti-kidnap unit, Armando
Martinez Salgado.
<p>
   Martinez Salgado and four other officers have been charged with
belonging to a kidnapping gang. Forty other anti-kidnap police
officers have disappeared, apparently fleeing. Authorities in the
nearby state of Guanajuato announced recently that Martinez Salgado
was also under investigation there for allegedly collecting a ransom
for a man who had been abducted. Martinez Salgado's family and lawyer
declined comment, but he has reportedly testified that his superiors
knew of his actions.
<p>
   The scandal has spread to the Morelos state police chief and
attorney general, who have been charged with involvement in the death
of the man in the pickup. According to media reports, the victim
allegedly was a criminal tortured and slain during a dispute with
police over ransom.
<p>
   Then, in one of the most stunning turns in the case, officials
announced that Martinez Salgado had admitted protecting drug lords. He
reportedly said he met with Amado Carrillo Fuentes, one of Mexico's
top traffickers before his death last July, and his lieutenant, Juan
Jose Esparragoza.
<p>
   The confession outraged residents, who have discovered in recent
years that the traffickers owned mansions in Morelos and appeared to
operate freely.
<p>
   ``The same people who should be guaranteeing justice are the
delinquents,'' protested Ricardo Alvarez, a 73-year-old photographer
who snaps tourists in Cuernavaca's colonial plaza. ``Everyone is very
upset.''
<p>
   Morelos' governor, Jorge Carrillo Olea, a prominent figure in the
long-ruling Institutional Revolutionary Party, or PRI, insists that he
knew nothing of his subordinates' alleged wrongdoing. Federal
authorities say they have no evidence that the governor was involved.
<p>
   But the revelations have fueled a movement to remove him from
office. For two years, human rights and citizens groups have been
calling for action against the police for failing to stop the
kidnappings, which had affected a growing number of people  &MD;  wealthy,
working class and even some foreigners.
<p>
   Now the movement has swelled. Nearly 10,000 residents marched
through Cuernavaca in February demanding the governor's resignation.
More than 100,000 residents  &MD;  one-quarter of those who voted in the
last gubernatorial election  &MD;  turned out last month for a nonbinding
unofficial referendum on whether the governor should stay. More than
94 percent said no.
<p>
   The protesters have ranged from left- to right-wing politicians,
from Chamber of Commerce leaders to supporters of the Zapatista
rebels. The denunciations have spread from plaza to pulpit: The local
Roman Catholic bishop publicly excommunicated Martinez Salgado, the
anti-kidnap chief.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   ``The people of Morelos are the face of the new Mexico, where
participation is key,'' said Santiago Creel, a member of Congress from
the conservative National Action Party, or PAN.
<p>
   In the past, he noted, the decision to fire an unpopular governor
came from the president himself. But in this case, ``the decision is
being built from below.''
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   But the scandal is not only a test of citizens' power as Mexico
moves from a one-party system to full democracy. It also represents
new muscle-flexing by opposition parties, which took control of the
Morelos state legislature for the first time in midterm elections a
year ago.
<p>
   The state Congress has announced it will begin proceedings shortly
to impeach Carrillo Olea. The opposition says it has the votes to
force him out and name a successor. But the possibility that the
governor could be replaced without a popular vote has left some
uneasy. PRI leaders and others charge that the opposition is using the
protest movement to install one of its own in the governor's mansion.
<p>
   ``This uproar has been created by a small group that is using the
(demonstrators) as a political trampoline,'' Carrillo Olea told the
daily Reforma last month. The governor declined to be interviewed for
this article.
<p>
   The impeachment process is expected to revolve around accusations
that the governor did not react to the crime crisis and allegations of
police involvement. Many critics, however, believe that the governor's
responsibility goes deeper. They say that Carrillo Olea, a retired
general who had directed Mexico's anti-drug force and its intelligence
agency, must have realized what his officials were up to.
<p>
   The governor has said he believed the police were combating crime,
noting they had jailed more than 400 alleged kidnappers. But human
rights groups and opposition politicians charge that many of those
were either small-time hoods or innocent people.
<p>
   Critics also are asking how Carrillo Olea could have missed the
fact that drug lords were moving into the state  &MD;  one reportedly
occupying a house just three blocks from the governor's mansion.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   Carrillo Olea has responded that it wasn't his job as governor to
investigate drug trafficking, which is a federal crime.
<p>
   Manuel Diego Castillo, head of a business executives' group that
has joined the protests against the governor, said it had been public
knowledge that traffickers were living in the area. But they were
never detained.
<p>
   ``This bothers us,'' he said. ``The evidence indicates that
Cuernavaca came to be a vacation center not just for tourists from
Mexico City, but also for drug traffickers.''
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-russia-cars </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0831 04-14 1061 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-russia-cars - a0831
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Foreign editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Kathy Lally </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Russian Car-Buyers Dazzled by Newfound Choices (Moscow) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   MOSCOW  &MD;  Oleg Kulikov moonlighted for months, saving every ruble he
could to buy a brand-new car. Finally, he amassed the small fortune of
$7,000 and triumphantly brought home a shiny red Niva. Within two
weeks, the carburetor went out.
<p>
   ``I'm happy as an elephant,'' Kulikov, a 40-year-old army colonel,
proclaimed a few days later. So far only a bad carburetor!
<p>
   For years, almost all Russian drivers were like Kulikov,
supplicants so abjectly grateful when manufacturers deigned to sell
them a car that they never dared hope it would actually run well.
Those days are fast disappearing, and car buyers are finding
themselves in an unfamiliar position: masters of the market instead of
slaves to it.
<p>
   Foreign-car makers are driving into Russia at full speed, in
pursuit of what is expected to become the world's largest car market
over the next few years. And consumers are encountering something they
never expected: a choice.
<p>
   Russian drivers once counted themselves lucky if they could lay
their hands on a Zhiguli (exported as the Lada), the ubiquitous, boxy
little car based on a now-30-year-old Fiat design. In Soviet times,
consumers didn't actually use the verb ``to buy'' when discussing car
purchases; they used a verb that means ``get hold of.'' That's because
money was not enough  &MD;  you had to wait in line, usually for seven or
eight years, sometimes 20, before you got the privilege of buying a
car.
<p>
   Everyone knew what they were getting  &MD;  a noisy car, cramped and
hard to steer, but very easy to fix. The easy repairs always have been
cherished, because the cars break down constantly. Informed consumers
think of their new Russian car as a kit, which they must take apart
and reassemble before driving.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Masha Gessen, a Moscow writer, says new car owners typically know
to take the car apart, oil all the nuts and bolts, then put it back
together again.
<p>
   ``If you don't do it from the start,'' she writes, ``after a few
months the nuts and bolts meld into one another, becoming inseparable.
So if you are not careful, next time you have to change the air
filter, you'll have to cut the carburetor cover off with a torch.''
<p>
   She drives a Volvo.
<p>
   For additional insurance, new owners have their cars blessed by
priests, who traditionally performed similar blessings on carriages
and animals.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   Russia has tried to protect its market by imposing import taxes of
up to 80 percent. But with car sales expected to reach 15 million a
year within six or seven years (car ownership currently is 15 percent
of what it is in the United States) foreign manufacturers have not
been deterred.
<p>
   They have been setting up assembly plants within Russia and other
former Soviet republics to avoid import taxes. The locally assembled
foreign cars are astounding shoppers, says Aleksandr S. Arakelyan, who
opened a Kia showroom last month in a space that had housed a small
supermarket.
<p>
   ``Their first question,'' he says, ``is this: `Have we reached the
level that in this country we can make such good cars?' ''
<p>
   The smallest Kia sells for under $12,000, with air conditioning and
other luxuries that dazzle Russian buyers. The bare-bones Zhiguli
starts at $6,000, but the more attractive one goes for $11,000. Many
customers would rather wait until they can save a little more and buy
a car like the Ford Escort, assembled in Belarus and costing about
$13,000.
<p>
   The competition from foreign brands is forcing Russian
manufacturers to begin modernizing. The latest Volga  &MD;  the traditional
car of officialdom  &MD;  has rounded edges that faintly resemble a
Mercedes. Even the Zhiguli has begun to look less like a can of Spam
and more like a real car.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Car dealerships are opening everywhere. One big one, called Alan,
operates from a fifth-floor walk-up. (There's a winding ramp inside
the building for the cars.)
<p>
   Arakelyan hopes to turn his dank former supermarket, which now has
about half-a-dozen cars, into a plush Kia showroom where buyers can
select a car and then sit in a lounge watching television and drinking
coffee while the car is registered or an alarm is installed.
<p>
   Perhaps, someday, prospective buyers will even be able to
test-drive a car. ``It might get dirty!'' one Kia salesman said in
shocked tones when the idea was suggested.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   The big automakers like Ford and General Motors are counting on an
emerging middle class for future car sales and have tried to tap that
new market by arranging for customers to buy cars on credit. Most
buyers now arrive with stacks of hundred-dollar bills; there are no
checks here.
<p>
   Banks have been offering car loans since last year, but the word
has been slow getting out despite banners strung across major streets
to advertise them.
<p>
   Sergei Andreichenkov, director of a General Motors dealership in
Moscow, hopes that credit will allow ordinary people to buy his big
seller  &MD;  the Chevrolet Blazer. The four-wheel-drive Blazer costs about
$40,000 here, and he says 90 percent of his customers have been
gangsters.
<p>
   But when buyers discover that the banks offering loans want 30
percent down and charge interest of 22 percent or more for dollar
loans and 45 percent for ruble loans, their eagerness to buy on credit
abates somewhat.
<p>
   ``You have to pay interest!'' said one shopper, Sergei Ivanov, as
if discovering the trick in the fine print.
<p>
   One bank requires a certificate of mental health of anyone
requesting a loan of more than $5,000  &MD;  perhaps assuming you'd have to
be crazy to do that. Still, Andreichenkov said, two people took out
loans last month to buy Blazers from him.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   Not everyone is enamored by the foreign cars. Dmitri Turkin always
has bought a Moskvich  &MD;  the notoriously unreliable car made in Moscow
 &MD; 
<p>
   and he always will. Turkin loves to spend his time outside his
Moscow apartment, repairing his car.
<p>
   ``With a Moskvich,'' he says, ``I know I'll always have plenty of
work.''
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-russia-cars--magazine </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0832 04-14 0366 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-russia-cars--magazine - a0832
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: Foreign editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Kathy Lally </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Car Magazine Reflects Changes in Russian Market (Moscow) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   MOSCOW  &MD;  Perhaps nothing reflects the changing Russian market as
much as the car magazine Behind the Wheel, published since April 1928.
<p>
   Once a combination instruction manual and Communist propaganda
pamphlet, it has become a glossy, full-color consumer guide printed in
Italy.
<p>
   Russian and foreign cars are evaluated on the basis of test drives.
``We are independent,'' says Vladimir Arusha, the deputy editor.
<p>
   ``I am very proud we don't belong to any bank or any enterprise
producing cars. We live on what we earn and can say what we want.''
<p>
   A new feature, called Women's Club, tries to entice woman drivers,
who were nearly nonexistent until a few years ago.
<p>
   ``The attitude is conservative,'' Arusha says.
<p>
   ``Older people don't approve of woman drivers. What makes me happy
is people don't care about this conservatism. People are not waiting
for attitudes to change. They sit behind the wheel and drive.''
<p>
   Arusha says that not one of the approximately 200 women who live in
his Moscow apartment building drove until two years ago. Now, there
are about 10 female drivers.
<p>
   In the latest Behind the Wheel, Women's Club columnist Natalya
Yefimova offered some personal driving tips.
<p>
   ``One of my attractive, black-eyed girlfriends drives with a sign
on her car that says `Invalid,' '' Yefimova wrote.
<p>
   She hoped the sign would make male drivers take pity on her. Many
men sneer at the idea of women driving and enjoy tailgating them,
cutting them off at high speed, then slamming on the brakes.
<p>
   Yefimova advises against perfume because it can mix with the smell
of gasoline and give you a headache or make you sick.
<p>
   She offered some stern advice about fur coats: Don't wear one when
behind the wheel. She was wearing her mink when she leaned over to
look at a tire. A shoulder seam split.
<p>
   Also, mink tends to produce static electricity in a car, sending
off sparks if your boyfriend is a passenger.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

   What do you call a deer with no eyes?

<p>
   An envelope.  &MD; Juliann Ryan
<p>
   What did Ali Baba order at McDonald's?
<p>
   Two all-beef patties on an open sesame seed bun.  &MD; Chris DePietto
<p>
   What is black and white and green all over?
<p>
   A referee who fell into the Gatorade bowl.  &MD; Brendan Quigley
<p>
   How can you tell if a bee is on the phone?
<p>
   You keep getting a buzzy signal.  &MD; Alexandra Loverro
<p>

   Quacker Barrel.  &MD; Donna Ossenfort

<p>

<p>

<p>

<SLUG> bc-kidsday-recipes </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0834 04-14 0670 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-kidsday-recipes - a0834
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: Feature editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; KIDS IN THE KITCHEN: Recipes for Kids &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
    &UR; COOKIE CAKE &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; By Corey Robson &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; Kidsday Staff Reporter &QC; 
<p>
   2 cups flour
<p>
   1 teaspoon baking soda
<p>
   1 teaspoon salt
<p>
   2 sticks butter, melted
<p>
    cup granulated sugar
<p>
    cup packed brown sugar
<p>
   1 teaspoon vanilla extract
<p>
   2 eggs
<p>
   12 ounces semi-sweet chocolate chips
<p>
   Combine flour, baking soda and salt in small bowl. Beat butter,
granulated sugar, brown sugar and vanilla in large bowl. Add eggs one
at a time, beating well after each addition; gradually beat in flour
mixture. Stir in chips. Spread batter out on a cookie sheet.
<p>
   Bake in preheated 375-degree oven for 9 to 11 minutes, or until
golden brown. Cool for 2 minutes, then remove to wire rack to cool
completely.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; CINNAMON CHOCOLATE-CHIP PANCAKES &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; By Alyssa Camacho &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; Kidsday Staff Reporter &QC; 
<p>
   3 tablespoons sugar
<p>
    teaspoon cinnamon
<p>
   1 egg, beaten
<p>
   1 cup flour
<p>
    cup quick-cooking oats
<p>
   1 teaspoon baking powder
<p>
   2 tablespoons butter, melted
<p>
   1 cup club soda
<p>
    cup semi-sweet chocolate chips
<p>
   In a small mixing bowl, mix the sugar and the cinnamon; set aside.
Combine egg, flour, oats, baking powder, melted butter, club soda,
chocolate chips and 2 tablespoons of cinnamon-sugar mixture. Heat a
griddle or skillet; spray with cooking spray. Drop batter by
tablespoonsful on hot griddle. When the edges are golden brown, flip
the pancakes over and cook the other side. Remove when done. Sprinkle
with remaining cinnamon-sugar mixture while still warm. Serve with
maple syrup or jam, if desired. Makes about 12 pancakes.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; PEANUT BUTTER MERINGUES &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; By Donna Ossenfort &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; Kidsday Staff Reporter &QC; 
<p>
   2 egg whites
<p>
    teaspoon cream of tartar
<p>
    cup sugar
<p>
    cup peanut butter
<p>
    cup M&AMP;Ms or semisweet chocolate chips
<p>
   In a large bowl, beat egg whites and cream of tartar with an
electric mixer until stiff peaks form. Gradually add sugar, 1
tablespoon at a time, beating well after each addition. Lightly fold
in peanut butter and M&AMP;Ms, or chocolate chips, until mixed.
<p>
   Drop dough by teaspoonfuls onto a greased cookie sheet. Bake in a
preheated, 300-degree oven for 25 minutes, or until lightly browned.
Remove from cookie sheet immediately. Makes about 2 dozen.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; CHOCOLATE CARAMEL WALNUT FUDGE &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; By Bridget Lovett &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; Kidsday Staff Reporter &QC; 
<p>
   3 cups semisweet chocolate pieces
<p>
   14-ounce can sweetened condensed milk
<p>
   2 tablespoons water
<p>
    1/8 teaspoon salt
<p>
   1 cup walnuts, chopped
<p>
    cup caramel ice cream topping
<p>
   1 teaspoons vanilla extract
<p>
   In a saucepan, over low heat, melt chocolate, sweetened condensed
milk, water and salt. Remove from heat; stir in walnuts, caramel
topping and vanilla extract. Spread evenly into foil-lined
8-inch-square pan. Chill two hours, or until firm. Turn fudge onto
cutting board; peel off foil, cut into squares. Makes about 2 pounds.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; FETTUCCINE ALFREDO &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; By Andrew Gordon &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; Kidsday Staff Reporter &QC; 
<p>
   1 pound fresh fettuccine pasta
<p>
    stick unsalted butter
<p>
    cup fresh parsley, chopped
<p>
    cup Parmesan cheese
<p>
    teaspoon white pepper
<p>
    teaspoon salt
<p>
   1 cup lite evaporated milk
<p>
   Cook fresh pasta according to directions; drain and set aside.
Place butter in heated skillet. Add parsley and cook about 2 minutes.
Add Parmesan cheese, white pepper, salt and evaporated milk. Cook 3
minutes; stir in cooked pasta. Makes 4 servings.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; FRUIT ON A STICK &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; By Beth Crescenzo &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; Kidsday Staff Reporter &QC; 
<p>
   2 apples
<p>
   1 banana
<p>
    pound grapes
<p>
   1 pint strawberries
<p>
   2 oranges
<p>
   1 cup vanilla yogurt
<p>
    cup shredded coconut
<p>
   Wooden skewers
<p>
   Cube fruits; place on skewers. Mix together yogurt and coconut. Dip
fruits into mixture.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>
