<SLUG> bc-kidsday-shopper </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0834 04-14 0725 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-kidsday-shopper - a0834
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: Feature editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; STUFF KIDS BUY: New Books, Toys and Games &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
    &UR; DRESSING IN STYLE &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; By Andrew Brooks, Scott Gorski and Michael Rogers &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; Kidsday Staff Reporters &QC; 
<p>
   Have you heard of And 1? Well, we think it is the most popular
clothing around for kids. And 1 is a basketball term, and it means
that if you get fouled while taking a shot and the basket goes in, you
then get a foul shot and a chance to make a three-point play.
<p>
   And 1 sells T-shirts, pants, tank tops, shoes, shorts, sweatshirts
and sports equipment. It has a wide selection of items for kids and
adults. Basketball star Stephon Marbury of the Minnesota Timberwolves
promotes And 1 products. You can find And 1 at many stores.
<p>
   We think kids like And 1 because of the clever quotes on the back
of the T-shirts. For example, on the back of one of them it says
``Forget the circus, I'm the greatest show on Earth.'' Other quotes we
liked were ``Take some aspirin, this is gonna hurt'' and ``Home is
where the hoop is.'' The average price for a kid's short-sleeve
T-shirt is $15, and $20 for a long-sleeve shirt. A hooded sweatshirt
is $30, without a hood is $25, and shorts are about $20.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; MICRO MACHINES V3 &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; By Erin Brennan &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; Kidsday Staff Reporter
<p>
   The video game ``Micro Machines V3'' ($49.95, PlayStation) is
really fun. You are a race car driver, and you have to try to get
through many different, cool levels. Before you begin, there are these
neat drivers such as Spider, Dwayne, Cherry, Jade, Walter, Chen,
Bonnie and Jethro that you get to choose as your racer. There are sly
turns, tough curves and little ``gifts'' that give you bonus help so
you can have an advantage while playing. These gifts are torpedoes and
hammers, and they can be used to hit or slam other cars. There are
lots of traps and force fields to make your racing more adventurous.
The 3D graphics make everything look real, and up to eight kids can
play at once. I was definitely pleased with this game.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC;

<p>
    &UR; `ALLSTAR SPORTSTORY' SERIES &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; By Nicole Lord and Leeza Menon &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; Kidsday Staff Reporters &QC; 
<p>
   If you are a sports fan or just love sports books, you should check
out the new ``AllStar SportStory'' series by Fred Bowen (Peachtree
Publishers, $4.95). We read ``The Golden Glove'' and ``Full Court
Fever,'' and they are books you cannot put down.
<p>
   The reason they are so appealing is you can identify with many of
the characters, and what they go through to achieve their dreams. The
author also combines fiction with real sports history. For example, in
``Full Court Press'' a basketball team goes up against a difficult
team, and they end up using a defensive strategy  &MD;  the full-court
press  &MD;  that was first used by the UCLA basketball team during the
1964 NCAA tournament.
<p>
   In ``The Golden Glove,'' you'll read history on the origin of
baseball gloves  &MD;  the different phases, shapes and looks the gloves
went through in order to reach its present-day perfection. This
magical book is also about a 12-year-old boy, Jamie, whose most prized
possession is his baseball glove with Cal Ripken's signature on it.
Jamie believes his glove is the lucky charm that helps him win games  &MD; 
until he loses his glove. Then he finds out that he, not his glove, is
responsible for his success.
<p>
   Bowen is a natural when it comes to writing sports stories, and
informing his readers about the past. These books are perfect for kids
ages 8 to 12. Other books in the series are ``T.J.'s Secret Pitch,''
``The Kid Coach'' and ``Playoff Dreams.''

<PREAMBLE>
bc-kidsday-mazzello - a0835
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: Feature editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Beth Crescenzo and Meghan McDonald </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; KIDSDAY TALKS WITH: Movie Star Joseph Mazzello &QL; 
 &UR; Kidsday Staff Reporters &QC; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   We recently interviewed 14-year-old movie star Joseph Mazzello, who
has been in 14 movies, including ``Jurassic Park'' and ``The River
Wild.'' Most recently he starred in ``Star Kid.''
<p>
   Q: Did your parents support you when you decided to pursue a movie
career?
<p>
   A: I was only 5, so they had to help. What happened was my sister
had been doing a lot of commercials. She would study her lines and go
over them so many times that I would end up memorizing them. My
parents thought that I was pretty good at it so they asked me if I
wanted to try it. I was really just following my big sister.
<p>
   Q: When you are not acting, do you consider yourself just a normal
kid?
<p>
   A: At school, and with my friends, I try to put acting aside. I try
to be a normal person and not even think about it.
<p>
   Q: Does anyone else in your family besides your sister act?
<p>
   A: My sister stopped when she was in sixth grade and she is 16 now.
My brother acts off and on; he is in my new movie, ``A Small
Miracle.'' My parents own a dance studio and they have been dancing a
long time. My father was on Broadway.
<p>
   Q: Have you ever had a stunt double?
<p>
   A: All the time. They really don't let kids do many of their own
stunts in case they get hurt. A broken arm is not going to look too
good when I'm wearing a cast for half the movie.
<p>
   Q: Do you have any advice for kids who want to become actors?
<p>
   A: You have to get an agent. It is not so much fun at first. You
have to go to all auditions and then go to all the callbacks. You
might be disappointed at first. If you go into acting, make sure it is
not your whole life. In acting you have your highs and lows, and it is
not something you can always rely on.
<p>
   Q: What are some of your hobbies?
<p>
   A: Acting is my hobby. I am just lucky that I get paid for it. I
think baseball is my favorite sport, but I don't play organized sports
because the school is too small.
<p>
   Q: Does acting interfere with your schoolwork?
<p>
   A: It didn't used to, but now that I am in high school, it is
getting a little harder.
<p>
   Q: How do your friends treat you?
<p>
   A: I had been going to the same school for nine years. In seventh
and eighth grade I only had eight kids in my class. Everyone got to
know one another well. Now that I am in high school some kids are
still getting used to me being an actor.
<p>
   Q: Do you want to keep this career?
<p>
   A: Yes. I am going to go to film school and maybe try to be a
director.
<p>
   Q: What movies do you have coming up?
<p>
   A: I just finished the movie ``A Small Miracle.'' Jim Carrey
narrates it.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Write to Joseph Mazzello c/o Trimark Pictures, 2644 30th St., Santa
Monica, Calif. 90405-3009.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC;

<BYLINE> By Sean Rubin, Heather Tennie and Meagan Pascal </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; KIDSDAY TALKS WITH: Singer Celine Dion &QL; 
 &UR; Kidsday Staff Reporters &QC; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   We recently interviewed singer Celine Dion. Her latest album is
``Let's Talk About Love,'' which contains the hits ``My Heart Will Go
On,'' from the movie ``Titanic,'' and ``Tell Him,'' a duet with Barbra
Streisand.
<p>
   Q: Were you named after anyone?
<p>
   A: My mom decided to name me Celine because there was a song in
France called ``Celine.'' While my mom was pregnant with me she kept
listening to it on the radio. So she decided to name me after that
song.
<p>
   Q: Where did you grow up?
<p>
   A: I grew up in the Province of Quebec. It is a beautiful place. It
has 6 million people and four seasons with beautiful summers and cold,
cold winters. Fifty percent of the people speak French and English,
and the other 50 percent speak only French. I am from the a little
town that is like the town on the TV show ``Little House on the
Prairie.'' Everybody knows everyone. I went to school in Charlemagne.
It is a little town about 15 minutes from Montreal. We had 16 people
living in the house and we slept three kids in a bed.
<p>
   Q: Do your songs tell true stories?
<p>
   A: They tell true stories, but not necessarily my stories. All the
songs I sing are not my dreams or my life, but I might sing something
that is someone else's dream. When I sing the song, I become the
character in my song.
<p>
   Q: Do you dedicate your songs to anyone?
<p>
   A: To anyone who is willing to open their hearts. I dedicate them
to my husband (Rene), because we are very much in love. When I sing my
songs I think of him, and I sing with more feeling.
<p>
   Q: Have you been blessed with other gifts besides singing?
<p>
   A. I'm not too bad a cook, and my husband loves my cooking. There
are so many things I feel lucky about. For one person to have so many
things is just too much. I feel that someone is looking over me. I can
do what I love. I travel the world, I've met some wonderful people. I
have wonderful parents and brothers and sisters. I have my health and
I have good friends. I have realized so many dreams.
<p>
   Q: What do you like, and not like, about being famous?
<p>
   A: I love to sing. My songs can touch people. People come up to me
and tell me that they got married to a song of mine. Touching people
with music is very rewarding. I've been able to travel the world, and
being able to talk with people, I learn from everyone. That is
important to me because I stopped going to school very early. The
not-so-good is I miss my family very much. I want to be with them
more.
<p>
   Q: What else do you want in your life?
<p>
   A: I would like to have children. It is wonderful to have a career
and have lots of stuff, but show business is not a real life. I would
love to have a family of my own  &MD;  not 14 children like my parents. I
would like to have children and share my happiness with my newborn.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   You can write to Celine Dion c/o Sony 550 Music, 550 Madison Ave.,
New York, N.Y. 10022-3211.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

<BYLINE> By Brian Barnwell, Louisa Cataldo and Sara Eirich </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; KIDSDAY TALKS WITH: Musical Group Sugar Ray &QL; 
 &UR; Kidsday Staff Reporters &QC; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   We recently interviewed Stan, 28, and Craig, 26, (also known as
D.J. Homicide), two members of the group Sugar Ray, at their Atlantic
Records offices in New York City. Their second CD, ``Floored,'' went
multiplatinum, and it contains the song ``Fly.''
<p>
   Q: Why did you name the band Sugar Ray?
<p>
   Stan: We like boxing. There were two boxers: One was Sugar Ray
Robinson and the other Sugar Ray Leonard. When we were deciding what
to call the band, we were working at a place where we practiced every
day. The guy who owned our practice space used to work with one of the
Sugar Rays and he would tell us stories about him, so we named the
band after them.
<p>
   Q: Why did you write ``Fly'' and what does it mean?
<p>
   Craig: It just kind of came out. We were in a fight one night and
actually wrote it in the studio. Me, Stan and Rodney (the guitar
player) and Murphy (bass player) were saying let's not fight. We
stayed at the practice hall and it came out in like five minutes. My
job is to make beats and stuff. So, I had a beat and Murphy had a bass
line for background music. Stan came out with ``I just want to fly.''
He didn't write it, he just sang it. It just went from there.
<p>
   Q: Do your parents like your music?
<p>
   Stan: My mom likes the song ``Fly'' very much. She doesn't like a
lot of the other songs on our record.
<p>
   Craig: My mom likes ``Fly'' and ``RPM,'' too. She doesn't like
anything else.
<p>
   Q: How did you feel about ``Fly'' being the most played video on
MTV?
<p>
   Craig: When we sold our first 500,000 records, that justified us as
a band. It made me cry. It meant out of every video on MTV, we were
No. 1.
<p>
   Q: Which CD of yours do you like better, ``Lemonade and Brownies''
or ``Floored''?
<p>
   Stan: I like our CD ``Lemonade and Brownies'' better because it was
at a time when we were very excited about being signed to Atlantic
Records. It was just a very innoncent period for the band. I think
``Floored'' will have the same feeling in about six months.
<p>
   Q: What does your family think of your success?
<p>
   Craig: I have a younger sister. She is the most popular kid in
school right now. Her name is on the record. She says, ``You have to
come to my school and pick me up.'' I don't have a cool car, so I
don't want to do it. She is having as much fun as I am.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Write to Sugar Ray c/o P.O. Box 46066, Los Angeles, Calif.
90046-0066, or visit the Web site at www.SUGAR-RAY.com &QL; 
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC;

<SLUG> bc-health-drivers </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0319 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-health-drivers
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Health editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Sally Squires </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Vision Test May Help Screen Unsafe Drivers &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   A new vision test may help predict which drivers can still sit
safely behind the wheel and which ones should stop driving.
<p>
   A study of nearly 300 people, aged 55 to 87 years, found that an
experimental computer vision exam seems to be helpful in identifying
drivers who are more likely to have traffic accidents.
<p>
   The findings suggest ``that there may be a way to protect older
drivers and the community in a very reasonable way,'' said Jared B.
Jobe, chief of the National Institute on Aging's adult psychological
development branch, which sponsored the study.
<p>
   Researchers at the University of Alabama in Birmingham used
computer screens filled with visual distractions to gauge how well
drivers could pick out cars, trucks and other objects scattered on the
screen. Unlike standard vision tests, this test was designed to
measure how well people were able to process what they saw  &MD;  something
that is key to safe driving.
<p>
   People in the study were tracked from 1990 to 1993. The team of
researchers, which was led by Cynthia Owsley with the clinical
research unit at the Eye Foundation Hospital in Birmingham, also
collected traffic-accident reports to identify crashes involving the
drivers.
<p>
   During the three-year follow-up period, 56 people in the study were
involved in at least one traffic accident. The study found that people
who showed a 40 percent or greater impairment in their ``useful field
of view'' were twice as likely as others in the study to be involved
in a traffic accident. The study also found that for every 10-point
reduction in a driver's useful field of view, the risk of being in an
accident rose 16 percent, regardless of the driver's age.
<p>
   The study was in last week's Journal of the American Medical
Association.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-health-weight </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0551 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-health-weight
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Health editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Susan Okie </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Weight Loss After 50 Linked to Shorter Life &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Significant weight loss after age 50 is correlated with higher
death rates after age 65 in both men and women, a new study has found.
On the other hand, being overweight did not lead to increased
mortality.
<p>
   Researchers from the University of Washington and several other
institutions studied 4,317 nonsmoking adults who were between 65 and
100 years old. All participants were nonsmokers and none were
wheelchair-bound or undergoing cancer treatment.
<p>
   The researchers noted the participants' weight at entry into the
study as well as their weight (based on their recollection) at age 50.
They also collected information on recent weight loss, smoking
history, history of disorders such as diabetes, heart disease and
cancer, and other data. Then they recorded all deaths in the study
group over a five-year period, and analyzed the relationship between
mortality and body mass index (BMI), a ratio of weight to height.
<p>
   The thinnest women had a significantly higher death rate than women
in all other categories, even after results were adjusted for age and
the presence of various diseases. For men, there was no significant
relationship between BMI and mortality. Thus, among elderly people of
both sexes, the study found no evidence that being overweight
increased the risk of death.
<p>
   However, recent, unintended weight loss was a definite risk factor.
People who had lost 10 or more pounds without trying in the year
before entering the study had high death rates during the ensuing five
years: 33 percent for men, 16 percent for women. This may reflect the
fact that spontaneous weight loss often signals the presence of cancer
or chronic illness. (Death rates were not elevated among people who
had intentionally lost weight in the previous year, or among those who
had gained weight or stayed the same.)
<p>
   But the most striking finding was the correlation of significant
weight loss after age 50 (whether intentional or not) with higher
death rates after age 65. Of men who reported having lost 10 percent
or more of their body weight since age 50, 30 percent died during the
study period. Among women with this degree of weight loss, 16 percent
died. Those death rates were about twice as high as the rates seen
among people whose weight had remained stable.
<p>
   For many older people, the protective effects of obesity may
outweigh the negative aspects, write University of Washington
biostatics professor Paula Diehr and coauthors. Fat ``provides a
nutritional reserve to the individual in times of stress, such as
illness or trauma'' as well as a cushion protecting against injuries
during falls. Long-term weight loss after age 50, on the other hand,
appears to increase mortality for reasons that are not well
understood.
<p>
   Losing weight makes sense for people with certain conditions  &MD; 
diabetes, high blood pressure, arthritis  &MD;  but it may not be advisable
for all overweight seniors, the authors conclude. ``What we really
should be thinking about is fitness and function, rather than
obsessing about weight,'' Diehr said in an interview.
<p>
   The study appears in the April issue of the American Journal of
Public Health.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-health-pain </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0291 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-health-pain
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Health editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Sandra G. Boodman </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Preoperative Drugs Lessen Post-Op Pain, Doctors Report &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The pain that patients experience after prostate cancer operations
can be greatly diminished by the administration of anesthetic drugs
before surgery, doctors at the University of Pennsylvania Medical
Center reported last week.
<p>
   In a study published in the Journal of the American Medical
Association, a team of anesthesiologists and urologists found that the
use of preoperative anesthesia, administered through an epidural
catheter, stops pain by blocking the natural response of the central
nervous system. The effect was evident as long as nine weeks after
surgery.
<p>
   Anesthesiologist Allan Gottschalk and his colleagues studied 90 men
in otherwise good health who underwent removal of the prostate for
cancer that had not spread beyond the organ.
<p>
   Patients were randomly assigned to one of three groups. The first
received no preoperative painkiller, while patients in the second and
third groups received either fentanyl or bupivacaine before surgery.
All three groups were given a standard general anesthetic and received
similar postoperative drugs for pain.
<p>
   Gottschalk and his colleagues found significant differences between
the groups that received preoperative anesthesia and the group that
did not. The two preoperative groups reported 33 percent less pain
during their hospitalization than the group that received pain
medication only after surgery.
<p>
   A telephone survey conducted nine weeks after discharge found that
86 percent of those who received preoperative painkillers had no pain
compared with only 47 percent who received no such medications.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-kidsday-adviser </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0837 04-14 0743 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-kidsday-adviser - a0837
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: Feature editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; KIDSDAY ADVISER: Kids Answer Kids' Problems &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Dear Kidsday: My younger brother really gets me upset. He always
seems to get his way with our parents. What can I do?  &MD; Angry
<p>
   Dear Angry: Talk to your parents and explain to them how you feel.
There might be a reason why they give him special attention and not
you. When you talk to them make sure you can back up the things you
say. Tell them specific times when you are not favored.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Dear Kidsday: My younger brother is always fighting with my younger
sister. When my parents come in to break it up, all of us get
punished. I don't think it is very fair, because I don't fight with
either of them.  &MD; Surrounded
<p>
   Dear Surrounded: It seems like your parents don't care who's
fighting, they just want the fighting to stop. They should already
know you don't do any of the fighting. When it is calm in your house,
have a private talk with your mom and dad. Maybe they want you to try
and take charge, and help stop the trouble.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Dear Kidsday: We just got a new cat. Can you think of a good name
for him?  &MD; Nameless
<p>
   Dear Nameless: For a unique name, look in the back of Webster's
Dictionary. It has sections with biographical, Biblical, mythological
and geographical names. Or look at your cat's coloring and give him a
name that is appropriate. For example, if his fur is a golden color,
you could name him Sunshine. Or name him after one of your favorite
characters on a TV show or in a movie.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Dear Kidsday: I am so bored! My mom tells me to get a hobby. I'm an
11-year-old boy. Do you have any ideas?  &MD; Bored
<p>
   Dear Bored: Save up your money and start to collect something, such
as trading cards  &MD;  it's a fun hobby and a good investment. You can get
ideas for other collections by going to your local bookstore and
buying a hobby magazine. Or visit your local library and take out a
book about making arts and crafts. Or join a local sports team or
after-school club.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Dear Kidsday: I have a problem with one of my friends. She is kind
of sensitive. If I so much as look annoyed, she blows it out of
proportion. She then has her mom call up and complain about how I
alienate myself from the group. All the kids in our group follow her.
They all even act like her. What I want to know is how can I be myself
in a group that is all alike?  &MD; Confused and Different
<p>
   Dear Confused and Different: You are right. You can't be an
individual in a group like that. You might have to form your own group
that enjoys doing things that you like to do. A few kids on this panel
think it is a good idea to break away from that girl. If she is really
annoying, you can have your mom call her mom but that may not get
anywhere. One other thing you could try is just to pull her aside and
tell her how you feel. That will give you an idea of how you should
act when you are with her.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Dear Kidsday: I feel like I am betraying my friend. She is going
out with this really hot kid. Two days after their relationship
started, he asked me out. I like him, but if my friend found out, our
friendship would be ruined. My two other friends know about him asking
me out, and they say they wouldn't tell a soul because I said yes.
After a day of thinking about it, I can't choose between my friend and
this boy.  &MD; Stuck In Between
<p>
   Dear Stuck In Between: You are betraying your friend. You should
tell your friend what you did, but you should also tell your friend
what this boy did. We're not sure if he is the right person for either
of you. These 22 panelists all agree you should not go out with him.
He is too sneaky. We also think you should choose the friend over the
boyfriend.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Dear Kidsday: I have a problem. These two girls make fun of me
because I wear thick glasses. They call me cross-eyed and threaten my
dog. It really hurts my feelings. What should I do?  &MD; M.T.
<p>
   Dear M.T.: They threatened your dog? What is going on here? They
seem silly to us. As hard as it might be, you have to ignore them. If
they see you are bothered by their teasing they are going to keep
doing it. Make sure you play with friends who like you for who you
are. If the glasses look bad to you, then ask your mom if you can get
new frames.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC;

<p>
    &UR; DO YOU KNOW BASKETBALL? &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; By Louis Camacho, Ryan Tierney and David Weinsto &QC; 
<p>
    &UR; Kidsday Staff Reporters &QC; 
<p>
   See what you know about the NBA:
<p>
   1. Who is the only NBA player to score 100 points in a game?
<p>
   2. Who is the current coach of the Dallas Mavericks? Hint: he once
coached the New York Knicks.
<p>
   3. Who was named NBA Rookie of the Year in 1997?
<p>
   4. Who was named NBA Rookie of the Year in 1985?
<p>

once played for the Orlando Magic.
<p>

State Warriors. Name him.
<p>

school, and he now stars for the Los Angeles Lakers. Name him.
<p>
   11. Name the Washington Wizards who were college teammates at
Michigan.
<p>
   12. Who was drafted No. 1 in the 1997 NBA draft?
<p>

Vancouver?

Michael Jordan (5) Shaquille O'Neal (6) Tim Hardaway (7) Isiah Thomas
(8) Karl Malone (9) Magic Johnson (10) Kobe Bryant (11) Juwan Howard

Grizzlies.

<SLUG> bc-kidsday-spicegirls </SLUG>

<PREAMBLE>
bc-kidsday-spicegirls - a0838
 &UR; (ndy) (ATTN: Feature editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Nicole Cestaro, Lauren Kriete and Jennifer Sonnick </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Newsday
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; KIDSDAY TALKS WITH: The Spice Girls &QL; 
 &UR; Kidsday Staff Reporters &QC; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   We recently interviewed the Spice Girls  &MD;  Emma Bunton (Baby Spice),
Geri Halliwell (Ginger Spice), Melanie Brown (Scary Spice), Melanie
Chisholm (Sporty Spice) and Victoria Adams (Posh Spice)  &MD;  along with
many other journalists from around the country at a press conference
in New York City for the opening of their movie, ``Spice World.''.
From England, they formed their pop group in 1993, and their first
single, ``Wannabe,'' went to the top of the charts. To date they have
sold more than 10 million singles worldwide.
<p>
   Q: How did you get your group's name?
<p>
   Melanie B.: We actually wrote a song ``Sugar and Spice.'' I think
that subconsciously planted the seed. And then one day we were in
aerobics class bouncing around and we came up with the name.
<p>
   Q: All of you say Girl Power a lot, but what do you believe that
actually is?
<p>
   Emma: All of us have been brought up with very strong mothers. They
taught us to create our own destiny. We call it People Power as well.
Girls can wear their skirts short, but it doesn't mean they don't have
a brain.
<p>
   Melanie B.: For us it confirms that no matter who you are if you
have a dream and you really want to get there, you can. We all come
from normal backgrounds. We were struggling in the industry from the
ages of about 13, so it just goes to show for any girl out there, that
if you want to do something you can. I think the '90s is more about
the equalization between female and male people. A lot of industries
are run by males, and I think that should change.
<p>
   Q: How did Princess Diana's death affect you?
<p>
   Victoria: I think people didn't realize how much good she was able
to do until it was too late. Everybody was more interested in writing
about her personal life.
<p>
   Melanie B.: She was the one person everyone would have loved to
have met. Life is a learning process, and we learned a lot through our
success, fame and money. We should treat people nicer and with a lot
more respect.
<p>
   Q: Your clothing is very unique; are you trying to make a
statement?
<p>
   Victoria: If you go to London you will see a lot of people dressed
like this. We are not trying to make a statement.
<p>
   Emma: People do dress quite funky in England.
<p>
   Melanie B.: We wear what we like, but sometimes we coordinate the
color.
<p>
   Geri: If you think about the '90s, whether it be music, fashion or
film  &MD;  anything goes. There is no set code anymore. ... You don't have
to conform to any particular genre, you can just be yourself and still
stand together.
<p>
   Emma: One of the reasons I wear the shoes I wear is because I am
not tall.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   You can write them c/o Girl Power, P.O. Box 8863, Red Bank, N.J.
07701.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-ulster-comment </SLUG>
<HEADER> 1stld-pickup5thgraf   04-14 0458 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-ulster-comment 1stld-pickup5thgraf
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Editorial-Page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Fintan O'Toole </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; In 4th graf, deletes ``in Dublin'' from quote &QL; 
 &UR; Peace in N. Ireland Long in the Making &QL; 
 &UR; O'Toole is a columnist for the Irish Times and &QL; 
 &UR; the New York Daily News &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The last week of the Northern Ireland peace talks was anything but
a stately journey toward historic destiny. There were accusations,
histrionics, last-minute hitches. At an alarmingly late stage, it
seemed that basic questions had not been answered, that some key
concepts had not been worked through. And when, after a sleepless
night, a complex deal was reached by exhausted negotiators, suspicions
lingered. Is this real change or just a mutual respite for worn-out
enemies? Was the desire for peace driven by something more than the
momentum generated by deadlines, expectations and the massed ranks of
television news crews?
<p>
   The questions are not abstract. The deal announced Friday will have
to withstand terrible pressures. Fringe terror groups on both sides
will test it with atrocities. Two of the most important parties in the
process  &MD;  Gerry Adams' Sinn Fein and David Trimble's Ulster unionists
 &MD;  will face considerable internal dissent. The political mechanics of
establishing both a workable administration in Northern Ireland and
cross-border bodies with the Republic of Ireland will be extremely
complex. If the deal does not have deep roots, it will be blown away.
<p>
   There are, though, good reasons to believe that the peace package
arises not from a passing moment but from some fundamental shifts. For
one of the more paradoxical effects of 30 years of conflict has been
to give an illusion of continuity to a period of profound change. The
epic language of immemorial and irreconcilable divisions that tends to
be used about Northern Ireland has hidden the fact that many of the
assumptions that held good in 1968 when the killing began simply no
longer apply.
<p>
   In 1922, when the last attempt at a comprehensive settlement
between Ireland and Britain was being debated in the British House of
Commons, Winston Churchill famously contrasted the vast changes in
Europe after World War I with the obstinate constancy of religious
divisions in Northern Ireland. Everything else had undergone ``violent
and tremendous changes in the deluge of the world,'' he said. ``But as
the deluge subsides and the waters fall short, we see the dreary
steeples of Fermanagh and Tyrone emerging once again. The integrity of
their quarrel is one of the few institutions that has been unaltered
in the cataclysm which has swept the world.''
 &UR; PICKUP 5thgraf: Now, as the flood xxx.
<p>
    &UR; Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

   TAXES-COMMENT  &MD;  Many tax reforms that are sensible and achievable  &MD; 
including closing loopholes, reducing rates and simplifying taxes  &MD; 
also would reduce tax penalties on marriage and help families;
politicians would do well to improve the existing system rather than

<p>
    &UR; Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC;

AP-NY-04-14-98 1218EDT &QL;

<DOCID> latwp980414.0088 </DOCID>
<STORYID cat=k pri=r sel=tm--a> X1900 </STORYID>
<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-taxes-comment </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a0919 04-14 0813 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-taxes-comment - a0919
 &UR; (ATTN: Editorial page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By William G. Gale </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to the Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; PERSPECTIVE ON TAXES: Don't Toss Out the Baby With Reform &QL; 
 &UR; Gale is a senior fellow at the Brookings Institution in Washington. &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Every politician favors marriage, family and improvements in the
tax code. Many routinely criticize the income tax as biased against
marriage and families. Some go much further and claim that we could
eliminate these biases by throwing out the current system and starting
over with a flat tax or a national retail sales tax.
<p>
   While some of the charges against the income tax are valid, they
are exaggerated. But fundamental tax reform will not eliminate the
biases and could even make them worse.
<p>
   The income tax creates penalties for 42 percent of married couples,
because as income rises, tax rates rise and items like the earned
income, child and education credits are phased out. But the tax also
subsidizes 51 percent of couples, through tax brackets that differ
based on marital status and other features. Marriage bonuses actually
exceed penalties by $4 billion.
<p>
   Under the flat tax proposed by Rep. Richard Armey, R-Texas, and
Sen. Richard Shelby, R-Ala., households would pay taxes on their wages
and pension income above exemption levels of $11,600 for single
people, $14,850 for heads of household and $23,200 for couples. Armey
says that the flat tax eliminates the marriage penalty, but this is
incorrect. Two people with children could obtain $26,450 in exemptions
if they were not married  &MD;  one filing singly, one as household head  &MD; 
but only $23,200 if they were married. At a flat tax rate of 20
percent, this would impose an annual marriage tax of $650 per year on
couples with children in which each spouse earns at least $14,850.
<p>
   The proposed national retail sales tax also contains marriage
penalties. To partially offset the regressivity of the sales tax,
households would receive ``demogrants'' or rebates equal to the tax
rate times the poverty line. This year's poverty line set by the
federal government  &MD;  the income families need to stay out of poverty  &MD; 
is $7,890 for one person, plus $2,720 for each additional person. At a
sales tax rate of 30 percent, a conservative estimate, two single
people would be entitled to rebates of $2,367 each for a total of
$4,734. If they married, they would receive $3,183, so their marriage
penalty would be $1,551.
<p>
   Advocates like to assert that tax reform is ``pro-family'' and
point to the demogrants in the sales tax and large exemptions for
children in the flat tax. Children and families benefit
disproportionately from numerous features of the current system,
however, including dependent exemptions, child care credits, earned
income credits and education credits. And the preferential treatment
of housing, health insurance and state and local tax payments also
helps families, since they consume relatively more housing, medical
services and government-provided services such as education. All of
these preferences would be eliminated under fundamental tax reform.
<p>
   Moreover, compared with childless couples, families with kids
generally have high consumption relative to income, so switching from
an income tax to a consumption tax would further raise tax burdens
during years when family needs were highest. A study based on 1996
data found that a broad-based, flat-rate consumption tax like a sales
tax or flat tax would hurt families with incomes less than $200,000,
because of the loss of tax preferences, but would help families with
income above $200,000 due to the dramatic reduction in the top tax
rate.
<p>
   Advocates might claim that families would benefit from reduced
hassles of tax filing and increased economic growth. While tax
complexity is a real problem, many families actually have quite simple
tax returns: 22 percent file simplified 1040A or 1040EZ forms, and 56
percent do not itemize their deductions. Economic growth effects also
can be overstated. Reasonable estimates suggest that a well-designed
consumption tax would raise income per person by at most 2 percent
over the first 10 years, and a poorly designed reform could reduce
growth.
<p>
   Thus there are some cautionary lessons here. First, the existing
system may have some redeeming features to go along with its obvious
headaches. Second, special attention needs to be given to unintended
effects of tax reform. Third, finding fair ways to tax married versus
single taxpayers and those with children versus those without is not
easily addressed by dogmatic approaches.
<p>
   Fortunately, many reforms that are sensible and achievable  &MD; 
including closing loopholes, reducing rates and simplifying taxes  &MD; 
also would reduce tax penalties on marriage and help families.
Politicians would do well to improve the existing system rather than
radically change it.
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-04-14-98 1221EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>
</DOC>

<DOCID> latwp980414.0089 </DOCID>
<STORYID cat=a pri=r sel=tm--a> X1902 </STORYID>

<SLUG> bc-aged-minnesota </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0781 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-aged-minnesota
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Jon Jeter </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; At Minnesota School, the Aged Still Seek Wisdom (Northfield, Minn.) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   NORTHFIELD, Minn.  &MD;  Spring quarter at the Cannon Valley Elder
Collegium began last week. Students made their way to classes rather
slowly, but hardly anyone was late. Some came in wheelchairs, others
used walkers. Some steadied themselves by leaning on friends, arriving
arm-in-arm with a classmate.
<p>
   Blind in one eye, Julia Savina sat pitched forward in her seat,
using a magnifying glass to read the course syllabus she had just been
handed. The instructor prints everything in extra large type for the
students in his technology class, but Savina still needs a little help
seeing.
<p>
   She is 81.
<p>
   Last semester, she enrolled in a course on Amish history but had to
drop out when she fell and hurt her hip. The retired schoolteacher
returned to her studies after four weeks of physical therapy.
<p>
   ``My husband died in 1995, and I really need this mental
stimulation,'' Savina said, easing into a chair to talk after class.
``I had so much fun last term learning to write poetry, which I never
thought I could do.''
<p>
   Gray-haired and frail, she smiles as she leans back into her chair.
``You can only play so much bingo, you know.''
<p>
   And so it goes here at perhaps the nation's only institution of
higher education designed specifically for the aged. The students'
bodies may be weakened, but their spirits are willing. If some are
hard of hearing, their minds are hungry still.
<p>
   The Cannon Valley Elder Collegium opened last fall in this cozy
little two-college town 40 miles south of Minneapolis. A group of
retired professors and academics rounded up roughly $8,000 in
government grants and recruited nearly 30 of the former faculty
members who live in town to teach college-level courses with titles
such as ``The Drama of Henrik Ibsen,'' ``Goethe's Faust'' and ``The
Fur Trade in North America.'' No arts and crafts here.
<p>
   Enrollment this quarter has jumped to about 50 students, most over
65 and many in their eighties, although no one knows for sure because
``at our age we don't go around asking people how old they are,'' said
Ron Ronning, 68, a retired high school humanities teacher and CVEC's
program director.
<p>
   Collegium students can receive continuing education credits, but no
grades are given, no degrees conferred. No one here is looking for any
of that.
<p>
   ``Everyone here really wants to be here,'' said George Soule, a
retired literature professor who is teaching ``Hamlet'' to about eight
seniors this quarter. ``They're not looking for a better job or to
further their career by being here. They're not here to impress their
parents. All they want to do is learn.''
<p>
   At the Elder Collegium, there are no fraternities, no backpacks, no
baseball caps worn backward, no hungover students nodding off in the
back of the classroom. Students here are more likely to wear
orthopedic shoes than Nikes, hearing aids than earrings. Each class
lasts for eight weeks and costs $50, and the source of students'
financial aid is more likely to be a pension than a parent or Pell
grant. Most of the students enrolled in a course on the Great
Depression last quarter had actually lived through it.
<p>
   ``We initially wanted to call it Last Chance College,'' Ronning
jokes. ``Or Old Geezers University.''
<p>
   As the elderly live longer, the number of people 65 or older
enrolled in college courses has risen steadily over the last decade.
Hazel H. Reinhardt, director of marketing research at the Newspaper
Management Center of Northwestern University's Medill School of
Journalism, said the trend is likely to accelerate in years to come as
the baby boomer generation, with its higher rate of education, moves
into retirement.
<p>
   ``I think this whole area is one that is going to develop,'' she
said in a telephone interview.
<p>
   With two highly regarded liberal arts colleges  &MD;  Carleton and St.
Olaf  &MD;  the quaint, bucolic town of Northfield is home to an uncommon
brain trust: Of nearly 15,000 residents, roughly 150 are retired
professors. A road sign on the periphery of this learned but
fun-loving town bills Northfield as a place for ``cows, colleges and
contentment.''
<p>
   ``Everybody here is in a book club or two,'' Ronning said. He said
he and his wife, Bettye, had attended some casual group discussions
held at senior citizen centers that ``frankly, we found ... a little
patronizing. It's as if people think that seniors are either unwilling
or unable to engage in serious study. The collegium provides just
that. There are assignments, and people come prepared.''
</TEXT>

AP-NY-04-14-98 1318EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-aged-minnesota-hamlet </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0473 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-aged-minnesota-hamlet
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Jon Jeter </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Never Too Old to Learn (Northfield, Minn.) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   NORTHFIELD, Minn.  &MD;  The Cannon Valley Elder Collegium's ``Hamlet''
course
<p>
   is held at Northfield's Retirement Center. Eight seniors at a
conference table pay rapt attention to George Soule, a white-haired,
animated man who lectures at a rapid-fire pace on Shakespeare.
<p>
   ``His characters tend to be very complex,'' says Soule, a retired
literature professor who is teaching the play to about eight seniors
this quarter. ``They're far deeper than many people I've known in real
life.''
<p>
   Soule engages his students in a discussion of the play's opening
verse. Virtually all of the students in the classroom contribute
something to the dialogue. One of Soule's students is Marilyn Will,
59, a retired schoolteacher whose former students include Soule's
daughter, now 31. Widowed two years ago, Will enrolled in two courses
last quarter. She came back for more this quarter and brought along a
friend, 81-year-old Jane Eckardt.
<p>
   ``I have always loved education from both sides of the desk,'' says
Will, a small woman who first attended college 40 years ago. ``I just
came to class expecting to be filled up back then,'' she says. ``Now I
feel as though I have something to contribute to the class and to the
discussion.''
<p>
   As she says this, an exaggerated grimace spreads across Eckardt's
face. ``It won't be any different for me,'' says Eckardt, who first
enrolled in college in 1934. ``I still expect to just be filled up.
I'm dead between the ears.''
<p>
   Will smiles at her friend's familiar, self-deprecating brand of
humor. The two women taught school together and have been friends for
a quarter century.
<p>
   ``Our husbands were friends,'' Eckardt says. ``We gave each other
advice on our kids.'' She leans forward and lowers her voice. ``We
tried to figure out what was wrong with them.''
<p>
   During a break, Will asks Eckardt if she could pick up a copy of
``Hamlet'' for her. ``I'll just shop for both of us, and you can lay
the money on me later,'' she says to her older friend.
<p>
   Eckardt pauses. ``Did I remember to lay money on you for my
registration?'' she asks. ``Yes, you did,'' Will reassures her.
<p>
   ``That gets to be a problem when you're 81,'' Eckardt says. ``But
really I'm just here because I love Marilyn. I just love to be around
her.''
<p>
   At his wife's urging, 66-year-old Donald Krause enrolled in
``Cultural Conversations.'' On Tuesday evenings, he'll debate topics
such as the role of an activist federal judiciary with his peers and a
group of local high school students.
<p>
   ``This is fun,'' said Krause, a retired schoolteacher. ``You know,
most senior citizens aren't just waiting to die. We can actually
discover a thing or two.''
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-04-14-98 1319EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>
</DOC>
<DOC>
<DOCID> latwp980414.0091 </DOCID>
<STORYID cat=k pri=r sel=tm--a> X1905 </STORYID>
<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-glassman </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0898 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-glassman
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Editorial page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By James K. Glassman </BYLINE>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Bigger, Better Banks (Washn) &QL; 
 &UR; Glassman, a fellow at the American Enterprise Institute, &QL; 
 &UR; writes frequently for The Post on financial affairs. &QL; 
 &UR; Speical to The Washington Post &QC; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  A couple of guys named McColl and McCoy have been
shaking up the banking business for the past 10 years. Now, in
separate deals revealed this week, they are creating the second- and
fifth-largest banks in the country.
<p>
   And just last week Citicorp, which was bumped from the number one
spot when Chemical Bank merged with Chase Manhattan in 1996, announced
its own mega-merger with Travelers Group Inc., forming the world's
largest financial services company.
<p>
   ``It's elephant mating season,'' says a banker friend. The best
thing to do is stand at a safe distance and watch.
<p>
   What you'll see is something clumsy but healthy. Thanks to
regulations enacted more than 70 years ago and to the incredible
political clout of small-town bankers, this country's banking system
has been a balkanized mess. The mergers are making banking more
efficient  &MD;  costs squeezed out, better services offered  &MD;  and that's a
good thing.
<p>
   In fact, what would be really good to see is more and bigger
mergers  &MD;  especially across international, not just state, borders.
Global banking would not only be less costly and more convenient but
also less risky and more transparent.
<p>
   Alan Meltzer, economics professor at Carnegie-Mellon University,
uses Korea as an example. Its economy is the 11th largest in the
world, which puts it on a par with the economy of Los Angeles County.
But banks serving L.A. alone would find themselves in deep trouble if
the local economy went sour-just the situation with Korean banks
today. If Korea were the site of global banks, however, profits from
loans in the United States, Latin America and Europe would offset the
Korean losses. There would be no need to call in the IMF for a
bailout.
<p>
   The mergers also point out that it's time, at last, to eliminate
that hoary anachronism the 1933 Glass-Steagall Act, which was intended
to divide commercial from investment banking. Banks have circumvented
the law, but investment firms such as Merrill Lynch &AMP; Co. cannot. Not
only is this distinction unfair but ultimately it hurts consumers, who
would benefit from hotter competition.
<p>
   Even with the changes over the past 15 years, there are still far
too many separate banking companies in the United States: 11,000,
including credit unions and savings and loans. Most of these banks, of
course, are very small. The typical one has just seven branches and
total assets of less than $100 million. For decades, most of them,
while poorly run, had profitable franchises because they lacked
competition. As consolidations unfold, the better ones will survive as
boutiques for clients willing to pay a little more for custom services
and extra attention. But most Americans, whose needs for loans,
insurance, brokerage, retirement accounts, savings and checking are
straightforward, will bank at a financial supermarket.
<p>
   Could a few of these elephants (to switch metaphors) muscle all
their competitors out of the way and stomp on consumers with large
costs and lousy services? That's possible, but doubtful. Citibank,
Chase and BankAmerica account for just 19 percent of the loans
outstanding at U.S. commercial banks. If the latest deals are approved
by regulators (as they should be), then the three biggest banks will
have $1.2 trillion in assets, and the 10 biggest will have $2.2
trillion. Sounds like a lot, but it's only one-third of all the
banking assets in the country.
<p>
   The merger between Hugh McColl's NationsBank, based in Charlotte,
N.C., and BankAmerica of San Francisco will produce an institution
with $532 billion in assets-still smaller than banks in Germany and
Japan.
<p>
   But what really makes U.S. banks different from their counterparts
around the world is their robust pursuit of profits  &MD;  and that's the
objective of the mergers. Through competition, this single aim
produces remarkable variety, and it ultimately gives individuals and
businesses better services at lower prices. For instance, NationsBank
and BankAmerica are fashioning a truly national bank, with branches
from Washington state to Florida, and they figure they can slash their
back-office expenses by more than $1 billion.
<p>
   By contrast, Banc One's merger with First Chicago creates a
fortress franchise in the Midwest  &MD;  plus a behemoth (ranking number
two) in the credit-card business. John B. McCoy, CEO of Banc One,
based in Columbus, Ohio, has further national ambitions as well.
Travelers, meanwhile, merged with Citicorp largely to access the
world's best distribution network for financial products.
<p>
   There are dangers. The mergers make more institutions ``too big to
fail.'' Knowing that regulators won't close them down in a crisis,
bank managers may get reckless in their lending. A second danger is
simply that it will take years for managements to get their arms
around these giants they've created. If a recession occurs before new
controls are in place, the consequences to these banks, and to the
U.S. economy, could be severe.
<p>
   Elephants mating can certainly trample the underbrush, but in
general we we have little to fear when they merge, and a great deal to
gain.
</TEXT>

<TEXT>
<p>
   The Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service Budget for
morning papers of Wednesday, April 15, 1998. The editors are Kate
Carlisle at The Washington Post and Michael J. Kaeser at the Los
Angeles Times. For questions about Post stories, call (202) 334-7666;
for questions about Times and other Times Mirror stories, call (213)
237-7832.
<p>
    &UR; National:
<p>
   RAINES  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  Director of the Office of Management and
Budget Franklin Raines resigns, and President Clinton nominates
Raines' deputy Jack Lew to succeed him as budget chief. Developing, by
Clay Chandler (Post).
<p>
   DRUGS  &MD;  Properly prescribed medications kill as many as 100,000
people a year, according to a new analysis showing that prescription
drugs are a leading cause of death in the United States, taking more
lives than diabetes or accidents; the new study appears Wednesday in
the Journal of the American Medical Association. Developing.
<p>
   TAX  &MD;  In a show of political theater, Republican leaders will dump
the entire U.S. tax code into Boston Harbor Wednesday, insisting that
tax reform will soon make the loathsome load of loopholes a thing of
the past; but, the tax books will be removed from the water as soon as
the cameras are turned off, proving symbolically what Washington knows
full well: Politicians don't seem as eager to kill current system as
they profess. Developing, by Jonathan Weisman (Sun).
<p>
   TAX-SNITCH  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  Americans voluntarily hand over most of
the $1.3 billion collected by the Internal Revenue Service each year,
but a tiny fraction of federal tax depends on an obscure and
increasingly controversial IRS program of using paid informants; the
president of the nonpartisan National Taxpayers Union calls on the IRS
to end the program. Developing, by Ralph Vartabedian (Times).
<p>
   RACE  &MD;  HOUSTON  &MD;  President Clinton participates in an evening forum
on race in sports as he tries to revive his special initiative to
promote racial dialogue. Developing (late).
<p>
   PACKWOOD  &MD;  PORTLAND, Ore.  &MD;  Former Sen. Bob Packwood, who has
largely stayed away from Oregon since resigning in disgrace in 1995,
has hinted he might run for office, but observers say his higher
public profile may be aimed only at rehabilitating his image. 840
words, by Terry McDermott (Times). Moved.
<p>
   AGED-MINNESOTA  &MD;  NORTHFIELD, Minn.  &MD;  As the elderly live longer,
the number of people 65 or older enrolled in college courses has risen
steadily over the last decade; the Cannon Valley Elder Collegium, 40
miles south of Minneapolis, may be the only institution of higher
education designed specifically for the aged. 795 words, by Jon Jeter
(Post) (a). With AGED-MINNESOTA-HAMLET.
<p>
    &UR; International:
<p>
   EXECUTE  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  In a case with international implications,
the governor of Virginia weighs a request from Secretary of State
Madeleine Albright to stay the planned 9 p.m. EDT execution of a
Paraguayan national convicted of murder. Developing.
<p>
   FUGITIVE  &MD;  BUENOS AIRES, Argentina  &MD;  Argentine authorities say they
plan to arrest the alleged commander of a World War II Croatian death
camp who has been living here for 50 years; the State Deparment and
Croatian officials have been urging the capture of Dinko Sakic, 76,
who is accused of commanding the Jasenovac death camp where thousands
of prisoners were killed. Developing, by Tracy Wilkinson and Sebastian
Rotella (Times).
<p>
   IRAQ  &MD;  GENEVA  &MD;  The Iraqi government summarily executed more than
1,500 people for political offenses last year as the human rights
situation in that country reached a new low, a special United Nations
investigator reports. Developing, by Craig Turner (Times).
<p>
   MEXICO-PROTEST  &MD;  CUERNAVACA, Mexico  &MD;  In a microcosm of the crisis
in the nation's criminal justice system, Morelos state officials are
under arrest, accused of helping drug and kidnapping gangs or covering
up police abuse; citizens have launched protests that threaten to
bring down the powerful governor. 1,300 words, by Mary Beth Sheridan
(Times). Moved.
<p>
   RUSSIA-CARS  &MD;  For years, Russian drivers were so grateful to be
able to buy a car that they never dared hope it would actually run
well, but now car buyers are finding themselves in an unfamiliar
position: masters of the market instead of slaves to it. 1,130 words,
by Kathy Lally (Sun). With RUSSIA-CARS-MAGAZINE. Both moved.
<p>
   TIBET  &MD;  KATMANDU, Nepal  &MD;  Thousands of Tibetans fleeing repression
in their own land take one of the world's most harrowing paths to
freedom; they climb across the Himalayas. 1,475 words, by Dexter
Filkins (Times). With TIBET-REFUGEES. Both moved.
<p>
    &UR; Financial:
<p>
   ECONOMY  &MD;  WASHINGTON  &MD;  The Consumer Price Index was flat in March
and retail sales dropped by 0.1 percent, the government says in two
new reports that point to a cooling of the economy. Developing.

<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-circus </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 1932 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-circus
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Entertainment editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Elizabeth Kastor </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; One Big-Top Family (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  In the old days, when the circus was the circus and
children dreamed of running away with it, the clowns got rained on. It
was their fate.
<p>
   David Larible remembers the day he told his father he wanted to be
a clown.
<p>
   ``He was not happy,'' Larible remembers. Not that his father didn't
like the circus  &MD;  the family had been in the circus for generations.
But a clown? The son of an equestrian-acrobat mother and a
trapeze-artist father becoming a clown? ``He said, `Why do you want to
be a clown?' ''
<p>
   It is between shows at Ringling Bros. and Barnum &AMP; Bailey Circus,
and Larible is in makeup and costume. His dimples are black, his
eyelids powder blue, his nose bulbous, his shoes huge. He is a clown,
a famous one now. How could his father know a clown could be a star?
<p>
   Back then, in the ``cook tent'' where the circus ate, the social
diagram was clear. The owners and star performers  &MD;  the bareback
riders, the trapeze artists, the headliners  &MD;  sat in the center of the
tent, separated by a curtain from the tumblers and jugglers, the
troupe's middle class. Beyond them were the daredevils  &MD;  the human
cannonballs and such  &MD;  and finally the clowns, considered mere filler
between acts, relegated to the outermost circle, where the wind blew
tent flaps and the rain splattered them while they ate.
<p>
   Near the bottom of the social ladder was the working class  &MD;  the
roustabouts and others who raised the tents, set up the chairs and
swept the rings, so low that they were supposed to speak to performers
only when spoken to.
<p>
   Most of the tents are long gone; Ringling Bros. and Barnum &AMP; Bailey
moved into arenas more than 40 years ago, and the hierarchy that
Larible grew up in has loosened. His beaming face is even an
advertising staple for Ringling Bros. (at the D.C. Armory until April
19), though most clowns still rank near the bottom. The circus has
changed, but it remains a closed, old-fashioned world, a nomadic
society and an inbred one. Still defined by the ties of blood and
sawdust, it has its mores and aristocracy.
<p>
   Anyone seeking a map to the social hierarchy of the circus need
only ask who gets a whole car on the Ringling Bros. train and who has
to wedge an entire life into a ``roomette.''
<p>
   ``It all depends,'' says Anthony Johnson, who handles public
relations for Ringling Bros., ``where you fall on the food chain.''
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Families of performers, often Eastern European families, have
traditionally been the glue holding the circus together. They possess
a mystique that is easy to market: ``The Flying Wallendas! Dad, Mom
and the kids defying death together!'' They have a loyalty, savvy and
potential for breeding more performers, and this makes them attractive
to circus owners.
<p>
   In this show, there are the Flying Caballeros on the trapeze, the
Guerreros and their seven-person pyramid on the high wire, and the two
families at the pinnacle  &MD;  the Laribles and the Gebels.
<p>
   Four of the Laribles perform: David's wife is an aerialist and both
his mother and his 8-year-old daughter appear in his act.
<p>
   And at the head of the Gebel family reigns Gunther Gebel-Williams,
the legendary animal trainer and longtime star of Ringling Bros. He
recently ceded the ring to his son, the not-yet-as-legendary Mark
Oliver Gebel, whose wife, Christina, performs with baby elephants.
Although Gunther is officially retired, he still lives with the
circus, trains all the animals and is known around the place as The
Boss.
<p>
   The Laribles and the Gebels are whole-car families. Families are
still families, even when walking on a tightrope. They have their
internal fissures. At least one circus family is currently split and
performing with three different companies, with some of the members
not even speaking to others. And between the clans tensions can
develop as well.
<p>
   Author Bruce Feiler, who wrote ``Under the Big Top: A Season With
the Circus,'' about his tour as a clown with the Clyde Beatty-Cole
Bros. Circus, remembers hearing about two competing tumbling families
who traveled with the circus the year before he did. ``They used to go
into the ring and try to outdo each other and taunt each other,'' he
says. ``That's the kind of thing that just doesn't happen on Ringling.
Someone would just say, `Put an end to this right now!' ''
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   People like to say that the circus is a small town, or a village on
wheels, or one big family  &MD;  no description quite works because it's
all so familiar and so strange at the same time. People who spend
their work-lives bouncing off seesaws and onto the shoulders of
colleagues go home to family cookouts and Tupperware parties. They all
travel 48 weeks a year, visiting 95 cities and performing 975 times in
the course of a two-year tour.
<p>
   ``Some people think it's a real unstable life, but it's stable
within its environment,'' says paymaster Linda Cimini.
<p>
   Feiler says: ``As paradoxical as it seems, the overwhelming feeling
I had while I was in the circus and the recollection I have of it now
was how safe it was. Because of the fact that it exists on the fringes
of America, because the people are often maligned by mainstream
Americans, circus people are so protective of their own. To this day,
I could walk onto any circus lot anywhere in the world  &MD;  and I've done
this in about half a dozen different countries  &MD;  and say I spent a
year as a circus clown in America, and within 10 minutes I'm eating
dinner in someone's trailer. This not just my natural charm. It's `You
are part of us.' ''
<p>
   There's a school, taught by a teacher and by a clown with an
advanced degree in education. There is a priest: Assigned by the
Catholic Church to tend the roving flock, he has an e-mail address of
circusrev. There's a newspaper, the Red Review, named for the Red Unit
(Ringling has two different circuses traveling at all times, the Red
and Blue units).
<p>
   Those seeking parties on the Red Unit gravitate toward what is
known as Clown Alley. Many of the 18 clowns on this show (15 men,
three women) are exceptionally young, some just out of high school.
Anthony Johnson laughs and says, ``It's like a fraternity. They're
doing the dumb things young guys do.''
<p>
   For years, the Ladies' Wardrobe was another neighborhood, one Chris
Cross aspired to when she joined the circus as a young newlywed in
1981. She started out selling programs and dreamed of the wardrobe.
<p>
   ``Ladies' Wardrobe was the ``wives' ' job  &MD;  it was the place to
congregate. You did needlepoint, you knitted, they had birthday
parties,'' she says fondly, remembering the cozy female niche with its
sequins and companionship.
<p>
   Her husband, Matt, was in the band, and they had joined the circus
for a little adventure after childhoods in small-town Ohio. Their
adventure turned into 15 years. The traveling life, the constant
change, the camaraderie, keep them on the train.
<p>
   By now, Cross is long past Ladies' Wardrobe, where she worked in
the late '80s. She is a unit controller for the Red Unit, overseeing
payroll and tallying up profits from each show. Matt plays the
keyboard and is in charge of the sound effects, such as the fabricated
hippo roar he created by fooling around with the vocalizations of a
senior vice president of Feld Entertainment, a Vienna, Va., company
which owns Ringling Bros.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Some stay. Some leave.
<p>
   And some come back.
<p>
   Linda Cimini has been on the road off and on since 1977. She lived
in Florida, started working for a theme park Ringling ran down there,
then went on the road. She sold souvenirs, worked in the wardrobe, did
makeup for the clowns, went to clown college herself. She married a
Ringling clown, toured with the Clyde Beatty circus for a while, and
after 15 years of marriage got divorced and thought she might be done
with it.
<p>
   She left for two years. Got a good job, working for Marriott in
Florida. Went back to school. Lived in an actual house. And then
Ringling called.
<p>
   ``I had some unfinished business,'' she says now. Had she traveled
for her husband, she wondered, had the circus been all about him? ``Or
was I on the road for me? I had to decide that.''
<p>
   She returned as paymaster, and within six weeks was asked out by
the stablemaster and vet technician who for years was known as Camel
Pete. By the time they dated, Pete Cimini had added postmaster to his
workload, along with editor of the troupe newsletter  &MD;  few people in
the circus do only one thing  &MD;  and been renamed Postal Pete.
<p>
   They've been married two years and are still on the road, but Linda
now knows why she's doing it. They have plans to leave, to buy a
campground somewhere, but the time is not yet right. Pete keeps
getting new opportunities and she doesn't want to rush him. If they'd
already left he might never have learned that when animal trainer Emil
Popescu gets a sore throat, his sea lions will soon stop eating  &MD; 
whether because their throats hurt too or out of neurotic attachment,
Pete can't say, but he puts human and beasts on antibiotics just to be
safe.
<p>
   When he heard there would be a trained hippo in this year's show
(``ZUSHA, QUEEN OF THE NILE!''), he thought, ``A hippopotamus! That
will look great on my resume!''
<p>
   But Linda's sister sends her ads of campgrounds for sale, and Linda
fantasizes about planting a flower and not having to take it in at
night for the move to the next town. And as she changes, the circus is
changing, too.
<p>
   Almost every day, the real world seems to seep deeper into the
circus. Animal rights activists picket and protest. In some towns,
it's now illegal to present live animal acts. Insurance payments are
so high Ringling Bros. long ago stopped letting local celebrities ride
the elephants from the train to the arena. Just the other day Mark
Oliver Gebel noticed the guys hanging the wires and rigging were
wearing hard hats. ``I've never seen it before,'' he says, shaking his
head. ``I grew up in the circus  &MD;  hard hats!''
<p>
   Ladies' Wardrobe is no longer a place for the wives  &MD;  the costumes
are cared for by professionals. The performers are younger, and fewer
come from circus dynasties. ``People once said there was a certain
magic about circus families,'' Cimini says. ``It's not so much the
families now. It's people who want to be performers, people who have
watched families and said, `I could do that.' ''
<p>
   The success of New Age troupes like Cirque du Soleil, with their
theatrical lighting and direction, have prompted Old Age circuses  &MD; 
and Ringling Bros. in particular  &MD;  to adopt sophisticated lighting
systems, smoke that billows out of machines to create ``atmosphere''
and a pacing to match TV. What happens in the three rings is now
closer to a theatrical performance, further from a venture into what
David Larible calls ``a court of wonders.'' It is more like Las Vegas,
more like Hollywood, more like everything else.
<p>
   There are those who say the circus risks losing its identity, and
its appeal. In fact, the Blue Unit, which played New York while the
Red Unit was in Washington, has a more traditional circus feel, with a
giant and a dwarf and a touch of the sideshow. Rumor has it the Red
Unit will emerge from its winter redesign a little closer to the old
circus as well.
<p>
   But the 127th show still has months to go on this tour. Come Sunday
night, the wires will come down, the citizenry will pack up, and the
small world of the circus will move on.
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-04-14-98 1505EDT &QL;

<STORYID cat=e pri=r sel=tm--a> X1910 </STORYID>

<SLUG> bc-circus-gymnast </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0505 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-circus-gymnast
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Entertainment editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Elizabeth Kastor </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; A Gymnast Joins the Circus Family (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Here is how blood and sawdust create circus rank, and
here is how one woman, teamed with an old circus family, works to rise
in this world.
<p>
   The woman with the body of a lithe preteen introduces herself this
way: ``I am Lucy Petrov. That's not actually my name. It's Kirlova.''
<p>
   But Lucy Kirlova joined up with the performing Petrov Family, so
she is Lucy Petrov. Trained as a gymnast, the Bulgarian signed on with
Todor Petrov and his wife, son and daughter-in-law nine years ago.
They can walk wires, work with animals, juggle, balance and
reconstitute themselves over and over into new acts: Whatever the
circus needs, they will become. In a world in which adaptability is
perhaps the highest virtue, such families are invaluable. Lucy has
been riding elephants, but she is moving up.
<p>
   The Petrov family rates nothing near the entire train car bestowed
upon the Gebel and Larible families. They live in a trailer and are
far from headliners, but they are true circus. The Petrovs performed
around Europe, traveled with Clyde Beatty-Cole Bros. (that
old-fashioned tented circus will settle at the Montgomery County Fair
Grounds in Maryland May 4 and 5), and last year won a contract with
Ringling Bros.
<p>
   Some circus people claim Ringling, which is owned by the Feld
Entertainment empire of Vienna, Va., does not pay as well as some of
the lesser-known circuses. It is also more tightly controlled, more
carefully managed. Because of all that, as well as the limits on
autonomy that go with living on a company train (rather than the
trailers most tent-circus people drive), some performers prefer the
smaller circuses. But Ringling is a resume item that can only help a
career. As Lucy Petrov says: ``It is the Greatest Show on Earth.''
<p>
   Hours before show time, she's working on her ambitions. There are
no elephants in sight. She hangs by her knees 20 feet above a juggling
clown and a band rehearsing fanfares. Her trapeze soars, her hair
ripples like a curtain in the wind. Todor Petrov stands below,
coaching. She swings forward, then back, and in an instant has bent at
the waist, straightened her legs, flipped and twisted around so
quickly that she is a blur of hair and limbs. But then, instead of
catching the trapeze again with her knees as she is supposed to, she
falls.
<p>
   The wire attached to her harness catches her before she can drop
more than a few inches. She spins on the wire and lurches for a
moment, then grabs at the swing and remounts it. She starts the trick
again. When she finally gets it right, when she can assemble the flips
and slides into a routine that looks fluid and assured before an
audience, Lucy Petrov will be ready to rise above the elephants. She
will become a Ringling Bros. and Barnum &AMP; Bailey Woman of the Rainbow,
part of the act that opens the 127th edition of the Greatest Show on
Earth.
</TEXT>

<STORYID cat=k pri=r sel=tm--a> X1913 </STORYID>
<FORMAT> &D3; &D1; </FORMAT>
<SLUG> bc-kennedy-comment </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0933 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-kennedy-comment
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Editorial Page editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Kathleen Kennedy Townsend </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; A Campaign That Never Ended &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Thirty years ago, my father, Robert F. Kennedy, launched his
campaign for the presidency. The spring of 1968 was a dark time for
our country, and was to become darker still. But the memory of my
father's campaign, and the principles and values at its heart still
resonate because of his hopefulness and because he appealed to
Americans' moral courage and faith in one another to overcome whatever
lay ahead.
<p>
   For many, the core mission of that campaign never ended. Scarcely a
day goes by when I am not approached by people who tell me that their
lives were changed by my father because they worked on his campaign or
were inspired by his words and actions.
<p>
   On March 18, 1968, speaking before 18,000 students at Kansas
University, he gave his first major campaign speech. He noted the
great strength of the economy but cautioned us against measuring
ourselves by wealth alone. The GNP, he said, ``counts air pollution
and cigarette advertising, and ambulances to clear our highways of
carnage. It counts special locks for our doors and the jails for those
who break them. Yet the gross national product does not allow for the
health of our children or the joy of their play, the intelligence of
our public debate or the integrity of our public officials. It
measures neither our wit nor our courage, neither our compassion nor
our devotion to our country. It can tell us everything about America
except why we are proud that we are Americans.''
<p>
   It is uncanny how contemporary his words sound. Our country has
never been more prosperous than it is today. My father would certainly
welcome our economic vitality and the good it has brought to the lives
of Americans. But at the same time, I can hear his voice warning us
that the true measure of a person, and of a nation, is found in
something more profound than economic well-being. Precisely at this
moment, he would be impatient for us to turn our minds and our hearts
to the deeper needs of our country.
<p>
   Without question, Robert F. Kennedy would be heartened by the
progress our country has made in specific areas he focused on: the
reduced threat of nuclear war; the collapse of communism; our steps
toward peace with former enemies; the resurgence in community service
and volunteerism; the realization that cigarette companies must be
held accountable for the damage they have done; and the expansion of
health insurance to millions more children.
<p>
   But my father saw progress as an opportunity to rise to tougher
challenges, and he would find no shortage of challenges today. Drug
cartels and organized crime syndicates are quickly becoming the de
facto powers in many countries around the world. Rogue regimes raise
the specter of chemical or biological terrorism. Some of the world's
largest economies are undergoing wrenching change and dislocation that
could open the door for totalitarian regimes.
<p>
   Our education system fails to prepare many children for the future.
One out of two children can expect to spend some time growing up in a
single-parent home. Crime rates have indeed fallen sharply, but a
young African-American man now has nearly a 1 in 3 chance of spending
time in prison.
<p>
   We have a moral obligation to tackle these problems, and to a large
extent, we know what works. At every level  &MD;  national, state or local
 &MD;  and on every issue  &MD;  crime, health, education  &MD;  public successes
have come when citizens are fully engaged in the problems of their
community and work with government, nonprofits and business to solve
them.
<p>
   Sadly, too many of our citizens and especially our children seem to
lack the will to devote themselves to meeting these challenges. A
recent poll showed that just 27 percent of our young people said it is
important to them to be aware of what is going on in the political
system. Only 17 percent said they were interested in influencing that
system.
<p>
   The fault is not theirs. All too rarely do we speak to our youth
about their ability and their duty. If we did so on a national scale,
we would see a quick turnaround. For instance, 95 percent of high
school students who fulfilled Maryland's community service requirement
 &MD;  the first of its kind in the nation  &MD;  said they believed they would
get involved in volunteerism after graduation. It gives me great
personal satisfaction, as a public official and as Robert F. Kennedy's
daughter, to have worked on an initiative that builds upon his vision.
<p>
   My father was never afraid of the truth, and he had an unflinching
optimism and faith in the power of democracy. He believed no
challenge, however complex, was beyond the ability of the American
people if we confronted it honestly, debated it frankly and worked
together to find and apply a solution.
<p>
   That conviction led him to go to Mississippi to witness the depths
of want and poverty suffered by blacks in the South. He returned to
our home in the suburbs of Washington as the entire family was sitting
down to dinner. He entered our dining room ashen-faced and said, ``A
whole family lives in a shack the size of this room. The children are
covered with sores and their tummies stick out because they have no
food. Do you know how lucky you are? Do you  &UR; know &LR;  how  &UR; lucky &LR;  you
are? Do something for your country.''
<p>
   If my father were alive today, I know he would say those same words
to us all.
<p>
    &MD;   &MD;   &MD; 
<p>
   Townsend, eldest child of Robert and Ethel Kennedy, is lieutenant
governor of Maryland.
</TEXT>
