<SLUG> bc-montserrat </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0875 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-montserrat
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Foreign editors)(optional trim included) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Serge F. Kovaleski </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Volcano Ravages Life, Economy of Montserrat &QL; 
 &UR; (Little Bay, Montserrat) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   LITTLE BAY, Montserrat  &MD;  It is little more than a shrubby swath of
undulating mountainside perched above the sea in the northern part of
this tiny Caribbean island.
<p>
   But government officials envision that Little Bay will eventually
be transformed into the seat of government, commerce and culture in
Montserrat, a 39-square-mile British colony that over the last three
years has seen virtually every aspect of its society ravaged by the
Soufriere Hills volcano.
<p>
   Since the volcano roared back to life in July 1995 after centuries
of dormancy, flows of ash, rock and gas have buried the colony's
capital, Plymouth, and forced the evacuation of the southern
two-thirds of the island, including the town of Salem, the island's
nerve center for much of the crisis.
<p>
   The population has withered from an estimated 11,000 people before
the eruptions began to just over 3,200. The economy has been
obliterated: Only about 30 businesses are left, among them just two
banks and a pair of restaurants. The three remaining insurance firms
offer no new property coverage, threatening future construction and
the ability of residents to secure mortgages.
<p>
   Against this backdrop, Montserrat is embarking on a formidable
task: in effect, re-creating itself on the island's northern side, an
area shielded from the volcano by large hills and considered
relatively safe, barring what scientists say would be the unlikely
event of a massive eruption.
<p>
   The ultimate challenge, officials say, is to lure back the
Montserratians who have emigrated, many of whom were the colony's
wealthiest, brightest and youngest.
<p>
   ``It is not often that a society has a chance to start over and
look at it in the end and say, `This is our creation.' Many times,
societies begin sporadically and just grow,'' said David S. Brandt,
Montserrat's chief minister. ``The most important thing is to convince
all Montserratians wherever they are that this is a worthwhile
exercise we are doing and we need everybody to participate.''
<p>
   Brandt said he hopes that Montserrat's economy can reemerge as one
driven largely by light industry and information services, using its
telephone ties to the world via a microwave link to Antigua, 30 miles
northeast. Observers say diversifying the economy is crucial to the
island's future; now about 33 percent of the jobs are in government
and more than 20 percent are in construction.
<p>
   While so much for this eastern Caribbean island hinges on the
behavior of Soufriere Hills, there has been cause for encouragement
the last six weeks, during which the volcano logged its longest and
most quiet period since 1995.
<p>
   ``There is still a low probability of a catastrophic event, but it
is more likely that it is gearing down,'' said a scientist at the
Montserrat Volcano Observatory. He stressed, however: ``This does not
mean it is over. These things operate on long time scales, versus
human time scales. ... We are waiting to see whether the eruption is
over or whether its behavior is changing.''
<p>
   In the meantime, 100 new homes financed by the British government
have been completed in the northern section of the island. As part of
the $92 million that Britain has either committed or spent in aid to
Montserrat over the last three years, another 150 houses are to be
built later this year.
<p>
   The Caribbean Community and Common Market, a regional economic
organization, is also helping to fund 30 other prefabricated dwellings
from Cuba.
<p>
    &UR; -(optional add end)- &QC; 
<p>
   Nearly three years after the volcano began erupting again, 517
displaced Montserratians remain in shelters. Many people here question
London's commitment to Montserrat's future, saying that perhaps the
British are concocting excuses when they say red tape has hampered
delivery of more aid.
<p>
   Montserrat Gov. Tony Abbott, a British official, defended his
country's handling of the crisis, saying that Britain has been as
forthcoming as possible with assistance.
<p>
   ``I can fully understand the feelings of the Montserratians who
remain on the island. On the other hand, I can understand the caution
that the British government is exercising here because of the volcano,
as well as the difficulties in getting aid money released when there
are so many global demands on it,'' Abbott said. ``I believe the
British government has been very generous.''
<p>
   Many Montserratians are eager to return, and several hundred have
done so this year, marking the first population increase since the
Soufriere Hills calamity began. More are expected to try to come back,
as their relocation benefits run out soon. But a lack of housing will
invariably limit that number.
<p>
   Those who have stayed in Montserrat have done so out of either love
for the island or apprehension about starting a new life elsewhere.
<p>
   ``If this place goes up, it goes up with me. If it goes down, it
goes down with me,'' said Joe Phillip, 48, a taxi driver for 22 years.
``Why leave here to go someplace else to struggle? I am too old to go
and start from scratch.'' &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-deception </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 1085 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-deception
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Lifestyle editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Don Oldenburg </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; The Truth About Deception &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Never mind who's telling the lies. The real question is, why are we
so easily deceived?
<p>
   From the half-truths that proliferate in the marketplace to the
intimate deceits of a lover, from the little white lies of everyday
life to the denials of the nation's leaders, deception is pervasive in
our daily interactions  &MD;  and for the most part it succeeds.
<p>
   ``We are sort of like Diogenes looking for that one honest person
out there, and in looking for that one honest person we are more
likely to be duped,'' says Chris Wetzel, professor of psychology at
Rhodes College in Memphis.
<p>
   Teaching a research seminar titled ``Detecting Con Artists and
Impostors,'' Wetzel has been analyzing a fundamental paradox of these
dishonest times: How come we so often see deception coming and still
get blindsided by it? The answer, he and other psychologists who study
deceit say, is that suckers are indeed born every minute. In fact, our
mental health and the social fabric of society practically depend on
it.
<p>
   ``We are predisposed to believe,'' says Wetzel, adding that, except
for the most cynical or paranoid people, we live by what is called
``the truth bias.'' People generally want to believe something is
true. And, while that proves day after day to be a shaky assumption,
it is nonetheless a prerequisite to comprehending what someone is
saying. ``Only after you comprehend what is said,'' he explains, ``can
you make adjustments and consider its validity.''
<p>
   But to scrutinize the veracity of everything would take an
extraordinary cognitive effort that's beyond most people  &MD;  in effect,
substantiating the aphorism ``ask no questions, hear no lies.'' Most
people ``don't have the energy or time to go to the next level of
comprehension and question something's validity,'' says Wetzel. ``The
busier we are, the less likely we'll do it. So we just continue on the
assumption that it's true.''
<p>
   To assume what people tell us is true also goes a long way toward
not upsetting the apple cart of social interaction. To assume the
opposite creates chaos, says Wetzel. In a course last semester, he
announced that he would mix one lie into each lecture. And he would
count it against students who failed to detect the lies and included
them in exam answers, he warned. After several weeks, he had to stop
the experiment. His students had begun to question everything he
mentioned.
<p>
   ``It became disruptive to the class,'' he says. ``You almost have
to become a paranoid to question everything and see what's going on,
and most of us are not willing to pay that price. It would almost
drive you nuts to be that vigilant for the truth.''
<p>
   Brad Blandford believes people are incapable of knowing when others
are lying unless they themselves are totally honest. ``We'll buy
anything,'' says the Stanley, Va., psychologist, author of ``Radical
Honesty: How To Transform Your Life By Telling The Truth'' and leader
of Radical Honesty workshops that teach people to live honestly.
<p>
   Mentioning a survey of 40,000 Americans in the book ``The Day
America Told the Truth,'' Blandford says 93 percent admitted they lied
regularly and habitually at work. Of the married people surveyed, 35
percent admitted they either had had or were currently having an
extramarital affair. ``Lying is of epidemic proportions,'' he says,
blaming it for much of the anxiety, depression and psychosomatic
illnesses so prevalent in society today. ``So it turns out that
everyone is just guessing about everyone else.''
<p>
   In many ways, the entire dynamic of telling and believing lies is
what makes truth more of a stranger than fiction, as Mark Twain put
it. Accepting even suspect lies turns out to be the path of least
resistance many people choose to follow. Whether it's the auto
mechanic calling to say the clutch also needs to be replaced, or it's
an employee calling in sick on a sunny spring day, confronting a
suspected lie ``can make for a very ugly scene and a lot of people
aren't willing to go through that trouble,'' says Wetzel. ``Once we
believe we're being fed a line, it is more difficult and socially
awkward to call someone on a lie than to let it go'' or give them the
benefit of the doubt. Allegations surrounding the Clinton sex scandal,
with its his-word-against-her-word dynamics, says Wetzel, ``has been
an ugly scene for about three months now  &MD;  and that's one reason
people just want it to go away.''
<p>
   Further complicating matters is the element of unconscious
collusion  &MD;  when the deceived prefers the lie to the truth. In
January, University of Virginia psychologist Bella DePaulo released
the latest findings from her continuing research on everyday lies in
close and casual relationships. She found that about a quarter of lies
told to people close to us are ``altruistic lies''  &MD;  the
no-you-don't-look-fat kind of fibs supposedly contrived for the
person's own good.
<p>
   ``They'd tell people they looked good, or made a great dinner, or
that they understood just how they felt,'' reports DePaulo. ``They are
lying to spare someone else's feelings, to make them feel better, or
spare them embarrassment.
<p>
   ``In the abstract, I think we understand that when people are
saying these things they don't always mean them. And yet you want to
believe it. It's hard to step back and say `Oh, but did they really
like my dinner?' Because you do really want to believe these
altruistic lies.''
<p>
   And even the more serious lies, says DePaulo. ``Take, for example,
an affair. You don't know for sure if it is happening and you wish it
is not happening. And if you see through the lie to the truth that is
being hidden, it can be a disaster. If you put everything out on the
table, nothing that seemed in place is secure. It's all potentially
over.''
<p>
   Besides, through no fault of our own we simply are not good at
detecting lies. Despite hundreds of studies trying to identify the
telltale behaviors or linguistic signals that accompany a lie, ``there
is no perfectly reliable sign of lying, no Pinocchio's nose,'' says
DePaulo.
<p>
   Part of that is because different people tell lies differently. And
the very behaviors and attributes that make someone seem to be
believable are also what she has found common among those who tell the
most lies. ``If you feel really sure someone is lying,'' warns
DePaulo, ``it is just as likely they are telling the truth.''
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-mexico-deport </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 1247 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-mexico-deport
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By John Ward Anderson </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Mexican Government Deports Foreign Observers in Chiapas &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   SAN CRISTOBAL DE LAS CASAS, Mexico  &MD;  Tom Hansen, a human rights
activist and social worker from Chicago, was buying supplies in a
small town near here two months ago when he was detained by Mexican
immigration officials and handed over to four heavily armed men in
street clothes.
<p>
   They ushered Hansen, who had been in the conflict-ridden state of
Chiapas for two days working with indigenous people, into an old
yellow sedan. ``When I asked them for identification, they laughed and
told me they could beat me up and put me in a crate,'' Hansen
recounted in a recent interview. ``I was pretty well convinced I was
going to be killed.''
<p>
   Instead, he spent the next 24 hours being ferried from Chiapas to
Mexico City to what he described as an immigration prison and being
interrogated by various Mexican officials who refused to allow him to
see an attorney, make a telephone call or contact the U.S. Embassy.
They finally put him on a plane and expelled him from the country for
committing a cardinal offense  &MD;  meddling in Mexico's internal affairs.
<p>
   Hansen was one of the first in a wave of foreigners  &MD;  including a
French priest who served here for 32 years and an American student on
spring break  &MD;  who recently have been thrown out of Mexico for
allegedly participating in the long-simmering conflict in the southern
state of Chiapas, where masked rebels launched an armed rebellion four
years ago demanding greater rights for Indians.
<p>
   Mexico escalated the crackdown on foreigners on Sunday, deporting
12  &MD;  including three Americans  &MD;  who were arrested when about 500
state and federal policemen stormed a small community that had
recently declared itself autonomous from the local elected government
and sympathetic to the rebels, known as the Zapatista National
Liberation Army. News services identified the Americans as Jeffrey
Conant, Michael Zap and Travis Loller, all of San Francisco.
<p>
   Police also beat two news photographers who were taking pictures of
the foreigners at the airport in Tuxtla Gutierrez, the capital of
Chiapas, continuing a recent government campaign to hinder press
coverage  &MD;  particularly by foreign reporters  &MD;  in the state.
<p>
   Mexican officials said the roughly two dozen foreign visitors
deported so far this year were expelled for violating their tourist
visas by working, which requires a special visa. But the officials
acknowledged that the underlying reason for the expulsions was the
belief that the foreigners were intervening in Mexican politics, which
is a violation of the country's constitution.
<p>
   Many analysts said they believe the true motive was to intimidate
human rights workers and stymie international criticism of Mexico's
human rights record following the December massacre of 45 unarmed
Chiapas peasants by a group aligned with the ruling political party.
The matter is particularly sensitive now because Mexico is negotiating
a new trade agreement with the European Union, which weighs a
country's human rights record when considering trade accords.
<p>
   ``This xenophobia campaign is an attempt to discredit international
observation and to lump us with the enemy  &MD;  to make it appear that we
are engaged in political activity,'' said a European human rights
worker based in San Cristobal de Las Casas, who asked not to be
identified because he is in Mexico on a tourist visa. He added that
many human rights workers were coming to Mexico on tourist visas
because the government refused to give them working visas.
<p>
   ``We don't want our people demonstrating in the (town square) or
marching or making statements to the Mexican press. Our role is to
distribute information outside of Mexico, to put international
pressure on the government.''
<p>
   Fernando Solis Camara, a top immigration official in Mexico City,
said the government welcomed humanitarian workers and human rights
observers with the proper visas, which he said were easy to get. But
foreigners engaging in political activities ``are simply a factor that
complicates (finding) a solution to the Chiapas problem,'' he said,
because they help polarize the politics, spread propaganda and
discourage communities from accepting government aid.
<p>
   The issue has been complicated by Mexico's long-standing tradition
of allowing visitors to participate in a variety of activities on a
tourist visa; by the isolated communities in rural Chiapas, which have
always viewed foreigners with suspicion; by the role of the Roman
Catholic Church, which has encouraged international observation and
which the government accuses of siding with the rebels; by Mexico's
equating any foreign meddling with a threat to its sovereignty; by the
outpouring of international support enjoyed by the Zapatistas; and by
the influx of foreigners wanting to visit Chiapas following the
December massacre. Many of the visitors are trained observers,
academics and relief workers, but others are more akin to adventure
tourists who want to be more deeply involved.
<p>
   ``Some are earnest and good-willed people who do not know what they
are getting into and want to be in solidarity with poor people,'' said
Eric Olsen of the Washington Office on Latin America, a private think
tank. ``Sometimes they don't have the political savvy to know when
they've crossed the line.''
<p>
   That was apparently the case during a reporter's recent visit to
Oventik, the small community sympathetic to the Zapatistas that has
roped itself off and declared itself autonomous from the elected local
government. When a military troop transport drove by, a half-dozen
Italians raced to the main gate and took pictures as community
officials implored them not to bother the army.
<p>
   A foreign anthropologist who has worked in Chiapas for more than 10
years said that at some point the conflict here became a sort of
``phenomenon'' that attracted all types, in addition to the academics
and social workers who have been visiting for decades. ``Those kids
coming down to make revolution on spring break are probably
particularly irksome'' to the government, he said.
<p>
   Sometimes, it is a fine line that separates tourism from
observation from activism, as illustrated by Global Exchange, a San
Francisco human rights and education group that since 1994 has sent
more than 500 Americans on ``reality tours'' to observe the situation
here. Participants meet with community leaders and are encouraged to
write up and share their impressions of Chiapas  &MD;  with friends, with
family, with congressmen  &MD;  when they return home.
<p>
   ``The fact that they are observing reality in Chiapas is our
intention, but they are not working in any sense,'' said Ted Lewis,
head of the organization's Mexico project.
<p>
   Others, like Hansen, 44, acknowledge that they were working while
on a tourist visa, but say it was with the obvious knowledge of the
government. In fact, he said, his project  &MD;  a $35,000 program to
provide video cameras to Indian communities ``so they can tell their
own story in their own words''  &MD;  was partially funded by the Mexican
government.
<p>
   ``I think they're trying to scare the foreigners out of Chiapas,''
he said, ``and the people who suffer the most are the indigenous
communities, because they are left with less access to technical and
humanitarian aid, and they're more vulnerable to human rights
abuses.''
</TEXT>

<STORYID cat=e pri=r sel=tm--a> X1920 </STORYID>

<SLUG> bc-books-johnson </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 1504 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-books-johnson
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Book, Feature editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By David Streitfeld </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Novelist Charles Johnson's Doctrine of Nonviolence (Seattle) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   SEATTLE  &MD;  It was a warm spring day, the first of the season, so
Charles Johnson had opened the window in his college dorm room.
Through it, he heard a voice shouting: Martin Luther King Jr. had been
shot. There was no context, no further information. Across the country
the riots were beginning. Johnson was in black studies at Southern
Illinois University, a Marxist, a respectful if distant admirer of
King. But he didn't riot, didn't even leave his room.
<p>
   Instead, he drew an editorial cartoon for the campus newspaper, a
variation of a drawing that became popular after the assassination of
JFK: the Statue of Liberty on one knee, weeping, covering her face
with her hands. Johnson doesn't remember anything else about that
fateful 1968 day. All he has left is the tangible product, the
drawing.
<p>
   Typical, he says. ``Even as a kid, my way to respond to things was
through drawings or writing. Through art. That's how I expressed
myself. That's my whole life. That's all I've ever done. I get
passionate and experimental in the work, but I live a quiet, simple,
boring life.''
<p>
   Oh, sure. That's why the novelist is pummeling George, an
amiable-looking dummy suspended from the ceiling of Johnson's workout
room. Thwack. He hits George again. Does anyone in this pleasant
neighborhood know what goes on in here, the savage beatings that are
administered on a regular basis?
<p>
   ``George's purpose in life is to be used for the purpose of being
beaten on. The thing that's good about him is that he looks like a
person.'' He takes down a lance from the wall and stabs George with
it. George looks like he's been through a lot.
<p>
   On the wall, there's a machete that looks like it's also gotten
some use recently. (For cutting the grass, Johnson insists.) There's a
bow for shooting arrows. Plus a poster of the ``Basic Striking
Points''  &MD;  those sensitive areas where a blow can disable or, better,
kill. Johnson plans to highlight them on George.
<p>
   The writer is just fooling around tonight, but he's taken his
martial arts seriously for 30 years  &MD;  ever since he was working in
Chicago as what was then called a garbage man. One of his co-workers
told him about a guy he knew who had used martial arts training to
defend himself against a rampaging drunk. Killed him, too. Johnson
attended the school, learned how to throw 45 punches in 10 seconds and
made it through the critical first cut.
<p>
   ``Me and one of the other guys got double promotions. I was
sailing. I missed my train back to school. I just walked around
Chicago all night waiting for someone to jump me.''
<p>
   No one did, then or later. Johnson is probably the only winner of
the National Book Award who could kill you with his hands, but martial
arts also taught him how to avoid dangerous situations. One of the
best things he says he learned in the school was what to do if someone
wants to fight you: You run. If the guy chases you? You keep running.
If you run into a wall? Tear the wall down.
<p>
   After he explains this, it all makes sense. Johnson, a professor at
the University of Washington, kung-fu instructor and practicing
Buddhist, has learned the tools of violence to keep violence at bay.
It's a lifelong project that, for the last eight years, has been
coupled with a more intellectual undertaking: understanding the life
and legacy of the Rev. Dr. King, America's greatest apostle of
nonviolence.
<p>
   Out of these two strands has arisen ``Dreamer,'' a tightly furled
account of King's last years, partly a respectful close-up of the
civil rights leader himself, partly the tale of a man who looks so
much like King that many people assume he is.
<p>
   ``I can't walk down the street or go to the store without somebody
stoppin' me. Some of 'em spit in my face. That's colored as well as
white. That's why I come here. I figure if I'm catchin' hell 'cause of
you, I might's well catch it for you instead,'' Chaym Smith tells
King, offering to become his double.
<p>
   Sardonic, brutal, an ex-mental patient and possible murderer, Smith
is not only King's look-alike but his opposite. ``There's two kinds of
people in this world,'' Smith says. ``Predators and prey. Lions and
lunch. You see it any other way, buddy, and people will chump you
off.'' Not exactly the reverend's way of looking at things, which is
the point.
<p>
   A double  &MD;  someone who matches another in appearance but not
necessarily in essence  &MD;  is a useful device for exploring the black
and white conflict in America. ``Blacks and whites are mirrors,''
Johnson believes. ``They can only fully see themselves in relationship
to the other.''
<p>
   The Founding Fathers, he adds, cherished freedom enough to break
away from England, but at the same time were slaveholders. ``As
Frederick Douglass pointed out, if you value freedom that much, you've
got to value it for everyone. And King made white Christians more
acutely conscious of the necessity of justice for black people, if
they really believed in those Christian ideals.''
<p>
   All this was a long time ago. ``Dreamer'' had its roots in
Johnson's realization that, for all of the public veneration of King  &MD; 
the national holiday, the schools and boulevards named in his memory  &MD; 
his message had been largely lost. ``The tragedy of his absence is
acutely felt in every fiber of our public and personal lives,''
Johnson recently wrote in CommonQuest: The Magazine of Black/Jewish
Relations.
<p>
   No one quotes the right things from King, he says now. ``You have
guys here in Washington state who are pro-gun who quote King to say he
would be in their favor, that owning guns is a civil rights issue. You
have Louis Farrakhan quoting King. It's like Malcolm X's daughter
said, people take pieces, they don't take the whole thing. That's what
they do with King, too.''
<p>
   Johnson reflects for a second, then speeds on. ``They don't take
the right things. King has this quote that is so important, in which
he says we have to fight on two fronts. The first one is the external
war, the battle against segregation, and the other one is internal. He
said the two have to go together. I don't think they did. One became
predominant, and the other''  &MD;  the battle for self-improvement  &MD;  ``got
swept under the rug.''
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Johnson writes heavily philosophic novels that are very popular  &MD; 
the announced first printing for ``Dreamer,'' which has been getting
generally enthusiastic and some rave reviews, was 75,000.
<p>
   For that, he can thank ``Middle Passage,'' his third novel, which
appeared in 1990. Like ``Dreamer,'' it was the product of considerable
research, in this case on the slave trade. Reviews were good but sales
were modest; the novel might have disappeared if it hadn't been
nominated for and then, in an upset, won the National Book Award. That
made it a best-seller. As a bonus, sales of Johnson's two earlier
novels picked up, he got screenwriting jobs and speaking engagements.
<p>
   Being mildly well-known  &MD;  he gets recognized sometimes in stores in
this city  &MD;  and having all these offers to do all these other things
didn't slow down the fiction. He's always taken his time.
<p>
   ``I never had any desire to be a writer, to have the writer's
life,'' he says. ``All that stuff never appealed to me. It still
doesn't. This isn't a career. I'm not interested in careerism. Screw
that.''
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   In the CommonQuest essay, the writer cites the ``egregious news
stories about black crime, drugs and illegitimacy'' before asking,
``How did we allow this to happen? Between my generation and that of
so many of the children I've seen there has been no transmission of
the triumphs, personal and political, that strengthened black
Americans for centuries, allowing our predecessors to overcome
staggering obstacles in the pre-civil rights era.''
<p>
   Still, redemption, even at this late date, remains a possibility.
While Chaym Smith is an ambiguous figure and his fate in the book's
final pages uncertain, the novel concludes on an optimistic note. Says
the narrator, a young disciple of King's: ``If the prophet King had
shown up the depths of living possible for those who loved
unconditionally in a less than just universe engraved with inequality,
and that only the servants should lead, then Chaym had in his covert
passage through our lives let us know that, if one missed the Galilean
mark, even the pariahs, the fatherless exiles, might sometimes  &MD;  and
occasionally  &MD;  doeth well.''
<p>
   This is a stern admonition and maybe even a call to action,
something novels used to do routinely back in the '60s but only rarely
since. But neither philosophy professors nor Buddhists qualify as
rabble-rousers.
<p>
   ``Christians proselytize,'' says Johnson. ``They say, `If you don't
do this, you're going to Hell.' But I never do. You have to make your
own karma.''
<p>
   Spoken like a true Buddhist.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-iraq </SLUG>

bc-iraq - a1402

<BYLINE> By Craig Turner </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Iraq Executed More Than 1,500 Last Year, U.N. Told (Geneva) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   GENEVA  &MD;  The Iraqi government summarily executed more than 1,500
people for political offenses last year as the human rights situation
in that country deteriorated to a new low, according to a U.N. special
investigator who Tuesday urged the Security Council to devote as much
attention to that issue as it does to dismantling Baghdad's illegal
weapons programs.
<p>
   Max van der Stoel, a former foreign minister in the Netherlands,
has since 1991 periodically documented the excesses of what he called
Iraqi President Saddam Hussein's ``regime of terror.''
<p>
   But van der Stoel's latest findings come amid increased
international debate over whether to consider lifting economic
sanctions against Baghdad. The United States and Britain are likely to
cite his report in opposing efforts by Russia, France, China and
others to ease the restrictions.
<p>
   A regular review of the sanctions by the Security Council is
expected soon.
<p>
   Van der Stoel told a news conference Tuesday in Geneva that,
despite Baghdad's claim that the sanctions are the underlying cause of
human rights problems in Iraq, there is no evidence that their
elimination would improve conditions. ``Any Iraqi who is even the
slightest bit critical of the government is in danger, even of their
life,'' he said. ``I don't think if the sanctions would end tomorrow
that the regime would change its ways. It only feels secure by
maintaining its present policy of oppression.''
<p>
   He added that the situation is likely to remain unchanged while
Hussein retains power.
<p>
   Van der Stoel acknowledged some frustration that the human rights
situation in Iraq has failed to draw the kind of international
attention devoted in recent months to the U.N. program for inspecting
Iraq's weapons programs. ``It has not come to the top of the
international agenda,'' he said. He suggested that the Security
Council, which has jurisdiction over the sanctions, should apply more
scrutiny to Iraq's human rights records.
<p>
   Van der Stole presented his report to the annual meeting here of
the 53-nation U.N. Human Rights Commission, the world organization's
main deliberative assembly on human rights. Because Iraq refuses to
let him into the country, he relies on reports from human rights
watchdogs and other observers in bordering countries and on interviews
with refugees and exiles.
<p>
   In its reply to the commission, the Iraqi government rejected the
report, blamed its internal problems on the U.N. embargo and accused
van der Stoel of being a tool of Washington.
<p>
   Van der Stoel cited as the most egregious new violation the
estimated 1,500 executions last year, most in a November and December
``prison cleansing campaign'' that began after one of Hussein's sons,
Qusai, the head of Iraq's special security organization, visited one
of the prisons.
<p>
   Prisoners were shot, hanged or electrocuted on charges ranging from
the attempted assassination of Hussein to belonging to an outlawed
political group, he said. Four Jordanians, however, were executed for
smuggling $850 of auto parts, a matter that severely strained
relations between Jordan and Iraq.
<p>
   Family members who collected the bodies were charged the price of
the bullet in those cases where the inmate had been shot. The
condition of some bodies suggested they were tortured before being
killed.
<p>
   Van der Stoel reported that ethnic and religious minorities,
especially Kurds and Turkomens, continue to be targeted in forcible
relocation campaigns intended to create ethnic Arab majorities in all
regions of the country. Van der Stoel told reporters an estimated
500,000 people had been arbitrarily displaced in the last 10 years.
<p>
   He also cited Iraq's nearly 20,000 officially recorded cases of
forced or involuntary disappearances, the most of any country in the
world; criticized the dismembering, disfigurement and other torture of
prisoners; condemned laws that prescribe the death penalty for those
who insult Hussein; and said delays by the Iraqi government in
cooperating with the United Nations' ``oil-for-food'' program had
contributed to the suffering caused by the sanctions. The oil-for-food
project permits Iraq to sell limited amounts of oil in exchange for
food, medicine and other humanitarian goods that are distributed under
U.N. supervision.
</TEXT>

<HEADER> 04-14 0429 </HEADER>

bc-italy-mafia

</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Vera Haller </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
Special to The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Italian Police Arrest Mafia Figure (Rome) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   ROME  &MD;  Police said Thursday they have arrested Italy's second most
wanted Mafia fugitive, a reputed up-and-coming candidate to lead
Sicily's organized crime network.
<p>
   Vito Vitale, 39, was a hitman who had consolidated power over clans
in and around the Sicilian capital of Palermo, police said. ``Vitale
was a rising boss who in only a few years had been able to reach the
highest levels of the Sicilian Mafia,'' Palermo's police chief,
Antonio Manganelli, said.
<p>
   He said Vitale was the most-wanted Mafia suspect behind Bernardo
Provenzano, the highest ranking boss to have evaded arrest.
Provenzano, a fugitive for more than three decades, is elderly and
believed to be in poor health.
<p>
   Manganelli said that after two failed attempts to arrest Vitale in
the past eight months, the suspect was apprehended around noon
Thursday in a farmhouse under construction in the countryside south of
Palermo just yards away from a Benedictine monastery. The owner of the
property and another man in the farmhouse at the time of the raid also
were arrested. Police said Vitale tried to run away, then surrendered
on charges of Mafia association and other counts dating back to 1995.
<p>
   According to investigators, Vitale had risen quickly in the Mafia
power structure in large part because he was the protege of Leoluca
Bagarella, a Mafia boss arrested in 1995. They said Mafia members who
had turned state's evidence told police that Bagarella was impressed
with Vitale's abilities as a hitman and with his willingess to host
Mafia summits on his property.
<p>
   Vitale also was said to have had the support of the so-called boss
of bosses, Salvatore ``Toto'' Riina, who was arrested in 1993 after 23
years on the run. Investigators said Riina, boss of the Corleonese
clan immortalized in the ``Godfather'' movies, had offered Vitale
protection in disputes with other Mafia members seeking to increase
their power bases.
<p>
   ``He is the only true heir of Bagarella and Riina,'' said Palermo
prosecutor Alfonso Sabella who led the investigation that resulted in
Vitale's arrest.
<p>
   Ottaviano Del Turco, president of parliament's anti-Mafia
commission, praised police and said Vitale's arrest showed that law
enforcement was continuing to successfully fight organized crime. The
government began an intense crackdown on the Mafia after the 1992 car
bombings that killed anti-Mafia prosecutors Giovanni Falcone and Paolo
Borsellino.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-russia </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0567 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-russia
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Foreign editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Daniel Williams </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Key Figure Eases Up on Opposition to Yeltsin Nominee (Moscow) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   MOSCOW  &MD;  The first crack appeared Tuesday in the wall of
parliamentary opposition to President Boris Yeltsin's choice for prime
minister.
<p>
   The speaker of the lower house, the State Duma, declared that it
would be better to approve the nomination of Sergei Kiriyenko than to
force Yeltsin to order new parliamentary elections in the event of a
stalemate. ``It's a hundred times more important for me to preserve
(the legislature) and let it work to the end of its term,'' said
Gennady Seleznev, a Communist Party member.
<p>
   His statement suggests that a split has developed among the
Communists  &MD;  who comprise the biggest faction in the Duma  &MD;  since
Communist leader Gennady Zyuganov has insisted his party will oppose
Kiriyenko at all costs.
<p>
   Kiriyenko received 143 votes in the first ballot on his nomination
Friday but needs 226 to be confirmed. It is unclear if Seleznev's
assertion will generate enough new support to give Kiriyenko the post,
since the ballot was secret, and it is not certain which lawmakers
voted for or against him.
<p>
   In any event, Yeltsin renominated him immediately after the vote,
and a second ballot is scheduled for Friday. If the Duma resists
again, Yeltsin can nominate Kiriyenko a third time; if he is rebuffed
yet again, Yeltsin can call early parliamentary elections. Otherwise,
the Duma's term is scheduled to end next year.
<p>
   The speaker, who predicted Kiriyenko will get 236 votes in this
week's vote, made his remarks after Yeltsin rejected an offer to
present three alternative candidates to the Duma for a straw poll.
Yeltsin refused to consider any other nominees, Seleznev said.
<p>
   Otherwise, Yeltsin has been conciliatory to the Duma all week in an
effort to gather support for his nominee. He invited members with
personal housing needs or other bureaucratic problems to take the
matters up with a presidential assistant, and he pledged that
Kiriyenko would consult closely with the Duma on all legislation.
<p>
   Kiriyenko's opponents have complained that, at 35, the former
energy minister is too young and inexperienced to head the government.
However, a deeper resistance stems from Yeltsin's unwillingness to
reveal who will serve in Kiriyenko's cabinet other than the current
defense, finance and foreign ministers. Before the first vote on his
nomination, Kiriyenko promised to make the choices public this week
but has since backed off.
<p>
   Since neither the prime minister nor his cabinet need belong to the
Duma or be elected to any post to serve in the cabinet, the Duma is
essentially being asked to act on faith.
<p>
   Yeltsin frequently ignores parliamentary opinion and rules by
decree. Tuesday, however, he signed a Duma measure forbidding the
return to Germany of art looted by Soviet troops during World War
II-but only because the Constitutional Court insisted he had to sign
it after both houses of parliament overrode a presidential veto.
<p>
   Presidential aides said, however, that his signature was only a
prelude to another court challenge; he signed it, they say, but still
does not accept the legislation. Yeltsin has been under pressure from
Germany, a major investor and creditor, to return the art, which is
currently in the custody of Russian museums. &QL; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-tax </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1600 04-14 1125 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-tax - a1600
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Jonathan Weisman </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; ANALYSIS: Unlike Titanic, Tax Code Won't Sink Easily (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  In a time-tested show of political theater, Republican
leaders Wednesday will dump the entire U.S. tax code into Boston
Harbor, as though confident that tax reform will soon make the
loathsome load a thing of the past.
<p>
   But unlike the crates of tea jettisoned by colonial patriots, the
tax books will be attached to a strong tether. And once the cameras
switch off and the crowds dissipate, workmen will haul all 101,295
pages back from the deep, proving symbolically what Washington knows
full well: Neither the politicians nor the public is ready to bury the
tax system just yet.
<p>
   ``It is as certain as the Easter Bunny that Republicans are going
to talk about tax reform at this time of year,'' said William
Schneider, a political analyst at the American Enterprise Institute.
``But this year, with inflation gone and the economy doing so well, it
just doesn't have as much resonance as usual.''
<p>
   Late last year, Republican leaders promised that 1998 would be the
year for tax reform, pledging to pass legislation that would abolish
the current tax code by 2001. Last fall offered an ideal political
moment  &MD;  a brief firestorm created by Senate hearings that exposed
alleged abuses of taxpayers by overzealous Internal Revenue Service
agents.
<p>
   Republicans used that moment to highlight what most Americans
recognize: The tax code is freighted with special-interest loopholes
and mind-numbing complexity.
<p>
   But as Washington turned its attention to presidential sex
scandals, a Social Security overhaul and Republican leadership turf
wars, the tax reform issue has faded. Even as they talk up a new
system that is ``fairer, flatter and simpler,'' Republicans and
Democrats alike are pushing new tax credits and shelters that will
make the system only more cumbersome.
<p>
   ``There is an inability for Republicans to stay on one issue over a
long period of time,'' fumed Frank Luntz, a Republican pollster and
tactician. ``Democrats are great at repeating themselves again and
again and again. Republicans say it once, and it's over.''
<p>
   But tactics may not be the only problem. The fervor for tax reform
that Republicans had hoped to tap may never have been real, said
Karlyn Bowman, a public policy analyst at the American Enterprise
Institute. About 58 percent of Americans say their taxes are too high,
down from last year and 1994, according to a recent Gallup poll.
<p>
   Two competing GOP plans are vying for attention. One would replace
the income tax with a 15 percent national sales tax on all goods and
services. The other would scrap the graduated income tax and all its
loopholes, deductions and shelters and replace it with one flat rate
of 20 percent.
<p>
   Republican leaders hope to force the issue by passing a law this
summer that would automatically scrap the current code on Jan. 1,
2001. President Clinton has denounced the idea, saying that abolishing
the code without presenting a credible alternative would rattle
financial markets and unhinge the booming economy.
<p>
   But by setting a deadline for action, proponents insist that the
public and the politicians will unite around a single new system.
<p>
   ``We have to prove to skeptical Americans we're serious about
getting rid of (the current system),'' said Rep. Bill Paxon, R-N.Y,
one of the sunset bill's authors.
<p>
   ``I can't even imagine what would be more responsible than what
we're trying to do: to lay out a thoughtful process and timetable to
draw up and implement a new tax code,'' insisted Rep. Steve Largent,
R-Okla., who is the legislation's other chief sponsor.
<p>
   Three years may well not be enough time, tax experts warn. The
political courage would have to be summoned to overcome the special
interests that helped create so many popular write-offs and loopholes
in the first place.
<p>
   ``Quite frankly, the current code creates enormous amounts of
(campaign) contributions'' from interest groups seeking to keep their
tax breaks or open new ones, said Jim Miller, a budget director in the
Reagan administration who now leads tax reform efforts for Citizens
for a Sound Economy, a free-market interest group.
<p>
   Consider what happened early this month when the Senate tried to
pass a non-binding resolution calling on Washington to scrap the
current tax code. By the time the resolution passed, senators had
inserted clauses to preserve the mortgage interest deduction, the
deduction for charitable donations, and protections for Medicare and
Social Security.
<p>
   They even inserted a clause that defeated the purpose of the
resolution. It read: ``Sunsetting the entire income tax code without
describing a replacement threatens our nation's economic growth and
unwisely eliminates existing tax incentives that are crucial for
taxpayers.''
<p>
   ``It's indicative of how difficult this is,'' said William Gale, a
Brookings Institution economist. ``This (resolution) is barely
sticking a toe in the water, and you've already preserved two of the
most major deductions.''
<p>
   A flat tax that still allows deductions for mortgage interest and
charitable giving would have to be set at 25 percent, Gale said, if,
as proponents say, some reforms would be phased in over several years
to help ease taxpayers' transition to the new system.
<p>
   In addition, political pressure would almost certainly save the
earned income tax credit for the working poor. Businesses would insist
that erasing the deduction for worker health plans would throw more
people into the ranks of the uninsured. According to Gale, that would
push the rate to 27.5 percent or higher, enough to give any politician
sticker shock.
<p>
   The national sales tax might have even more flaws. It could create
incentives for businesses to take transactions underground, offering
customers illegally tax-free purchases if they pay a little higher
price.
<p>
   Worse still politically, both the flat tax and the sales tax would
sharply lower taxes on the rich, mainly by exempting investment and
savings income from taxes.
<p>
   That is not to say the current system can remain indefinitely, said
Michael J. Graetz, a Yale University law professor. Graetz called the
system's demise inevitable, but said it will not happen until
politicians stop fixating on politically unfeasible options.
<p>
   ``You need a solution without the major tax cut for the wealthy
that the flat tax or sales tax would mean,'' Graetz said. ``The right
question is not, `What happened to tax reform?' but `When will we get
to a more realistic agenda?' ''
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QL; 
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-04-14-98 1903EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-economy-post </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0522 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-economy-post
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) (Includes Optional Trim) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By John M. Berry </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; No Rise in Consumer Prices (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  For the second time in three months, consumer prices
didn't rise at all in March, with food prices stable and falling
energy, apparel and tobacco costs offsetting increases in other
prices, the Bureau of Labor Statistics reported Tuesday.
<p>
   The consumer price index was flat in January and up only 0.1
percent in February. The CPI was up at an annual rate of only 0.2
percent in the first quarter.
<p>
   ``Inflation remains utterly absent,'' said Bruce Steinberg, chief
economist at Merrill Lynch &AMP; Co. in New York, who predicted that
consumer prices will ``rise less than 1.5 percent in 1998. . . .
Inflation has never been so low so far into an (economic) expansion.''
<p>
   In a separate report, the Census Bureau said retail sales declined
0.1 percent last month to $218.4 billion, after two strong months of
growth. The Bureau also revised February's results upward to a 0.7
percent gain from the 0.5 percent rise reported earlier.
<p>
   Sales fell at auto dealers by 0.7 percent and purchases at both
building materials and furniture stores also fell 0.6 percent.
<p>
   Analysts weren't sure whether the small drop in sales was a sign
that consumer demand may be growing less rapidly than at the beginning
of the year  &MD;  which could signal a slowing in U.S. economic growth  &MD; 
whether it was a one-month blip. Some analysts said the very late date
for Easter, which was last Sunday, may have caused consumers to delay
some of their spring spending until this month.
<p>
   ``We will need to average the March and April data to get a more
accurate view,'' said Dana Saporta of Stone &AMP; McCarthy, a New York
financial markets research firm. ``To the extent the later Easter
lowered March sales, they may get a boost this month.''
<p>
   However, even with the March decline, consumer spending in the
first three months of the year likely rose at a 5 percent annual rate
or more after adjustment for inflation, the analysts said.
<p>
   With prices for many goods either flat or falling, retail sales
naturally rise less rapidly than when inflation is higher. For
instance, sales at gasoline stations dropped 1.4 percent last month to
a level 8.1 percent below the level of March 1997, primarily as a
result of falling gasoline prices. Those prices fell 3.5 percent last
month and are down 14 percent over the past six months, according to
the BLS report.
 &UR; (Optional Add End) &QL; 
<p>
   Excluding often volatile food and energy prices, the so-called core
CPI rose 0.1 percent last month. Over the last 12 months, the core CPI
increased 2.1 percent, the smallest rise for a 12-month period since
April 1966.
<p>
   Maury N. Harris, chief economist at PaineWebber Inc. in New York,
said his firm's ``leading indicator of inflation'  &MD;  a composite index
of seven statistics that give early warning signals of future
inflation  &MD;  have fallen for five consecutive months. That suggests
``that inflation should remain in the ``no problem' category this
year,'' Harris said.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-korea-families </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0971 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-korea-families
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Foreign editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Mary Jordan </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Families Separated by Korean War See Glimmer &QL; 
 &UR; Of Hope for Reunions (Seoul) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   SEOUL, South Korea  &MD;  For the last 50 years, Cho Dong-young would
close his eyes and see his little sister, Chae, dancing around their
childhood home, singing a song called ``Spring Lady,'' when springtime
was brighter in the northern part of Korea.
<p>
   For Cho, 74, the fate of his five brothers and sisters and his
parents has been a haunting mystery locked inside the sealed borders
of North Korea. He last saw them when he left home to attend college
in Japan in 1947, shortly before the North's new Communist government
closed off access to the world.
<p>
   As the decades passed, Cho had no word about his family until 10
days ago, when he received a one-paragraph letter from Chae. It had
been routed through Los Angeles and a series of mutual friends, and it
hit Cho like an aching from deep inside. ``She said everybody was dead
but her. I cried all night long.''
<p>
   Cho apologized for smoking and shook with emotion as he showed a
visitor the simple note that began, ``To my dearest brother whom I
miss so much.''
<p>
   For the past four days, North Korean and South Korean government
officials meeting in a Beijing hotel have been discussing uniting
hundreds of thousands of families like Cho's. The talks, widely
described as historic in front-page news accounts here, stalled
Tuesday, with impoverished North Korea's negotiators asking for
shiploads of fertilizer before they would discuss a timetable for
reunions. But there still is guarded optimism about eventual reunions
because both sides are saying they want aging citizens to see their
relatives before they die.
<p>
   North Korea recently has indicated it will help collect addresses
and set up the first direct-mail exchange between families in a
half-century. By June, South Korea is to set up a government
information center to help families locate their relatives in the
North.
<p>
   Since the 1950-53 Korean War, no letters, phone calls or any other
contacts have been permitted between civilians on opposite sides of
the border. While Cold War frontiers have toppled elsewhere in the
world, this holdout is still guarded by a fierce collection of
artillery and soldiers.
<p>
   Kang In-duck, Seoul's national unification minister, announced
Tuesday a new step South Korea will take to help separated families  &MD; 
amending national security laws so people in the South can send small
amounts of money directly to relatives in the North.
<p>
   The sudden flurry of government concern is helping to melt the ice
between the Cold War enemies. In 1970, private groups estimated that 5
million South Koreans had a parent, sibling or child in the North, but
so much time has passed since the isolated state shut its doors to the
world that estimates of how many have immediate family members in the
North now range from 400,000 to 1 million.
<p>
   Millions more have some more distant kin in the North. Cho helped
found a private group that sifts through North Korean newspapers and
other available sources to try to track family members for relatives
here.
<p>
   For decades, Cho tried to find out about his own family. He assumed
when communism took hold that they had moved from the grand home he
grew up in because his father was a well-off landowner. He had no idea
whether they were struggling or happy or dead.
<p>
   ``Being able to see family is a right that everyone in the world
has except here in Korea,'' Cho said. ``It's indescribable the pain,
the tragedy of waiting for so long in the hopes of seeing your own
family.''
<p>
   South Korean President Kim Dae-jung agrees. His willingness to
engage the North since his election in December is a large part of the
reason there is new optimism that families will be able to communicate
with relatives soon, and perhaps even see them.
<p>
   As always, there is skepticism about North Korean intentions.
Several times North Korea said it would allow family visits and then
at the last moment it demanded something that killed the deal. In
1985, 50 relatives from each side visited and held emotional reunions
in both countries. But then, without explanation, the visits were
stopped.
<p>
   Time is running out for old men like Cho, and as they age their
voices are rising.
<p>
   ``What I want to tell the world is that people like me are getting
old and they deserve to see their loved ones before they die,'' Cho
said. ``This is the best opportunity in years. I am too old for many
more disappointments.''
<p>
   As Cho spoke, an 82-year-old man walked in to his group's office in
Seoul, asked for help in finding his family and poured out another
story of a family divided.
<p>
   When the Korean War started in 1950, the man said, he fled to the
South, along with millions of other men who feared being shot,
imprisoned or forced into military service. He left behind his
pregnant wife and 6-year-old son. ``I hoped I would be able to go back
and see them in a week,'' said the man, who asked not to be
identified.
<p>
   But his wife died during the war, apparently from lack of food,
before she delivered their second child. He has received letters from
his son via China and once waited along the Chinese-North Korean
border for 15 days, trying to arrange for his son to swim across the
river from the North to freedom. But at the last moment, the plan
seemed too dangerous.
<p>
   The father, a retired electronics salesman, said he saw a photo of
his son as a grown man  &MD;  he was tall and appeared to weigh only about
100 pounds. He said all he can imagine is that the happy boy of his
memories is now sick, hungry and needing him.
<p>
   ``It is tearing my heart apart,'' he said, as Cho tried to comfort
him. ``I need to see him before I die.''
</TEXT>

<HEADER> 04-14 0574 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-federal-diary
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Mike Causey </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Uncle Sam Wants Computer Experts! (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Not-so-over-the-hill computer experts, rejoice! Uncle
Sam wants  &MD;  and will pay  &MD;  you.
<p>
   To prevent U.S. government computers from suffering the world's
greatest memory loss at the turn of the century, Uncle Sam will take
extraordinary steps to reclaim retired brainpower. The folks are
needed, quick, to help solve the so-called year 2000 problem.
<p>
   Many computers read only the last two digits of a year. Many
experts predict a global computer crash on the last day of this
century, when computers may confuse the year 2000 with the year 1900.
If that happens, some predict it could wipe out, scramble or misplace
trillions of items of data, key records and the like. Many private
firms and banks have already scooped up some of the best retired
programmers and computer scientists, either as regular employees or
consultants. They can draw their retirement pay plus full salary or
consultant fees.
<p>
   But government rules about reemploying federal civilian or military
workers make it financially unattractive for most people to come back
to work for the government. Salaries are generally reduced by the
amount of the retirees' pensions.
<p>
   Many of the nation's top computer experts are former feds or
military personnel. Many of the civilians left in the past couple of
years  &MD;  while the government was downsizing 300,000 jobs  &MD;  in order to
get special $25,000 buyout payments. Now, some of them are needed back
on the job. Immediately.
<p>
   To lure the retirees  &MD;  both military and civilian  &MD;  back into
federal service, the government plans to waive so-called dual
compensation rules that cut into the paychecks of rehired civilian or
military retirees. It also will relax premium pay rules, on a
case-by-case basis, to make working holidays and overtime, on
emergency projects, more worthwhile. The cost to the government would
be minimal, but the financial incentives for the individual retirees
would be tremendous.
<p>
   Under current rules, retired civilian workers who return to work as
``reemployed annuitants'' have their salaries offset by the amount of
their pensions. Many retired military personnel-mostly ex-officers-who
are hired by federal agencies also are subject to the dual
compensation rules, which reduce their civilian salaries. Both
safeguards were put into law to prevent civilian and military agencies
from allowing workers or officers to retire, on fully indexed
pensions, then be rehired at full federal salaries.
<p>
   But the Office of Personnel Management, in response to pleas from
federal agencies trying to avoid any kind of year 2000 crash, has
decided to waive reemployment pay rules in this instance.
<p>
   A special memo to agency heads from OPM Director Janice R. Lachance
says:
<p>
   ``We want to make every effort to give you the human resources
management tools you need to resolve the year 2000 computer conversion
problem.'' To allow agencies to recruit top experts  &MD;  now retired from
government or the military  &MD;  OPM will ``waive dual compensation
reductions, making it easier to reemploy annuitants who have the
programming skills and system knowledge that you may need.'' The order
will allow agencies to pay high-ranking employees, who can't get full
overtime or premium pay, their full hourly rate for work on emergency
projects.
</TEXT>

<BYLINE> By John-Thor Dahlburg </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; N. Ireland Protestant Politicians to Clinton: Stay Home(Londonderry) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   LONDONDERRY, Northern Ireland  &MD;  Some Protestant politicians Tuesday
sent a message  &MD;  loud and clear  &MD;  to President Clinton, who has said
he might visit here to campaign for a ``yes'' vote in this
British-ruled province's May referendum, part of the recently approved
peace process.
<p>
   The message: Stay home.
<p>
   ``I don't think it will be helpful, at this stage, for the
president of the U.S. to become directly involved in a democratic vote
here in Northern Ireland,'' said Reg Empey, chief negotiator for the
largest Protestant party, the Ulster Unionists, at talks that led to a
proposed peace settlement last Friday. ``The people of Northern
Ireland will want to make up their own mind on a very important
question without people from outside the U.K. becoming involved.''
<p>
   A member of Parliament from Free Presbyterian firebrand Rev. Ian
Paisley's smaller hard-line Democratic Unionist Party, which is
scheduled to launch a campaign Wednesday for a ``no'' vote May 22, was
far less diplomatic, accusing Clinton of meddling. ``Northern Ireland
always welcomes world leaders who are coming in friendship, but those
who are coming to interfere in the internal electoral process are
clearly not welcome,'' Peter Robinson said.
<p>
   On Monday, Clinton said he hadn't yet decided whether he will visit
Northern Ireland during next month's referendum campaign and said he
was awaiting advice from the British and Irish governments and
political parties here. ``If it would help, of course I would be
willing to go,'' Clinton said. ``If they said I should go I'd be happy
to go. ... But it would be up to them.''
<p>
   His telephone calls to participants in the 22-month-long
negotiations, including David Trimble, head of the Ulster Unionists,
played a crucial role in clinching last Friday's agreement. It
provides for power-sharing between Protestants, who generally want
Northern Ireland to remain under British rule, and Catholics, most of
whom would like unification with the Irish republic.
<p>
   Trimble, whose party netted 33 percent of the vote last year, more
than any other, has not spoken out against Clinton's proposed visit;
like other politicians, he may be in favor of it. For now, though,
Trimble is trying to quell a mutiny in Ulster Unionist ranks, with at
least half the party's 10 members of Parliament believed to oppose the
peace deal. Last Saturday, the party's executive committee backed
Trimble and the proposed settlement by a 2-1 margin. But both face
another hurdle at a meeting of the party's 700-member council this
weekend.
<p>
   Meanwhile, the Grand Council of the Orange Order  &MD;  a powerful group
with more than 30,000 members that for two centuries has seen itself
as standard-bearer for Protestants in Ireland  &MD;  is to discuss the
proposed settlement Wednesday at a meeting in Belfast, the provincial
capital. Rejection could doom the peace package's chances among
Protestants, 54 percent of Northern Ireland's 1.6 million people.
<p>
   At the other end of the political spectrum, the policy-making
executive of Sinn Fein met in secret Tuesday to consider the
agreement. Starting this Saturday in Dublin, Ireland, Gerry Adams and
other leaders of the party, the political wing of the Irish Republican
Army, will seek backing from the party's council.
<p>
   If support isn't overwhelming, the IRA's 9-month-old cease-fire
could be undermined.
<p>
   From May 15-17, Clinton will be in Birmingham, England, for a
summit of leading industrialized nations. White House spokesman Joe
Lockhart said Monday that adding a stop in Northern Ireland was under
consideration.
<p>
   In other developments, officials in Ireland announced Tuesday that
they had released nine IRA members from a high-security prison, the
first such gesture since the agreement reached last Friday.
</TEXT>

<HEADER> 04-14 0756 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-tobacco
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By John Schwartz </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Minnesota Jurors View Contested RJR Memo &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Jurors in Minnesota's landmark suit against the tobacco industry
Tuesday got their first glimpse of one of the 39,000 hotly contested
company documents unsealed by the U.S. Supreme Court on April 6. The
1969 R.J. Reynolds memorandum discusses removing or declaring
``invalidated'' scientific research findings about tobacco that could
prove damaging to the company in court.
<p>
   The brief document, written by RJR scientist Murray Senkus to Max
Crohn of the company's legal department, states, ``We do not foresee
any difficulty in the event a decision is reached to remove certain
reports from research files. Once it becomes clear that such action is
necessary for the successful defense of our present and future suits,
we will promptly remove all such reports from our files.''
<p>
   The memo does not detail exactly what kinds of reports were being
considered for removal.
<p>
   The memo goes on to describe seven main reasons that ``about 15
reports each year'' are ``invalidated,'' including confidentiality,
discoveries of inaccuracies or ``erroneous interpretation of data.''
As for the reports ``which you are recommending to be invalidated,''
Senkus suggested that ``we can cite misinterpretation of data as
reason for invalidation. A further reason is that many of these are
needless repetitions and are being removed to alleviate overcrowding
of our files. As an alternative to invalidation, we can have the
authors rewrite those sections of the reports which appear
objectionable.''
<p>
   ``It shows very, very clearly and dramatically how these lawyers
and scientists were conspiring together, joining together to destroy
documents and alter research,'' said Minnesota Attorney General Hubert
H. Humphrey III in an interview.
<p>
   R.J. Reynolds attorney Daniel Donahue accused Minnesota's lead
attorney, Michael Ciresi, of ``grandstanding'' with the document
despite the fact that Ciresi should have known from the company's
filings that no documents were actually destroyed, and that the
reports discussed in the memo have been produced in a number of cases,
including Minnesota's. ``To characterize this as a sudden discovery is
not only disingenuous,'' Donahue said in an interview, ``but is, to
put it generously, wrong.''
<p>
   Since the industry delivered the more than 100 boxes of disputed
documents to the offices of the law firm representing the plaintiffs  &MD; 
the state and insurer Blue Cross and Blue Shield of Minnesota  &MD; 
lawyers have been combing through the 200,000 pages for anything that
could strengthen the case against the tobacco industry. The state and
insurer contend the tobacco industry lied to consumers about the
health risks and addictiveness of smoking, and are seeking $1.77
billion in reimbursement for tobacco-related health care costs as well
as changes in the ways the industry markets its products.
<p>
   Humphrey has long called the disputed documents the ``smoking
Howitzers'' in his case. They were drawn from an initial collection of
more than 240,000 internal documents over which the industry had
claimed the legal protections that generally cover communications
between attorneys and their clients and work performed by attorneys
for litigation purposes. Minnesota tried to break the protection by
claiming that the papers are part of a crime or fraud. In March,
Ramsey County District Judge Kenneth Fitzpatrick ordered 39,000 of the
documents at issue released, following the recommendation of the
special master in the case, and the industry exhausted its appeals at
the Supreme Court two weeks ago.
<p>
   Minnesota and Blue Cross filed their suit in 1994, the second of 41
states suing the industry. Tobacco companies settled the first three
cases to come to court, brought by Mississippi, Florida and Texas, for
some $30 billion in anticipation of legislative action on the national
settlement proposal negotiated last year with states and private
attorneys suing the industry. Last week, however, the tobacco
companies announced they were abandoning efforts to mold legislation
based on the settlement and would instead focus their energies on
fighting the leading bill, by Sen. John McCain, R-Ariz.
<p>
   That decision makes the case in Minnesota even more important as a
measure of the value of the legal protections sought by the industry.
<p>
   Humphrey said he hoped the newly released documents would ``have a
dramatic impact in Washington.''
</TEXT>

bc-pulitzer
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Blaine Harden </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; N.Dakota Paper Wins Pulitzer for Flood Coverage (New York) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   NEW YORK  &MD;  The Grand Forks (N.D.) Herald won the Pulitzer Prize for
public service Tuesday for stories and photographs ``that helped hold
its community together'' amid floods and fires that devastated much of
the city, including the newspaper's own printing plant. The New York
Times won three prizes-for criticism, international reporting and beat
reporting-and the Los Angeles Times won two, for breaking news and
feature photography.
<p>
   The public service award, newspaper journalism's most prestigious
recognition, was the first Pulitzer won by the North Dakota daily,
whose circulation averages 37,000. The paper came out during the flood
with help from other Knight-Ridder Inc. newspapers. Mike Jacobs, the
editor, described himself Tuesday as ``sticky and stinky'' from
champagne being poured on his head in the newsroom.
<p>
   ``I think that the Herald in the days immediately after the flood
was the identity of this town,'' he said. ``It was something that came
from Grand Forks and it went to residents of the community who were
widely dispersed. It was a touchstone for them.
<p>
   ``We have a very fine newspaper staff. It was ready in case a big
story ever came along. And, boy, did a big story come along,'' said
Jacobs of the epochal floods and fires, from which Grand Forks is
still recovering and which destroyed the homes of most of the paper's
57 newsroom employees.
<p>
   Bernard L. Stein, editor of the Riverdale Press in the Bronx, a
weekly with a circulation of 14,000, won the editorial writing award
for pieces on New York City politics. Twice before a Pulitzer
finalist, Stein said Tuesday that his honor ``affirms that there are
wonderful writers and reporters working on community newspapers all
over the country. This award recognizes that fact for all of them, not
just for me.''
<p>
   The annual prizes are administered by Columbia University.
<p>
   Among the New York Times awards, Michiko Kakutani won for criticism
for her book reviews and writing on contemporary literature, and the
Times staff won for international reporting for a series about the
effects of drug corruption in Mexico. The four reporters who worked
the series were Sam Dillon and Julia Preston, a husband-and-wife team
in the paper's Mexico City bureau, as well as investigative reporters
Tim Golden and Craig Pyes. While working on the series for more than a
year, the reporters received death threats and were sued by
politicians they had linked to drug trafficking.
<p>
   Linda Greenhouse of the Times won for beat reporting on the Supreme
Court, in a decision that generated frustration and some discontent
among the five nominating judges for beat reporting. The Pulitzer
Prize Board, which usually picks from among three finalists chosen by
a group of judges in each reporting category, moved Greenhouse from
being a finalist in explanatory reporting and awarded her the Pulitzer
in beat reporting.
<p>
   Kay Tucker Addis, editor of the Virginian-Pilot in Norfolk and
chairman of the beat reporting panel, said that ``there was
disappointment (among the judges in her group) that after 2 days of
looking at 160 entries that the board made another choice, but again
that is their prerogative.''
<p>
   The Pulitzer for reporting of breaking news went to the staff of
the Los Angeles Times for its coverage of a botched bank robbery and
police shootout in North Hollywood. Clarence Williams, a photographer
for the newspaper, won the feature photography prize for portraits of
children whose parents are addicted to drugs and alcohol.
<p>
   Gary Cohn and Will Englund of the Baltimore Sun won for
investigative reporting for a series about the dangers to workers and
the environment when discarded ships are dismantled.
<p>
   The award for explanatory reporting went to Paul Salopek of the
Chicago Tribune for his story about the human genome diversity
project, which attempts to chart the genetic relationships among all
human beings.
<p>
   Russell Carollo and Jeff Nesmith of the Dayton Daily News won for
national reporting for stories about flaws and mismanagement in the
military health care system.
<p>
   Mike McAlary of the Daily News won the Pulitzer for commentary for
columns that scooped the New York City press corps and helped trigger
a citywide examination of police brutality. With a hospital room
interview, McAlary broke the story of Abner Louima, a Haitian
immigrant who was badly injured when police allegedly rammed a stick
up his rectum while beating him in the bathroom of a Brooklyn police
station.
<p>
   Thomas French of the St. Petersburg Times won the feature writing
award for his story about a mother and two daughters murdered on a
Florida vacation.
<p>
   Stephen P. Breen of the Asbury Park Press won the award for
editorial cartooning.
<p>
   The spot news photography award went to Martha Rial of the
Pittsburgh Post-Gazette for her portraits of the survivors of ethnic
massacres in the central African countries of Rwanda and Burundi.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-drugs-post </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0791 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-drugs-post
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Rick Weiss </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Reactions to Drugs Make More than 2M Ill Annually &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   More than two million Americans become seriously ill every year
because of toxic reactions to correctly prescribed medicines taken
properly, and 106,000 die from those reactions, a new study concludes.
That surprisingly high number makes drug side effects at least the
sixth, and perhaps even the fourth, most common cause of death in this
country.
<p>
   The analysis, the largest and most complete of its kind, suggests
that one in fifteen hospital patients in the United States can expect
to suffer from a serious reaction to prescription or over-the-counter
medicine, and about five percent of these will die from it.
<p>
   If the findings are accurate, then the number of people dying each
year from drug side effects may be exceeded only by the numbers of
people dying from heart disease, cancer and stroke, and may be greater
than the number dying from lung disease, pneumonia or diabetes.
<p>
   Experts said the study, which appears in Wednesday's issue of the
Journal of the American Medical Association, is stronger than previous
ones because it looks only at cases in which drugs were taken
correctly. Previous hints of similarly high side-effect rates had been
attributed in large part to people getting the wrong medicines or
taking them in the wrong doses.
<p>
   Only one-quarter of the reactions were due to patients being
allergic to the drug in question. In theory, those reactions could be
avoided by more carefully asking people about known allergies. The
rest of the side effects were classified as essentially inevitable,
bound to affect a certain percentage of the population for unknown
reasons.
<p>
   Pharmaceutical manufacturers, drug regulators and the researchers
themselves warned against overreacting to the numbers, noting that the
study made no effort to measure the benefits of the same medicines  &MD; 
an equally important part of the cost-benefit calculation that
determines the overall usefulness of a drug.
<p>
   ``We're not saying, 'Stop taking drugs,''' said Bruce H. Pomeranz,
the University of Toronto neurophysiologist who initiated the study.
For example, he said, blood thinners may cause fatal bleeding in some
but also save countless lives by preventing heart attacks.
<p>
   Pomeranz called for additional research to determine which drugs
are most problematic and which patients are most at risk  &MD;  information
that the current analysis did not try to gather. Meanwhile, he said,
hospitals should set up improved systems for tracking adverse
reactions as they occur, and for reporting them to federal regulators
so medicine labels can be updated and physicians and consumers can be
better informed about the relative risks and benefits of their
medicines.
<p>
   Michael Friedman, acting commissioner of the Food and Drug
Administration, said the agency in recent years has implemented new
systems for preventing, identifying and keeping track of adverse drug
reactions. A nationwide electronic network now allows doctors to
report adverse reactions easily over computer lines. And a growing
number of pharmacies are using an FDA-supported system that
automatically prints out side-effect warnings and other information
for consumers when they pick up their medications.
<p>
   ``The important message is not to be afraid of your medication but
to be respectful of the possibility of side effects,'' Friedman said.
``More people are using medications today than ever before, and for
more serious and extreme conditions than ever before. There are real
benefits these patients achieve with these medications, but every one
has a side effect.''
<p>
   The Pharmaceutical Research and Manufacturers Association, a
Washington-based trade association, yesterday released a brief
statement urging consumers not to take the findings ``out of
context.''
<p>
   ``The public should be confident that the benefits of medicines far
outweigh the risks and should contact their doctor or pharmacist with
any questions,'' the statement said.
<p>
   Alan Holmer, the group's president, said in an interview that side
effects were inevitable given the increased potency and effectiveness
of modern drugs. ``Drugs are powerful substances and affect different
people differently,'' he said. ``They cannot be made completely safe
for all patients in all circumstances.''
<p>
   According to Pomeranz, however, who conducted the study with
Toronto colleagues Jason Lazarou and Paul N. Corey, the problem is
bigger than had been recognized before and is deserving of more
serious attention than has been accorded. ``Nobody really knew what
was going on with this problem, how serious it is, until now,'' he
said.
</TEXT>

bc-latin
