</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Thomas W. Lippman </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Summit in Chile Is Next Stop in Clinton's World Tour &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   President Clinton, racking up air miles in a busy schedule of
international travel, departs Wednesday night for a four-day visit to
Chile, where he will be the guest of President Eduardo Frei and
participate in the second Summit of the Americas with leaders from all
Western Hemisphere nations except Cuba.
<p>
   Senior advisers said his objectives include expanding trade,
marshaling international resources to support education, and cementing
friendly relations with countries such as Chile and Brazil that once
were at odds with Washington over government and economic systems but
that in recent years have joined the Latin American parade toward
democracy and free-market reforms.
<p>
   Like many presidents before him, Clinton is spending more time
abroad in his second term than in his first. Last month he visited six
countries in Africa. After Chile, he is scheduled to go to Germany,
England and probably Northern Ireland in May and to China in June.
<p>
   His international ventures provide some respite from the relentless
media and prosecutorial scrutiny of his private life and past
associations. But his senior advisers said the swing through Africa
and the upcoming Americas summit were scheduled long before anyone in
Washington had heard of Monica Lewinsky, the former White House intern
whose relationship with the president is under investigation by
special prosecutor Kenneth W. Starr.
<p>
   ``The state visit to Chile and the Santiago summit are the
culmination of years-long engagement with the Americas,'' White House
national security adviser Samuel R. ``Sandy'' Berger said at a
pre-summit briefing.
<p>
   Clinton convened the first hemispheric summit in Miami in December
1994, and Berger said the president is committed to forging a new
relationship with Latin American and Caribbean countries based on
their ``quiet revolution,'' the transition to democratic governments
and free-market economies.
<p>
   If investment of presidential time is an accurate gauge of
presidential interest, Clinton can fairly claim to be more involved
with Latin America and the Caribbean than any of his recent
predecessors. As Berger noted, Clinton ``last year went to Mexico,
Central America and the Caribbean in May, and in October went to
Venezuela, Brazil and Argentina.''
<p>
   At the time of the 1994 gathering in Miami, Washington was just
beginning to celebrate a new era in the Americas in which
authoritarian rule, military dictatorship, arms races and controlled
economies were giving way to democracy and economic reform. Large
countries such as Brazil and Argentina were leaving behind the era of
hyperinflation and systematic human rights abuses; Central America was
emerging from the civil conflicts of the Cold War era.
<p>
   As Clinton heads for Chile on Wednesday, his agenda contains few
reminders of those painful years. As Berger and other senior officials
never tire of pointing out, only Cuba has resisted the hemisphere's
democratic wave  &MD;  although democracy appears shaky in Paraguay and
Colombia  &MD;  and only Cuba remains committed to a centrally planned
economy.
<p>
   According to U.S. Trade Representative Charlene Barshefsky, the
result has been a burst of economic opportunity for U.S. business.
Last year, she said, ``our exports to Latin America and the Caribbean
grew more than three times as fast as our exports to the rest of the
world. During the second half of 1997, we exported more to our
hemisphere than to the entirety of the European Union.''
<p>
   Clinton had hoped to go to Santiago armed with ``fast track''
authority from Congress to negotiate the creation of a hemisphere-wide
Free Trade Area of the Americas, as was agreed at the 1994 Miami
summit. Congress balked at giving the president that authority, which
would have enabled him to negotiate an agreement subject to
congressional approval but not amendment, but Barshefsky and other
officials said negotiations will begin in Santiago anyway.
<p>
   ``As important as that political declaration was in 1994,''
Barshefsky said, ``the formal launch of these negotiations following
3 years of preparatory work ensures the realization of the Miami
vision. There will be a free-trade area of the Americas; a
comprehensive launch will be initiated in Santiago along with all that
that implies.''
<p>
   Thomas F. ``Mack'' McLarty, Clinton's chief adviser on the
Americas, said last week that the administration is confident it
eventually will gain fast-track authority but does not need it right
away to begin negotiations.
</TEXT>

bc-iraq-post
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: Foreign, News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By John M. Goshko </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Report Hints of Renewed Tension Over Inspections of Iraqi Sites &QL; 
 &UR; (United Nations) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   UNITED NATIONS  &MD;  A report by U.N. experts says the first inspection
of formerly off-limits Iraqi presidential buildings revealed no sign
of prohibited weapon systems but warns that attempts at further
inspections could encounter Iraqi resistance, U.N. sources said
Tuesday night.
<p>
   An annex to the report, also described by the sources, cites
statements by Iraqi officials that they do not regard the United
Nations' right to search the buildings as open-ended and hints that
Iraq might now take the position that it has discharged its
obligations toward U.N. inspections. If so, that could lead to a
renewal of the recent tensions that led to a crisis in which
Washington threatened to bomb suspect sites in Iraq.
<p>
   These are the main points of the report detailing the findings of
inspections that were carried out at eight presidential sites in
various parts of Iraq between March 26 and April 3, the sources said.
The inspections followed a four-month confrontation over President
Saddam Hussein's refusal to give the United Nations access to the
palaces and subsequent U.S. threats of air and missile strikes against
Iraq.
<p>
   The crisis was defused, at least temporarily, when Secretary
General Kofi Annan went to Baghdad and negotiated with Saddam Hussein
an agreement for U.N. inspectors to search the palaces and other sites
accompanied by diplomats under the direction of Jayantha Dhanapala,
undersecretary general for disarmament affairs. Dhanapala's secret
report was sent to Annan Tuesday. The sources said they tentatively
expect it to be forwarded to the Security Council on Wednesday.
<p>
   Richard Butler, head of the U.N. Special Commission (UNSCOM)
charged with eliminating Iraq's weapons of mass destruction, already
has said that the searches by teams of UNSCOM inspectors and diplomats
turned up no evidence of secret Iraqi nuclear, chemical or biological
weapons programs. That point is spelled out in greater detail in the
report, the sources said.
<p>
   However, the sources added, the report notes that UNSCOM expected
that result since Iraq had several weeks during the standoff with the
United Nations to remove any incriminating material or documents from
the palaces and their subsidiary buildings.
<p>
   Butler and other officials had said their main aim in the first
round of inspections was twofold: to establish the principle that
UNSCOM has the right under its mandate from the Security Council to
search the presidential sites, and to get a baseline idea of the size
and nature of these buildings to aid future inspections. In that
respect, the report says, the initial inspections were regarded as
successful.
<p>
   More ominous, the sources said, is an annex to the report written
by Charles Duelfer, the deputy head of UNSCOM, which describes hints
of a possible future return to obstruction tactics by the Iraqis.
<p>
   Although UNSCOM officials have said the Iraqis generally were
cooperative during the searches, the annex, as described by the
sources, cites several instances when Iraqi officials objected
strongly to the inspectors taking photographs inside the palaces and
surveying them with overhead helicopter flights. The report says the
Iraqis relented in their objections only after the inspectors made
clear they would not budge on these points.
<p>
   In addition, the sources said, the Iraqis indicated at various
times to the inspectors that they understand the agreement with the
United Nations to be of limited duration. Iraqi Deputy Prime Minister
Tariq Aziz has said that his government was permitting the now
completed round of inspections to prove its contention that Iraq no
longer has any prohibited weapons and should be freed of the burden of
U.N. economic sanctions.
<p>
   However, UNSCOM says that it has considerable more work to do
before it is able to piece together the whole story of Iraqi weapons
programs. Accordingly, UNSCOM contends that the agreement between
Saddam Hussein and Anan gives the United Nations the power to keep
conducting inspections for as long as it believes necessary. Annan
also has said that is his interpretation of the agreement.
</TEXT>

<PREAMBLE>
bc-pulitzer-sun - a1700
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: National editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Scott Shane </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Series on Ship Scrapping Hazards Wins Journalism Prize (Baltimore) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   BALTIMORE  &MD;  Gary Cohn and Will Englund, reporters for The Baltimore
Sun, won a Pulitzer Prize in investigative reporting Tuesday for a
series of stories exposing the extraordinary hazards to workers and
the environment caused by the little-known industry that scraps old
ships.
<p>
   Their account of migrant workers maimed and killed in sloppy and
unregulated shipbreaking operations from Baltimore to India prompted
the U.S. Navy to drop a plan to send retired warships overseas for
scrapping. The stories also sparked congressional hearings and
prompted the Defense Department to order a study on how naval vessels
can be scrapped safely.
<p>
   ``We were very impressed with how the Sun reporters had taken a
good local story and pursued it to national and even international
scope,'' said Paul C. Tash, executive editor of the St. Petersburg
Times, chairman of the five-member Pulitzer jury on investigative
reporting. ``Some of the scenes were horrifically gripping. That
speaks to the quality of the storytelling.''
<p>
   The Pulitzer for public service went to The Grand Forks Herald of
North Dakota for its coverage of a flood and fire that devastated the
city  &MD;  and the 37,000-circulation newspaper's own newsroom  &MD;  a year
ago.
<p>
   The flood had already put the Herald's presses underwater when the
fire gutted the newsroom. The paper was put together in a makeshift
facility and run off on borrowed presses in St. Paul, Minn., more than
300 miles away.
<p>
   The New York Times won three Pulitzers, for beat reporting,
international reporting and criticism. The Los Angeles Times won for
breaking news coverage and feature photography.
<p>
   The Pulitzer Prizes, considered journalism's highest honor, are
awarded each year by Columbia University on the recommendation of a
board of veteran editors. The 14 journalism prizes come with $5,000
each, except for the prize for public service, for which a gold medal
is given.
<p>
   Englund, 45, now serving a second tour in The Sun's Moscow bureau,
returned to Baltimore Tuesday to join Cohn, 46, a Baltimore-based
investigative reporter, for a champagne celebration in the newsroom.
They gave much credit to photographer Perry Thorsvik, whose haunting
portraits of workers illustrated the series, and Assistant Managing
Editor Rebecca Corbett, who supervised more than a year of reporting
and edited the series.
<p>
   The series was distributed to newspapers worldwide by the Los
Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   The Sun's 14th Pulitzer can be traced to a brief announcement in
September 1995 of a tour of shipwrecks of Baltimore harbor. Englund
went on the tour and wrote about the wrecks but also made note of a
hulking Navy ship, the Coral Sea, in Baltimore for scrapping.
<p>
   Englund looked into the scrapping of the Coral Sea , where
environmental and safety problems lead to the criminal conviction in
May of the salvager , and discovered that the pattern of oil spills,
asbestos exposure and lax safety standards extended far beyond
Baltimore's harbor.
<p>
   He found that with the Cold War over, the Navy had begun selling
off surplus warships for scrap in an attempt to turn a profit from the
obsolete vessels. But in port after port, the scrapping program was
leaving a trail of injured workers, polluted harbors and complex
lawsuits.
<p>
   In May 1995, Englund was joined by Cohn for an odyssey tracking
shipbreaking operations from Wilmington, N.C. to Brownsville, Texas,
where they coaxed wary Mexican migrant workers into describing
hazardous working conditions.
<p>
   Englund and Cohn interviewed ship brokers in New York and London
and eventually followed the story to Alang, India, where more than
30,000 laborers cut ships apart with torches in hellish conditions and
lived in squalid shacks. They described  &MD;  and Thorsvik photographed  &MD; 
barefoot workers toting tons of steel and makeshift funerals for
workers who did not survive.
<p>
   ``I read the stories weeks ago, but I still remember vividly the
scenes in India,'' said Tash, the Pulitzer juror.
 &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QC; 
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-raines-lew </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0741 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-raines-lew
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Eric Pianin </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; OMB Choice Lauded for Budgetary, Political Savvy (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  In selecting Jack Lew as his new budget director,
President Clinton has turned to a consummate budget professional and
master of legislative detail who once served as chief policy adviser
to the late House Speaker Thomas P. ``Tip'' O'Neill Jr, D-Mass.
<p>
   Although he has operated largely behind the scenes as a special
assistant to the president and more recently as deputy director of the
Office of Management and Budget, Lew was credited with helping launch
Clinton's national service program and providing critical technical
and political advice during the talks that led to last spring's
balanced-budget deal.
<p>
   In the final frenetic negotiations before the White House and
Republican leaders announced their historic agreement May 2, Lew was
the one who scoured the language for political minefields and
unwelcome last-minute changes by the Republicans.
<p>
   ``Jack has tremendous detailed knowledge of the budget process and
individual programs and an excellent knowledge of politics,'' said
Franklin Raines, who resigned Tuesday as OMB director. ``His job was
to make sure that in all of these negotiations everything fit so that
at the end of the day there wasn't any buyer's regret on our part.''
<p>
   Lew, 42, a Harvard graduate who holds a law degree from Georgetown
University, will be the fourth budget director to serve in the Clinton
administration, after Leon E. Panetta, Alice Rivlin and Raines, who
announced yesterday that he is stepping down to become chairman and
chief executive of home mortgage lender Fannie Mae. Lew was acting
director for five months before Raines took over.
<p>
   Although some Republicans believe Lew has a harder political edge
than Raines, he is described as well-regarded on Capitol Hill and
likely to be easily confirmed by the Senate. ``Jack can be a little
more partisan from time to time, but he will do fine,'' said a senior
Senate GOP aide.
<p>
   Lew's appointment is similar to that of CIA Director George Tenet,
another relatively young former Hill staff member and skilled
bureaucratic operator who moved up to head the agency last summer.
<p>
   ``The president didn't blink one second in choosing Jack,'' said
Gene Sperling, chairman of the president's National Economic Council.
``Through both budget wars and bipartisan budget negotiations, he's
earned the trust of the top budget experts throughout Washington on
both sides of the aisle.''
<p>
   Lew has been described by colleagues and associates as a smart,
somewhat nerdy technocrat and legislative craftsman with a passion for
the federal food stamp program and other social welfare policies that
can better the lives of the poor. A Jew whose father immigrated from
Poland, Lew considers community cohesion to be a central part of
religious faith, according to author Steven Waldman, who wrote about
Lew's role in the passage of Clinton's national service legislation in
1993. Lew would regularly take his two children to nursing homes to
visit seniors who had been congregants in his synagogue.
<p>
   Robert Greenstein, head of the liberal-leaning Center on Budget and
Policy Priorities, said that while Lew has ``long cared deeply about
the incidence of poverty in the country,'' he has also been ``quite
tough-minded at OMB in working at various poverty programs and trying
to distinguish those that are more effective from those that are less
effective.''
<p>
   ``He's not one of those who would say everything done in the name
of poverty is untouchable,'' Greenstein added.
<p>
   ``He was a total true believer in social policy as (providing)
real-life answers to people's problems,'' recalled Christopher
Matthews, a newspaper columnist and television commentator who served
as O'Neill's press secretary. ``He's a very humble, honest guy.''
<p>
   As O'Neill's domestic policy adviser starting in 1979, Lew was
involved throughout the speaker's often stormy relations with the
Reagan administration. He emerged as an honest broker during the
intense talks that led to passage of reforms of the Social Security
program in 1983. As the talks reached a critical point, Lew was asked
by congressional leaders to call Reagan and then-Ways and Means
Committee Chairman Dan Rostenkowski, D-Ill., at a golf tournament to
simultaneously brief them on the emerging deal.
</TEXT>

bc-mascara
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL;

<BYLINE> By Wendy Melillo </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Making Up Lost Time Lands Va. Driver in Court &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Valerie Nordstrom was late to work and decided to apply her mascara
as she was driving on Interstate 95 during rush hour.
<p>
   Although she wasn't speeding or weaving in and out of lanes, a
Virginia State Police trooper thought she was endangering the lives of
other motorists and gave her a ticket for reckless driving, a
misdemeanor that carries a penalty of up to a year in jail and a
$2,500 fine.
<p>
   Tuesday, a Fairfax County, Va., judge found Nordstrom guilty of the
lesser offense of improper driving and fined her $25.
<p>
   ``Put your makeup on at home,'' General District Court Judge
Jonathan C. Thacher told the 40-year-old Prince William County, Va.,
woman.
<p>
   But Nordstrom was upset by the judge's decision, which would put
three points on her driving record, and said she will appeal.
<p>
   ``Putting on makeup is not any different from dialing a number on
your cell phone, lighting a cigarette, changing radio stations or
drinking a cup of coffee,'' Nordstrom said. ``I don't understand why
it is construed as reckless.''
<p>
   Her attorney, Mark Yeager, asked Thacher to dismiss the case
because there was no evidence that Nordstrom was engaging in any type
of driving behavior that could be called reckless. Virginia law
defines reckless driving as a wanton or willful disregard for the
safety of others.
<p>
   Trooper Robert Thomas testified that Nordstrom was headed north on
I-95, just north of Route 1, shortly before 9 a.m. on Feb. 23 when he
noticed her holding the steering wheel in her left hand and putting on
mascara with her right hand while looking in the rearview mirror.
<p>
   Thomas said that it had been raining and that there was heavy
traffic on the road that morning. He said he watched her put on
mascara for 1 miles before he pulled her over. He said Nordstrom was
``stretched forward'' as she looked in the mirror.
<p>
   ``I asked her why she was putting on mascara in this nasty weather
with heavy traffic, and she said she was running late for work,''
Thomas said.
<p>
   Nordstrom said she will be in Fairfax Circuit Court on June 11 to
argue her case.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-imf </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1712 04-14 0665 </HEADER>

bc-imf - a1712
 &UR; (ATTN: News, Financial editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL;

<BYLINE> By Art Pine </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Administration Proposes Modest IMF Reforms (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  The Clinton administration Tuesday proposed modest
changes in the international financial system designed to help prevent
the kind of major economic turmoil that has enveloped several Asian
countries over the past several months.
<p>
   The package, outlined in a speech by U.S. Treasury Secretary Robert
E. Rubin, includes a few measures aimed at forcing big international
banks and other investors to take more of a loss in cases where they
have made shaky loans  &MD;  a politically charged issue in Congress.
<p>
   The proposals, all incremental and some rather technical in nature,
are expected to be discussed by the world's finance ministers in
connection with this week's annual spring meeting of the 182-country
International Monetary Fund here.
<p>
   On Wednesday, Rubin and his counterparts from the United States'
six major trading partners will discuss the issue, and Thursday the
plan will go to a special group of 22 finance ministers charged with
hammering out a consensus on key elements.
<p>
   The U.S. treasury secretary also called on Congress to approve an
administration proposal to expand the United States' line of credit to
the IMF by $18 billion to help replenish some of the organization's
lending resources in case there is another major financial crisis.
<p>
   Although the probability of such a crisis is low, Rubin said, every
day the IMF continues to be underfinanced ``is another day of
vulnerability for American workers, farmers and businesses.'' He said
Congress ``should act, and ... act now.''
<p>
   In general, the package Rubin outlined centers on taking steps to
bolster the existing financial system by encouraging countries  &MD; 
particularly so-called emerging-market nations such as those in Asia  &MD; 
to strengthen their own regulation of domestic banks and finance.
<p>
   It includes these major elements:
<p>
    &MD; Providing more economic and financial data to the public about
what is going on in developing countries' economies so investors would
have ample warning about potential risks.
<p>
    &MD; Strengthening the ability of these countries to regulate their
financial systems by adopting global standards for banking supervision
and having the IMF monitor each country's effort to carry them out.
<p>
    &MD; Changing present procedures to eliminate the built-in protection
now given to banks that make bad loans. The IMF no longer would be
asked to help ensure that borrowers repay banks on time. Instead,
banks would be prodded to negotiate their own debt-rescheduling deals.
<p>
   Rubin also suggested that the major industrial nations help enforce
the proposed changes by refusing to allow in banks from
emerging-market countries unless their home countries comply with
accepted international regulatory practices.
<p>
   The U.S. plan conspicuously avoided any proposals that would seek
to control or rein in unstable markets  &MD;  reflecting Washington's
belief that the Asian crisis was caused more by the region's financial
practices than by any flaws in international markets themselves.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   Rubin and other officials have suggested that the Asian crisis was
caused by a combination of secretiveness in Asian financial dealings,
poor regulation and so-called ``crony capitalism,'' which eschews
Western-style arm's-length transactions.
<p>
   International officials said it was not clear yet whether the other
finance ministers would accept Rubin's proposals as is or make major
substantive changes. Some finance ministers from other countries are
expected to propose additional measures.
<p>
   On another issue, Rubin again warned Japan that it must do more to
stimulate its domestic economy, but cautioned that it may be
``premature'' to criticize Tokyo's latest economic package as
inadequate  &MD;  at least until officials see the details of the Japanese
plan.
</TEXT>

bc-economy-times - a1713
 &UR; (ATTN: National, Financial editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Art Pine </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Consumer Price Index Shows Inflation Continues Under Control (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Americans received a double dose of good news on the
inflation front Tuesday: The government reported that consumer prices
remained unchanged in March, and there were signs that the economy may
no longer be in danger of overheating.
<p>
   The Labor Department's monthly consumer price index was flat last
month after edging up 0.1 percent in February and holding steady in
January. Over the first three months of the year, the combined rise in
consumer prices has held to an annual rate of 0.2 percent.
<p>
   At the same time, the Commerce Department reported that retail
sales levels across the United States edged down by 0.1 percent in
March, reflecting dampened demand for a wide range of products, from
autos and building materials to furniture.
<p>
   The new figures came as a relief to the financial markets, which
have been fearful that the Federal Reserve Board, worried that
inflation could rebound, would raise interest rates to help slow down
the economy. Stock prices rose after the price index was reported.
<p>
   Bruce Steinberg, chief economist of Merrill Lynch &AMP; Co., said the
reports showed ``an utter absence of inflation,'' and predicted a
significant slowdown  &MD;  both in consumer spending and in overall
economic growth  &MD;  in the April-June quarter.
<p>
   The halt in consumer prices came partly as a result of another
sharp drop in energy prices, which have been plummeting as a result of
falling worldwide demand. Energy prices plunged 1.3 percent in March,
with gasoline prices falling by 3.5 percent. Grocery prices dipped by
0.1 percent in March.
<p>
   At the same time, the department said its so-called core rate of
inflation  &MD;  excluding food and energy prices, which often are volatile
 &MD;  edged up by only 0.1 percent in March, following increases of 0.3
percent in February and 0.2 percent in January.
<p>
   Analysts have offered a variety of theories to explain the current
good fortune on the inflation front, from recent increases in
productivity  &MD;  output per hour of work  &MD;  to advances in technology and
competition from foreign imports.
<p>
   Nevertheless, forecasters say the economy still is a long way from
a period of widespread ``deflation,'' which some doomsayers fear might
push the world into a major recession. For that to happen would take a
generalized decline in wages and prices.
<p>
   The slight falloff in retail sales levels for March is the latest
of several recent signs that the economy might be slowing. The
government reported earlier this month that factory orders fell 0.9
percent in February. And job growth actually declined slightly in
March.
<p>
   Economists have been predicting that the U.S. economy would slow
somewhat as a result of the Asian financial crisis, but so far the
impact has been more modest than expected.
<p>
   The hold down over the past three months has stemmed largely from
energy prices, which have plunged during the period at an annual rate
of 21.1 percent. Transportation prices also dropped sharply, falling
at a 5.2 percent pace, while apparel prices declined at a 2.1 percent
annual rate.
</TEXT>

bc-outage
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Mike Mills </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; AT&AMP;T Data Network Up and Running Again &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   AT&AMP;T Tuesday fixed its broken data-transmission network, after 24
hours of failure that crippled much of the nation's credit card
authorization system and thousands of bank machines and computer
terminals.
<p>
   AT&AMP;T Corp. Chairman C. Michael Armstrong issued an apology to
thousands of customers who suffered business losses, and said the
company was working hard to ``identify, isolate and fix'' what
analysts are calling the largest data-networking failure in history.
<p>
   The outage began Monday afternoon on AT&AMP;T's ``frame relay'' system,
which businesses and governments use to send computerized information
to far-flung offices, suppliers, banks and customers.
<p>
   Engineers working through the night narrowed the cause of the
system's crash to two of the 145 electronic switching stations in the
network. They managed to get the system working again, but remained
uncertain on what caused the crash.
<p>
   ``The nature of frame-relay is such that an outage at a couple of
switches can spread like a contagion everywhere else,'' said Melanie
Posey, a senior analyst with International Data Corp. in New York.
``It just shows you that no matter how great and wonderful a certain
technology is, you have to have a backup plan.''
<p>
   The shutdown affected daily data transactions that most people take
for granted. Retailers including Home Depot and Radio Shack lost the
ability to approve credit card sales, and many could not transmit bulk
sales and payroll information or perform inventory control.
<p>
   In most cases, backup systems kicked in, but at greatly reduced
efficiency. ``We were affected by it, but it was a minor
distraction,'' said Carolyn Brookter, spokeswoman for the Target
retail chain.
<p>
   For others, however, the trouble was more serious: One Northern
Virginia defense contractor was out of touch with 700 employees in
four cities across the nation, according to Timothy Jasionowski, a
Rockville consultant to the company. ``They were completely isolated
from each other for 20 hours, unable to use the Internet, send e-mail,
or communicate back to the corporation,'' he said.
<p>
   Hardest hit among credit card companies was MasterCard
International Inc., which recently gave up private data lines in favor
of AT&AMP;T's less-expensive frame relay service.
<p>
   ``It did cause some problems for us to get authorizations,'' said
spokesman Edward Dixon. But he said that ``obviously MasterCard has
lots of redundancies built into its network, and all of that kicked
in.''
<p>
   Visa USA Inc. still uses its own private lines for its main
network, though Visa spokesman David Sandor said merchants and banks
that connect to the Visa network using AT&AMP;T's service could not get
through.
<p>
   The shutdown was good news, of sorts, for John J. McDonnell Jr.,
president and chairman of Transaction Network Services Inc. in Reston,
Va., which transmits roughly half of the nation's dial-up retail
credit card authorizations. He said business shot up 25 percent
Tuesday, because merchants were making repeated attempts to complete
credit card approvals. The shutdown shifted much of the credit risk
onto retailers and their banks, McDonald said. Faced with losing a
big-ticket sale, he said, many merchants will approve a charge without
an authorization.
<p>
   Most federal government agencies also were affected. AT&AMP;T and
Sprint Corp. evenly divide the government's telecommunications
contract, called FTS 2000. Medicaid claims, defense contracting, State
Department communications and the Federal Communications Commission
all experienced data slowdowns or shutdowns.
<p>
   AT&AMP;T said it would not charge customers until it has clearly
identified the problem and fixed it.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-tax-snitch </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1720 04-14 1371 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-tax-snitch - a1720
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Ralph Vartabedian </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Paid Informants Are Controversial Part of IRS Arsenal (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Americans voluntarily hand over most of the $1.3
trillion owed to the Internal Revenue Service each year, but a tiny
fraction of tax collections depends on an obscure and increasingly
controversial IRS program of using paid informants.
<p>
   Motivated by a combination of greed and revenge, informants are
typically business associates, employees, acquaintances, neighbors or
former spouses of tax cheats. Many experts say the program is one of
the most unseemly parts of the U.S. tax system.
<p>
   However, IRS officials say they exercise great care in handling the
informants, weeding spurious allegations, and that the rewards play an
important role in the nation's tax enforcement system.
<p>
   The IRS pays the informants up to 15 percent of the taxes it
recovers from their tips  &MD;  up to a maximum of $2 million  &MD;  though the
vast majority of informants end up empty handed.
<p>
   After a series of recent congressional disclosures about widespread
taxpayer abuses, watchdog groups are growing concerned about the
ethics of the agency's informant reward program.
<p>
   ``We should refocus our efforts on good citizenry, not bribing
people to answer questions,'' said John Berthoud, president of the
nonpartisan National Taxpayers Union, who called on the IRS to end the
program in an interview with the Los Angeles Times.
<p>
   The program, authorized by Congress, has been sharply criticized by
individuals who say they were victimized by bogus allegations, and
even by informants, who say the IRS has stiffed them on their rewards.
<p>
   The Senate Finance Committee, which has been broadly investigating
IRS abuses over the past year, is expected to unveil new evidence
later this month that taxpayers have been devastated by aggressive IRS
investigations based on phony information from snitches.
<p>
   Tax attorneys and accountants generally decry the informant reward
system, asserting that the government is on thin ice in offering money
to taxpayers to turn each other in. They argue that a cornerstone of
the U.S. tax system is the protection of taxpayer privacy, and that
the IRS is wrong to encourage people to breach confidential business
or family relationships. In one case, a St. Louis tax accountant
informed on his own client.
<p>
   ``It smacks of communism, turn in your parents if you catch them
cheating,'' said San Francisco tax attorney Frederick Daily, author of
the book ``Stand Up to the IRS.''
<p>
   Bruce Hockman, a top Los Angeles tax attorney whose clientele
includes the rich and famous, refuses to help clients snitch to the
IRS. ``I have had people come in and ask me to take them downtown to
IRS district headquarters,'' Hockman said. ``I say no way. The Nazis
did it, turn people in. It is unseemly.''
<p>
   Tipsters are one of the important parts of the IRS tool box for
enforcing tax compliance, says Thomas J. Smith, assistant IRS
commissioner for examination and chief of the agency's informant
reward program.
<p>
   IRS figures for 1996, the last year for which data is available,
show that 9,430 Americans sought rewards. Of those, the IRS acted on
just 650  &MD;  meaning that 93 percent of the tips ended up in the IRS
garbage can. The IRS paid out $3.5 million in rewards and recovered
$103 million in taxes.
<p>
   ``If you look at the last three years, we have had 2,000 cases
closed, resulting in taxes of $797 million,'' Smith said. ``So, in
terms of dollars, most people would judge that as reasonably
significant. It does supply a very useful source of information for
us.''
<p>
   The IRS has a national informant hot line, (800) 829-0433, though
many informants walk in or call in to the IRS's 33 district offices or
10 regional service centers, Smith said.
<p>
   With little fanfare and with no explanation, the IRS last year
decided to boost the maximum allowable award to $2 million from
$100,000. It also set a minimum reward of $100, eliminating a lot of
penny ante payments.
<p>
   In 1996, the agency's largest award was a jackpot-size $1.06
million. (The agency does not disclose who gets the awards or what
cases they involve.) The agency's smallest was just $18  &MD;  less than
the typical reward advertised in newspapers for lost dogs.
<p>
   Under the new guidelines, rewards range from 1 percent to 15
percent of the tax recovered, depending on the assistance provided by
the informer. But all awards are at the ``discretion'' of IRS
officials, who make their decisions behind closed doors. Of course,
the rewards are taxable income.
<p>
   The IRS takes a low-key approach, not seeking to send the message
that the federal government is actively recruiting paid stool pigeons.
The agency does not make Form 211, which informants must fill out to
claim a reward, widely available. It isn't even kept in the IRS
national headquarters lobby, where the agency has almost every form on
display.
<p>
   Asked if the IRS encourages Americans to inform on others, Smith
said he could offer no advice and suggested that individuals do what
they feel is right. But former IRS officials are more blunt.
<p>
   ``Informants rewards are pretty distasteful to everybody except the
person who gets one,'' said Phillip Brand, a tax expert at KPMG Peat
Marwick LLP and former IRS chief of compliance. ``People have a
different feeling about informing when they do it as good citizens.''
<p>
   Another problem with paying for information is that the IRS gets a
lot of garbage information. Brand recalled a tipster once sought a
reward for the disclosure that the secretary of state was dealing
drugs to Queen Elizabeth and not reporting the sales on his taxes.
<p>
   But weak allegations are less humorous when the IRS pursues them
against innocent taxpayers. That apparently happened to John
Colaprette of Virginia Beach, Va., whose home and two restaurants were
raided in 1994 by armed IRS agents after his bookkeeper, Deborah A.
Shofner, made phony allegations.
<p>
   The bookkeeper was later arrested and charged with stealing from
Colaprette's restaurant, The Jewish Mother. She was sentenced to six
years and 11 months in Virginia.
<p>
   ``This case was investigated for just 1 days before they obtained
a search warrant, which was then executed 12 hours later,'' said
Colaprette, who is expected to testify later this month before the
Senate Finance Committee's hearings on IRS abuses.
<p>
   Although the committee is saying little about its planned hearings,
it is expected they will focus on the IRS's criminal investigation
division, which handles most of the paid informants and conducts a
wide range of undercover operations.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   While it isn't surprising that the targets of allegations feel
abused, informants also are often frustrated over how the agency
treats their claims.
<p>
   Mary Case, of Los Angeles, tipped the IRS in 1985 to Stanley D.
Hexom, a San Jose, Calif., real estate broker later accused of
swindling millions of dollars from elderly California investors in
fraudulent real estate deals. She has never received a reward from the
IRS, but neither has the agency closed her case.
<p>
   Each time she checks, the agency tells her it is still working on
the matter.
<p>
   As Hexom's bookkeeper, Case provided IRS agents boxes of evidence,
including copies of doctored tax returns and locations of bank
accounts, as well as testifying to a federal grand jury.
<p>
   Under IRS guidelines, an informant that provides such specific
information is supposed to get 15 percent of the back taxes. But a big
caveat is that the IRS has to actually collect the back taxes. So, if
the agency comes up empty handed, so does the informant.
<p>
   There is no doubt that the IRS went after Hexom, who was convicted
on two counts of bank fraud and one count of preparing a false tax
return. IRS agents tried to collect from Hexom's wife, though she may
have escaped assessment by claiming she was an innocent spouse, said
Richard Blos, Hexom's attorney in San Jose.
<p>
   Hexom was released from prison in 1993 and is currently living in
the Phoenix area. He could not be reached for comment.
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>

bc-japan - a1725
 &UR; (ATTN: Foreign, Financial editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Valerie Reitman </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Japanese Companies Suffer Worst Bankruptcy Year Since WWII (Tokyo) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   TOKYO  &MD;  The latest fiscal year stacked up as the worst for
corporate bankruptcies in Japan since World War II, with the slump in
the world's second-largest economy claiming bigger companies with
bigger debt loads, a leading credit agency said Tuesday.
<p>
   The report could generate fresh concerns that Japan's economic
crisis is worse than projected, especially since the report's figures
exclude two of the largest failures, Yamaichi Securities Co. and
Hokkaido Takushoku Bank, which collapsed under an avalanche of bad
loans.
<p>
   It also could exacerbate concerns among the industrialized nations
gathering at an economic summit Wednesday in Washington that Japan
needs to do more to stimulate its limping economy beyond the $124
billion stimulus package unveiled last week by Prime Minister Ryutaro
Hashimoto.
<p>
   Because of a withering credit crunch and shrinking domestic
spending, the number of Japanese businesses that declared bankruptcy
in the year ending March 31 surged 17 percent, to 17,439, according to
Teikoku Data Bank, which compiled the data. That was the highest
figure in 12 years and represents about 1.6 percent of Japan's
registered businesses.
<p>
   The amount of debt the new bankruptcy-filers had amassed surged an
eye-popping 65 percent to a postwar record 15.12 trillion yen, the
agency said, or roughly $116 billion at today's exchange rates.
<p>
   ``It's dire,'' said Gary Evans, a strategist at HSBC Securities
Ltd. in Tokyo. ``The banks have stopped lending and supporting small
companies, and the economy's in terrible shape.'' Nevertheless, he
noted that some of those companies should have been closed long ago
and had been propped up by banks trying to beef up loan portfolios.
<p>
   But now banks are tightening the loan spigot amid growing
competition, tighter regulatory requirements and a mountain of
nonperforming loans.
<p>
   About 380 companies blamed their bankruptcies on inability to
obtain capital, including the unidentified president of a medical
information-processing business interviewed by public television
station NHK on Tuesday. The bank demanded he repay a 55 million yen
loan before being furnished with fresh credit to produce new products,
he said. He paid back the money, but the bank still refused to honor
its end of the bargain and furnish new loans, he said.
<p>
   In the television report, International Trade and Industry Minister
Mitsuo Horiuchi promised that the government will appropriate more
loans for small businesses as part of the new stimulus package.
Details of the package are to be unveiled at the end of the month.
<p>
   As for the two companies excluded from the bankruptcy list,
Yamaichi ``went out of business voluntarily,'' explained Hiroyuki
Shimizu, a data analyst at Teikoku. Although he acknowledged that
Hokkaido Takushoku is bankrupt for all practical purposes, it isn't
technically ``liquidated'' because it is still paying back money to
its depositors, Shimizu said.
<p>
   Nevertheless, the fallout from Hokkaido Takushoku is contributing
to the mounting failures of other companies that were either owned or
dependent on the bank for business, the analyst said.
<p>
   Bankruptcies have been escalating rapidly since last fall, Shimizu
said, with no relief in sight. Among the most vulnerable companies are
construction firms, which are expected to lay off about 690,000
workers, or about 10 percent of their work force, by 2000.
</TEXT>

<BYLINE> By Mark Z. Barabak </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Poll: Californians Up About Economy, State, Clinton (Los Angeles) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   LOS ANGELES  &MD;  Californians are feeling better about the state's
economy and overall direction than they have in years, and their high
spirits are benefiting lawmakers from President Clinton to state
legislators, according to a new Los Angeles Times poll.
<p>
   Despite worries about education and crime  &MD;  which top the list of
Californians' concerns  &MD;  the overall mood is upbeat, with residents of
every age and political stripe sharing the positive outlook.
<p>
   For the first time in the 1990s  &MD;  a decade wracked by rioting,
recession and a series of epic natural disasters  &MD;  a majority of
Californians, 52 percent, believe the state is moving in the right
direction. The percentage is up from 47 percent in a Times poll at the
end of last year  &MD;  and from 12 percent in September 1992 in the throes
of the recession.
<p>
   On the economic front, two-thirds of Californians describe the
state's economy as ``robust''  &MD;  up from 55 percent six months ago.
<p>
   ``While people worry about the state of public education and the
crime rate, a powerful economy trumps those concerns,'' said Sharon
Pinkerton, associate director of the Times poll.
<p>
   The poll surveyed 1,409 adults statewide from April 4-9. The margin
of sampling error is plus or minus 3 percentage points.
<p>
   Political incumbents both far and near are reaping the benefits of
the state's economic whirlwind.
<p>
   Californians give Clinton a 72 percent job approval rating, up 11
percent from a Times poll last October, despite a steady stream of
sexual misconduct allegations. The president's ratings are high among
Californians of all ages, incomes and educational levels.
<p>
   Even about half the registered Republicans, 49 percent, give the
president high job approval ratings, along with 67 percent of
registered independents and 90 percent of his fellow registered
Democrats.
<p>
   Misconduct allegations aside, ``As the Dow Jones rises, so do
Clinton's approval ratings,'' Pinkerton quipped.
<p>
   Republican Gov. Pete Wilson does not fare nearly as well as the
president. Even so, his approval rating has climbed from his nadir of
28 percent in October 1992. Currently, Californians approve of the
governor's job performance 47 percent to 43 percent, virtually
unchanged from last October.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-postal </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0655 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-postal
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Bill McAllister </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Postal Service's Future Looks Good, Runyon Says (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Retiring Postmaster General Marvin T. Runyon painted a
rosy future for his federal agency Tuesday, predicting that the U.S.
Postal Service would survive the onslaughts of e-mail and faxes to
become ``the shipper of choice to America's homes'' by 2020.
<p>
   ``And until there is a way to beam things from Point A to Point B,
like they do in `Star Trek,' the nation will continue to need someone
to do the legwork,'' Runyon told the National Press Club in a farewell
address. ``More and more, we will be the one doing the job.''
<p>
   Runyon offered his optimistic view of what has become the
government's largest civilian employer three hours after he was
sharply criticized by one of his biggest competitors. James P. Kelly,
chairman and chief executive of United Parcel Service, accused Runyon
of running ``an anti-competitive, anti-free enterprise, government
bureaucracy that wouldn't last one day in the free and open market of
real competition.''
<p>
   Also speaking at the Press Club, Kelly accused the agency of using
its first-class mail monopoly to subsidize an international package
service that directly competes with UPS and other private shippers.
Kelly also blasted the agency's request for a 33-cent stamp  &MD;  a
one-cent increase  &MD;  as ``totally unnecessary'' and appealed to
Congress to enact legislation to curb the agency's powers.
<p>
   The UPS chief executive's harsh words reflected the increasingly
competitive stance Runyon has brought to the Postal Service in his 5
years as head of what was once an annual money-losing operation. With
the agency aiming at a record fourth year of billion dollar-plus
profits, postal officials are attacking UPS and other competitors by
name in ads. And they lambasted UPS when it was struck by its drivers
last summer.
<p>
   ``We must be doing something right,'' Runyon joked when he arrived
at the Press Club. Once at the lectern, the 73-year-old postal
executive who will retire next month rejected Kelly's criticism.
<p>
   ``That's not true,'' he said of UPS's charges that the agency uses
profits from first-class mail to subsidize less-profitable forms of
mail. The postmaster general said Kelly had mixed ``apples and
oranges'' to complain that the agency had an unfair price advantage
for its international mail products.
<p>
   Runyon also took a few subtle shots at UPS, saying the shipper had
``a monopoly on the parcel business'' and adding that the ``power of
PAC dollars'' would not determine his agency's future. UPS is a major
political donor to members of Congress, and postal officials have
complained that gives the company an unfair lobbying advantage.
<p>
   The postmaster general said that by 2020  &MD;  50 years after Congress
enacted legislation that created the Postal Service as an independent
agency  &MD;  technology would make the current monopoly on first-class
letters out of date. ``By the year 2020, there will be so many ways to
communicate, advertise and ship merchandise, the monopoly will simply
be irrelevant,'' Runyon said.
<p>
   Even so, he maintained that for now, the agency needs to maintain
its monopoly to support universal mail service. And he denounced
legislation that Kelly praised, a measure being pushed by Rep. John M.
McHugh, R-N.Y., chairman of the House Postal Service subcommittee.
``It falls short,'' Runyon said. ``It doesn't reinvent government, as
much as it regulates it.''
<p>
   Shippers such as UPS and Federal Express have expressed support for
the legislation because it would have the Postal Service place its
nonmonopoly products, such as its two-day Priority Mail, under control
of a separate profit-making organization. The shippers say this would
create a level playing field, but postal officials said it would
create an administrative and accounting nightmare.
</TEXT>

<PREAMBLE>
bc-conkling-obit - a1753
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Myrna Oliver </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Recording Executive James B. Conkling, 83, Dies &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   James B. Conkling, former director of the Voice of America and
recording executive who helped create the Grammy awards in music, has
died. He was 83.
<p>
   Conkling, who was former president of Columbia Records and founding
president of Warner Bros. Records, died Sunday at Sutter Oaks
Alzheimer's Hospital in Sacramento, Calif. His family said he had
suffered from the disease for several years.
<p>
   President Reagan appointed Conkling to head the Voice of America in
June 1981. But he resigned only nine months later in frustration with
``the way government activities work.'' He had been under pressure
from the right to make the federal agency a more active propaganda
machine and was also buffeted by criticism from liberal elements.
<p>
   Conkling had long before established himself in recording history.
A jazz trumpeter, he started working for then-new Capitol Records in
1944 fresh out of Navy service in World War II. His first job was to
produce a Stan Kenton album, and he went on to work with such artists
as Nat King Cole, Jackie Gleason, Mel Torme, Bozo the Clown, Bob Hope
and Mel Blanc. He was Capitol's first vice president.
<p>
   In 1951, Conkling became president of Columbia Records, where he
initiated the Columbia Record Club (now Columbia House) and helped
introduce long-playing, or LP records. He also helped organize and
served as president of the Record Industry Association of America.
<p>
   In 1957, he joined four other record executives to found the
National Academy of Recording Arts &AMP; Sciences, which sponsors the
Grammy awards. Conkling served as the group's founding chairman and
produced the first Grammy television programs. In 1995, the Grammy's
MusiCares Division honored him with a lifetime achievement award and
staged a fund-raising dinner for Alzheimer's research.
<p>
   Asked by Jack Warner to create Warner Bros. Records in 1958,
Conkling served as the company's first president and signed such
recording artists as Bob Newhart, the Everly Brothers, Peter, Paul and
Mary, John Raitt and Connie Stevens.
<p>
   Always considered a recording industry ``wunderkind,'' Conkling
retired at age 46 to devote his time to charity and public causes. He
headed the Nat King Cole Cancer Foundation, helped set up a recording
industry in China, was active in the National Association for the
Advancement of Colored People and joined the Church of Latter-day
Saints, where he served on the board of directors for its broadcast
arm, Bonneville International Corp.
<p>
   Conkling is survived by his wife of 55 years, Donna King Conkling;
one son; four daughters ; a brother; three sisters; 23 grandchildren;
and two great grandchildren.
</TEXT>

bc-fugitive - a1800

</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Sebastian Rotella and Tracy Wilkinson </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Failure to Arrest War Crimes Suspect Raises Concern (Buenos Aires) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   BUENOS AIRES, Argentina  &MD;  While Argentine and Croatian authorities
pledged Tuesday to arrest and extradite to Croatia the commander of a
World War II concentration camp living in Argentina, a high-ranking
Argentine official expressed frustration that the suspected war
criminal has not been arrested more than a week after his presence
became public.
<p>
   As Croatian officials declared they would file formal extradition
papers shortly and two Argentine judges continued investigating, the
whereabouts and legal status of Dinko Sakic were uncertain.
<p>
   The 76-year-old Sakic admits to having commanded a concentration
camp in Jasenovac, the ``Auschwitz of the Balkans,'' where tens of
thousands of prisoners were killed in death and labor camps between
1941 and 1945. But he claimed in a television interview aired last
week that no one was killed in the camp during his two years as
commander.
<p>
   ``I am very concerned  &MD;  it would be a disaster if he escapes,''
Victor Ramos, head of the Interior Ministry's Institute Against
Discrimination, Xenophobia and Racism, said in an interview. ``I
believe the justice system is moving too slowly. I asked the courts to
have him arrested immediately. We are not talking about a highway
robber, we are talking about the chief of a concentration camp. A real
butcher.''
<p>
   Acting under orders of President Carlos Menem, Ramos said he filed
charges requesting Sakic's arrest April 6, the day after Argentine
television broadcast an interview in which Sakic proclaimed his
innocence.
<p>
   Ramos said it was the first time Sakic's presence came to the
attention of his institute, formed six months ago to fight hate crimes
and hunt war criminals.
<p>
   Sakic's capture apparently has been slowed by the Easter holiday
and legal bureaucracy; the case has been assigned to federal and
provincial courts. Lacking an arrest warrant, Argentina's intelligence
service is conducting surveillance on Sakic to prevent him from
fleeing, Ramos said.
<p>
   Sakic, the retired former owner of a textile factory, has left his
home in the beach resort of Santa Teresita and apparently traveled to
Buenos Aires but technically he is not a fugitive, Ramos said. Still,
``even if I had doubts about this man's identity, it is better to act
quickly in a case like this,'' Ramos said.
<p>
   Croatia shares blame, another Argentine official said, because it
has yet to file a formal extradition request. That request is
imminent, said a spokeswoman at the Croatian Embassy.
<p>
   Jewish groups and the U.S. State Department, which this week urged
speedy action, say Sakic is responsible for the deaths of thousands of
prisoners, mostly Serbs and Jews but Gypsies, too. The toll in
Jasenovac is estimated as high as 600,000, said Tommy Baer,
international director of B'nai B'rith.
<p>
    &UR; (Optional add end)
<p>
   There are doubts about Croatia's capacity to judge war criminals
because of the country's pro-Nazi past and its modern-day human rights
abuses of minorities, particularly Serbs.
<p>
   Some officials in the right-wing government of President Franjo
Tudjman remain sympathetic to Croatia's fascist Ustasha regime, the
World War II Nazi puppet state. As Croatia seceded from Communist
Yugoslavia in 1991, it revived many Ustasha symbols, including
elements of its flag and the name of its currency. Tudjman has made
public statements that appeared to minimize the guilt of Ustasha
commanders. He invited world scorn when he suggested burying Ustasha
soldiers alongside their Serb and Jewish victims in a new memorial at
Jasenovac.
<p>
   Meanwhile, Argentina has a history of providing shelter to Nazi
fugitives after the war and has endured anti-Jewish violence by
security forces in its recent past. Rather than keeping a low profile
after he came to Argentina 50 years ago, the reportedly outspoken
Sakic has been active in the sizable Argentine-Croatian community. He
traveled to Europe to espouse Croatian nationalist causes and boasted
about having clout with Argentine politicians, Argentine officials and
Jewish activists say.
<p>
   Croatia's Justice and Foreign ministers say Sakic will be brought
to Croatia for trial and promise to satisfy international demands for
fair, complete proceedings.
</TEXT>

bc-guns - a1804
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Kenneth Reich </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Two Arrested for Allegedly Selling Cannons to Feds (Los Angeles) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   LOS ANGELES  &MD;  An Idaho peddler of used airplanes and a Southern
California intermediary have been arrested in a sting operation for
allegedly trying to sell to undercover federal agents five 30-mm
cannons capable of firing 1,200 explosive projectiles a minute.
<p>
   Steven L. Picatti, 53, of Boise, Idaho, and Kasi Gabbita, 55, of
Hacienda Heights, Calif., were taken into custody by U.S. Alcohol,
Tobacco and Firearms and U.S. Customs agents on Monday and Tuesday,
federal officials said Tuesday.
<p>
   The arrest of a third man is anticipated, agents said.
<p>
   Agents said at a news conference that Picatti, the principal in the
alleged deal, apparently believed he was dealing with suppliers of a
drug cartel when he transferred the deadly weapons to Los Angeles.
<p>
   Instead he was arrested near the Van Nuys Airport in Los Angeles'
San Fernando Valley Monday as he awaited a $24,000 payment for four of
the cannon he had allegedly driven to Los Angeles from Boise in a
pickup truck.
<p>
   Gabbita was picked up at his home Tuesday morning. Both arrests
were peaceful, agents said.
<p>
   Picatti is accused of selling and delivering a fifth cannon to
federal undercover agents in February for $12,000.
<p>
   The British and French-manufactured Aden cannon, developed in the
late 1940s, was once used as armament in Hawker Hunter fighter
aircraft and also once employed in U.S. Marine Harrier fighters. It is
classed as an ``implement of war'' and is prohibited to civilians
under a 1968 U.S. law.
<p>
   ``I think we're fortunate to intercept these weapons before they
hit the streets of Los Angeles,'' said Barry M. Caires, agent in
charge of the ATF's Van Nuys Field Office.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Caires and other ATF and Customs officials described the Aden
cannon as primarily useful in air-to-air and air-to-ground combat, but
they said it also could be fired from ground to ground if properly
anchored against a powerful recoil.
<p>
   Each armor-piercing projectile, 1 inch in diameter, carries an
explosive and incendiary charge that would set afire anything it hit,
they said.
<p>
   ``The Aden cannon has no legitimate civilian use,'' said ATF agent
Richard A. Curd. ``One of them in the wrong hands has the potential to
cause catastrophic destruction.''
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   Agents said Picatti, a U.S. citizen who they said has no criminal
record, has sold airplanes throughout the world. He works at the Boise
Airport for a business called Jet Aviation Services and also operates
Picatti Aero Service.
<p>
   ``Let's say you wanted to buy a MiG fighter,'' an ATF agent said.
``Picatti would locate it for you, fly it to Boise, recondition it and
deliver it to you.''
<p>
   But under U.S. law, such a fighter sold to a civilian could carry
no arms.
<p>
   The agents said they did not know if Gabbita, who is from India, is
a U.S. citizen, and they did not immediately describe his precise
occupation.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-clinton </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0980 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-clinton
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Roberto Suro </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Justice Department to Fight Starr on Forcing Secret &QL; 
 &UR; Service to Talk (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  The Justice Department has decided to fight efforts by
independent counsel Kenneth W. Starr to compel testimony from Secret
Service officers who protect President Clinton, setting the stage for
another long court battle in the Monica S. Lewinsky investigation.
<p>
   Breaking off weeks of negotiations over whether he could gain
access to Secret Service personnel, Starr filed a sealed motion Friday
seeking a court order to require their grand jury testimony. Now, top
Justice Department officials  &MD;  at the behest of the Treasury
Department, which oversees the Secret Service  &MD;  are drawing up papers
countering Starr's motion on the grounds that a unique legal privilege
should shield the relationship between Secret Service officers and
those they protect, administration officials said.
<p>
   Since early in his investigation of whether Clinton had a sexual
relationship with Lewinsky and tried to cover it up, Starr has wanted
to determine whether Secret Service officers saw Clinton with the
former White House intern or heard about their encounters from others
at the White House.
<p>
   But as a result of the Justice Department's decision to fight the
independent counsel, Starr is unlikely to know whether any Secret
Service officers have direct knowledge of the Clinton-Lewinsky
relationship by the time he makes his report to the House of
Representatives on the Lewinsky matter. Starr's goal is to complete
that report by the end of next month.
<p>
   In addition, Starr, who already is fighting the president over
claims of executive privilege for White House aides, will have to
devote resources to a second major procedural battle. Meanwhile, the
Justice Department finds itself in a position it has sought to avoid
since Attorney General Janet Reno approved Starr's Lewinsky
investigation in January: appearing to shield Clinton from the
independent counsel's probe.
<p>
   Clinton could face another drawn-out legal battle in the Paula
Jones civil lawsuit. Jones has scheduled a news conference for
Thursday in Dallas, where advisers expect her to announce that she
will appeal the dismissal of her sexual harassment lawsuit to the 8th
U.S. Circuit Court of Appeals. A decision to pursue her claim after
the suit was thrown out by a federal judge would ensure that Clinton
will continue to be confronted by the matter, possibly through the end
of his presidency.
<p>
   Her lawyers have told Jones that an appeal could take a year or
two, particularly if the case ends up at the Supreme Court, which has
already heard the matter once. Even if they win a reversal, a trial
may not occur until after Clinton leaves office in January 2001.
<p>
   In Starr's investigation, the independent counsel and the Justice
Department, which acts as a lawyer for the Treasury, have been engaged
in talks over potential testimony by Secret Service officers since
late January. But while both sides characterized the negotiations as
friendly throughout, the discussions came to an abrupt end last week.
<p>
   Persuaded by the Treasury Department's position, Justice Department
officials have argued that the special relationship between presidents
and the Secret Service would be forever harmed if members of the
protective detail were obliged to testify about what they observed
while on duty.
<p>
   The national interest in preserving a relationship of total trust
is so great, the Justice Department claimed, that a special privilege
should shield the Secret Service from the intrusion of a criminal
investigation.
<p>
   The Justice Department claimed that the privilege would cover any
information gained while on protective duty and that it would apply to
both the Secret Service's plainclothes agents and uniformed officers.
No such privilege has ever been asserted before, and officials at the
Justice Department said that the concept faces uncertain prospects if
it is tested in court.
<p>
   Starr has argued that as sworn law enforcement officers, Secret
Service personnel have an obligation to assist a criminal
investigation  &MD;  even if the president is a potential target of the
probe.
<p>
   Already, several Secret Service agents and officers have made
claims of privilege in refusing to answer some questions raised during
depositions or interviews with Starr's prosecutors, Justice Department
officials said.
<p>
   The most sensitive questions involve inquiries about what officers
observed while on protective duty and queries related the means and
methods used by Secret Service protective units.
<p>
   In recent weeks, Justice officials and Starr's office were seeking
to find a compromise in which questions could be tailored narrowly
enough to avoid violating the privilege that Treasury wanted to
assert.
<p>
   Starr effectively ended the negotiations Friday by filing a sealed
motion with U.S. District Judge Norma Holloway Johnson asking to force
the Secret Service personnel to answer the questions they had claimed
were covered by privilege. The Wall Street Journal first reported the
Starr motion Tuesday.
<p>
   Meanwhile, Starr is considering whether to ask the Justice
Department's former top internal watchdog to investigate whether one
of his chief Whitewater witnesses, David Hale, received money from
conservative activists working to uncover scandalous material on
Clinton, officials said.
<p>
   Starr has had talks with Michael E. Shaheen Jr., former counsel at
Justice's Office of Professional Responsibility, about conducting the
Hale investigation. The Justice Department referred the matter to
Starr last week but advised him that he had a possible conflict of
interest because of his own ties to conservative philanthropist
Richard Mellon Scaife, the source of the alleged payments to Hale.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-gore </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1821 04-14 0755 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-gore - a1821
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Jonathan Peterson </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Many Puzzled by Gore's Charitable Contributions (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  A single line in Vice President Al Gore's highly
detailed, 34-page federal tax return, which was released publicly
Monday, is transforming a yawner of a story into something awkward for
a political leader who wants to be the next president.
<p>
   It is the $353 Gore and his wife, Tipper, reported as gifts to
charity last year on a return that shows adjusted gross income of
$197,729. The donations reported by Gore, who has often lauded the
value of social responsibility, add up to less than one-tenth the
typical contribution amount for his income level, according to
Internal Revenue Service records.
<p>
   The $353 is also causing some bewilderment in philanthropic
circles, where Gore has a ``good guy'' image as an advocate for public
services and social causes.
<p>
   ``I would assume that he would want to do something to demonstrate
that he was being socially responsible through his giving,'' said
Stacy Palmer, managing editor of the Chronicle of Philanthropy. ``It's
not adding up for me in a way that makes a whole lot of sense.''
<p>
   The taxing flap sparked an unusual statement Tuesday from the vice
president's office. Aides urged that the Gores be judged by their
record over many years, not just the dollar figures on their 1997 tax
return. In addition, the aides said, the tax return did not encompass
all the Gores' donations, because the couple did not claim deductions
for donations to their church or for Tipper Gore's contributions of
food and clothing to the homeless.
<p>
   ``Contributing financially to charitable organizations is certainly
noble and should be encouraged and is something that the Gores have
done when the resources were there,'' said Chris Lehane, a spokesman
for the vice president. ``However, to truly judge a person's
commitment to helping others, you need to consider what they have done
with their lives and how they have spent their time  &MD;  and by that
standard the Gores are extraordinarily committed.''
<p>
   Their financial commitment, however, appeared to slip last year. In
1996, for example, the Gores gave $35,530 to charity, most of it with
proceeds from Tipper Gore's book, ``Picture This,'' which went to a
program for the homeless.
<p>
   In 1992, aided by royalties from the vice president's own book,
``Earth in the Balance,'' they gave $50,000 to the University of
Tennessee to endow a chair in memory of Gore's late sister. In the
interim years, the Gores did not itemize their deductions.
<p>
   The Gores are indeed active in volunteer activities. Tipper Gore
continues to participate in efforts to fight hunger and help the
homeless. The two have worked together in projects for Habitat for
Humanity, which builds housing for the working poor, and in efforts to
raise money to combat breast cancer.
<p>
   ``When you look at the core of charity, it is not just about giving
money. It is not a contest to see who contributes more,'' Lehane said.
``It is about what you have done to improve people's lives.''
<p>
   But in the political shark tank that Gore has chosen for his
professional career, the reported $353 can hardly prove a convenient
disclosure. According to a survey by the pro-philanthropy Independent
Sector, the average American household gave $696 to charity in 1996.
<p>
   ``When you're in public life and you come from a well-to-do family
and your charitable giving seems to be minuscule ... I would think it
would be the butt of jokes on late-night TV,'' said Stuart Rothenberg,
an independent political analyst in Washington.
<p>
   ``This is the vice president of an administration that has
constantly reminded the public of the personal responsibilities we
have. It raises questions,'' he added.
<p>
   In fact, a household's annual contributions can vary markedly from
year to year, charity experts said. And even with a vice presidential
salary of $171,500, it is possible that the Gores, who pay Harvard
University tuition for two of their children and private high school
tuition for another, sometimes feel pinched.
<p>
   ``If you were just looking at that year (1996), you'd say this is
an amazingly generous household,'' said Ann Kaplan, research director
of the AAFRC Trust for Philanthropy in New York, referring to the
Gores' contributions of $35,530 that year. ``If you were looking at
the next year you'd say the opposite. ... It sounds like a very
complex and unusual tax situation.''
</TEXT>

</TRAILER>

<SLUG> bc-drugs-times </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1822 04-14 0958 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-drugs-times - a1822
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Prescription Drugs May Be Killing 100,000 People a Year, Study
Says &QL;  &UR; By Terence Monmaney &QC; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Properly prescribed medications may kill more than 100,000 people a
year, taking more lives than diabetes or pneumonia, according to a new
analysis that suggests prescription medications cause more harm than
previously believed.
<p>
   The new study, which will appear Wednesday in the Journal of the
American Medical Association, estimates that 76,000 to 137,000 people
died in 1994 from such treatments. That would make so-called adverse
drug reactions between the sixth and fourth leading cause of death in
the United States.
<p>
   Moreover, of the 33 million hospitalized patients in 1994, some 2.2
million had a nonfatal reaction serious enough to require medical
attention, the researchers say.
<p>
   Although some experts questioned the study methods, the new
estimates put the problem in the most dramatic light yet, suggesting
that despite saving perhaps millions of lives, medications bring on
more misery than previously acknowledged.
<p>
   The study ``puts into clear perspective that adverse drug reactions
are a major form of death and injury that can be prevented,'' said Dr.
Sidney Wolfe, director of the Public Citizen Health Research Group and
author of the book ``Worst Pills, Best Pills.'' He said the injuries
and deaths detailed in the study are nearly twice as high as estimates
recently done by his consumer group.
<p>
   The findings should not encourage people to abandon vital
medications, said the study's leader, Dr. Bruce Pomeranz of the
University of Toronto, who added he was surprised by the death toll.
``What's needed is more awareness of the potential problems with
taking some drugs. Before you take a medication you should know about
its risk-benefit ratio,'' he said.
<p>
   The Pharmaceutical Research and Manufacturers of America, a
<p>
   drug industry group, cautioned patients not to panic.
``Prescription medicines are safe and effective when used properly,''
Alan Holmer, the group's president, said in a statement.
<p>
   The analysis did not specify which drugs were most risky or which
diseases patients had when the adverse reactions occurred. But other
studies have found that the drug types causing the most serious
medical problems in hospitalized patients are painkillers: from
narcotics that halt breathing to aspirin pills that induce stomach
bleeding; antibiotics and anti-viral drugs, which can cause severe
diarrhea; and cardiovascular and anticoagulant drugs, which can cause
a range of problems including internal bleeding.
<p>
   In addition, some people are allergic or sensitive to particular
drugs, while other bad reactions happen when two or more drugs are
combined. For instance, the widely prescribed allergy drug
terfenidine, or Seldane, was withdrawn from the market after reports
that it triggered heart-rhythm problems in people also taking the
commonly administered antibiotic erythromycin.
<p>
   Adverse drug reactions especially trouble the elderly, experts say,
because they are more likely to have multiple underlying health
problems and also tend to have a weakened liver and kidneys, which
break down and eliminate medications.
<p>
   Medical researchers believe that adverse drug reactions have been
underestimated for years. That is chiefly because hospitals and
physicians seldom report such reactions, dismiss them as unavoidable
or mistake them for disease symptoms.
<p>
   The U.S. Health Care Financing Administration last fall proposed
new federal reguations requiring hospitals to step up drug-reaction
monitoring. But some physicians have criticized the proposed
monitoring system, which would involve periodic reviews of patient
charts, as intrusive and costly.
<p>
   In their study, the Toronto researchers pooled and analyzed data
from 39 U.S. studies on adverse drug reactions published between 1964
and 1996. They looked at two groups: patients who underwent an adverse
drug reaction while in the hospital that was at least as serious
enough to prolong their stay, and also outpatients who had a drug
reaction bad enough to hospitalize them.
<p>
   While other studies have looked at those two groups separately,
this was the first to combine them, leading to the ``extremely high''
prevalence of drug reactions, as the researchers called it.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   Between the upper and lower fatality estimates is the midpoint of
106,000 drug-induced deaths. That means that in 1994  &MD;  which the
researchers chose as a representative year  &MD;  0.32 percent of patients
on a prescription drug in or three out of every 1,000, had a fatal
reaction to it.
<p>
   Their approach was ``conservative,'' the researchers said, in that
they focused only on correctly prescribed drugs. Their analysis did
not even consider other sources of prescription drug problems, such as
patient compliance errors, intentional overdoses, narcotic abuse and
accidental poisonings.
<p>
   ``The truth is we missed a lot of people,'' Pomeranz says,
including those who ``died at home.''
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   Other researchers questioned aspects of the study because it is a
``meta-analysis,'' which involves statistically analyzing data pooled
from other studies, rather than studying real people. It is often
difficult to establish that a very sick person died from a drug
reaction rather than an underlying illness, said Dr. John Burke, a
medical epidemiologist at LDS Hospital in Salt Lake City, who has
studied adverse drug reactions.
<p>
   He urged caution in accepting the study as fact, but also praised
it as a ``spur to action'' in attacking the problem of adverse drug
reactions.
</TEXT>
