<SLUG> bc-singleton </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1823 04-14 0603 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-singleton - a1823
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Mike Clary </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Singleton Is Sentenced to Die in Florida Electric Chair (Miami) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   MIAMI  &MD;  Twenty years after he raped a California teen-ager, chopped
off her forearms and left her naked in a ditch, Lawrence Singleton
Tuesday was sentenced to die in Florida's electric chair for the fatal
stabbing of a Tampa prostitute in his own living room.
<p>
   Singleton, 70, showed no emotion as he listened to Judge Bob
Anderson Mitcham seal his fate in words laden with undisguised
disgust.
<p>
   ``This was an unprovoked, senseless killing of a human being,'' the
judge said of the bloody February 1997 slaying of Roxanne Hayes, 31.
``We are living in times worse than Sodom and Gomorrah.''
<p>
   In Los Angeles, some 3,000 miles away from the Tampa courtroom
where Singleton was sentenced, Mary Bell Vincent, now 35, appeared at
a news conference to say that she was ``relieved that justice has been
served.
<p>
   ``I didn't want to play God and don't want anyone's death on my
hands,'' said Vincent, who is fitted with two prosthetic hooks. ``But
I think there's a little bit of relief. I think I can start all over
and put everything behind me and hopefully be safe and happy.''
<p>
   Six weeks ago Vincent flew to Tampa from her home in the Pacific
Northwest to testify in the penalty phase of Singleton's murder trial.
From the witness stand she pointed a gleaming metal hook at Singleton
and then offered a brief, haunting account of what happened in 1978,
when she was 15, after she got into his van while hitchhiking in
Berkeley.
<p>
   ``I was attacked,'' she said. ``I was raped and my hands were cut
off.''
<p>
   How? she was asked.
<p>
   ``He used a hatchet.''
<p>
   Singleton, a onetime merchant seaman, was convicted of seven
felonies in connection with his assault on Vincent, including
attempted murder, rape and kidnapping. He was sentenced to 11 years in
prison, the maximum then possible under California law, and served a
little more than eight years before he was freed.
<p>
   Singleton's release from prison was met with such an outcry that he
was forced to move from community to community before finally serving
out his parole in a rented trailer on the grounds of San Quentin
Prison. Public outrage over his release prompted California
legislators to pass tougher sentencing laws.
<p>
   Singleton had lived quietly in his native Tampa for several years
before police were called to his house on Feb. 19, 1997. A house
painter testified at the trial that he peered in the front window of
Singleton's home and saw a naked man repeatedly plunging a boning
knife into a woman as she lay sprawled on the couch.
<p>
   Singleton testified that he had picked up Hayes, the mother of
three children, on a Tampa street after agreeing to pay her $20 for
sex. When she tried to take more from his wallet, he said, he
accidentally stabbed her  &MD;  seven times  &MD;  as he tried to stop her.
<p>
   A jury took four hours to find him guilty last February and just
one hour to recommend that he be sentenced to death.
<p>
   Judge Mitcham was not bound by the jury's recommendation. And
Hayes' longtime boyfriend, Clifford Tyson, the father of two of her
children, said that he did not favor Singleton's execution.
<p>
   ``She was a human being,'' Tyson said Tuesday. ``But as long as he
was in prison for life, that would have been enough for me.''
<p>
   But Mitcham said that, although Singleton was depressed and
alcoholic, his deed warranted a seat in ``Old Sparky,'' Florida's
three-legged wooden electric chair. Four persons have been
electrocuted in it this year.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-race-times </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1824 04-14 0763 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-race-times - a1824
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Elizabeth Shogren </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Clinton Headlines Discussion of Race Amid Criticism (Houston) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   HOUSTON  &MD;  President Clinton tried Tuesday to invigorate his
national race initiative by headlining a televised discussion of race
and athletics, but critics said the event epitomized one of the
fundamental problems afflicting the yearlong effort.
<p>
   The discussion panel initially assembled by cable network ESPN to
discuss the impact of racial stereotyping and discrimination on
professional sports consisted of six blacks and four whites, including
Clinton.
<p>
   It was expanded at the last minute to include a Latino, St. John's
University basketball player Felipe Lopez, but the damage was done.
Latino advocacy groups continued to complain that the panel  &MD;  and
Clinton's race initiative in general  &MD;  focuses too much on black-white
conflicts and not enough on the complicated multiracial issues that
reflect America today.
<p>
   ``Race is a hard issue,'' acknowledged Judith Winston, executive
director of the president's advisory commission on race, before the
90-minute televised discussion began Tuesday evening. ``We knew that
starting out. There's always something.''
<p>
   Other participants in the 90-minute session were former Cleveland
Browns running back Jim Brown, New York Jets wide receiver Keyshawn
Johnson, Olympic gold medalist Jackie Joyner-Kersee, Minnesota Vikings
Coach Dennis Green, Georgetown University basketball Coach John
Thompson, and ESPN analyst and baseball Hall of Famer Joe Morgan, all
of whom are black.
<p>
   The panel's four white participants were Clinton, San Diego Padres
Owner John Moores, and San Francisco 49ers President Carmen Policy and
University of Georgia Athletic Director Vince Dooley.
<p>
   Sunday, after a Latino advocacy group wrote to Clinton objecting to
the composition of the panel, ESPN announced that it was adding Lopez,
a Dominican. The network said it had tried unsuccessfully to include
more Latinos and noted that the audience would reflect their views.
<p>
   Nonetheless, some Latino groups remained upset, and said the entire
incident underscored a a broader problem with the race initiative that
Clinton launched with high hopes and great fanfare last year.
<p>
   ``They always talk about how important it is to be inclusive and to
get away from the black-white paradigm,'' said Raul Yzaguirre,
president of La Raza, a Latino advocacy group. ``But it's difficult to
see a lot of concrete evidence that they've gotten away from that
paradigm. In this event, as with the others, you see the obsession
with the past. The black-white issue is a framework of the past, and
it is totally ignorant of the new framework of the country.''
<p>
   In Texas and California, Latinos already have become the largest
single ethnic minority, Yzaguirre noted, and New York is expected to
soon join the list.
<p>
   Latino groups have not been alone in expressing their
disappointment with the president's initiative.
<p>
   Although the ESPN event is only the second ``town meeting'' on race
personally attended by Clinton, his advisory commission has convened a
number of similar sessions across the United States to talk about
racial issues.
<p>
   At a commission event in Denver last month, American Indian
activists held a raucous protest that prevented the session from
proceeding as planned. They complained that the president's advisory
board includes no American Indians and is not adequatedly addressing
issues important to the hundreds of tribes in this country.
<p>
   ``The insult added to the injury that has occurred over numerous
years is to be excluded from an initiative that is set up to bridge
the race gap,'' said Joann Chase, executive director of the National
Congress of American Indians, and a citizen of the Mandan, Hidatsa and
Arikara tribes of North Dakota. ``As the people who were here first,
we gave up so much so that America could flourish. We should at least
have a seat at the table when we're discussing issues of race in
America.''
<p>
   Clinton advisers say they have faced many obstacles in trying to
expand the dialogue to include ethnic groups other than blacks and
whites.
<p>
   ``It just seems that folks ... are looking for their own faces, and
if they don't see them or they see them added in a way that appears to
be an afterthought, people get frustrated,'' said Maria Echaveste, who
heads the White House public liaison office.
<p>
   ``We consistently go beyond black and white, but it doesn't seem to
get any traction,'' Echaveste said.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-pulitzer-arts </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 1382 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-pulitzer-arts
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National, Entertainment editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By David Streitfeld </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Katharine Graham, Philip Roth Win Literary Pulitzers (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  She spent nearly a decade writing her first book. It
appeared when she was 79. It became a No. 1 bestseller. Tuesday,
Katharine Graham's ``Personal History'' won a Pulitzer Prize.
<p>
   ``This was not even on my wish list,'' said Graham, who chronicles
in the book her transformation from an awkward child and insecure
widow to pioneering head of The Washington Post Co. Speaking very
softly before the assembled newsroom, she said winning the Pulitzer
was ``sort of like a fairy tale.''
<p>
   Among the Pulitzers for journalism, the New York Times got three
awards, the Los Angeles Times received two, and the Grand Forks, N.D.,
Herald, which published through a devastating flood and fire, won for
public service.
<p>
   In the other arts and literary awards, novelist Philip Roth, who
has chronicled the American scene and his own internal landscape for
four decades, got his first Pulitzer for ``American Pastoral.'' Paula
Vogel took the drama prize for ``How I Learned to Drive,'' and Charles
Wright won the poetry prize for ``Black Zodiac.''
<p>
   ``I'm going to go take a walk to celebrate,'' said Wright, who
teaches at the University of Virginia. He put the Pulitzer in
perspective: ``Poetry is the great ocean of language we put our boats
on. A prize every now and then is a desert island. How nice to find
one. But you're not sailing for desert islands. You're trying to get
across the water.''
<p>
   Vogel, 46, grew up in Washington's Maryland suburbs, the setting of
her winning play, and graduated from Catholic University. The story of
a woman looking back at her childhood and coming to terms with the
uncle who molested her, ``How I Learned to Drive'' was written and
developed at Perseverance Theatre in Juneau, Alaska. Perseverance is
run by Molly D. Smith, who will succeed Douglas C. Wager next season
as artistic director of Washington's Arena Stage.
<p>
   ``Molly Smith had a huge impact on the writing of this play,''
Vogel said Tuesday. ``I really want to thank her. She thought the play
was extraordinary and I didn't.''
<p>
   ``How I Learned to Drive,'' directed by Smith, will be the final
offering next season at Arena's Kreeger Theater, and the playwright
seems likely to have a still greater Washington profile.
<p>
   ``I'd very much like to write larger pieces for Arena, for the
Washington area,'' said Vogel, who's on a leave of absence from Brown
University, where she runs the playwriting program. ``Work that really
comes from the history of Washington, D.C., and Maryland. ... If
there's any way to contribute to the new play-writing scene there, I'd
like to do it.''
<p>
   The award for history was won by University of Georgia Law School
Prof. Edward J. Larson, 44, for ``Summer for the Gods: The Scopes
Trial and America's Continuing Debate Over Science and Religion.'' The
Scopes ``monkey'' trial is largely known because of the play and movie
``Inherit the Wind,'' which presents an idealized view of the
Tennessee biology teacher who was arrested and tried in 1925 for
teaching Darwin's theory of evolution. Larson puts the trial in the
cultural context of the time  &MD;  detailing, for instance, how towns in
Tennessee sought to get one of their teachers arrested to put their
community on the map.
<p>
   Aaron Jay Kernis, 38, won the Pulitzer Prize for Composition for
his 39-minute String Quartet No. 2, ``Musica Instrumentalis,'' which
received its world premiere at Merkin Concert Hall in New York this
January. It was played by the Lark Quartet.
<p>
   ``I haven't had a martini in years, but that's sort of what this
feels like,'' said Kernis, reached at New York's Kennedy Airport as he
was about to take a flight to Spain. ``I'm just floating on air. There
is a special intensity to a string quartet that is very different from
an orchestral piece, and I'm truly honored that the Pulitzer judges
chose to reward it.''
<p>
   Kernis' compositions tend to be very brash and colorful; he works
on a big palette. His winning piece, despite being a string quartet,
is long enough to retain his customary epic quality  &MD;  although not
much of the color or splashy orchestration.
<p>
   Winning a special Pulitzer citation was composer George Gershwin,
honored for his contribution to American music on the centennial of
his birth. Gershwin died in 1937.
<p>
   In general nonfiction, the Pulitzer went to ``Guns, Germs, and
Steel: The Fates of Human Societies'' by Jared Diamond, a professor of
physiology at UCLA.
<p>
   Diamond's book, which has been a word-of-mouth bestseller, attempts
to explain how the past 13,000 years of human history has proceeded
differently for people in different parts of the globe. ``Why weren't
Native Americans, Africans and Aboriginal Australians the ones who
decimated, subjugated or exterminated Europeans or Asians?'' he asks.
The book argues against racially based theories of human history,
making the case that such environmental factors as climate and
geography instead played the pivotal role.
<p>
   Pulitzers have been awarded in the category of
biography/autobiography since 1917. Almost always, the winner is a
biography. Among the very few of those who have won for telling their
own story were Henry Adams in 1917 for ``The Education of Henry
Adams,'' William Allen White in 1947 for ``The Autobiography of
William Allen White,'' Russell Baker in 1983 for ``Growing Up,'' Lewis
B. Puller Jr. in 1992 for ``Fortunate Son: The Healing of a Vietnam
Vet'' and, last year, Frank McCourt for ``Angela's Ashes.''
<p>
   ``I feel this is not only my story but our story,'' Graham told the
newsroom Tuesday. But what really seemed to captivate reviewers and
readers was not the inside story of the newspaper that she raised from
second-rate status, but the candor with which she discussed how her
own life had changed.
<p>
   When she took over the paper in 1963 after the suicide of her
husband, Philip, she was untrained and very insecure. The first
editorial lunch at which she asked a question was for Madame Nhu, the
fearsome sister-in-law of South Vietnamese President Ngo Dinh Diem.
``I have a very vivid recollection of nearly dying afterwards from
embarrassment and fear that I had looked stupid or ignorant,'' Graham
wrote in ``Personal History.''
<p>
   It was on-the-job training, and she rose to the occasion. ``She
turns out to have had not two lives but four, and the story of her
journey from daughter to wife to widow to woman parallels to a
surprising degree the history of women in this century,'' wrote Nora
Ephron in the New York Times Book Review.
<p>
   Philip Roth has been a well-regarded writer since his first book,
``Goodbye, Columbus,'' in 1959, and a famous one since ``Portnoy's
Complaint'' a decade later. He's very prolific  &MD;  ``American Pastoral''
was his 22nd book, and he is publishing the 23rd this fall  &MD;  and the
recipient of many honors. Recent books have picked up nearly every
major award except the Pulitzer.
<p>
   ``American Pastoral'' is about Seymour ``Swede'' Levov, a good man
whose daughter grows up to become a terrorist. ``What the hell is
wrong with doing things right?'' asks the anguished Levov, who sees in
his family's tragedy the descent of American civilization.
<p>
   In recent years, Roth has become increasingly inclined to let his
work speak for itself, doing almost no interviews or author
appearances. His publisher said Tuesday that reporters who wanted to
get a comment from him about the Pulitzer should fax questions to his
agent.
<p>
   When Charles Wright told his wife, Holly, that he had won the
poetry Pulitzer, ``she was excited,'' he said. ``But she was more
excited when I told her Philip Roth had won for fiction. `Now I'll
finally get to meet him,' she said. She just loved that book.''
<p>
   Wright, 62, isn't quite as laureled as Roth, but is getting close.
``Black Zodiac'' also won awards from the National Book Critics Circle
and the Los Angeles Times. He described his work as ``a linguistic
investigation of the idea of landscape, the idea of language and the
idea of God. Probably in that order.''
<p>
   The Pulitzers, which are presented by Columbia University, include
an award of $5,000.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-napalm-post </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0917 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-napalm-post
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By William Booth </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Midwestern Lawmakers Fight Napalm Shipments (Los Angeles) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   LOS ANGELES  &MD;  Two containers filled with 12,000 gallons of
Vietnam-era napalm wound their way across the country by rail Tuesday,
destination unknown. The Chicago-area waste facility that was to
recycle the notorious weapon and defoliant quit the project because of
what it described as political pressure and unfounded hysteria.
<p>
   The wandering napalm that nobody wanted led to a rapid-fire
exchange of charges and counter-charges on Capitol Hill and elsewhere.
There were accusations of know-nothing congressional demagoguery,
White House meddling and public relations bungling by the Navy. And at
the end of the day, there was no indication where the dreaded stuff
will end up.
<p>
   ``Good question,'' Defense Department spokesman Ken Bacon told
reporters when asked what the military will do now that its recycling
project has been derailed.
<p>
   All the heat was misdirected, according to Navy officials. They
maintained the canisters of napalm  &MD;  composed of gasoline, benzene and
polystyrene  &MD;  are not armed to detonate, not explosive and no more
dangerous or flammable than the fuels, chemicals and plastics that
routinely travel the nation's rails and roads.
<p>
   Millions of gallons of napalm were used during the Vietnam War; it
was an indiscriminate but deadly weapon and defoliant, capable of
carpeting the jungle with a flowing river of raging, clinging flame.
Its use, however, was condemned by the United Nations and the U.S.
military discontinued the practice a quarter century ago.
<p>
   Since the early 1970s, the Navy has stored about 23 million pounds
of napalm in 34,000 canisters at a weapons site north of San Diego.
The canisters sit exposed to the elements, but they are not armed with
detonators.
<p>
   ``Napalm is gasoline and benzene. You drive around with gas and
benzene in your car every day,'' said Lee Saunders of the Navy's
Weapons Support Facility in Fallbrook, Calif., where the napalm is
stored. ``These kinds of materials are shipped routinely all over the
country.''
<p>
   Saunders said the Navy was planning to literally turn swords into
plowshares. The wood crates holding the napalm canisters were to be
shredded, shipped and burned as fuel at a power plant in Tulsa, Okla.
The canisters were to be melted and turned into things such as
airplane landing gear. Finally, the napalm was to be mixed with other
fuels and used to fire kilns to make cement.
<p>
   The first shipments of napalm, which left Saturday and made it as
far as New Mexico, were to arrive this week at Pollution Control
Industries (PCI) in East Chicago, Ind., a waste recycling facility
that won the multiyear, $24 million contract.
<p>
   But as the first trainload of napalm was headed its way, PCI
decided to back out of the contract. In a news conference Tuesday, PCI
President Robert Campbell said his company became ``caught in a
tug-of-war'' among various branches and departments of the federal
government, that opponents ``toyed with the facts'' and that ``the
possibility for political gain by some overruled what is an excellent
environmental solution to deal with this material.''
<p>
   A number of members of the Illinois and Indiana congressional
delegations have denounced the napalm recycling project in their area,
raising questions about PCI's track record and complaining of the
Navy's failure to calm fears  &MD;  even while some of the politicians
suggested that napalm recycling was a potentially dangerous
undertaking.
<p>
   Rep. Rod R. Blagojevich, D-Ill., who gave an emotional news
conference attacking the project in January, said in an interview
Tuesday that napalm was a weapon of war, designed to stick to things
and was much more potentially hazardous than gasoline. He suggested
shipping the material to a weapons disposal site in Utah or to the
Johnson atoll in the Pacific Ocean.
<p>
   Rep. Peter J. Visclosky, D-Ind., said that he initially, albeit
cautiously, supported the project, but has been dismayed in recent
weeks by what he alleged was the Navy's failure to assure the public
that the napalm recycling program would be safe.
<p>
   ``Any problems the Navy is having are largely their own fault,''
Visclosky said.
<p>
   He added that it was naive to think that people  &MD;  or their leaders
 &MD;  would not be disturbed by shipments of napalm through their
neighborhoods. The gelatinous substance has been a particularly
evocative symbol of the horrors of war for many Americans because of a
widely published photograph showing a 9-year-old Vietnamese girl
suffering from napalm burns and running naked down a road with anguish
on her face.
<p>
   All this seems like a lot of political meddling, said Rep. Ron
Packard, R-Calif., in whose district the napalm is now baking in the
California sun.
<p>
   ``We've said this has been political all along and this is as much
proof as one would need to believe it,'' Packard said. ``The only
reasonable conclusion that I can make is that the Clinton
administration, at the urging of members of the Illinois and Indiana
congressional delegation, threatened PCI and forced them off the
contract.''
<p>
   Administration officials, however, denied any interference, and
instead said that the White House Council on Environmental Quality
tried to mediate a dispute between the Navy and the Environmental
Protection Agency. In addition, a number of Republicans opposed to the
napalm recycling.
</TEXT>

bc-raines
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: News editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Clay Chandler </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Raines to Leave Budget Director Post for Fannie Mae (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Franklin D. Raines, whose low-key pragmatism helped
President Clinton bridge partisan differences to close the 1997
balanced-budget deal with congressional Republicans, announced Tuesday
he will step down after less than two years as White House budget
director to become chairman and chief executive of home mortgage
lender Fannie Mae.
<p>
   Raines has long been a quiet but effective player on both the local
and national political scenes, gliding smoothly between high-paying
private sector jobs and public service. At various points in his
career he has advised mayors, corporate chieftains and the president,
using a combination of financial savvy and personal charm.
<p>
   At Fannie Mae, Raines will replace James A. Johnson, the
high-profile and well-connected chairman, who wooed Raines for the
job.
<p>
   As head of Clinton's Office of Management and Budget, Raines was
part of a new White House team that helped set a tone of cooperation
that proved critical in ending the administration's long-running
budget battle with Republicans in Congress last year. ``I have the
luxury of not having been there for the old fight,'' Raines said in an
interview soon after joining the administration. ``If people want to
have the old fight, they don't really need me.''
<p>
   Raines was also the point man on administration efforts to promote
political and financial revival in Washington. And the 49-year-old
Seattle native was one of the most powerful African-American civilians
ever to serve in the executive branch.
<p>
   Raines' announcement surprised many of his White House colleagues,
and his departure comes at a delicate time for Clinton politically.
Following the announced resignation of Energy Secretary Federico Pena,
White House aides are sensitive to the appearance that senior
officials are heading for the exits at a time when Clinton is still
enmeshed in an obstruction of justice investigation.
<p>
   Clinton  &MD;  who aides said was informed by Raines of his decision
Friday  &MD;  said in a statement he regretted Raines' departure and hailed
him has a ``brilliant OMB director.''
<p>
   The White House said Clinton plans to nominate OMB's deputy
director, Jacob J. Lew, as Raines' successor. Lew, 42, is a career
bureaucrat who, for a brief stint, served as acting OMB director in
1996, prior to Raines' confirmation.
<p>
   Raines said he had hoped to remain at OMB for at least another year
until Clinton and Congress reached agreement on a plan for shoring up
the Social Security system. But Raines has made no secret of his
desire to return to the private sector and run a major firm after
leaving the administration. The chance to take over at Fannie Mae, he
said, was an offer too good to refuse.
<p>
   ``There is never a good time to stop being budget director,''
Raines said. ``If I'd had my preference, this would have happened a
year from now after the Social Security debate.'' But an invitation to
head Fannie Mae ``is a once-in-a-lifetime opportunity.''
<p>
   Raines' annual salary at OMB is just under $150,000. In 1996, the
most recent year for which information is available, Johnson earned an
estimated $7.2 million in cash, stock options and other compensation
as Fannie Mae's chief.
</TEXT>

bc-pulitzer-times - a1900
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) (Includes optional trims) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By David Shaw </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Grand Forks Paper Wins Journalism Award for Public Service &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   The Los Angeles Times won two Pulitzer Prizes and The New York
Times won three Tuesday, but the most coveted Pulitzer of all  &MD;  the
gold medal for meritorious public service  &MD;  was awarded to the small
Grand Forks, N.D., Herald (circulation 37,000) for its coverage of the
March 1997 floods and fire that destroyed more than 10 percent of the
city's homes and ravaged the newspaper's own offices.
<p>
   The Pulitzer Prize Board praised the Grand Forks paper for its
``sustained and informative coverage, vividly illustrated with
photographs, that helped hold its community together in the wake'' of
disaster.
<p>
   The Los Angeles Times won its Pulitzers in the spot news categery
for ``comprehensive coverage of a botched bank robbery and subsequent
police shootout'' in North Hollywood last spring and in the feature
photography category, for Clarence Williams' ``powerful images
documenting the plight of young chidren with parents addicted to
alcohol and drugs.'' The photographs illustrated the paper's series of
stories on ``Orphans of Addiction'' written by Sonia Nazario.
<p>
   Most Pulitzers are given to individuals, but the prize for the
shootout coverage was given to The Times' staff, 30 of whom worked on
various aspects of the story on the first day alone.
<p>
   This was the third Pulitzer in six years awarded to The Times for
coverage of a Southern California tragedy. The staff also won
Pulitzers for coverage of the 1992 Los Angeles riots and for the 1994
Northridge earthquake.
<p>
   The New York Times' citations Tuesday went to Linda Greenhouse, in
beat reporting, for her ``consistently illuminating coverage of the
United States Surpeme Court''; to Michiko Kakutani, in criticism, for
her ``passionate, intelligent writing on books and contemporary
literature''; and to The New York Times staff for international
reporting, for its ``revealing series that profiled the corrosive
effects of drug corruption in Mexico.''
<p>
   The Pulitzers, the most prestigious prizes in journalism, have been
awarded annually since 1917 by Columbia University, and the
announcement Tuesday that the Grand Forks Herald had won the public
service award is in keeping with the Pulitzer Prize Board's long
tradition of honoring smaller newspapers for overcoming tremendous
obstacles.
<p>
   The Herald's presses were already under water from floods triggered
by melting snow and ice left by winter blizzards when electrical fires
broke out last April 19, gutting the newsroom and circulation
department of the paper and forcing the staff to relocate. The next
day's paper was written and edited in makeshift offices at the
University of North Dakota, and when floods hit the university the
next day, the staff relocated again, to an elementary school 12 miles
away, in Manvel, N.D.
<p>
   The paper's newsroom staff of 57  &MD;  most of whom were flood victims
themselves  &MD;  worked out of the school library, and the paper was
actually printed at the plant of the Herald's fellow Knight-Ridder
newspaper, the St. Paul Pioneer Press, 300 miles away.
<p>
    &UR; (Begin optional trim)
<p>
   The Grand Forks Herald was not the only small newspaper honored in
Tuesday's Pulitzer announcement.
<p>
   Bernard L. Stein, editor and publisher of the Riverdale Press, a
New York City weekly newspaper with a circulation of 11,800, was
honored for ``gracefully written editorials on politics'' and other
local issues. Stein, a Pulitzer finalist on two previous occasions,
said his prize honored excellent journalists working for small
community newspapers throughout the country.
<p>
   As usual, however, most of the Pulitzers went to big-city dailies  &MD; 
The Baltimore Sun (Gary Cohn and Will Englund, in investigative
reporting, for their ``compelling series on the international
shipbreaking industry that revealed the dangers posed to workers and
the environment when discarded (Navy) ships are dismantled''); to the
Chicago Tribune (Paul Salopek, in explanatory reporting, for his
``enlightening profile of the Human Genome Diveristy Project, which
seeks to chart the genetic relationship among all people,''); to the
New York Daily News (columnist Mike McAlary, in commentary, for his
``coverage of the brutalization of a Haitian immigrant'' allegedly at
the hands of police officers); and to the Pittsburgh Post-Gazette
(Martha Rial, in spot news photography, for her ``life-affirming
portraits of survivors of the conflicts in Rwanda and Burundi'').
<p>
   The remaining Pulitzers in journalism were awarded to Russell
Carollo and Jeff Nesmith of the Dayton (Ohio) Daily News, in national
reporting, for disclosing ``dangerous flaws and mismanagment in the
military health care system,'' stories that prompted reforms in the
system; to Thomas French of the St. Petersburg (Fla.) Times, in
feature writing, for his ``detailed and compassionate narrative
portrait of a mother and two daughters slain on a Florida vacation,
and the three-year investigatiion into their murders''; and to Stephen
P. Breen of the Asbury Park (N.J.) Press, in cartooning.
<p>
    &UR; (End optional trim)
<p>
   Each Pulitzer Prize carries with it a $5,000 award, except for the
prize in public service, which carries a gold medal. All the prizes
will be presented May 28 during a luncheon at Columbia University.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-napalm-times </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a1902 04-14 0844 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-napalm-times - a1902
 &UR; (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Eric Lichtblau </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, Los Angeles Times
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Train Shipment of Napalm Generates Political Firestorm &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   A decade ago, it was a barge loaded with garbage that generated an
international stink, floating homeless down the East Coast because no
one would take its contents.
<p>
   Now it is a rail car filled with napalm, bound for the Chicago area
from Southern California this week amid a runaway debate about how
best to dispose of the deadly chemical remnants of the Vietnam War.
<p>
   The train left Fallbrook in northern San Diego County Saturday
carrying two 6,000-gallon drums of napalm in one of its cars. It
rumbled through Texas Tuesday, headed for an Indiana treatment plant
as the first step in a two-year plan to dispose of 3 million gallons
of the jellied gasoline and turn it into industrial fuel.
<p>
   But there is a hitch: The treatment plant no longer wants the
stuff, and an array of powerful people in the Midwest wants the train
to turn around.
<p>
   The political maelstrom that the disposal has generated in Chicago
in recent weeks ``has made it impossible to continue with this napalm
recycling project,'' Pollution Control Industries President Robert L.
Campbell said Tuesday. The ``tug of war'' over the issue has given the
company no choice but to pull out of the $1.7 million contract, he
said.
<p>
   Navy officials, hoping to finally overcome numerous false starts in
their 16-year-old effort to get rid of the napalm, were blindsided by
the company's withdrawal. And while officials scrambled to find a
solution Tuesday, no one seemed certain what would happen to the train
bound for nowhere.
<p>
   ``We really don't know what's going to happen at this point. We're
reviewing all our options,'' said Lee Saunders, spokesman for the
Navy's environmental division in San Diego.
<p>
   Rep. Rod Blagojevich, D-Ill., knows what he wants to happen.
<p>
   ``I'm hopeful the train will simply turn around and go back to (the
Naval Weapons Station) at Fallbrook and return the napalm there. Then
we can roll up our sleeves and work with the Navy to address an urgent
need to get rid of it in a safe and sensible way,'' he said.
<p>
   Since 1973, the Navy facility in Fallbrook has housed the
military's entire arsenal of napalm in 33,000 unfused bombs. But
Southern California politicians have been pushing with increasing
stridence for its disposal, noting that some of the bombs were leaking
toxins.
<p>
   As part of the plan approved by the Navy at a total cost of $25
million, hundreds of shipments of the napalm were to be sent by train
over the next two years to the PCI plant in East Chicago, Ind.,
outside Chicago. There it was to be treated and turned into fuel for
cement kilns. Trains would head out almost daily from Fallbrook,
officials said.
<p>
   But Blagojevich and other politicians have skewered the plan in
recent weeks, saying the Navy thrust it on them with no real warning
or explanation. The congressman said he is worried about potential
leaks and explosions from the shipments as they pass through numerous
residential areas.
<p>
   ``This was a serious weapon of war that was dropped from the U.S.
arsenal because it was considered inhumane, and I just don't think it
makes sense to send it two-thirds of the way across the country like
this,'' he said in an interview. ``The risk is overwhelming.''
Blagojevich wants the napalm taken to disposal sites in Utah or
elsewhere.
<p>
   But Navy officials and others involved in the disposal insisted
Tuesday that the napalm is safe for transport, despite the firestorm
that has erupted over it.
<p>
   ``Unfortunately, people are reacting to the emotional nature of
this issue,'' said Greg Koller, spokesman for the Ohio-based Battelle
company, a nonprofit scientific firm that was awarded the job of
overseeing the disposal project and hired PCI to convert it to fuel.
<p>
   ``Just the word `napalm' conjures up a lot of images,'' he said.
``People remember the famous picture of the little girl with her back
burned, running, and they don't understand from a technical aspect
that this stuff is safer than the gas at their local gas station.''
<p>
   Indeed, federal railway standards rate napalm as a low to moderate
risk, safer than propane and other gases, said Jerry Jenkins,
spokesman for Burlington Northern Santa Fe Railroad, the line that is
carrying the napalm.
<p>
   Burlington would not pinpoint the train's location Tuesday, saying
only that it is expected to be somewhere between Amarillo, Texas, and
Kansas City, Mo., by Wednesday.
<p>
   As of late Tuesday, neither the Navy nor Battelle officials had
asked the rail line to alter its course, and the train  &MD;  carrying the
napalm along with dozens of cars of other commercial cargo  &MD;  was
continuing on as planned, Jenkins said. The napalm was scheduled to
reach East Chicago sometime next week.
<p>
   At Battelle, Koller said he is not sure whether the company could
ask the train to turn around or even stop its route, because of
environmental laws governing the transport of contaminants.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-district </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0621 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-district
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Peter Slevin </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; D.C. to Investigate How Killer Was Freed (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  After a murderer on parole in Washington stabbed their
daughter to death, it wasn't money Gertrude and Gerfried Pruckmayr
wanted. They sued instead for answers, demanding that the government
tell them how a killer could be freed to kill again.
<p>
   District of Columbia attorneys promised the Pruckmayrs Tuesday that
the city's new inspector general will investigate the 1993 parole of
Leo Gonzales Wright and the apparent government lapses that preceded
the murder of Bettina Pruckmayr on Dec. 16, 1995.
<p>
   Inspector General E. Barrett Prettyman Jr. will report his findings
no later than January 1999, describing how Wright was freed and
allowed to remain free despite parole violations. The report will
document the errors, if any, of individual government workers.
<p>
   ``It will be fair and complete,'' Prettyman pledged. ``We're going
to attempt to improve the system.''
<p>
   For the Pruckmayrs, who drove from Media, Pa., to sign the
eight-page agreement, the announcement marks a bittersweet turning in
their lawsuit and their lives. Dropping their complaint, they are
investing in the hope that a shocking death might be better explained
and never repeated.
<p>
   ``I feel this is some progress. We don't want people to just shrug
it off and say, `Oops,' '' said Gerfried Pruckmayr, 65, a chemist.
``We have some pieces of the puzzle, but we don't know how they fit
together.''
<p>
   Bettina Pruckmayr, a 26-year-old human rights lawyer and graduate
of Georgetown University Law Center, was parking her car near Logan
Circle after Christmas shopping when Wright spotted her. He told
police he wanted drugs and was looking for something to steal.
<p>
   Holding a 5-inch knife at Pruckmayr's neck, Wright drove his
victim 18 blocks to an automated teller machine. After he robbed her,
she wrote down her ATM code, admitted she had only about $20 left in
her account, then tried to escape. Wright grabbed her and stabbed her
38 times.
<p>
   ``You are the most violent, dangerous person I've ever
encountered,'' U.S. District Judge Emmet G. Sullivan told Wright when
he sentenced him to life without parole in September 1996. ``You will
die in jail. This court will do everything in its power to ensure that
you ... will never walk the streets of this country or any place
again.''
<p>
   Gertrude Pruckmayr, 62, biting her lip, her eyes glistening with
tears, said Tuesday that the signs of Wright's awfulness were there to
see: ``Anybody could've predicted it.''
<p>
   Wright shot and killed Washington taxi driver Joseph N. Woodbury in
1976 when Woodbury refused to turn over $19. Sent to prison for a term
of 20 to 60 years, Wright was freed after 17 years. The Pruckmayrs
contend that his prison records failed to show his full disciplinary
record.
<p>
   After his release, Wright's parole was not revoked, even though he
repeatedly missed drug tests and tested positive at other times for
cocaine and heroin use. Nor was he returned to prison after a 1995
arrest on a charge of cocaine possession.
<p>
   The initial 1995 drug charge was dropped when a D.C. narcotics
detective failed to show up for a preliminary hearing, but a grand
jury soon indicted Wright in the case. No one notified the D.C. Parole
Board, however, which decided nine days later that there was not
enough evidence to keep Wright behind bars.
<p>
   The Pruckmayrs believe their daughter died because of a ``breakdown
in the prison and parole system,'' one of their attorneys, Paul M.
Flicker, said Tuesday. The city government, in the settlement
agreement, explicitly denied allegations of wrongdoing.
</TEXT>
<TRAILER>
AP-NY-04-14-98 2242EDT &QL; 
</TRAILER>

<DOCID> latwp980414.0145 </DOCID>
<STORYID cat=a pri=r sel=tm--a> X2005 </STORYID>

<SLUG> bc-pulitzer-graham </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 1020 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-pulitzer-graham
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Megan Rosenfeld </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; For Katharine Graham, Proof in the Pulitzer (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  In her autobiography, ``Personal History,'' Katharine
Graham writes in sometimes heart-rending detail about overcoming
shyness and a sense of never being quite good enough  &MD;  which she
continued to feel even as her book became a bestseller. That
diffidence was still palpable Tuesday as she stood before a newsroom
full of Washington Post employees and heard the announcement: She had
won a Pulitzer Prize.
<p>
   `` &UR; Now &LR;  do you believe you wrote a good book?'' asked editorial
page editor Meg Greenfield, one of the author's closest friends. Mrs.
Graham  &MD;  and to most of the several hundred people gathered in the
newsroom to congratulate her she will always be  &UR; Mrs. Graham &LR;   &MD;  is
notorious for being quick to minimize her own abilities and
accomplishments.
<p>
   ``She is a wonderful combination of insecurity and total
authority,'' said Robert Gottlieb, the book's editor, Tuesday. ``I
said to her this morning, `Kay, stop trying to deflect the glory.'
It's not that she doesn't love it. ... Maybe now she can accept her
achievement. If she doesn't, she needs to be spanked!''
<p>
    &UR; Mrs. Graham? Spanked? &LR;  Not likely.
<p>
   One of the story lines that carry through the 625-page book tells
how she worried all her life about pleasing others and how she
struggled to overcome that, which seems to have resonated particularly
with women readers. Memoirist and former college president Jill Ker
Conway, reviewing the book for The Post, wrote that ``what Graham
describes rings true for those of us old enough to remember the
culture that told women that their relationship with a man was their
life.''
<p>
   But reviewers and readers also found intriguing the story of her
upbringing as a child of privilege whose bright and accomplished
parents left their offspring to be raised by nannies in New York for
four years while they moved to Washington. Her father, financier
Eugene Meyer, worked for the War Industries Board. In 1933, he bought
The Post at a bankruptcy auction and spent the rest of his life trying
to make it both profitable and respectable.
<p>
   Many memoirs are vanity productions, based on personal
recollections, letters, old diaries and maybe a news clip or two. But
Graham, who is now chairman of the executive committee of The
Washington Post Co., undertook her project like an investigative
reporter, conducting more than 250 interviews and digging through
boxes of old letters, company memos and official documents.
<p>
   Researcher Evelyn Small sorted through the material and later
transcribed the author's handwritten narrative, through which a
phenomenal array of people walk, from Graham's mother's friend
Constantin Brancusi, the legendary sculptor, to, of course, Richard M.
Nixon. Her wedding pictures were snapped by Edward Steichen. She tells
how she danced the twist with President Kennedy (her daughter Lally
told her to pretend she was drying her rear end with a bath towel) and
(discreetly) reveals a ``romance'' with a waterfront union organizer
while she was a young reporter covering a strike in San Francisco.
<p>
   And always in the background of her story Washington is visible,
growing from the slow muddy city of the 1920s into the center of world
power  &MD;  from the panicky preparations for World War II to the sinking
confusion of Vietnam, from her decisions to publish the Pentagon
Papers and pursue Watergate to her friendship with Ronald and Nancy
Reagan.
<p>
   Some readers found it hard to believe that a woman who has had such
an extraordinary life could see herself as a kind of accidental
tourist at the helm of a high-speed yacht. But reviewers liked her
frankness and her lack of whining or scandal-mongering. ``Personal
History'' became a bestseller, with more than 231,000 copies sold in
hardcover. A paperback edition was recently released, with 80,000
copies in the first printing. Christopher Lehmann-Haupt wrote in the
New York Times: ``It is frank, self-critical, modest when necessary,
proud when justified and, above all, one that can tell a good story,
whether detailing an embarrassing anecdote (from which its owner was
far from immune) or outlining contemporary American history (to which
she was a frequent eyewitness).''
<p>
   The hardest part of the book for her to write, she said, was the
section about her husband. For a while she thought that describing in
detail the story of his mental illness and suicide would be
distressing for her four children, and suggested to Gottlieb that this
section be trimmed down. But the editor convinced her that unless she
told the whole story her husband would come across as ``an unpleasant
man who drank too much.'' The author decided that candor about mental
illness would help shed light on that problem for others.
<p>
   Her eldest son, Washington Post Publisher Donald Graham, has teased
her publicly about her tireless promotion of the book, which involved
numerous interviews, signings and appearances. That too was a new
frontier for her, and she did it with zest. ``A friend called while I
was in Boston and said, `Are you all right? I haven't seen you on
television for a couple of days,' '' she recalled.
<p>
   Graham, 80, once remarked that her goal was to win ``a Pulitzer
Prize for management,'' and she says she still wishes she had gone to
Harvard Business School. In her book she takes at least as much
pleasure in her company's rising profits as she does from her paper's
journalism.
<p>
   But she will gladly take the prize she got Tuesday. ``Let's be
frank, at my age to have this happen to you  &MD;  it's pinch yourself to
make sure it's really happening,'' she said.
<p>
   Perhaps only one person could outrank her in the newsroom Tuesday.
That was Herblock, The Post's legendary cartoonist, a multiple
Pulitzer winner himself and still drawing in his 89th year. He called
her ``Sweetie'' as he gave her a congratulatory kiss on the cheek, and
said, ``I told you if you hung around this newsroom long enough you'd
get the hang of it.''
</TEXT>

<PREAMBLE>
bc-source
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Annie Groer and Ann Gerhart </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; RELIABLE SOURCE: Notes on Newsmakers &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Dapper Mississippi liability lawyer Dick Scruggs had
spent the day on Capitol Hill a few weeks back tending to details of
the national tobacco settlement he helped broker. But he still had an
intriguing evening appointment to keep.
<p>
   Inside an elegant Willard Hotel suite, holding a script he'd never
seen, Scruggs read lines with Al Pacino for Michael Mann, who's
directing a still-untitled Touchstone Pictures production.
<p>
   For nearly a half-hour, Scruggs said, he auditioned to play himself
in a movie about Jeffrey Wigand, the Brown &AMP; Williamson Tobacco Corp.
whistle-blower, and Lowell Bergman, a ``60 Minutes'' producer. Bergman
wanted to air Wigand's allegations of tobacco industry chicanery, but
CBS brass killed the interview. Then Wigand retained Scruggs after the
tobacco giant sued him for breaking a lifetime secrecy pact.
<p>
   And how did Scruggs' audition go? ``After dinner, the director
patted me on the head and said, `Dick, we may be getting a
professional actor. You're fired.' ''
<p>
   There are, however, no hard feelings, Scruggs said. The film crew
has already been scoping out his Pascagoula home, and Mann starts
shooting next month in Louisville, home of Brown &AMP; Williamson's
headquarters.
<p>
   But that movie won't be the only screen version of the tobacco
wars.
<p>
   Washington producer Gerald Rafshoon has just bought the film rights
to author Peter Pringle's new book, ``Cornered: Big Tobacco at the Bar
of Justice,'' about the lawyers who took on the cigarette makers.
<p>
   Rafshoon wants to turn it into a TV mini-series because the subject
``needs to be longer than two hours. There are some very interesting
characters here, not just the lawyers, but those wonderful folks who
brought you nonaddictive nicotine.''
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   A tour of Houston's Johnson Space Center had President Clinton
orbiting with enthusiasm Tuesday.
<p>
   Clinton dropped in to observe the training regimen of Sen. John
Glenn, D-Ohio, 76, the once and future astronaut set to reprise his
1962 Earth orbit in October aboard a space shuttle.
<p>
   Clinton acknowledged that Glenn's mission had set him to thinking
about his own post-presidential career. ``Yeah, I like it,'' he said.
``I'm going to apply.''
<p>
   But could he pass the physical? In a video call with astronauts in
Cape Canaveral who will go up on a shuttle mission later this week,
Clinton said he looked forward to their research on the human
neurological system ``because I'm moving to those years where I'm
getting dizzy and having all these problems.''
<p>
   During Tuesday's tour, Clinton expressed special interest in NASA's
space suits, reports The Post's John Harris. Suit technician Amy Ross
sported one when Clinton greeted her. ``Does she look great or what?''
Clinton enthused, adding, ``Nice muscles.''
<p>
   Glenn, for his part, said NASA personnel seem like a brainier lot
than he remembers. ``These people are dealing with science at a level
we weren't even thinking about getting into back then,'' he said.
``These people are just half again smarter than we even thought we
were back then.''
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Washington is at its blooming best in spring. So glorious, in fact,
that Paul Newman and Joanne Woodward have popped down from their New
York and Connecticut digs for a short visit. He was spotted lunching
at the Oval Room Tuesday and she arrived in town by train later in the
day.
<p>
   ``We're here for a vacation, that's all it is,'' Woodward told The
Source. Wednesday they'll head south to continue their holiday.
<p>
    &UR; -0- &QC; 
<p>
   Nancy Hernreich, President Clinton's director of Oval Office
operations, supped at Galileo in Dupont Circle with Democratic
political consultant Tony Podesta Monday night. His brother, cozily
enough, is John Podesta, Clinton's deputy chief of staff.
<p>
   ``It wasn't business and it wasn't romance. We've been buddies for
years and get together every five or six weeks,'' said Tony Podesta.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-hinckley </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0400 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-hinckley
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Bill Miller </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Hinckley Loses Appeal on Leave (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Presidential assailant John W. Hinckley Jr., who wants
to leave St. Elizabeths Hospital for monthly visits with his parents,
was turned down Tuesday by a three-judge panel from the U.S. Court of
Appeals.
<p>
   The judges upheld a decision issued last June by U.S. District
Judge June L. Green, who said she believed that Hinckley remains a
danger to himself and others. Green denied Hinckley's request to spend
12 hours a month away from the Southeast Washington hospital with his
parents, John and Jo Ann.
<p>
   Hinckley, 42, has been confined at the hospital since June 1982,
after he was found not guilty by reason of insanity in the March 30,
1981, shooting of President Reagan. He also wounded three others in
the attack.
<p>
   Although Hinckley's treatment team was in favor of the monthly
visits, the hospital's review board denied his request last year. The
review board found that Hinckley deceived hospital staff about what
some viewed as an obsessive interest in the hospital's chief
pharmacist  &MD;  a woman who resembles actress Jodie Foster, whom Hinckley
imagined he would impress by shooting Reagan.
<p>
   Green cited the board's decision and Hinckley's behavior in her
opinion.
<p>
   Hinckley's attorney, Barry Wm. Levine, appealed Green's ruling,
saying Green should have permitted him to examine records concerning
the review board's deliberations.
<p>
   Levine said the board could have been influenced by improper
motivations, adding that its decision could have swayed Green
unfairly. He said Green should not have given the board's decision any
consideration.
<p>
   But the appellate judges noted that Green cited numerous other
reasons for denying Hinckley's bid, including testimony from a
psychiatrist called by the government who warned that Hinckley has a
history of masking symptoms.
<p>
   The judges also found that the board's internal deliberations were
privileged, or secret, discussions. They said that hospital review
board members could face safety concerns if deliberations were made
public in cases involving people acquitted of criminal charges by
reason of insanity. In addition, the judges stated, review board
members might be reluctant to criticize the findings of treatment
teams if their discussions could become public.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-execute-post </SLUG>
<HEADER> 04-14 0635 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-execute-post
 &UR; (wap) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Brooke A. Masters and Joan Biskupic </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Washington Post
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Despite Pleas, Va. Executes Paraguayan &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   Despite requests from the U.S. State Department and an
international court for a delay, a Paraguayan citizen convicted of
murdering an Arlington woman, Va., was put to death Tuesday night
after the U.S. Supreme Court declined to hear his case and Virginia's
governor refused to intervene.
<p>
   Angel Francisco Breard, 32, was pronounced dead at 10:39 p.m. after
being injected with lethal chemicals at the Greensville Correctional
Center in Jarratt, Va. The execution was delayed for an hour and a
half while corrections officials waited for the court's ruling and
Gilmore's subsequent decision.
<p>
   At 8:22 p.m., with the execution then scheduled for 9 p.m., the
high court rejected, 6 to 3, Breard's claim that he deserved a new
trial because he was not told at the time of his arrest that he had
the right under an international treaty to consult with a Paraguayan
consular official.
<p>
   That left Breard's fate in the hands of Gov. James S. Gilmore III
(R), who had made it clear during the afternoon that he was leaning
toward allowing Breard to be executed for the 1992 murder of Ruth
Dickie in her Arlington apartment.
<p>
   About a half-hour before the execution, Gilmore issued a statement
saying: ``As governor of Virginia, my first duty is to ensure that
those who reside within our borders  &MD;  both American citizens and
foreign nationals  &MD;  may conduct their lives free from the fear of
crime.''
<p>
   Delaying the execution, he said, ``would have the practical effect
of transferring responsibility from the courts of the Commonwealth and
the United States to the International Court.''
<p>
   Lawyers for Breard had argued that a consular official might have
been able to persuade the defendant to plead guilty and seek a life
sentence. But a majority of the Supreme Court justices, in an unsigned
opinion, said there was no legal basis for Breard's claims. They also
said that Virginia's violation of the international treaty probably
did not affect the outcome of Breard's trial.
<p>
   The court's majority was unmoved by last week's decision by the
International Court of Justice ordering the United States to halt the
execution to give them time to review Paraguay's claim on Breard's
behalf. The 15-member international body, also known as the World
Court, has no enforcement mechanism, and the U.S. Supreme Court
rejected the Paraguayan government's argument that the World Court
order was binding on the United States.
<p>
   Gilmore also had to weigh a highly unusual plea from Secretary of
State Madeleine K. Albright, who asked him to put off the execution
because it could harm Americans accused of crimes in foreign
countries. The Vienna Convention on Consular Relations, which Breard
alleges was violated in his case, also guarantees Americans arrested
abroad the right to consult with consular officers.
<p>
   Albright wrote Gilmore on Monday that she was acting ``with great
reluctance'' but she was ``concerned about the possible negative
consequences for the many U.S. citizens who live and travel abroad.''
She reiterated her request Tuesday during a speech at Howard
University. Describing Breard's crime as ``heinous,'' she said she was
seeking to protect ``reciprocity'' to make sure that ``if any of our
citizens find themselves in trouble, they will be accorded their
rights.''
<p>
   An Arlington jury convicted Breard, now 32, of capital murder for
stabbing Dickie, 39, five times in the neck during a sexual assault.
Breard said on the stand that he had killed her because he was under
the influence of a satanic curse. During the penalty phase of the
trial, prosecutors said he had tried to sexually assault two other
women.
</TEXT>

<SLUG> bc-execute-sun </SLUG>
<HEADER> - a2029 04-14 0880 </HEADER>
<PREAMBLE>
bc-execute-sun - a2029
 &UR; (bal) (ATTN: National editors) &QL; 
</PREAMBLE>
<BYLINE> By Lyle Denniston and Mark Matthews </BYLINE>
<CPYRIGHT>
(c) 1998, The Baltimore Sun
</CPYRIGHT>
<HEADLINE>
 &UR; Paraguayan Is Executed in Virginia (Washn) &QL; 
</HEADLINE>
<TEXT>
<p>
   WASHINGTON  &MD;  Virginia put to death a 32-year-old Paraguayan for
murder Tuesday night, after the governor brushed aside international
appeals to spare him and a highly unusual warning from Secretary of
State Madeleine K. Albright that his execution could serve to endanger
Americans abroad.
<p>
   Less than an hour before a scheduled lethal injection, the Supreme
Court set Virginia free to execute the condemned man, Angel Francisco
Breard, by lethal injection for a 1992 murder. The Supreme Court ruled
6-3 that Breard had raised his legal challenge too late.
<p>
   Virginia Gov. James S. Gilmore III, after taking more than 90
minutes after the court decision to consider Breard's last-minute
appeal, refused to delay the execution at a prison facility in
Jarrett, Va.
<p>
   ``Mr. Breard having committed a heinous and depraved murder, and
his guilt being unquestioned, and the legal issues being resolved
against him, and the U.S. Supreme Court having denied the petitions of
Breard and Paraguay, I find no reason to interfere with his
sentence,'' the governor said.
<p>
   In its ruling earlier in the evening, the Supreme Court said the
government of Paraguay had no legal right to ask U.S. courts to spare
Breard's life and had no right to expect U.S. courts to bow to the
wishes of the International Court of Justice that the execution be
postponed.
<p>
   The case reached into the sphere of international law because
Virginia had failed to inform Breard  &MD;  as he had a right to be told
under a treaty signed by 130 nations  &MD;  that he could seek help from
the Paraguayan consulor officer after his arrest for murdering a
neighbor in their apartment building.
<p>
   The International Court of Justice, known informally as the World
Court, has agreed to consider the dispute, which pits Paraguay
directly against the U.S. government and indirectly against the
Virginia state government.
<p>
   The Supreme Court said it was giving ``respectful consideration''
to the World Court's role in the treaty dispute. But the majority
justices said the World Court proceedings could have no effect on the
criminal justice system in the United States.
<p>
   Under U.S. law, the Supreme Court said, Breard's failure to raise
his treaty violation claim while his case was in Virginia's state
courts meant that he could not raise it now in the federal courts.
<p>
   ``It is unfortunate that this matter comes before us while
proceedings are pending before the (World Court) that might have been
brought to that court earlier,'' the court majority said in its
unsigned, eight-page decision.
<p>
   The majority noted that the U.S. government's executive branch was
free to use diplomacy and, in fact, had done so to try to satisfy
Paraguay earlier. It also noted that Albright had asked Virginia's
governor to postpone the execution because of the dispute pending at
the World Court.
<p>
   ``If the governor wishes to wait for the decision of the (World
Court), that is his prerogative,'' the court said. ``But nothing in
our existing case law allows us to make that choice for him.''
<p>
   The court majority included Chief Justice William H. Rehnquist and
five justices, Anthony M. Kennedy, Sandra Day O'Connor, Antonin
Scalia, David Souter and Clarence Thomas. The dissenting justices were
Stephen G. Breyer, Ruth Bader Ginsburg and John Paul Stevens.
<p>
   The secretary of state, in her direct plea to the governor, said,
``The execution of Mr. Breard in the present circumstances could lead
some countries to contend incorrectly that the U.S. does not take
seriously its obligations under the Convention.''
<p>
   Yesterday, Albright said the execution could not only undercut
accepted international rules but also pose dangers to Americans
abroad.
<p>
   ``We are part of an international system in which it is important
to respect the various rules and conventions and laws that have been
created to make that system work,'' Albright told students at Howard
University.
<p>
   ``There are a lot of Americans that travel abroad who sometimes get
into trouble and who need to have the availability of our consular
officers' being able to visit them if they have been jailed unjustly
or even if they've been jailed justly or if they find themselves in
some kind of a difficult situation.''
<p>
   She said her department now has orders ``to make very clear to
state and local officials that they have a responsibility to let a
foreign national know that he or she can, in fact, have consular
advice.''
<p>
   A senior White House official said Albright's intervention may have
blunted some of the negative reaction from Paraguay and neighboring
countries. President Clinton will leave Wednesday night for a state
visit to Chile followed by a summit with the leaders of all the
hemisphere's democracies, including Paraguay.
<p>
   At his trial for the stabbing murder of Ruth Dickie, Breard
confessed to the killing but said he had done so only because his
father-in-law had placed on him a Satanic curse. He has been on
Virginia's death row since he was sentenced in 1993 to die.
<p>
    &UR; Distributed by the Los Angeles Times-Washington Post News Service &QL; 
</TEXT>
